# PART V: GENDER, GENDER-BASED VIOLENCE, AND WOMEN'S EMPOWERMENT

---

*This chapter is the encyclopedic reference for gender policy, gender-based violence, and women's empowerment in the Maldives. It integrates content previously distributed across Part I (Historical Evolution), Part II (Legal Framework), Part III (Institutional Architecture), Part IX (Mental Health), Part XI (Health Systems), Part XII (Housing and Poverty), Part XIV (Climate Vulnerability), Part XV (Cross-Cutting Issues), and Part XVIII-C (CEDAW Periodic Report Draft). Those chapters retain summaries and pointers to this chapter for domain-specific content. The Statistics Compendium (Part XVII), CEDAW Periodic Report Draft (Part XVIII-C), Action Plans Analysis (Part XVI), Appendix G (Development Partners), and Appendix A (Bibliography) remain intact in full – this chapter draws from them with attribution.*

---


# Part 1: Historical Evolution and Cultural Context


## 5.1 Historical Evolution of Gender Relations and Policy in the Maldives


### 5.1.1 Pre-Modern Gender Dynamics (Pre-1965)

Gender relations in the Maldives follow a trajectory that defies easy categorisation. The country presents a paradox noted repeatedly by international observers and domestic researchers alike: women's educational attainment now substantially exceeds men's at secondary and tertiary levels, life expectancy for women exceeds that of men, and the historical record includes female sultanas exercising sovereign authority – yet labour force participation, political representation, and protection from violence remain deeply constrained by social norms, legal asymmetries, and persistent institutional capacity gaps that no legislative reform has yet fully overcome.

Prior to the consolidation of the modern state, Maldivian women occupied a relatively visible role in island-level economic life compared with women in much of South Asia. The Maldives historically exhibited **matrilocal marriage patterns**, where husbands moved to the wife's family island or household – a practice unusual in the Muslim world that gave women a degree of residential stability and social standing within the household economy. Women held property rights and inheritance rights as guaranteed under Islamic law, and female participation in fishing-adjacent economic activities – particularly *mas hiki* (fish drying and processing), weaving, and handicraft production – was substantial and socially recognised. This gave Maldivian women a degree of economic agency unusual in the region, even as it remained within traditionally feminised domains.[^1]

The historical record also documents **female sultanas**: Rehendhi Khadijah, who ruled three separate times in the twelfth century, is the most documented example. The existence of female sovereign authority, while exceptional, demonstrates that Maldivian political culture was not uniformly hostile to women in positions of leadership, even if such positions were confined to the uppermost elite and exercised within advisory structures dominated by male scholars and nobles. The broader population of island women exercised no formal political authority at any level of the pre-modern governance system.[^2]

At the same time, the pre-modern period was characterised by features that embedded gender inequality structurally into daily life. **Divorce** was extremely common – historians and sociologists have documented that the Maldives historically had one of the highest divorce rates in the world, with serial marriage and divorce a common feature of social life, particularly for men who could exercise unilateral *talaq* divorce under Islamic family law through a simple verbal declaration without judicial oversight or financial consequence. The social consequences fell disproportionately on women and children: divorced women returned to their natal island community but faced reduced social standing; children of multiple marriages moved between households with unpredictable stability; and property accumulated during marriage was often disputed without effective enforcement mechanisms. A Guinness World Record was recorded for the Maldives in 2002, when the country's divorce rate stood at 10.97 per 1,000 population – the highest nationally recorded divorce rate in history. Even after decades of family law reform, the divorce rate remained 5.52 per 1,000 in 2024, still among the world's highest.[^3]

**Island-level governance** concentrated power entirely in male hands. The *kadheeb* (island chief), appointed by the sultan, controlled local social administration, dispute resolution, and oversight of community welfare. Women had no formal role in this governance system. Decisions affecting family life – custody arrangements, marital disputes, inheritance – were made by male officials applying Islamic law in forms that, while providing some structural protections for women (notably inheritance rights and the *mahr* dower payment), did not address power asymmetries within marriage or provide remedies for intimate partner violence.

**Islamic education** (*edhuruge*) was gender-differentiated from the outset. Quran school attendance was common for both boys and girls, but higher religious education was exclusively male. The first modern school, Majeediyya School, established in 1927, initially served only boys. The **Aminiya School** opened in 1944 as the first formal educational institution for girls in Malé – one of the earliest girls' schools in the Indian Ocean region – marking the beginning of the gendered institutional educational trajectory that would eventually produce the reverse gender gap documented in contemporary data.[^4]

### 5.1.2 Early Republic Period and International Norms Adoption (1965–1984)

Independence from the British protectorate in 1965, and the establishment of the Second Republic in 1968 under President Ibrahim Nasir, did not immediately transform gender relations. Social welfare, including the welfare of women and children, remained primarily the responsibility of the extended family and the island community. There was no state mechanism for addressing domestic violence, no formal gender equality policy, and no dedicated institution for women's affairs.

The most significant development of this period for women was the **expansion of girls' education**. Under Nasir and later under Gayoom (from 1978), a national commitment to building at least one primary school on every inhabited island dramatically expanded girls' access to primary education. By the mid-1980s, girls' primary enrolment rates approached parity with boys'. The investment was transformational in its long-term consequences: the cohorts of girls educated in the 1970s and 1980s would become the generation of educated women who drove the domestic violence awareness movement, the civil society organisations, and the legislative advocacy of the 2000s.

**CEDAW ratification on 1 July 1993** marked a watershed in the formal international commitments of the Maldivian state on gender equality. The Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Discrimination Against Women had been adopted by the UN General Assembly in 1979; the Maldives was not among the earliest ratifiers but joined the Convention in 1993 with significant reservations.[^5] The government declared that it would comply with CEDAW provisions only insofar as they were not inconsistent with Islamic Shariah, effectively creating a blanket escape clause over the Convention's application to family law. Specific reservations were registered on **Article 7(a)** (the right of women to vote and stand for election, interpreted to exclude women from certain religious advisory bodies) and **Article 16** (equality in marriage and family relations, where Islamic law governs marriage, divorce, and inheritance in ways that structurally differ from the CEDAW standard). These reservations have not been withdrawn as of 2025, despite repeated calls from the CEDAW Committee in its Concluding Observations, including the most recent from November 2021.[^6]

*Note on dating:* Some earlier documents in this encyclopedia reference CEDAW ratification as 1984. The correct date, per the UN Treaty Collection and the Government of Maldives' own CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report (2025), is 1 July 1993.

### 5.1.3 The 1990s: Institutional Beginnings Without Legislative Foundation

The 1990s saw the first institutional acknowledgment that gender inequality was a distinct policy concern requiring a state response. Progress was slow and driven primarily by international norm diffusion – through UN conferences, CEDAW reporting obligations, and development partner programming – rather than domestic advocacy in an environment where civil society was constrained.

**1991:** CRC ratification created indirect pressure to address women's issues, since the Convention explicitly mandates non-discrimination affecting girl children and addresses family violence affecting children in its framework.

**1993:** Growing recognition of gender inequality led to the establishment of the **Ministry of Youth, Women and Social Welfare** – the first formal institutional acknowledgment that women's welfare was a distinct government responsibility, not merely a subsidiary of family affairs. The ministry had no dedicated legislation to enforce, no legal powers to intervene in domestic violence situations, and no regulatory framework for gender equality. Its primary functions were coordination of international reporting obligations and delivery of limited social welfare programmes.

**Mid-to-late 1990s:** Women's affairs remained under-resourced and without dedicated legislation. The concept of the state as an actor in preventing domestic violence or protecting women from intimate partner abuse had no legal basis. Divorce and family disputes were handled through Islamic family law administered by the Family Court. Social norms strongly discouraged women from seeking external – particularly state – intervention in family matters. Reporting domestic violence to police was extremely uncommon; police had no legal framework for intervention in what was understood as a private family matter; and the social cost of disclosure (family shame, community stigma, loss of housing if dependent on husband) was high.

The period also saw the beginning of **Migration and urbanisation** dynamics that would reshape gender relations over the following decades. As Male' grew from a predominantly elite city to the destination for island-educated youth seeking employment and further education, patterns of social supervision and community control that had constrained women's options in small islands began to give way. Urban women in Male' gained access to employment in government, education, and health services that was unavailable on most islands. This contributed to a slow but important shift in women's economic independence – and eventually, their willingness to report and leave abusive situations – that analysts of Maldivian DV trends have identified as a key structural factor.

### 5.1.4 The 2006 National DV Survey: A Policy-Defining Moment

**2006** marks a key moment in the modern history of gender policy in the Maldives: the publication of the **Maldives Study on Women's Health and Life Experiences**, the first systematic national survey of domestic violence and intimate partner violence. The survey was conducted by the Ministry of Gender and Family with international technical support, using the WHO multi-country study methodology developed by Emma Fulu. The findings were stark: **approximately one in three Maldivian women had experienced physical and/or sexual violence from an intimate partner**. For women in Male', the figure was even higher at 16.3% reporting childhood sexual abuse, with 12.2% nationally.[^7]

This documentation – consistent with global prevalence rates in countries that had systematically measured, but startling to policymakers who had not previously seen the numbers – provided the evidentiary basis for arguing that domestic violence was not an individual or family aberration but a systemic social problem requiring a policy response. The 2006 study was cited in every subsequent piece of gender policy analysis, every CEDAW report, and every legislative debate on GBV legislation. It became the foundational empirical anchor of gender advocacy in the Maldives.

Also in 2006, the **Family Protection Authority (FPA)** was established under the Ministry of Gender and Family to coordinate GBV response and prevention services. The FPA's creation predated the legal framework it would eventually operate under – the DVPA would not be enacted until 2012 – and it operated in the interim through ministerial mandate without specific statutory authority. This institutional-before-legislation sequencing meant that the FPA spent its early years building community awareness and referral pathways in an environment where the legal tools needed to act on disclosures did not exist.[^8]

### 5.1.5 Democratic Transition and the First Wave of GBV Legislation (2008–2015)

The 2008 democratic transition brought a significant shift in gender policy discourse. The **2008 Constitution** contained the first explicit constitutional mandate to actively pursue gender equality, with Article 36(a) – "the state shall ensure the rights of women, and work to achieve equality between men and women" – creating a positive obligation rather than merely a prohibition on discrimination.

The **Nasheed administration (2008–2012)** accelerated gender mainstreaming:
- The **Single Parent Allowance** provided monthly cash transfers to single parents, the overwhelming majority of whom were women, establishing the state's role in cushioning the economic consequences of marital breakdown
- The **National Child Helpline (1412)** was expanded to cover domestic violence calls
- Push for dedicated GBV legislation reached legislative drafting stage, though the DVPA was ultimately enacted only after Nasheed's departure in February 2012

**The Domestic Violence Prevention Act 2012 (Act 3/2012)** – enacted on 28 April 2012 during the political transition period following Nasheed's forced resignation – represented the culmination of six years of post-2006 advocacy. It was the first law in Maldivian history to specifically address domestic violence as a distinct legal matter, establish protection orders, mandate survivor support services, and create a legal obligation for state agencies to respond. However, a critical point that was frequently mischaracterised in early commentary: the DVPA did **not** criminalise domestic violence. DV under the DVPA remained a matter for civil remedies – protection orders, exclusion orders – rather than criminal prosecution. DV acts that constituted assault, battery, or other criminal offences were handled (in theory) under the Penal Code, but the DVPA itself created a civil framework, not a criminal one. This distinction has significant implications for the criminal justice response data: the "DV cases" tracked by the FPA are civil complaint cases, not criminal charges.[^9]

The **Yameen administration (2013–2018)** continued the legislative trajectory on gender:
- **Sexual Offences Act 2014 (Act 17/2014):** Modernised sexual offences law with contemporary definitions of rape (penetration without consent), sexual assault, and child sexual abuse, providing significantly higher penalties than previous law
- **Gender Equality Act 2016 (Act 18/2016):** A comprehensive framework law mandating gender mainstreaming across public institutions, establishing WDCs on every inhabited island as formal governance bodies, requiring Gender Equality Action Plans from all ministries, and introducing gender-responsive budgeting as a mandatory government process

However, critical gaps remained: The **marital rape exception** in the 2014 SOA categorically excluded sexual intercourse within marriage from the definition of rape. Section 130(b) of the Penal Code maintained a rebuttable presumption of consent within marriage, meaning that marital rape was not actionable as rape. UNFPA's 2023 Human Rights and Gender Review of GBV-Related Laws documented this gap explicitly, describing marital rape as "incompletely criminalised" under the legislative framework in force before 2021.[^10]

### 5.1.6 The Solih Administration: Marital Rape Criminalised, Local Quota Enacted (2018–2023)

The Solih administration (2018–2023) delivered two landmark achievements in gender law:

**1. The 2021 Amendment to the Sexual Offences Act** criminalised marital rape for the first time in Maldivian history. The amendment, passed by the People's Majlis in 2021, explicitly removed the marital exception from the definition of rape, directly responding to the 2021 CEDAW Concluding Observations (CEDAW/C/MDV/CO/6) that had identified this gap as a priority concern. The amendment aligned Maldivian law with international standards. In practice, documented prosecutions for marital rape remained rare through 2025, reflecting underreporting driven by stigma, fear of family consequences, and limited victim awareness of the new provision – but the legislative change removed a major legal barrier that had made thousands of women in abusive marriages invisible to the criminal law.[^11]

**2. The 33% Women's Quota for Local Councils** – enacted ahead of the April 2021 local government elections – produced the largest advance in women's political representation in Maldivian history. Prior to the quota, women held approximately 4% of island and atoll council seats. The 2021 elections produced women holding approximately one-third of all council seats – a transformation achieved by legislative mandate that voluntary measures had completely failed to deliver over three decades of democratic elections.[^12]

Other developments in this period:
- **Prevention of Family Violence Act 2020:** Strengthened and expanded the 2012 DVPA framework, broadening the definition of family violence, extending protection to relationships outside formal marriage, and clarifying enforcement duties for law enforcement agencies
- **AIM Project 2022–2025:** The "Accountability and Integration Matters" initiative – a joint EU-UNDP-MoSFD project – was launched specifically to address the GBV justice pathway's catastrophic attrition rate: only approximately 3% of DV/GBV cases reported to police were being submitted for prosecution
- **COVID-19 gender impacts:** Women were twice as likely as men to stop work during the pandemic; only 15% of emergency COVID-19 relief loans went to women despite women comprising approximately half the population; DV reporting to the FPA surged during lockdowns as victims were confined with perpetrators

### 5.1.7 The Muizzu Administration: Current Directions (2023–Present)

The Muizzu administration (November 2023–present) has emphasised family-centred social policy within an Islamic framework. The **Ministry of Social and Family Development (MoSFD)** – renamed from the previous Ministry of Gender, Family and Social Services – holds gender equality among its portfolio responsibilities alongside child protection, elderly welfare, and social protection. Key gender portfolio components remain: FPA operations continue, WDCs continue to function, and the AIM Project continues its 2022–2025 implementation cycle.

Women's political representation reached a **historic low** in the 2024 parliamentary elections: only **4 women out of 87 members** of the People's Majlis (4.6%) were elected – the lowest female parliamentary representation in Maldivian democratic history, a significant regression even from the already-low 6/85 seats (5.9%) won by women in 2014.[^13] No legislative proposal for a parliamentary quota had achieved passage as of 2025, despite the 2021 CEDAW Concluding Observations calling explicitly for such a measure.

---


## 5.2 Women in Maldivian History: Beyond the Sultanas


### 5.102.1 Historical Women of Note

The historiography of the Maldives – primarily preserved in the *Radavalhi* (royal chronicle) and oral traditions – records several women beyond the famous sultanas who shaped Maldivian society in ways relevant to understanding the roots of contemporary gender dynamics:

**The female sultanas:** Rehendhi Khadijah (12th century, three separate reigns), Sultanate records of two additional female rulers – represent not just individual achievement but evidence that Maldivian political culture was not uniformly and always hostile to women's authority, even if female sovereign rule was exceptional.

**Island women in oral tradition:** Local island oral traditions document the roles of women in island life – as craftswomen (*thundu kunaa* weavers), as fish processors, as keepers of household religious practice. These oral records suggest a historical gender dynamic that allocated women economic roles and social standing within designated domains – not equality, but not complete invisibility.

**Women in the independence movement:** While the formal leadership of the 1965 independence movement and the subsequent republican governments was male, women participated in the civil service and social service sectors that the early post-independence state built. The first generation of formally educated Maldivian women – educated at Aminiya in the 1950s-1960s – became the teachers, nurses, and civil servants who staffed the expanding state apparatus.

**Early activists:** The women who founded Hope for Women in the 2000s drew on personal experience – as survivors, as practitioners, as observers of systemic failure – that was shaped by the same social conditions documented in the Fulu 2007 study. Their names (Aishath Aniya and others whose public roles are documented in organisational history) belong in any comprehensive history of Maldivian women's rights.

### 5.102.2 The Historiography of Maldivian Women

The historical record of Maldivian women's lives – beyond the extraordinary women who appear in royal chronicles or post-independence official records – is sparse. This historiographical gap itself reflects a gender inequality: the lives, work, and experiences of ordinary Maldivian women were not considered worth recording by the men who produced the historical record.

Feminist historiography in the Maldivian context – recovering and recording women's historical experiences through oral history, community archaeology, and the analysis of material culture (handicrafts, fishing equipment, household artifacts) – is an emerging field. MNU's research capacity, combined with civil society interest, could develop a serious Maldivian feminist historiography that provides the historical depth missing from current gender policy analysis.[^538]

---


## 5.3 The Women's Rights Movement in the Maldives: Historical Trajectory


### 5.68.1 The First Wave: 1970s-1990s

The Maldivian women's rights movement – if it can be characterised as a movement – has been more episodic than sustained, reflecting the constraints of civil society development in a country with a historically limited public sphere for political organisation.

The earliest advocates for women's rights operated within government – women who had benefited from educational expansion and entered government employment in health, education, and welfare sectors in the 1970s and 1980s, and who used their institutional positions to raise awareness of women's health needs and educational access. Their work was primarily within the state apparatus rather than through independent civil society.

The 1990s saw the beginning of a more organised women's advocacy dimension, shaped by: CEDAW ratification in 1993 and the associated reporting obligations; participation in the 1995 Beijing Fourth World Conference on Women and the Beijing Platform for Action; and the emergence of a small number of women professionals in Malé with international exposure to gender equality discourse.

### 5.68.2 The Second Wave: 2000s – The Survey Moment

The publication of the Fulu 2007 study was the catalytic moment for the Maldivian women's rights movement. For the first time, concrete, nationally representative data documented that DV was not an individual tragedy but a structural condition affecting one in three women. The survey data transformed the advocacy environment: where previously advocates had argued from anecdote and international evidence, they now had Maldivian data that could not be dismissed.

The period 2006-2012 saw rapid development of civil society gender advocacy:
- **Hope for Women (Aadheys)** was established as a dedicated DV survivor support and advocacy organisation
- **The Family Legal Clinic** began providing legal aid services to women navigating family law
- **Transparency Maldives** and **the Human Rights Commission** engaged with gender equality as a rights and accountability issue
- The political transition of 2008 – which established competitive multi-party democracy – created both opportunities (greater space for civil society advocacy) and risks (political party dynamics that made gender equality advocacy instrumentally complex)

The DVPA (2012) was the direct product of this advocacy period – the translation of the Fulu 2007 evidence and Hope for Women's service experience into a legislative framework that civil society advocates had been calling for since 2008.[^484]

### 5.68.3 The Third Wave: 2016-2021 – Legislative Acceleration

The 2016-2021 period was the most legislative productive period in Maldivian gender equality history:
- GEA (2016)
- Family Act marriage age amendment (2016)
- Local council gender quota (2020-2021)
- Marital rape criminalisation (2021)

Each of these advances represented the outcome of sustained advocacy over multiple years – the GEA after a decade of CEDAW-driven demand; the marital rape criminalisation after years of international pressure and domestic survivor advocacy; the quota after two local council elections with near-zero female representation demonstrating the need for structural intervention.

The pattern of this period – advocacy organisations (Hope for Women, MDN, HRCM) providing the sustained pressure; development partners (UNFPA, UNDP, EU) providing technical support and international legitimacy; and specific political moments creating the window for legislative passage – is the template for future reform.[^485]

### 5.68.4 Current Phase: Implementation Over Legislation

The current phase (2021-present) is characterised by a shift from legislative advocacy to implementation advocacy – pushing for the laws and policies already in place to actually work. Hope for Women's advocacy now focuses as much on FPA resource adequacy as on legislative gaps. MDN's monitoring work tracks not just the number of women in councils but whether women councillors are given genuine authority. HRCM's gender assessments examine implementation failures rather than legislative deficits.

This shift from legislation to implementation is a maturing of the Maldivian women's rights movement – recognition that the work does not end when a law is passed, but continues through the harder work of ensuring that institutions deliver on legal commitments.

The challenges of implementation advocacy are different from legislative advocacy: they require sustained engagement with bureaucratic processes, data collection and analysis, and coalition-building with civil servants (who may be reluctant participants) rather than only with politicians (who respond to political pressure). Civil society organisations in the Maldives are developing these capacities, but the resource base for sustained implementation monitoring is limited.[^486]

---


# Part 2: Constitutional and Legal Framework


## 5.4 Legal Framework for Gender Equality and GBV: Provisions in Full Detail


### 5.2.1 Constitutional Foundations

The **Constitution of the Republic of Maldives (2008)** contains several provisions directly relevant to gender equality and women's rights.

**Article 17** establishes equality before the law and prohibits discrimination on grounds including sex: "Everyone is entitled to the rights and freedoms included in this Chapter without discrimination of any kind, including race, national origin, colour, sex, age, mental or physical disability, political or other opinion, property, birth or other status, or native island." The inclusion of sex as a prohibited ground is the constitutional anchor for all gender equality legislation.[^14]

**Article 20** provides that "individuals may not be discriminated against on the basis of the following grounds: sex, origin, religion, or social status." This provision adds explicit sex-based non-discrimination beyond the enumeration in Article 17.

**Article 36** establishes the positive state obligations:
- Article 36(a): "The state shall ensure the rights of women, and work to achieve equality between men and women" – this is an active duty, not merely a prohibition
- Article 36(b): "The state shall protect the rights of children, young people, the elderly, and disadvantaged groups"
- Article 36(c): "The state shall endeavour to provide for the basic necessities of persons who are destitute or unable to maintain themselves"

**Article 70** establishes the right of all citizens aged 18 and above to vote, regardless of sex, and to stand for election.

**Article 23(e)** includes "freedom from sexual abuse and sexual exploitation" among the rights specifically protected.

**Constitutional tension with Islamic law:** The Constitution also provides in the Preamble and Article 10 that Islam is the state religion and that no law contrary to the principles of Islam may be enacted. This creates a structural tension with the gender equality provisions, particularly in family law. Courts and the government have generally resolved this tension by treating Islamic law provisions as a constraint on the scope of gender equality legislation in matters of marriage, divorce, custody, and inheritance – the areas covered by the reservation to Article 16 of CEDAW.[^15]

### 5.2.2 Domestic Violence Prevention Act 2012 (Act 3/2012) – Full Analysis

The **Domestic Violence Prevention Act 2012 (DVPA, Act 3/2012)** is the principal legislation governing state response to domestic violence. It was enacted on 28 April 2012 and came into full effect following promulgation of implementing regulations.

**Scope and definitions:** The DVPA defines domestic violence broadly to include physical, sexual, emotional, and financial abuse occurring within a "family relationship" – defined to include spouses, former spouses, persons who cohabit or have cohabited, persons who have a child in common, and family members including parents, siblings, and extended family. The breadth of this definition was significant for the Maldives, where extended family cohabitation is common and violence between relatives other than spouses is not uncommon.

**Critical legal character – civil not criminal:** The DVPA is a civil law framework. It provides for civil protection orders (orders restricting the perpetrator from contacting or approaching the victim), exclusion orders (removing the perpetrator from the shared home), and custody arrangements pending resolution of a DV matter. The Act does not create new criminal offences of "domestic violence" – acts of physical violence within the family remain criminal under the Penal Code as assault and battery, but they are not prosecuted as a distinct DV offence category. This distinction is frequently misunderstood in policy documentation, which sometimes describes DV as "criminalised" when in fact the DVPA addresses it through civil mechanisms.[^16]

**Mandatory reporting obligations:** The DVPA establishes mandatory reporting duties for professionals including health workers, social workers, teachers, and police. Any person working in a professional capacity who suspects domestic violence is obligated to report to the FPA. This was intended to create institutional detection pathways beyond victim self-reporting. In practice, mandatory reporting compliance has been uneven, particularly in outer atolls where professional understanding of the requirement and consequences of non-compliance are poorly understood.

**Protection orders:** The DVPA establishes a system of protection orders issued by the Family Court on application by the victim or the FPA. Protection orders can include prohibitions on contacting the victim, approaching specified locations, and possession of weapons. Emergency protection orders can be issued ex parte (without notice to the perpetrator) for up to 21 days pending a full hearing. The Family Court processes these applications, with the DVPA requiring courts to consider the victim's safety as the paramount concern.

**Implementation gap – the Male' concentration problem:** The FPA, which receives and processes DV complaints and facilitates protection order applications, operated with only **three dedicated DV caseworkers for the Greater Male' area** as of 2024 – an area containing approximately 40% of the national population, comprising Male', Hulhumale', and Villimale'. No dedicated DV caseworkers are stationed at atoll level. This staffing level – three trained professionals serving 200,000+ residents – is critically insufficient given the volume of cases: 696 cases received by the FPA in 2021; 537 in 2024.[^17]

**Prevention of Family Violence Act 2020:** This Act expanded and strengthened the 2012 DVPA framework, broadening the definition of family violence to include "coercive control" patterns (not merely discrete acts of violence), extending coverage to relationships outside formal marriage including partners who have cohabited, and clarifying law enforcement duties including mandatory attendance at the scene of DV reports. The 2020 Act represents an important legal development but, as with the DVPA, its effectiveness is constrained by enforcement capacity.

### 5.2.3 Sexual Offences Act 2014 (Act 17/2014) – Provisions, Evidence Requirements, and Gaps

The **Sexual Offences Act 2014 (Act 17/2014)** is the principal legislation criminalising sexual violence. It replaced earlier provisions that had been spread across the Penal Code and Special Provisions Act, providing more comprehensive and contemporary definitions.

**Definition of rape:** The SOA defines rape as sexual penetration without consent – a significant modernisation from the earlier definitional framework. The consent-based definition in principle covers all forms of non-consensual penetration regardless of the relationship between perpetrator and victim.

**Marital rape: The 2021 Amendment:** Before the 2021 amendment, Section 130(b) of the Penal Code maintained a rebuttable presumption of consent within marriage, and the SOA as originally enacted contained a provision categorically excluding sexual acts within marriage from its rape provisions. This marital exception meant that non-consensual sexual intercourse by a husband with his wife was not legally rape – a position explicitly criticised in the 2021 CEDAW Concluding Observations. The **2021 Amendment to the Sexual Offences Act** removed this exception, explicitly criminalising marital rape for the first time. As of 2025, the 2021 amendment is in force. Prosecutions for marital rape under the amended law remain rare, reflecting underreporting, evidentiary challenges, victim safety concerns during prosecution, and gaps in police training on the new provisions.[^18]

**Section 52 – Evidence Requirements:** A critical provision affecting prosecution rates is Section 52 of the SOA, which specifies the types of evidence that may be used in sexual offence prosecutions. Under Section 52, prosecution may rely on five categories of specified evidence: testimony of the victim, testimony of witnesses, medical evidence, forensic evidence, and the confessions of the accused. This enumerated evidence framework has been interpreted by police and prosecutors in ways that create high evidentiary thresholds. Sexual offences – which typically occur in private without witnesses – are inherently difficult to prosecute under any evidentiary system, but the SOA's Section 52 framework has been cited by UNFPA's 2023 legal review as a structural barrier to prosecution.[^19]

**Section 53 – Delayed Reporting and Prior Relationship:** Section 53 permits the court to consider delayed reporting or a pre-existing relationship between perpetrator and victim in assessing the credibility of the complainant. In practice, this provision has functioned to disadvantage complainants in intimate partner sexual violence cases – precisely the most common scenario – since the prior relationship between spouses or partners can be used to cast doubt on the survivor's account. The 2023 UNFPA Human Rights and Gender Review of GBV-Related Laws identified this as a provision requiring reform.[^20]

**Past sexual history evidence:** Under the SOA's evidentiary framework, a complainant's past sexual history is admissible in proceedings, a provision inconsistent with international best practice in sexual violence law (which typically shields past sexual history from introduction as evidence). The admissibility of past sexual history is particularly damaging given the intersection with Maldivian social norms around female "respectability."

**Penalties:** Rape carries a maximum penalty of 25 years' imprisonment and a minimum of 10 years. Sexual assault carries 7–15 years. Child sexual abuse offences carry enhanced penalties. The penalty framework is severe on paper; the problem lies in the prosecution and conviction rates, not the sentencing tariff.

### 5.2.4 Gender Equality Act 2016 (Act 18/2016) – Full Analysis

The **Gender Equality Act 2016 (Act 18/2016)**, enacted on 30 June 2016, is the comprehensive framework legislation for gender equality in the Maldives. It is the domestic law instrument most closely aligned with CEDAW's definition of discrimination.

**Definition of gender discrimination:** The GEA defines gender discrimination as any distinction, exclusion, or restriction made on the basis of gender that has the purpose or effect of nullifying or impairing the recognition, enjoyment, or exercise of human rights and fundamental freedoms, in the political, economic, social, cultural, civil, or any other field. This definition substantially mirrors CEDAW Article 1 and importantly covers both direct and indirect discrimination – meaning that facially neutral policies that have disproportionately negative impacts on women are also captured.

**Private sector application:** A significant innovation over constitutional non-discrimination provisions is the GEA's extension of the prohibition on gender discrimination to the private sector, including employment and business activities. Prior to 2016, the non-discrimination framework operated primarily in relation to state actors; the GEA extended obligations to private employers, educational institutions, and service providers.

**Gender Equality Action Plans:** The GEA requires all government institutions – ministries, departments, and state agencies – to develop and implement Gender Equality Action Plans (GEAPs) detailing how they will advance gender equality within their sectoral mandate. These plans are submitted to the Gender Equality Division of MoSFD and are supposed to inform annual budget allocations. As of 2024, MoSFD's internal audit found that fewer than 40% of ministries had submitted gender-disaggregated budget analyses for fiscal years 2022–2023, and GRB compliance remained inconsistent.[^21]

**Gender-Responsive Budgeting (GRB):** The GEA mandates GRB as a process across all government ministries, requiring them to analyse how budget allocations affect women and men differently. With UNDP and UN Women capacity-building support, some progress has been made in developing GRB methodology and training budget officers. The ADB's Strengthening Gender Inclusive Initiatives Project (2024 onwards) includes a specific component on integrating GRB into government systems as a priority intervention.

**Women's Development Committees:** The GEA formalised the legal basis for WDCs, previously established under the Decentralisation Act in a weaker form. The GEA explicitly describes WDCs' mandate to address gender-specific community needs, facilitate women's participation in local decision-making, and serve as a referral point for gender equality complaints. The Act requires island councils to provide support to WDCs, but falls short of mandating independent budgets – a gap that has significantly constrained WDC effectiveness (see §5.3.4).

**Affirmative action provisions:** The GEA explicitly permits temporary special measures to achieve substantive equality – affirming that quota mechanisms and targeted programmes are consistent with the non-discrimination framework. This provision provided the legal basis for the subsequent 33% women's council quota.

**The 43 institutions / zero complaints paradox:** One of the most revealing failures of GEA implementation concerns the workplace harassment complaint mechanism. The Act requires public and private institutions above a certain size to establish internal complaint committees for gender discrimination and harassment. The HRCM monitored compliance in a 2019 assessment. Forty-three institutions established formal complaint mechanisms as required. The number of complaints received by those mechanisms: **zero**. This was not because gender discrimination and harassment did not occur – the qualitative evidence of women's experiences in the workplace (documented in surveys and focus groups) is consistent and substantial – but because formal complaint mechanisms in the Maldivian institutional context are not trusted, not used, and do not provide safety for complainants.[^22]

### 5.2.5 Prevention of Sexual Harassment Act 2014

The **Prevention of Sexual Harassment Act 2014** requires employers to establish written anti-harassment policies and internal complaint committees. All employers, public and private, above a minimum workforce threshold are covered. Employers are required to post information about the policy and the complaint procedure in the workplace. The HRCM surveys found women consistently reporting sexual harassment in workplaces – particularly in government offices, schools, and tourism-adjacent employment – as a common experience, treated as "just the way things are." The disconnect between the formal requirements of the 2014 Act and the lived experience of Maldivian working women is one of the most striking examples of implementation failure in the gender policy framework.

### 5.2.6 Family Act (Law No. 4/2000) – Marriage, Divorce, Custody, and Maintenance

The **Family Act (Law No. 4/2000)** governs marriage, divorce, child custody, maintenance, and related family law matters. It was introduced specifically to address the extremely high and socially disruptive divorce rates of the late 1990s by bringing judicial oversight to a process that had previously been largely unilateral.

**Minimum marriage age and exceptions:** The Family Act sets the minimum marriage age at 18. However, **Section 4(b)** creates a judicial exception allowing marriage below 18 where the Family Court determines it is in the child's interest. This exception has been used: ADB data from 2025 documents a child marriage rate of 2.2% before age 18. The 2014 Census recorded 106 children aged 15–17 who were married, of whom 58.5% were girls. Child marriages have been documented primarily in outer atolls where court supervision of marriages is less rigorous and social and economic pressures toward early marriage remain.[^23]

**Wali requirement:** Under the Family Act, a woman's marriage requires the participation of a *wali* (male marriage guardian) – typically her father, brother, or another male relative – who provides formal consent. This requirement derives from classical Islamic jurisprudence and is applied in the Maldives as a legal formality for marriage registration. In practice, the wali requirement creates a structural dependency of women on male family members for accessing marriage, a dependency that can be weaponised to block or facilitate marriages against women's interests.

**Polygamy:** Polygamous marriage – permitting a husband to have up to four wives simultaneously – is legal under Maldivian law subject to judicial approval and fulfilment of conditions drawn from Islamic jurisprudence, including the ability to treat wives equitably. Family Court statistics document 165 polygamous marriages in 2015 and 157 in 2016. While these numbers are relatively small in absolute terms, polygamy as a structural possibility embedded in law has significant effects: it creates economic insecurity for existing wives; it affects custody arrangements; and it reinforces the legal framework's asymmetrical treatment of women's and men's rights within marriage.[^24]

**Tahlil marriage ("chain breaking"):** Islamic jurisprudence provides that a man who has divorced his wife three times may not remarry her unless she first marries another man and that marriage is consummated, after which she may divorce and remarry the first husband. This provision – known as *tahlil* or *nikah halala* – has been documented in the Maldivian context, creating a practice where women are temporarily married (and must have sexual intercourse with) another man to enable remarriage with a former husband. This practice is deeply degrading to women's bodily autonomy and has been documented by HRCM as occurring in some Maldivian communities.[^25]

**Divorce procedures and asymmetry:** The Family Act brought judicial oversight to divorce: divorces must now be registered with the Family Court and processed through a formal procedure. However, the procedural asymmetry between husband-initiated and wife-initiated divorce remains stark. A husband may initiate divorce through *talaq* (unilateral repudiation) through the court, a straightforward process that, even through court channels, carries relatively low procedural requirements. The cost of divorce proceedings for a husband was documented at MVR 100 in formal fees. A wife seeking divorce must apply through *khul'* (divorce with potential forfeiture of the dower payment) or *faskh* (divorce on grounds of fault, including violence, neglect, or prolonged absence) – processes that are more complex, slower, and often more expensive. The result is that women in abusive or unwanted marriages face greater procedural and financial barriers to divorce than men.[^26]

**Divorce statistics and the Guinness Record:** The 2002 Guinness World Record for the world's highest divorce rate – 10.97 per 1,000 population – was set by the Maldives. Statistical Yearbook data for 2016 records 5,488 marriages against 3,417 divorces in a single year. By 2024, the national divorce rate had declined to 5.52 per 1,000 but remained among the highest globally. UNDP's 2011 assessment described "a typical Maldivian woman reported to marry three times by age 30." DJA 2019 data shows that 58% of female divorcees and 59% of male divorcees are in the 26–39 age group, with a combined divorce rate of 70% for individuals aged 18–39. The Census 2022 found that the proportion of divorced women exceeded divorced men by 42.86% – reflecting that divorced women in the Maldives have lower rates of remarriage than divorced men, leaving women to carry the social and economic consequences of marital breakdown in higher proportions.[^27]

**Custody provisions – Section 42:** Section 42 of the Family Act establishes that mothers are generally awarded care (*hadhanah*) of young children after divorce, with fathers retaining guardianship (*wilayah*) rights over major life decisions. This framework gives mothers day-to-day care responsibility while fathers retain formal authority over education, religion, and major decisions. However, Section 42 includes conditions under which a mother loses her priority for custody: (a) if she marries a person who is not the child's *mahram* (non-relative, non-family-member), and (b) if she moves to a different island or country. These conditions are structurally discriminatory: they punish women for remarrying (a socially normal activity for both sexes) and for migrating for employment or family reasons. A review of 24 High Court custody appeal cases found that every mother who had successfully maintained custody in contested proceedings was unmarried – the remarriage condition had been applied to transfer custody in every contested case where the mother had remarried.[^28]

**Maintenance enforcement:** The Family Act requires non-custodial fathers to pay child maintenance. Enforcement, however, is weak. As of the period covered in this encyclopedia, MVR 2 million in child maintenance payments was reported as unclaimed at the Family Court – not because maintenance had been awarded and paid, but because maintenance had been awarded and not collected, reflecting either non-payment by fathers or non-pursuit by mothers for social reasons. Only 8 prenuptial agreements had ever been registered with the Maldivian courts, reflecting both legal unfamiliarity with the instrument and cultural resistance to pre-nuptial financial planning within marriage. The Musawah (2018) jurisprudential review noted that reform of discriminatory family law provisions is jurisprudentially possible within the *mu'amalat* framework of Islamic law – that is, that reform is not inconsistent with Islamic law per se – but that political will for reform has not materialised.[^29]

### 5.2.7 Penal Code Provisions Affecting Women

**Zina and Flogging:** The Penal Code of Maldives retains criminal penalties for *zina* – extramarital sexual intercourse – as a *hadd* offence under Islamic criminal law. Section 411(d) provides for 100 lashes as the punishment for zina. The application of this provision is heavily gendered: women constitute approximately **85% of those receiving flogging sentences** for zina, as documented by the HRCM in 2022. This disparity reflects the gendered evidence dynamic: pregnancy outside marriage is itself evidence of zina for a woman, meaning that women who become pregnant as a result of rape may be prosecuted for zina on the basis of the pregnancy. Men can only be convicted if there is independent witness or confession evidence, creating an inherently gender-discriminatory enforcement pattern.[^30]

**Criminalisation of pregnancy outside wedlock:** The interaction between the zina provision and pregnancy is a serious human rights concern in the Maldivian gender legal framework: a woman who is raped and becomes pregnant faces potential prosecution for zina based on the evidence of the pregnancy itself. This creates a deeply perverse incentive structure – rape victims who become pregnant may be simultaneously victimised and prosecuted – and almost certainly contributes to underreporting of rape, underreporting of pregnancy outside marriage, and concealment of births. The CEDAW Committee's 2021 Concluding Observations expressly identified this intersection as a priority concern requiring urgent legislative reform.[^31]

**Abortion law:** The current legal position on abortion in Maldives is restrictive. Under the post-2014 Penal Code framework, termination of pregnancy is not permitted under law except in cases of risk to the life of the mother. This represents a more restrictive position than some earlier ministerial health guidelines that had allowed termination on additional grounds (including foetal anomaly and for victims of rape and incest), and creates particular distress for rape survivors who become pregnant. The Thalassaemia Control Act (4/2012) separately provides for access to prenatal diagnosis and – within the 120-day window – for termination in cases of thalassaemia major diagnosis. The tension between these provisions and the Penal Code framework has not been legally resolved and creates uncertainty for medical providers.[^32]

### 5.2.8 Inheritance and Property Law

Under Islamic inheritance law as applied in Maldives, daughters receive one-half the share of a son from a parent's estate. This gender-differentiated inheritance structure is explicitly covered by the reservation to Article 16 of CEDAW and reflects the intersection of Maldivian family law with classical Islamic jurisprudence. The practical consequence is that women have structurally lower levels of inherited asset ownership than men, contributing to the documented pattern that land and property in the Maldives is predominantly registered in male names. This property ownership gap has cascading effects: women seeking SDFC business loans face collateral requirements that land in male names cannot satisfy; women leaving abusive marriages have less independent asset base; and women's financial security in old age is structurally lower than men's.

No official tracking exists of gender disaggregation in property and land ownership – the Statistics Compendium identifies this as a major data gap.

### 5.2.9 Employment Act 2008 (Act 2/2008) – Gender Provisions

The **Employment Act (Act 2/2008)** provides foundational labour law protections with several gender-specific provisions:

**Non-discrimination:** The Act prohibits discrimination in employment on grounds of sex in hiring, terms and conditions, promotion, and dismissal.

**Maternity leave:** 60 days of paid maternity leave, funded by the employer. This provision applies to the formal sector. There is no state-funded maternity insurance scheme, meaning that small employers – who employ many women in service industries – face a direct financial disincentive when hiring women of childbearing age, contributing to informal discrimination in recruitment. No paternity leave entitlement is currently mandated in law, limiting the redistribution of childcare responsibilities to fathers and reinforcing the social expectation that childcare is exclusively a female burden.[^33]

**Anti-harassment provisions:** The Act includes provisions against workplace harassment. Enforcement capacity is limited, and the interaction with the Prevention of Sexual Harassment Act 2014 creates some definitional overlap.

**Domestic worker gap:** Domestic workers – a sector employing significant numbers of both Maldivian and foreign women – are not fully covered by the Employment Act's protections. Domestic workers are excluded from formal minimum wage, leave entitlement, and anti-harassment frameworks, leaving women working as domestic employees in a legal void particularly exposed to exploitation.

**Unpaid care work:** The Employment Act does not address the significant unpaid care economy. DHS 2016-17 data indicates that women spend 19 hours per week on unpaid care and domestic work [Note: DHS 2016-17 reports women's unpaid care work as 19 hours per week; verify unit in original source.], compared to 8.1 hours for men. This 2.3:1 ratio in unpaid labour burden is not recognised, remunerated, or redistributed by any existing policy framework.

### 5.2.10 Anti-Human Trafficking Act 2013 (Act 12/2013)

The **Anti-Human Trafficking Act (Law No. 12/2013)** criminalises all forms of trafficking in persons, establishing penalties of up to 15 years' imprisonment. The law covers forced labour, sexual exploitation, debt bondage, and recruitment, transportation, transfer, harbouring, or receipt of persons through threat, force, or deception for the purpose of exploitation. It provides for victim identification, government-funded victim assistance, and coordination between the Maldives Police Service, immigration authorities, and MoSFD for case management.

The Employment Act separately prohibits forced labour under Article 3, and Maldives has ratified all eight fundamental ILO Conventions including the Forced Labor Convention (No. 29), the Abolition of Forced Labor Convention (No. 105), the Minimum Age Convention (No. 138), and the Worst Forms of Child Labor Convention (No. 182). Despite this legal architecture, implementation gaps are severe (see §5.16).

---


## 5.5 Constitutional and Legal Framework for Gender: Detail and Constraints


### 5.112.1 Article 36 and the State's Gender Equality Obligation

The 2008 Constitution's Article 36(a) provides the foundational constitutional basis for gender equality: **"The state shall ensure the rights of women, and work to achieve equality between men and women."** This is a positive obligation – the state must *actively work* toward equality, not merely refrain from discrimination.

Article 17 complements this by prohibiting discrimination "on the basis of... sex" among other grounds. Read together, Articles 17 and 36(a) create both a negative right (freedom from sex discrimination) and a positive duty (active work toward gender equality). These constitutional provisions are the basis on which the Gender Equality Act 2016 was enacted.

### 5.112.2 Article 10: The Islamic Law Constraint

Article 10 of the Constitution declares Islam as the state religion and provides that "no law contrary to any tenet of Islam shall be enacted in the Maldives." This provision is the constitutional basis for the CEDAW Article 16 reservation and creates a structural constraint on any gender equality reform that touches Islamic family law norms.

The intersection of Article 36(a) (equality obligation) and Article 10 (Islamic law supremacy) creates a constitutional tension that the courts have not definitively resolved. The government's consistent position – that the CEDAW Art. 16 reservation is required by Article 10 – treats the tension as resolved in favour of Islamic law. A constitutional challenge that Article 36(a)'s equality obligation requires reinterpretation of Islamic law provisions in light of contemporary Islamic jurisprudence (the approach adopted by Musawah, a global movement for equality within Muslim family laws) has not been tested in the Maldivian courts.[^568]

### 5.112.3 The Family Act's Bride's Guardian Requirement

The Family Act (Law No. 4/2000) requires, for valid marriage registration, the involvement of the bride's *wali veriyaa* (guardian) – a male relative. No equivalent requirement exists for male parties to marriage. This guardian requirement:

- Reflects classical Islamic jurisprudence (the *wali* condition for the validity of marriage)
- Creates a situation where a woman cannot legally marry without male family involvement, regardless of her age or wishes
- Has implications for women without male relatives (deceased fathers, brothers abroad) who may face difficulty establishing a valid guardian

The Family Law Regulation (2016) set 18 as the minimum marriage age but retained the *wali* requirement. The CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report (2025) did not identify the *wali* requirement as a reform priority, reflecting the government's consistent position that it is constitutionally required under Article 10.[^569]

---


## 5.6 The Gender Equality Act 2016: Detailed Legal Analysis


### 5.35.1 Overview and Legislative History

The **Gender Equality Act 18/2016 (GEA)** is the Maldives' primary legislation on gender equality in the public and private spheres. Its enactment in 2016 was the culmination of several years of civil society advocacy, CEDAW Committee pressure (reflected in the 2015 Concluding Observations), and development partner technical support. The GEA moved gender equality from a CEDAW-reporting aspiration to a domestic legal obligation enforceable through Maldivian law.[^373]

The GEA's legislative history reflects the political context of the Solih administration's reform orientation (2018-2023) which built on the legislative foundation laid in the prior period. The law was developed with input from UNDP, UN Women, and the Human Rights Commission of Maldives (HRCM), and was modelled on comparable gender equality legislation in other Commonwealth and SIDS contexts.

**Parliamentary passage:** The GEA passed the People's Majlis with significant debate on provisions that some members characterised as inconsistent with Islamic principles – specifically provisions on discrimination in employment and public services that could be interpreted as requiring equal treatment in contexts where Islamic jurisprudence provides differentiated treatment. The compromise result was that the GEA's non-discrimination provisions are qualified by consistency with Islamic law (an approach that mirrors the constitutional rights framework), leaving some interpretive uncertainty about the law's application in Islamic law-governed domains.[^374]

### 5.35.2 Key Provisions in Detail

**Section 1 – Definitions:** The GEA defines gender-based discrimination broadly to include direct discrimination (treating someone less favourably because of their gender) and indirect discrimination (applying a rule or condition that appears gender-neutral but disproportionately disadvantages one gender). The inclusion of indirect discrimination is legally significant – it creates a framework for challenging facially neutral policies (such as resort employment practices) that have a gender-disparate impact.[^375]

**Section 2 – State obligations:** The state is required to take active measures to achieve substantive gender equality – not merely avoid discrimination. This positive obligation framework goes beyond a prohibition-of-discrimination standard to require proactive state action, including the GRB requirement, gender mainstreaming in policy development, and data collection disaggregated by gender. The positive obligation framing provides the legal basis for gender equality advocacy that argues the state must do more, not merely refrain from discriminating.[^376]

**Section 3 – Employment non-discrimination:** Employers are prohibited from discriminating on grounds of gender in:
- Recruitment and hiring
- Compensation and promotion
- Terms and conditions of employment
- Termination

The employment provisions explicitly cover pregnancy and maternity as non-discrimination grounds – an employer cannot refuse to hire, promote, or retain a woman because she is pregnant or has recently given birth. The practical enforcement of these provisions through the Employment Tribunal is discussed in §5.30.

**Section 4 – Education non-discrimination:** Educational institutions must not discriminate on grounds of gender in admissions, curriculum, or educational opportunity. This provision is relevant to the career guidance stereotyping documented in §5.13 – systematically steering girls away from technical subjects and boys away from caregiving subjects could constitute indirect discrimination under the GEA.

**Section 5 – Healthcare non-discrimination:** Access to health services, including reproductive health services, must not be denied or restricted on grounds of gender. This provision provides the legal basis for challenging any healthcare access arrangement that systematically disadvantages women – including the geographic distribution of specialist reproductive health services.

**Section 6 – Sexual harassment:** The GEA defines sexual harassment broadly to include unwanted physical contact, verbal comments of a sexual nature, display of sexual material, and any conduct of a sexual nature that creates a hostile environment. Employers are required to establish internal harassment complaint procedures and are vicariously liable for harassment by employees.[^377]

**Section 7 – Gender-responsive budgeting:** Ministries and government agencies are required to apply gender analysis to budget decisions and to report on gender impacts of budget allocations. The Ministry of Finance is responsible for GRB oversight and guidelines. As documented throughout this chapter, fewer than 40% of ministries are in compliance.

**Section 8 – Gender Equality Committee:** The GEA establishes a Gender Equality Committee (GEC) – an inter-ministerial coordination body chaired by the Minister responsible for gender – to oversee GEA implementation, receive complaints, and produce annual gender equality reports.

**Section 9 – Enforcement and remedies:** Persons who experience discrimination contrary to the GEA may file complaints with the GEC, the HRCM (as the national human rights institution), or the Employment Tribunal (for employment discrimination). Remedies include cessation of the discriminatory practice, compensation, and injunctive relief. Criminal penalties for certain provisions have been discussed but are not established in the current GEA.[^378]

### 5.35.3 Implementation Record

The GEA's implementation record as of 2024-25 illustrates the gap between legislative commitment and operational reality:

**GRB compliance:** The most quantified implementation indicator – fewer than 40% of ministries complying with GRB – represents the most concrete data point available. Even among complying ministries, the quality of GRB analysis (whether gender analysis actually influences allocation decisions or merely adds a compliance checkbox to the process) varies widely.[^379]

**Sexual harassment provisions:** Employer compliance with the requirement to establish internal harassment complaint procedures is not systematically monitored. The Employment Tribunal has received a small number of GEA harassment cases, but the total is substantially below what any realistic assessment of workplace harassment incidence would predict – suggesting that survivors are not using the complaint mechanism, either because they are unaware of it, do not trust it, or fear retaliation.[^380]

**Gender Equality Committee:** The GEC's operational record is limited. Annual gender equality reports that the GEC is required to produce have not been consistently published in available records. The GEC's role as a coordination mechanism across government has not been fully activated – inter-ministerial gender equality coordination depends primarily on MoSFD's influence over other ministries, which is constrained by its position in the government hierarchy.[^381]

**HRCM complaint mechanism:** The HRCM is empowered to receive and investigate GEA discrimination complaints. The HRCM's gender-related complaint caseload has increased since the GEA's enactment, but the pattern of gender-discrimination complaints – like DV disclosures – likely represents a small fraction of actual discrimination incidents, reflecting the same barriers of awareness, access, and fear of retaliation that limit disclosure across the gender policy system.[^382]

### 5.35.4 The GEA and CEDAW Alignment

The GEA was designed to be the domestic legal instrument implementing CEDAW commitments. Its provisions align with several CEDAW articles:

- GEA Section 2 (positive obligations) → CEDAW Article 2
- GEA Section 3 (employment) → CEDAW Article 11
- GEA Section 4 (education) → CEDAW Article 10
- GEA Section 5 (healthcare) → CEDAW Article 12
- GEA Section 6 (harassment) → CEDAW Articles 2, 11
- GEA Section 7 (GRB) → CEDAW Article 3

The GEA does not, however, address the CEDAW reservations' domains – marriage, family, divorce, and inheritance remain governed by Islamic family law outside the GEA's scope. The GEA thus represents a partial CEDAW domestication – comprehensive for public sphere equality, absent for the private sphere family law domain where much of women's most acute inequality is located.[^383]

---


## 5.7 The GEA's Gender Responsive Budgeting Requirement: Ministry-by-Ministry Analysis


### 5.81.1 Why GRB Matters at Ministry Level

Gender Responsive Budgeting is not merely a technical compliance exercise; when meaningfully implemented, it transforms how each ministry allocates its resources – ensuring that spending decisions account for gender-differentiated impacts. The Gender Equality Act 18/2016's GRB mandate requires every government ministry and agency to:
1. Conduct gender analysis of proposed budget allocations – examining how each budget line will affect women and men differently
2. Set gender equality targets within the ministry's mandate area
3. Report on gender equality outcomes, not only aggregate spending

The CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report (2025) acknowledges that fewer than 40% of ministries comply. Understanding which ministries comply and which do not – and what non-compliance means substantively – requires a ministry-by-ministry consideration.

### 5.81.2 Compliance Analysis by Ministry

**Ministry of Health:** Among the better-performing ministries on GRB, reflecting the sector's inherent attention to gender (maternal health, reproductive health, GBV healthcare response). The RMNCAH Strategy's gender components provide a framework for health budget gender analysis. Specific GRB challenges: outer island facility distribution that disadvantages women in accessing specialist reproductive healthcare; mental health budget allocation that underweights the female majority of mental health service users.

**Ministry of Education:** Education GRB should address: the reverse gender gap in secondary dropout (where boys need more support); the near-absence of SRH and consent education in the curriculum; career guidance's occupational stereotyping of girls; and the gender composition of the teaching workforce vs. school leadership. Education GRB compliance status varies by administration's political commitment.

**Ministry of Finance:** The Ministry of Finance's GRB role is central – it sets the GRB guidelines and monitors compliance. Ironically, the Ministry of Finance's own internal gender analysis of the national budget is inconsistently applied. Budget allocations to care infrastructure (childcare, elder care) that disproportionately affect women are not consistently gender-analysed. The gendered impact of tax policy, tariffs, and public investment decisions are rarely assessed through a gender lens.

**Ministry of Economic Development:** Responsible for economic development policies that significantly affect women's LFPR, entrepreneurship, and access to credit. GRB compliance should require analysis of how trade, investment, and regulatory policies affect gender economic participation. In practice, economic development GRB has focused primarily on MSME financing gender targets rather than systemic policy gender analysis.

**Ministry of Tourism:** Given the gender dimensions of tourism employment documented in §5.30 – particularly the near-total exclusion of Maldivian women from resort employment – the Ministry of Tourism's budget and Maldivianisation policies have significant gender relevance. Tourism GRB compliance should require gender-disaggregated Maldivianisation targets; this has not been consistently applied.

**Ministry of Housing:** Housing budget decisions – including the Madhanee Mahala programme allocation – have direct gender implications for post-divorce housing security, single mother accommodation, and DV survivor shelter access. Housing GRB should require gender analysis of housing programme allocation criteria and outcomes; this is not documented as systematically occurring.

**Ministry of Gender and Family/MoSFD:** As the gender policy focal ministry, MoSFD should exemplify GRB compliance. Its budgets for the FPA, shelter system, and community programmes should be gender-analysed and disaggregated. The Ministry's own GRB compliance is generally higher than line ministries – but the FPA's chronic understaffing suggests that even MoSFD's budget allocation has not adequately reflected the gender analysis of service demand vs. supply.

**Ministry of Environment, Climate Change and Technology:** The GRFS 2023 represents this Ministry's commitment to gender-responsive climate policy. GRB compliance in the environment sector should ensure that climate adaptation investments are allocated with attention to gender-differentiated climate vulnerability – particularly female-headed households in low-lying outer islands. This is an area where the GRFS provides the framework; implementation is early.[^511]

---


## 5.8 The Sexual Offences Act in Detail: Legal Analysis and Implementation


### 5.44.1 Legislative Background and Structure

The **Sexual Offences Act (SOA)** – Act 17/2014, as amended – is the principal legislation governing criminal sexual violence in the Maldives. Its passage in 2014 represented the first comprehensive statutory framework for sexual violence in the country's legal history, replacing a patchwork of Penal Code provisions that had historically handled sexual violence primarily through Islamic criminal law (hadd) standards, with well-documented problems for survivors.[^417]

The SOA's structure covers:
- **Rape** (Section 3): Non-consensual sexual intercourse, defined by absence of consent rather than by force alone – a consent-based definition that is more protective than the force-based definitions common in older criminal codes
- **Sexual assault** (Section 4): Sexual acts short of intercourse without consent
- **Sexual harassment** (Section 5): Unwanted sexual conduct in various contexts
- **Sexual abuse of a person in a position of authority** (Section 6): Covering cases where professional relationships create power asymmetries
- **Childhood sexual offences** (Section 7-10): A range of offences specific to sexual acts involving minors, with aggravated penalties
- **Voyeurism, exposure, and other acts** (Sections 11-13): Digital and non-digital sexual misconduct

The SOA's consent-based definition of rape was a significant legal advance. Previous provisions focused on penetration by force; the SOA defines consent affirmatively, specifying circumstances under which consent is presumed absent (including where the victim is asleep, intoxicated, or in a state of fear) – provisions that reflect contemporary understanding of sexual violence dynamics rather than historical force-plus-resistance models.[^418]

### 5.44.2 The 2021 Marital Rape Amendment: A Landmark Change

The single most significant amendment to the SOA was the **2021 amendment explicitly criminalising marital rape**. Prior to 2021, the marital rape exception – derived from Islamic jurisprudence's position that a wife's consent to marriage includes consent to sexual intercourse within the marriage – meant that rape by a husband was not prosecutable as rape under the SOA. The 2021 amendment removed this exception, establishing that consent is required for all sexual acts regardless of marital status, and that non-consensual intercourse within marriage constitutes rape under Section 3.[^419]

The criminalisation of marital rape was a substantial human rights advance with immediate practical and symbolic significance:
- **Symbolic:** The law now acknowledges that married women have the right to refuse sexual intercourse – a right that the previous legal framework had implicitly denied
- **Practical:** Wives who are raped by their husbands can now file criminal complaints under the SOA without the marital exception defence being available to the perpetrator
- **Parallel DVPA:** Marital rape is also covered under the DVPA's definition of domestic violence (sexual violence within intimate relationships), so survivors have both civil (protection order) and criminal (SOA prosecution) pathways

The practical impact of the 2021 amendment on prosecution rates is not yet systematically documented. The AIM Project Baseline Assessment (2022) notes that the amended provision exists but that prosecution under it has been limited – reflecting the broader justice pathway attrition (3% prosecution rate) that applies across all DV and sexual violence cases, not specifically a problem with the marital rape provision.[^420]

**Remaining challenges for marital rape prosecution:**
- Evidence: Marital rape typically occurs in private without witnesses; forensic evidence of force (bruising, injury) is less likely where the perpetrator has regular access to the survivor and the rape may not involve physical force in addition to coercion
- Disclosure: The shame barriers to reporting any sexual violence are amplified in the marital context, where disclosure means accusing the husband and potentially triggering divorce proceedings
- Justice pathway: Police, prosecutors, and courts that are sceptical of rape claims generally may be even more sceptical of marital rape claims given the historically normalised understanding of sexual access within marriage

### 5.44.3 Evidence Requirements: The Forensic Gap

The practical application of the SOA in prosecution depends critically on **forensic medical evidence**. The prosecution of sexual offences typically relies on:

1. **Survivor testimony:** The survivor's account of the assault is the foundation of the prosecution case. The testimony must be detailed, consistent across multiple tellings (to police, FPA, medical staff, and court), and withstand cross-examination.

2. **Medical evidence:** Physical examination of the survivor documenting injuries consistent with assault. This examination is conducted at IGMH in Malé and requires:
   - Examination conducted promptly after the assault (ideally within 72 hours)
   - Trained forensic medical examiner – not all medical staff have forensic evidence collection training
   - Proper chain of custody for any biological samples collected (for DNA analysis)
   - Medical report prepared according to standards admissible in court

3. **Corroborating evidence:** Additional evidence supporting the survivor's account – witnesses (rare in sexual violence cases), digital communications, circumstantial evidence of relationship dynamics.

The **forensic evidence gap** for outer island survivors is one of the most acute access-to-justice failures in the Maldivian GBV system. A woman raped on an outer island must:
- Make the decision to report within 72 hours while in a state of acute trauma
- Arrange transport to Malé or the nearest hospital with forensic capacity – a journey that may take 1-3 days
- Present to medical services in a state of physical and psychological distress without having had the opportunity to process the experience or receive support
- Undergo an intrusive forensic medical examination by strangers in an unfamiliar environment

At each stage, barriers to proceeding mount. By the time the survivor arrives at IGMH – if she does – the physical evidence may be degraded, the survivor may have bathed (in a community where bathing after sexual assault is the culturally normal response to ritual pollution), and the emotional and logistical toll may lead her to withdraw from the process entirely.[^421]

The UNFPA's National Guidelines for Healthcare Providers on GBV (2024, 2nd edition) specifically address this forensic gap, providing clinical protocols for evidence collection by health post staff in outer islands who are not forensic specialists – a harm-reduction approach that collects the best available evidence given the geographic constraints. The chain of custody requirements for evidence collected in non-forensic settings and transported to Malé for analysis remain a legal challenge.[^422]

### 5.44.4 Consent Standard in Practice

The SOA's consent-based standard represents the legal framework; how courts apply the consent standard in practice is a separate and critical question. Key issues:

**What constitutes consent:** The SOA defines consent as voluntary, conscious agreement to a specific sexual act. The law specifies that consent must be ongoing – it can be withdrawn at any time – and that agreement to one act is not agreement to all acts. These provisions align with contemporary understanding but require judges to apply a standard that may differ from cultural norms in which sexual access within relationships is presumed and resistance is expected to be physical.
