**Capacity to consent:** The SOA specifies that consent is not valid when the person is: asleep or unconscious, intoxicated to the point of being incapable of making decisions, under duress or coercion, or affected by a mental disability that prevents understanding of what they are consenting to. These incapacity provisions are legally clear but factually contested in prosecution – the perpetrator will claim the survivor appeared to consent; the prosecution must prove incapacity beyond a reasonable doubt.

**Absence of consent vs. evidence of non-consent:** A consent-based standard should require only that the prosecution prove the victim did not consent – not that she actively resisted, fought back, or showed physical evidence of resistance. In practice, courts that have a tacit requirement for evidence of physical resistance are applying a force-based rather than consent-based standard. This distinction – between what the law requires and what judicial culture actually demands – is one of the most important implementation gaps in the SOA framework.[^423]

---


## 5.9 The Domestic Violence Prevention Act: Detailed Legal Analysis


### 5.45.1 Civil Law Framework and Its Rationale

The choice to make the DVPA a **civil law instrument** – rather than amending the Penal Code to create criminal domestic violence offences – was deliberate and reflects specific policy judgments. The rationale for the civil approach:

- **Lower evidentiary standard:** Civil proceedings require proof on the balance of probabilities (more likely than not), not beyond reasonable doubt. This makes protection orders more accessible to survivors who cannot meet the criminal standard of proof.
- **Speed:** Civil injunctions can be issued rapidly (interim protection orders without a hearing) in a way that criminal warrants cannot.
- **Focus on protection, not punishment:** The protection order's goal is the survivor's safety, not the perpetrator's punishment – an orientation more aligned with survivor-centred approaches.
- **Avoids criminalisation reluctance:** Survivors who do not want their partner imprisoned – perhaps for economic reasons (he is the income earner), for the children's sake, or genuinely wanting the relationship to change rather than end – may be more willing to use a civil protection order than to make a criminal complaint.

These rationales are well-founded, but the civil approach has limitations:
- **No criminal deterrence:** A protection order violation is a criminal offence, but the underlying DV itself is addressed only through the civil framework unless the survivor also pursues criminal charges. A perpetrator who violates without criminal consequences faces only civil sanctions – enforcement action whose ultimate remedy (contempt of court) takes time to apply.
- **Requires survivor engagement:** Unlike criminal prosecution (which can proceed without the survivor's cooperation), civil protection orders require the survivor to initiate and maintain the application. Survivor withdrawal – possible at any point – terminates the civil process.
- **Limited scope:** The protection order addresses the specific abusive relationship; it does not address the perpetrator's pattern of behaviour or the broader social norms driving it.[^424]

### 5.45.2 Section-by-Section Analysis

**Section 1 (Definitions):** The DVPA's definitional scope – covering physical, sexual, psychological, and economic violence; covering married, divorced, cohabiting, and dating partners – is one of the most comprehensive in the South Asian region. The definition of psychological violence is particularly broad: threats, humiliation, surveillance, isolation, and control of daily activities all fall within the DVPA framework. The breadth of definition creates opportunity; the challenge is in evidencing these forms of non-physical violence through the civil court process.

**Section 3 (State obligations):** The state, through the FPA, police, health services, and courts, is obligated to take affirmative action to protect DV survivors. This positive obligation includes the duty to receive complaints, investigate, and facilitate protection – not merely to refrain from interfering. The positive obligation framing provides the basis for civil society advocacy that the FPA's inadequate resourcing is itself a legal violation.

**Section 4 (Restraining orders/interim protection):** The interim protection order provision allows a court to issue protection within 24 hours of an application in cases of urgent need, without requiring the perpetrator to be present (ex parte). The interim order lasts until a full hearing can be scheduled. This provision is the DVPA's most powerful tool for immediate survivor safety – but as documented in §5.21, it is underused in outer atolls due to court capacity constraints.

**Section 5 (Full protection order):** The full protection order, issued after a hearing with both parties, can prohibit contact, require the perpetrator to vacate the home, establish no-approach zones, and grant temporary child custody. The order duration is specified in the Act and can be renewed. Violation of a full protection order is a criminal offence carrying specific penalties under the DVPA.

**Section 8 (FPA mandate):** This section establishes the FPA as the lead agency with specific operational responsibilities – case intake, safety planning, coordination, awareness, training of WDCs. It is the legislative basis for the FPA's existence and its budget claim on the national government.

**Section 10 (WDC role):** WDCs are formally integrated into the DVPA framework as the outer island first-response mechanism. The WDC's referral function – connecting island survivors to the FPA – is here given statutory grounding. The gap between this statutory role and WDC's actual capacity (no budget, limited training, no authority) is the central implementation failure of the DVPA's geographic coverage design.[^425]

### 5.45.3 Protection Orders Issued: Available Data

Available data on DVPA protection orders issued is incomplete in public records. The FPA's quarterly statistics track case intake but do not consistently distinguish between:
- Cases in which a protection order was sought
- Cases in which a protection order was applied for
- Cases in which a protection order was issued (interim or full)
- Cases in which a protection order was violated

The AIM Project Baseline Assessment (2022) provides the most detailed available breakdown, documenting that protection order grant rates are lower than in comparable jurisdictions and that the gap between cases reported to the FPA and protection orders ultimately issued is substantial – consistent with the broader justice pathway attrition analysis. Systematic publication of protection order statistics by the DJA or FPA would be a significant improvement in the accountability data available to policymakers and civil society.[^426]

---


## 5.10 The Family Act 2000: Evolution, Amendments, and Ongoing Gaps


### 5.51.1 Legislative History

The **Family Act 2000 (Act 4/2000)** consolidated and reformed Maldivian family law – replacing a fragmented collection of Islamic family law applications and judicial custom with a codified statutory framework. Its passage was a significant modernisation of the legal environment for marriage, divorce, custody, and maintenance. The Act has been amended several times since 2000, with key changes documented in the Legal Framework chapter (§2.X) and revisited here with gender-specific focus.[^440]

Key amendments relevant to gender include:
- **2016 amendment:** Raised the marriage age to 18 and eliminated the judicial exception that had allowed under-18 marriages in exceptional circumstances
- **2019 amendment:** Introduced provisions on prenuptial agreements (though social uptake remains near-zero) and strengthened some maintenance enforcement mechanisms
- Provisions on court-ordered mediation in family disputes (with the DV carve-out that DV cases should not be mediated but rather referred to the DVPA protection order process)

The Family Act's relationship with Islamic family law is constitutionally defined: the Act applies Islamic jurisprudence as the substantive law for marriage, divorce, inheritance, and custody, with statutory regulation of the processes by which these Islamic legal principles are administered. The Act is therefore not a secular family code but a Maldivian-Shafi'i statutory codification.[^441]

### 5.51.2 Marriage Provisions

**Marriage registration:** All marriages must be registered with the Family Court. The registration requirement creates a paper trail for subsequent divorce proceedings and maintenance claims, and ensures that the minimum age requirement is formally documented. In practice, unregistered marriages exist – particularly in outer islands and, historically, in the early years of young people's relationships – though the legal consequences of marriage rights (property, custody, maintenance) depend on registration.

**Marriage procedures:** The Islamic marriage contract (*nikah*) must be conducted by a licensed officiator (*quaadhi*) with the consent of both parties and the presence of a male guardian (*wali*) for the bride. The wali requirement – that a male family member (father, brother, uncle) must consent to and witness the bride's marriage – is a structural feature of the marriage process that gives male family members a formal role in women's marriages that has no equivalent for men. In practice, most brides have willing guardians; but the requirement can become a control mechanism when family members withhold consent to punish women who wish to marry against their preferences.[^442]

**Mahr determination:** The mahr amount is agreed between the parties at the time of marriage and recorded in the marriage contract. As documented in §5.22, mahr amounts tend to be symbolic rather than substantive. There is no minimum mahr requirement and no mechanism for ensuring that the mahr reflects the economic value to the wife – unlike some Islamic marriage contract reform initiatives in Morocco and other contexts that have developed standard mahr guidance.[^443]

### 5.51.3 Divorce Types and Their Gendered Implications

The Family Act provides for three principal forms of divorce, each with different procedural requirements and gendered power implications:

**1. Talaq (husband's unilateral right to divorce):** A husband can pronounce *talaq* – initiating divorce through verbal or written declaration – which, after a required reconciliation period (*iddah* of three months), terminates the marriage. Under Islamic jurisprudence, the husband can revoke a single talaq during the iddah period; three talaqs are irrevocable. Under the 2000 Act, talaq must be registered with the Family Court and cannot simply be pronounced verbally without a judicial process – a procedural requirement that provides some documentation and recourse period.

The talaq right gives husbands a structurally faster and more certain path to divorce than wives, who cannot unilaterally terminate the marriage. A husband who wants a divorce can initiate it; a wife who wants a divorce must either obtain her husband's agreement or petition the court.

**2. Khul (wife-initiated divorce with financial consideration):** A wife can initiate divorce by returning the mahr – or offering some other financial consideration – in exchange for the husband's agreement to divorce. This is the primary mechanism through which women who want to divorce but whose husbands do not agree can exit the marriage. The financial cost of khul – having to "buy" the divorce by forgoing the mahr or making an additional payment – is a barrier for economically dependent women. A wife who is already economically dependent on her husband may be unable to make the khul payment that would allow her to exit the marriage.

**3. Court-ordered divorce (faskh):** On specific grounds – failure to maintain, physical absence, harm (including domestic violence), and incapacity – a wife can petition the Family Court to dissolve the marriage without requiring the husband's agreement and without the financial cost of khul. The DVPA explicitly provides for DV as a ground for court-ordered divorce. However, the court-ordered divorce process requires demonstrating the specified grounds to the court's satisfaction – an evidentiary challenge in cases where the husband disputes the claimed grounds.

The interaction between these divorce mechanisms creates a gendered asymmetry: men can exit marriages unilaterally (through talaq) at essentially no cost; women must either have the husband's agreement (which the husband may withhold for leverage) or pay to exit (khul) or prove grounds in court (faskh). This asymmetry contributes to the divorce cases that the DJA statistics document, where women may remain in abusive marriages longer than is safe because exiting requires either financial resources they do not have or a court process they find inaccessible.[^444]

---


## 5.11 The Family Act: Full Structural Gender Analysis


### 5.115.1 The Gender Architecture of Maldivian Family Law

The **Family Act (Law No. 4/2000)** is the central instrument of Maldivian family law and the primary source of the structural gender asymmetries that shape women's legal position throughout their lives. While §5.112 introduced the constitutional framework and the *wali* requirement, this section provides the full operational analysis of the Family Act's gender dimensions – drawing directly on the Legal Framework chapter of this encyclopedia and the vulnerability mapping literature review (2026).

The Act operates alongside the Family Regulation of 2001, Supreme Court regulations on marriage guardians and minors' marriages, and various judicial practice directives. Its scope covers marriage, divorce, custody, maintenance, and guardianship – the entire life cycle of family formation and dissolution. At every point in this cycle, the Act embeds gender asymmetries that compound across a woman's life.[^574]

### 5.115.2 Divorce: Asymmetric Access and Asymmetric Burden

The Family Act's divorce provisions create fundamentally different legal experiences for men and women:

**Husband-initiated divorce (talaq):** A husband may divorce his wife unilaterally and without court involvement. Talaq must be registered with the Family Court but does not require judicial approval, grounds, or conciliation. The divorce takes effect upon pronouncement (subject to waiting period rules). The husband faces no evidentiary burden.

**Wife-initiated divorce (*khul'* and judicial divorce):** A wife must petition the Family Court on specified grounds – cruelty, harm, failure to provide maintenance, abandonment, impotence, or incompatibility – and must pass through mandatory conciliation before the court will consider the petition. The evidentiary burden falls entirely on the wife. Where a wife seeks judicial divorce on grounds of cruelty or harm, she must produce evidence of the harm in a legal system where documentation of domestic violence is systematically poor (only 3% of DV cases reach prosecution) and where the Small Claims Court process for maintenance enforcement is under-resourced.

**The court-assisted *khul'*:** In a *khul'* divorce (by mutual agreement), the wife typically returns the *mahr* (dower payment made at marriage) to the husband as consideration for the divorce. This effectively means that a woman leaving an abusive marriage may pay financially for the right to leave. Women who cannot afford to return the *mahr* may be trapped in the marriage.

**The costs of serial divorce on women:** The 2022 Census showed the proportion of divorced women exceeds divorced men by **42.86%**, reflecting the differential impact of serial marriage and divorce patterns on women. Data shows: in 2016, there were 5,488 marriages and 3,417 divorces nationally – approximately 60% of marriages ending in divorce. Divorce rates for women aged 26–40 in Greater Malé are 18.83% higher than comparable women in other atolls (annual totals: 874 in 2021, 927 in 2022, 931 in 2023, 779 in 2024). The higher female divorce rate reflects women's greater exposure to serial divorce: a man who divorces his wife and remarries appears once as "divorced" and then as "married"; his ex-wife remains "divorced" until she remarries.[^575]

### 5.115.3 Custody: The Section 42 Trap

The Family Act's custody provisions contain a provision – Section 42 – that functions as a structural trap for women who seek to rebuild their lives after divorce:

**The basic framework:** Under Islamic jurisprudence as codified in the Family Act, mothers have priority physical custody of children in the early years (daughters until puberty, sons until approximately age 7), after which custody shifts to fathers. The *wali* (legal guardian) of a child is always the father; mothers may have physical custody but not legal guardianship.

**Section 42 – the remarriage and mobility penalty:** A mother **loses her priority custody if:**
- She marries a man who is not a *mahram* (close blood relative) of the child – i.e., any man other than the child's uncle or close male relative
- She moves to a different island without the father's consent

The practical effects of Section 42 are profound:
- A woman who wishes to remarry after divorce must choose between her new relationship and retaining custody of her children from the previous marriage. Most divorced men do not face an equivalent choice.
- A woman who needs to move islands – to access employment, education, healthcare, or to escape proximity to an abusive ex-partner – can only do so with the father's consent or by losing custody.
- Analysis of **24 High Court custody cases** found that in every instance where the mother was awarded custody, she had remained unmarried. Fathers awarded custody frequently delegated actual care entirely to relatives while denying mothers contact – meaning the children were practically in the mother's care anyway, but legally and formally in the father's household.

This creates a situation where Section 42 effectively converts the custody determination into a mechanism for post-divorce control. An ex-husband can use custody as a threat to prevent his former wife from remarrying or relocating. The vulnerability mapping literature review documented this dynamic explicitly, identifying custody weaponisation as a significant feature of post-divorce coercive control.[^576]

### 5.115.4 Maintenance: Legal Obligation Without Enforcement

The Family Act requires that the court determine maintenance arrangements for the wife and children before a divorce is granted. The minimum child maintenance payment is MVR 2,000 per month for one child. In principle, this protects children's financial welfare after divorce. In practice, enforcement is effectively absent:

- Approximately **MVR 2 million in maintenance payments had accumulated unclaimed at the Family Court** as of the vulnerability mapping research period – representing years of unpaid obligations by non-compliant fathers
- Women reported maintenance going unpaid for years with no effective enforcement mechanism
- Court processing of maintenance orders was historically slow – 30–45 days to process child support payments through the Ministry of Finance system before recent improvements
- The standard enforcement pathway requires the ex-wife to return to court, a process that is costly, time-consuming, and practically inaccessible for women on outer islands or with limited legal literacy

The gap between the legal obligation and actual payment is not incidental. It reflects a family law enforcement system that places the burden of collecting maintenance on the women to whom it is owed, without providing accessible enforcement mechanisms or automatic transfer systems.[^577]

### 5.115.5 Marital Property: The Unrecognised Contribution

The most comprehensive structural gender asymmetry in the Family Act is one of omission: **women's unpaid care work carries no legal recognition whatsoever.** A wife who spends decades maintaining a household, raising children, and enabling her husband's economic participation exits divorce with no legal claim to marital property – unless a prenuptial agreement exists that specifically provides for property division.

Prenuptial agreements under the Family Act are theoretically available but **functionally nonexistent**: only approximately 8 such agreements are concluded annually across the entire country. The absence of a default matrimonial property regime means that all property acquired during the marriage is legally the sole property of whoever holds title – typically the husband, whose formal-sector employment generated the documented income and asset accumulation. Women's contribution of unpaid care – which averages 19 hours per week for women compared to 8.1 hours for men – is invisible in this framework.

A woman who exits a 20-year marriage having contributed two decades of household labour and childcare has no legal claim to a share of the family home, family savings, or family business. The only asset she can claim is the *mahr* (if it was agreed at marriage and not returned in *khul'*) and child maintenance going forward.[^578]

### 5.115.6 Polygamy: Documented Harms and Regulatory Failure

The Family Act permits polygamy (up to four wives) in accordance with Islamic law. Approximately 165 marriages to a second wife were registered in 2020 and 157 in 2021 – these represent only registered polygamous marriages; unregistered marriages may be more common.

The Musawah research (Shafeega, 2018) documents the **emotional and financial harms of polygamy on first wives and children**:
- First wives typically learn of the second marriage after the fact
- The emotional impact – feeling abandoned, inadequate, and betrayed – is severe and lasting
- The financial impact – income and assets that had been shared in a two-adult household are now spread across two households – frequently pushes the first wife's household into poverty
- Children in the first family experience both the emotional disruption of the changed family structure and the financial impact of reduced household income

The law requires court approval for polygamy on grounds of justification (including the husband's financial capacity to support multiple wives equitably), but approval data suggests these requirements are a formality rather than a substantive barrier.[^579]

---


## 5.12 Key Legislation Timeline: Gender Policy in Historical Context


The following timeline consolidates the key legislative and policy milestones in Maldivian gender equality history:

| Year | Development | Significance |
|------|-------------|-------------|
| 1944 | Aminiya School established | First formal girls' secondary school in Maldives |
| 1965 | Independence from British protectorate | New constitutional context |
| 1993 | CEDAW ratification (1 July 1993) | International gender equality commitment |
| 1993 | Ministry of Youth, Women and Social Welfare established | First women's affairs ministry |
| 2000 | Family Act enacted | Codified family law; marriage age; divorce procedures |
| 2004 | CRC Optional Protocols ratified | Child protection framework |
| 2007 | Fulu/WH&LE Study published | First national DV prevalence data |
| 2008 | New Constitution enacted | Rights framework; democratic transition |
| 2008 | Employment Act (Act 2/2008) | Labour rights; maternity provisions |
| 2012 | Domestic Violence Prevention Act (Act 3/2012) | Civil DV framework; FPA established |
| 2012 | Thalassaemia Control Act (Act 4/2012) | Premarital screening; abortion provision for thalassaemia |
| 2013 | Anti-Human Trafficking Act (Act 12/2013) | Trafficking criminalisation |
| 2014 | Sexual Offences Act (Act 17/2014) | First SOA; consent-based rape definition |
| 2014 | Penal Code (Act 9/2014) | Consolidated criminal code; zina; abortion restrictions |
| 2016 | Gender Equality Act (Act 18/2016) | Comprehensive gender equality framework; GRB mandate |
| 2016 | Family Act amendment | Marriage age raised to 18; under-18 exception removed |
| 2021 | Sexual Offences Act amendment | Marital rape explicitly criminalised |
| 2021 | Local council election with gender quota | 4% to 33% women in local councils |
| 2023 | Gender-Responsive Climate Financing Strategy | Gender-climate integration framework |
| 2024 | UNFPA GBV Healthcare Guidelines (2nd edition) | Updated clinical GBV response protocol |
| 2025 | CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report submitted | Current government gender policy self-assessment |

This timeline illustrates the legislative acceleration of the 2012-2021 period – five major gender-relevant laws or amendments in a decade – followed by a shift toward implementation (GRB, healthcare guidelines, climate strategy) rather than new legislative frameworks. The challenge of the current period is converting the 2012-2021 legislative output into operational reality across 187 inhabited islands.

---


## 5.13 Footnotes on Legal Interpretation: Specific Provisions of Note


### 5.86.1 The Reasonable Man Standard and GBV

Maldivian criminal law applies standard common law principles including the "reasonable man" or "reasonable person" standard in assessing criminal conduct. In GBV cases, this standard has specific implications:

- **Self-defence:** A DV survivor who kills or injures an abusive partner in self-defence may rely on the self-defence provisions of the Penal Code. The self-defence standard – whether the defendant's response was proportionate to the threat – may be applied in ways that do not account for "battered woman syndrome" (the recognised psychological condition of DV survivors whose perception of threat and available options is shaped by prolonged abuse). Maldivian courts have not documented application of battered woman syndrome as a factor in self-defence analysis; in comparable jurisdictions, this has required specific judicial training or legislative amendment.

- **Consent and the "reasonable belief" defence:** Some jurisdictions' sexual offence laws permit a "reasonable belief" defence – where the defendant claims he reasonably believed the victim consented. Whether the SOA contains an equivalent provision, and how it is interpreted, significantly affects prosecution outcomes. The UNFPA (2023) review of GBV laws includes analysis of this provision.[^518]

### 5.86.2 Constitutional Rights and GBV

The 2008 Constitution's rights provisions that are most directly relevant to GBV include:

**Article 20 (Right to liberty):** The right to personal liberty may be invoked in cases where DV constitutes wrongful imprisonment or restraint of movement – forms of DV documented in the Maldivian context.

**Article 21 (Right to life and security):** The most fundamental constitutional hook for state obligations in GBV – the state's duty to protect citizens' right to life and security may be argued to require adequate DV response. No constitutional litigation using this provision in DV cases has been documented.

**Article 17 (Non-discrimination):** Discrimination on grounds of gender is prohibited; the GEA operationalises this constitutional provision. The extent to which constitutional non-discrimination creates independent litigation opportunities for DV survivors who have been denied adequate state protection is unexplored in Maldivian jurisprudence.

**Article 54 (Family):** The constitution's family provisions, which address the state's interest in protecting the family unit, have been invoked in debates about gender equality – with conservative interpretations arguing that gender equality laws interfere with the family as a protected institution. Progressive interpretations argue that family protection requires protecting women within families from violence.

---


## 5.14 Legal Framework: Prosecution Rates and Justice Access


### 5.116.1 The Criminal Justice Funnel for GBV

The Legal Framework chapter provides the most precise statistics available on the GBV criminal justice process – a funnel that loses cases at every stage:

| Stage | Rate | Source |
|---|---|---|
| DV/GBV cases reported to police submitted for prosecution | **3%** | PGO/MPS data |
| VAW cases at police level sent forward for prosecution | **14%** | PGO/MPS data |
| Of prosecuted cases: conviction rate | **16.7%** (2016-2019) | Criminal Court data |
| Women who experienced violence receiving any legal assistance | **3.7%** | Vulnerability mapping |
| CEDAW recommendations fully implemented | **3%** of 55 | HRCM 2021; ADB GESI 2025 |

The 3% submission rate and 3.7% legal assistance rate are the most damning figures in the entire GBV response system. They mean that for every 100 women who experience domestic violence and report it to police, approximately 3 will have their case submitted for prosecution and fewer than 4 will receive any legal help. This is not a system that protects women from violence. It is a system that processes a small fraction of reported violence and leaves the overwhelming majority of survivors without legal remedy.

### 5.116.2 The Evidence Burden Problem: SOA Section 52

Section 52 of the Sexual Offences Act (2014) requires **five types of specified evidence** for rape prosecution. This evidentiary threshold – identified by civil society organisations (Equality Now, Hope for Women, Family Legal Clinic), UNFPA, and the UN Working Group on Discrimination against Women and Girls (2022) as "extremely burdensome and a major barrier to justice for survivors" – creates a structural filter that removes most sexual offence cases before they reach trial.

The specified evidence requirements reflect a legal design that prioritises false accusation prevention over survivor access to justice. In a country where reporting rates are already suppressed by stigma, community pressure, and fear of retaliation, requiring five categories of evidence before prosecution proceeds means that most sexual offences – which by their nature occur without independent witnesses or physical evidence – will never be prosecuted.

### 5.116.3 The Penal Code Marital Presumption: The Marital Rape Loophole That Survived 2021

The 2021 amendment to the Sexual Offences Act removed the marital exemption from the definition of rape – a significant legal reform documented in §5.39 of this chapter. However, the Legal Framework chapter identifies a surviving loophole in the **Penal Code (Section 130(b))**, which **maintains a rebuttable presumption of consent within marriage for sexual acts**.

The practical effect: even after the 2021 SOA amendment, a husband accused of marital rape can invoke the Penal Code's marital presumption of consent. The burden then falls on the victim to rebut this legal presumption – in addition to meeting the Section 52 evidence requirements. The combination of Section 130(b) and Section 52 means that marital rape prosecution remains practically near-impossible despite the nominal criminalisation achieved in 2021.

This creates a situation where the SOA amendment was legislatively real but procedurally hollow. The woman who is raped by her husband must overcome: the social barrier to reporting; the police response failures; the evidentiary requirements of Section 52; and the Penal Code's legal presumption of her consent. Each of these barriers operates independently, and a case must survive all of them to reach prosecution.[^580]

### 5.116.4 Zina Law: Pregnancy as Evidence Against Rape Survivors

The intersection of Shari'ah *zina* law and sexual violence law creates a situation without parallel in most jurisdictions: **pregnancy out of wedlock is criminalised**, including for women who became pregnant through rape or sexual abuse.

The Penal Code provisions on *zina* (extramarital intercourse) apply without exception for consent. A woman who is raped and becomes pregnant has produced, through the rape, the primary evidence of a crime for which she can be prosecuted. While prosecution for *zina* following reported rape does not appear common in practice, the legal possibility deters reporting: a rape survivor who becomes pregnant faces the prospect of being both a victim and a defendant.

The 85% female share of flogging sentences – documented in the HRCM's 2022 corporal punishment report – reflects this structural problem directly. The zina/SOA overlap means that women bear the disproportionate criminal consequences of sexual activity, whether consensual or coerced.[^581]

---


## 5.15 Key Legal Statistics Not Previously Documented in This Chapter


The Legal Framework chapter contains several statistics that are material to understanding gender equality and were not previously captured in this chapter:

**Family law statistics:**
- 5,488 marriages and 3,417 divorces in 2016 nationally – approximately 60% of marriages ending in divorce
- Proportion of divorced women exceeds divorced men by 42.86% (Census 2022)
- Annual divorce rates for women aged 26–40 in Greater Malé: 874 (2021), 927 (2022), 931 (2023), 779 (2024)
- ~8 prenuptial agreements concluded annually nationally

**GBV justice statistics:**
- 3% of DV/GBV cases reported to police submitted for prosecution
- 14% of VAW cases at police level sent forward for prosecution
- 16.7% conviction rate of cases that reach court (2016-2019)
- 3.7% of women who experienced violence received any legal assistance

**CEDAW implementation:**
- Only 3% of 55 CEDAW Committee recommendations have been fully implemented

**Gender and disability (from Legal Framework chapter):**
- 55% of disabled households are female-headed
- Persons with disabilities are 4 times more likely to experience violence
- 40–60% of visually impaired women report sexual abuse

**Gender and finance:**
- COVID-19 relief loans: 15% to women vs 74% to men
- 20% of SDFC-approved loans went to female-based businesses

**Gender equality attitudes:**
- Only 57.6% of men support gender equality (HRCM Rights Side of Life Survey 2019)[^584]

# Part 3: Institutional Architecture


## 5.16 Institutional Architecture for Gender Equality and GBV Response


### 5.3.1 Ministry of Social and Family Development (MoSFD) – The National Machinery

The **Ministry of Social and Family Development (MoSFD)** is the principal national machinery for gender equality in the Maldives, serving also as the CEDAW focal point for treaty reporting. Its functions include:
- Coordinating gender equality policy across government
- Managing the National Action Plan on Gender Equality (NAPGE)
- Overseeing GRB implementation across ministries
- Providing oversight and support to the Family Protection Authority
- Maintaining liaison with WDCs nationally
- Coordinating international treaty reporting (CEDAW, CRC, CRPD)

The ministry has been renamed multiple times through successive administrations – from Ministry of Gender and Family (pre-2018) to Ministry of Gender, Family and Social Services (Solih era) to the current Ministry of Social and Family Development (Muizzu era, from November 2023). This repeated renaming, which affects signage, stationery, databases, and institutional identity, has been identified by practitioners as a low-level but persistent disruptor of institutional continuity and stakeholder awareness.

**Capacity constraints:** As of 2024, MoSFD's Gender Equality Division had fewer than twenty dedicated gender specialists for national-level programming. This represents a significant staffing shortfall for a portfolio covering gender equality across 188 inhabited islands, CEDAW compliance, GRB implementation in all ministries, and oversight of 270 WDCs. Budget allocations to MoSFD, including the Gender Equality Division, have remained modest relative to the scope of this mandate.

**GRB compliance:** The Gender Equality Act (18/2016) mandates gender-responsive budgeting across all government ministries. As of 2024, MoSFD's internal audit found that fewer than 40% of ministries had submitted gender-disaggregated budget analyses for fiscal years 2022–2023. This level of compliance is insufficient to operationalise the GRB mandate in a way that materially affects resource allocation.[^34]

### 5.3.2 Family Protection Authority (FPA) – Structure, Functions, and Capacity

The **Family Protection Authority (FPA)** is an independent regulatory body established under the DVPA 2012, with a mandate to receive, investigate, and refer domestic violence complaints; issue protection orders (through the Family Court); oversee safe houses; publish quarterly GBV statistics; and provide training and oversight for DV response across sectors.

**Establishment history:** The FPA was initially established as an administrative unit under the Ministry of Gender and Family in 2006 – predating the DVPA by six years. It operated without a specific legal mandate until the DVPA formalised its role in 2012. The absence of legislation in its early years meant the FPA could receive complaints and provide support services but lacked the authority to compel responses from police, courts, or other agencies.

**Functions under the DVPA:**
- Receiving DV complaints directly from survivors, family members, and mandatory reporters
- Assessing risk levels and referring cases to appropriate agencies (police, social services, courts)
- Facilitating applications for protection orders through the Family Court
- Operating the national GBV case database
- Managing safe houses for DV survivors
- Training health, education, and social sector workers on DV identification and response
- Publishing quarterly statistics on DV cases

**Critical staffing shortage:** As of 2024, the FPA operated with **three dedicated DV caseworkers** for the Greater Male' area, serving approximately 250,000 residents. No dedicated DV caseworkers are stationed at atoll level. In outer atolls, initial DV response depends almost entirely on local police, island council members, WDC members, and health workers – few of whom have received specialised DV response training. The CEDAW Committee's 2021 Concluding Observations identified FPA staffing as a priority concern.[^35]

**Safe house capacity:** The FPA operates a safe house (refuge) for DV survivors in Male'. Capacity is extremely limited – insufficient to accommodate more than a small fraction of women seeking refuge in crisis. Hope for Women, the leading civil society organisation working on GBV, maintains a separate shelter facility but with even more limited capacity. Regional shelter capacity outside Male' is absent. The ADB's Strengthening Gender Inclusive Initiatives Project (54373-001, 2024 onwards) is financing the construction of three additional climate-resilient DV shelters at Addu City (Hithadhoo), Hulhumale', and Raa Atoll – which will, when completed, represent the first expansion of shelter infrastructure beyond Male'.[^36]

**DV Case Statistics – the FPA time series:** The FPA has maintained quarterly case statistics since its operational inception. The trajectory of case reporting is instructive:

| Year | Cases Received by FPA |
|------|----------------------|
| 2013 | 19 |
| 2014 | 149 |
| 2015 | 438 |
| 2016 | 642 (peak year) |
| 2017 | 539 |
| 2018 | – |
| 2019 | – |
| 2021 | 696 (new peak; post-COVID surge) |
| 2022 (Jan–Sep) | 181 (FPA) + 67 (MPS) |
| 2024 | 537 |

*Sources: Family Protection Authority Quarterly Statistics 2013–2024*[^37]

[Note: FPA case data series requires further clarification; figures across different FPA reporting documents show inconsistencies in baseline years and annual totals.]

The increase from 19 to 696 peak cases represents primarily a growth in reporting – driven by increasing awareness of the DVPA, growing trust (however limited) in the FPA, and community education campaigns. It does not represent a comparable increase in the prevalence of DV itself. DHS 2016–17 data indicating that 1 in 3 women experience violence in their lifetime significantly exceeds the FPA caseload, confirming substantial underreporting.

**GBV cases reported to MoGFSS/MoSFD:** Beyond FPA-reported DV cases, MoSFD tracks GBV cases more broadly:
- 2021: 494 cases (87% female victims, 13% male)
- January–September 2022: 378 cases (89% female victims, 11% male)

The high female share of victims – 87–89% – confirms the gendered nature of reported violence.[^38]

### 5.3.3 Police Family and Child Protection Wing (FCPW)

The Maldives Police Service operates a **Family and Child Protection Wing (FCPW)** as the specialist law enforcement unit for responding to domestic violence and child protection reports. The FCPW is responsible for receiving and investigating DV and VAC (violence against children) complaints, executing protection orders, and providing trained first response to DV callouts.

**Documented failures in police response:** Community-based research and case file reviews have identified recurring failures in police response to DV cases:

- **"Smooth it over" attitude:** Field research identified a documented pattern of responding officers attempting to mediate or reconcile parties rather than treat DV as a crime scene. Women have described officers telling them to "talk it out" and discouraging them from making formal complaints, particularly if the perpetrator is known to the officers or holds social standing in the community.

- **Month-long waits:** Women reporting non-acute DV situations – threats, harassment, economic abuse – described waiting over a month for police to respond or follow up after initial complaints. This delay discourages persistence with the complaint process.

- **Evidence handling delays:** In one documented case cluster, police held mobile phone evidence (containing relevant messages and photographic evidence) for eight months before providing it to the FPA or court, creating delays that allowed perpetrators to apply for protection order dismissal on grounds of case staleness.

- **Location disclosure:** Cases were documented where responding police officers disclosed the victim's refuge location to the perpetrator – potentially creating immediate safety threats for survivors who had left their homes to escape violence.

- **Information gap between agencies:** Protection orders issued by the Family Court are not systematically shared with the Maldives Correctional Service, meaning that staff managing persons in custody on other matters may be unaware of active protection orders, and perpetrators may leave custody without conditions enforced.[^39]

### 5.3.4 Women's Development Committees (WDCs)

Women's Development Committees exist on each of the approximately 270 inhabited islands of the Maldives and serve as the frontline community-level structure for gender equality, DV referrals, and women's empowerment activities. WDC members are elected by island communities and serve volunteer terms. The GEA formalised their legal mandate.

**Transparency Maldives 2015 Assessment:** The most systematic evaluation of WDC functioning is the 2015 Assessment of Women's Development Committees, conducted by Transparency Maldives with IFES support. Key findings:

- **Difficult relationships with island councils:** WDCs frequently reported exclusion from council meetings, lack of information sharing by council members, and dismissal of WDC concerns by the council – the body on which WDCs are structurally dependent for resources and authority.

- **No independent budget:** WDCs have no independent budget and depend entirely on island councils for operational funding. This financial dependency undermines autonomy: activities depend on the goodwill of council members who may not share WDC priorities.

- **Advisory not binding:** WDC decisions and recommendations are advisory – they have no authority to compel action by island councils, police, or other agencies. When a WDC receives a DV disclosure, it can refer – but cannot require – the relevant agency to act.

- **Double burden:** WDC members are volunteers who combine WDC responsibilities with domestic responsibilities, employment, and (often) other community roles. The unsupported volunteer nature of WDC service means that the communities most in need of active WDCs (smaller, poorer islands) often have WDC members least able to devote sustained time.

- **Training gaps:** The UNDP/MoSFD 2022 WDC Capacity Assessment found that fewer than 40% of WDC members had received any formal training from MoSFD in the preceding two years. Many members lacked understanding of their legal mandate, the DV referral pathway, or the mechanisms available to influence local decision-making.

- **Public awareness varies:** Community awareness of WDCs varied significantly across islands, from communities where the WDC was well-known and actively used to those where residents were unaware of the committee's existence or purpose.[^40]

**UNDP/MoSFD 2022 WDC Capacity Assessment** updated the Transparency Maldives 2015 findings, confirming that the structural constraints identified in 2015 had not been substantially resolved. The 2022 assessment's most significant finding was the training gap: the majority of WDC members could not describe the referral pathway for a DV disclosure or identify which agency they should contact first.

### 5.3.5 Human Rights Commission of Maldives (HRCM)

The **Human Rights Commission of Maldives (HRCM)**, established under Act 6/2006, serves as the national human rights institution with monitoring, complaint-receiving, and reporting functions across all human rights domains including gender equality, GBV, and discrimination.

**CEDAW monitoring:** The HRCM submits shadow reports to the CEDAW Committee alongside government periodic reports, providing an independent assessment of compliance. The HRCM's 2021 shadow report to the CEDAW process documented the gap between legal commitments and implementation, with the flogging sentencing disparity (85% of recipients being women) and the marital rape exception identified as priority concerns.

**"Rights Side of Life" survey:** The HRCM has conducted population-based surveys on human rights awareness in 2005, 2011, and 2019. The 2019 survey documented significant changes in rights awareness:
- Public awareness of the HRCM grew from 40.8% (2005) to 72.8% (2011) to 84.8% (2019)
- Women's awareness of HRCM was consistently high (84.9% in 2011; 87.0% in 2019), while men's awareness grew from 63.7% to 80.7%
- Respondents identified the right of the child as the most important human right from a list of 21 options (a change from 2011, when the right to education was paramount)
- **Women's right to equality was identified as the second most important human right** by 10.9% of respondents in 2019
- Migrant worker awareness of HRCM grew dramatically from 9.0% (2011) to 41.2% (2019)[^41]

**Complaint receiving:** The HRCM receives complaints from individuals alleging human rights violations, including gender discrimination. Regional offices in some atolls provide some complaint-receiving capacity outside Male', though access remains predominantly capital-centric.

### 5.3.6 The AIM Project (Accountability and Integration Matters, 2022–2025)

The **AIM Project** is a joint EU-UNDP-MoSFD initiative specifically designed to address the catastrophic attrition in the GBV justice pathway. With only approximately 3% of DV/GBV cases reported to police reaching the Prosecutor General's office for prosecution, and a conviction rate of 16.7% among cases that do reach court, the AIM Project represents the most targeted institutional intervention in the Maldivian GBV response system.

**Components:**
- Development of standard operating procedures for GBV case handling by the Maldives Prosecution Service (MPS)
- Improving evidence-gathering protocols for police investigating sexual and domestic violence cases
- Building judicial capacity on gender-sensitive adjudication of GBV cases
- Developing multi-agency coordination protocols between FPA, police, prosecutors, courts, and social services
- Psychosocial support services for GBV survivors – recognising that victims' ability to participate in the justice process depends on their mental health and safety
- A justice sector reform component improving case management within the prosecution and court system

The AIM Project baseline assessment (2022) documented the full picture of justice pathway attrition: of all DV/GBV cases that come to the attention of police, only 14% of violence against women cases reach prosecution at the police level; of those submitted for prosecution, only 3% ultimately result in charges being laid by the Prosecutor General; and of those that proceed to court, the conviction rate is 16.7%. The cumulative attrition from report to conviction is statistically near-total.[^42]

**The DV case fragmentation problem:** A domestic violence case on an outer island may involve the island council (first point of contact), the police FCPW (investigation), the FPA's Male'-based caseworkers (assessment and support), the Family Court (protection orders), the Maldives Prosecution Service (criminal prosecution of associated assault), the NSPA (welfare support), and the HRCM (rights monitoring) – each operating with different protocols, separate case management systems, and geographic constraints. No integrated case management system exists. The victim's experience is fragmented across agencies, with no single point of accountability for the overall response. The AIM Project's inter-agency coordination component directly addresses this fragmentation.

### 5.3.7 Family Legal Clinic

The **Family Legal Clinic** (operated under the aegis of civil society and government partnership) provides free legal aid for DV and family law matters. It is the only source of free legal representation for women in DV cases in Male'. The Legal Aid Commission of Maldives, established in 2016, provides civil and criminal legal aid but has insufficient staff and budget to meet demand.

Women seeking legal redress in DV, divorce, or custody matters in outer atolls have no equivalent access to legal aid. The Legal Aid Commission's geographic concentration in Male' means that atoll-based women navigating the court system typically do so without legal representation – a significant disadvantage in proceedings where the law is complex, judges have varying levels of training on gender-sensitive adjudication, and cultural pressures to withdraw or reconcile are strong.

### 5.3.8 Hope for Women and Civil Society

**Hope for Women** is the leading CSO working specifically on GBV prevention and survivor support. It operates a shelter facility in Male' (with limited capacity) and plans for regional shelters have been developed but not yet fully implemented. Hope for Women has been a consistent advocate for legislative reform, survivor support services, and public awareness on GBV.

**SHE (Sexual Health and Education)** – a youth-focused CSO – documented that 52% of surveyed Maldivian youth reported being deprived of access to sexual and reproductive health information, establishing the demand-side evidence for comprehensive SRH education reform.

**Maldivian Democracy Network (MDN)** has conducted research on women's political participation, documenting the barriers preventing women from standing for and winning election at parliamentary level.

**Moms Aid** (founded 2019; momsaid.com.mv) works at the intersection of anti-poverty and violence against women. The organisation provides temporary shelter for women escaping domestic abuse, referrals to mental health services, and skills training for independent livelihoods. Its Ramadan Aid programme distributes food baskets to verified vulnerable families (178 families through Dusit Thani partnership in 2025; targeting 500 families), while the Back to School programme provides school supplies to children from financially vulnerable families (BML donated MVR 100,000 in 2025). The Family Sponsorship Program provides ongoing daily support to verified vulnerable families. Corporate partnerships include Dhiraagu, Dusit Thani Maldives, EyeCare Maldives, Bank of Maldives, and Villa College (MOU for legal rights awareness and skills training for mothers). Led by Executive Director Fathimath Riyasa.

### 5.3.9 UNFPA – Gender Programme

The United Nations Population Fund has been the lead UN agency on GBV prevention and response in the Maldives since the 2006 DV prevalence study. Under the 7th Country Programme (2022–2026, indicative budget US$3.5 million), UNFPA focuses on:
- Sexual and reproductive health and rights (SRHR)
- Adolescents and youth
- Gender equality and GBV prevention
- Population and development data

Key UNFPA contributions in the recent period:
- **Human Rights and Gender Review of GBV-Related Laws (2023):** Commissioned an international human rights lawyer and gender expert to review all seven relevant national laws for compliance with international standards, identifying gaps including the Section 52/53 evidence provisions, the zina-pregnancy intersection, and the (then) marital rape gap
- **National Guidelines for Healthcare Providers on GBV (2024, 2nd edition):** Updated clinical guidelines for healthcare providers on GBV identification and survivor care protocols – a critical interface since healthcare workers are often the first non-family contact for DV survivors
- **COVID-19 GBV Analysis:** Analysis of FPA, police, and Prosecutor General case data during the pandemic, providing the most systematic available evidence on how pandemic conditions affected GBV reporting and the justice system response
- **Census 2022 support:** UNFPA has supported all Maldivian censuses for 45 years since 1977, contributing to the demographic data infrastructure on which gender policy depends

### 5.3.10 ADB – Gender Programme

The **Asian Development Bank's Strengthening Gender Inclusive Initiatives Project (54373-001, 2024 onwards)** is ADB's most significant direct engagement in the Maldivian social sector. It is a grant and technical assistance project targeting:

1. **Improving GESI Statistics:** Strengthening gender-disaggregated data infrastructure across government
2. **Integrating GRB into Government Systems:** Technical assistance for gender-responsive budget formulation
3. **Strengthening Social Service System Capacity:** Building institutional capacity for DV/GBV services, aged care, and early childcare
4. **Establishing DV/GBV Partnerships:** Public-private-CSO partnerships to expand service coverage
5. **Constructing Three Climate-Resilient Shelters:** Physical shelter infrastructure at Addu City (Hithadhoo), Hulhumale', and Raa Atoll – the first expansion of DV shelter infrastructure beyond Male'

The ADB's 2025 Gender Equality and Social Inclusion (GESI) Diagnostic examined GESI challenges across tourism, energy, and urban development, providing a cross-sectoral structural analysis to inform broader gender mainstreaming.

ADB's MSME Development Project separately included credit lines with limited collateral requirements specifically designed to enable women entrepreneurs – recognising that the standard collateral requirement (land or property in the borrower's name) structurally disadvantages women whose property is predominantly registered in male family members' names.

---


## 5.17 The Family Protection Authority: Operations, Capacity, and Case Management in Detail


### 5.28.1 Establishment and Legal Mandate

The **Family Protection Authority (FPA)** was established by the DVPA (Act 3/2012) as the lead government agency for domestic violence prevention and response. It operates under the Ministry of Social and Family Development (MoSFD). The FPA's legal mandate under the DVPA encompasses:

- Receiving and processing disclosures of domestic violence
- Providing or facilitating access to case management services, counselling, and legal assistance for survivors
- Coordinating inter-agency response to DV cases (police, courts, health, WDCs)
- Maintaining a registry of DV cases and generating aggregate statistics for policy planning
- Conducting public awareness and prevention programming
- Issuing temporary safety plans and facilitating protection order applications
- Operating the shelter system or coordinating with shelter providers
- Building capacity of WDCs and other local-level actors in DV response
- Monitoring compliance with protection orders[^324]

The FPA is the primary institutional actor in the Maldivian GBV response system, serving as the primary gateway through which survivors enter the formal response system. Understanding the FPA's operational reality – its physical capacity, staffing, geographic reach, case management protocols, and resource constraints – is essential to understanding why the system performs as it does.

### 5.28.2 Physical Capacity and Location

The FPA's main office is located in **Malé**. As of the most recent capacity assessment (AIM Project Baseline Assessment, 2022, and FPA Capacity Assessment, 2023):

- **Case intake facility:** The Malé office provides in-person intake, counselling rooms, and case management support. The facility is not purpose-built; it occupies rented space and has limited room for simultaneous client services.

- **Caseworker numbers:** The FPA operates with a caseworker cadre substantially smaller than the caseload demands. The AIM Project Baseline Assessment (2022) documented that FPA caseworkers carry caseloads significantly above recommended levels – the exact figures are not published in available reports, but case-to-caseworker ratios that permit adequate time for complex case management (safety planning, multi-agency coordination, follow-up) are not being achieved.

- **Outer atoll presence:** The FPA does not have permanent physical offices in most outer atolls. Case management for outer atoll survivors is handled through:
  - Remote telephone or video case management from the Malé office
  - Coordination with WDC members who act as informal local case managers
  - Periodic outreach visits to atolls by Malé-based caseworkers (resource-constrained and infrequent)
  - In serious cases, facilitated transportation of the survivor to Malé for in-person services

The absence of a permanent FPA atoll presence is the most significant structural gap in the GBV response system. For a survivor on an outer island, the FPA's services are primarily accessible by telephone – which requires privacy that may not be available in shared or small island housing, and relies on the caseworker being available at the time of call in a high-caseload environment.[^325]

### 5.28.3 Staffing Profile

The FPA workforce includes:
- **Caseworkers:** Social workers and community development professionals trained in trauma-informed DV case management. Most have undergraduate-level social science qualifications; FPA provides in-service training supported by the AIM Project and UNFPA. Caseworkers are predominantly women – a deliberate policy decision reflecting the importance of gender-concordant case management for most DV survivors.

- **Counsellors:** Clinical counselling support is available through the FPA but is capacity-constrained. The number of counsellors per caseload means that survivors requiring sustained therapeutic support often have limited session availability.

- **Legal advisors:** The FPA has a small legal advisory function supporting survivors navigating protection order applications and related court processes. The legal advisor capacity is not sufficient to provide full legal representation; the FPA coordinates with the Family Legal Clinic for representation.

- **Administrative and data management staff:** The FPA's case registry requires data entry and management staff. The case management information system, which the AIM Project is developing, will require staff trained in its operation.

- **Senior management and leadership:** The FPA is headed by a Director-General reporting to the Minister of Social and Family Development. Leadership continuity has been a challenge – the FPA has experienced multiple leadership changes with political administrations, disrupting institutional learning and priority continuity.[^326]

### 5.28.4 The Case Registry: What the FPA Data Shows

The FPA's published case statistics provide the most comprehensive time series on reported DV in the Maldives. The full case series, reconstructed from FPA Quarterly Statistics and the Statistics Compendium (§17.3.2), is:

| Year | Cases Reported to FPA | Notes |
|------|----------------------|-------|
| 2009 | 19 | First year of FPA operation under DVPA framework |
| 2010 | Data not consistently available | |
| 2011 | Data not consistently available | |
| 2012 | DVPA enacted | |
| 2013 | 149 | First full year of enhanced recording |
| 2014 | Data point available | |
| 2015 | Data point available | |
| 2016 | 438 | Significant increase; DVPA awareness growing |
| 2017 | 642 | Near-peak in pre-COVID series |
| 2018 | 539 | Year-on-year decrease |
| 2019 | 696 | New record high |
| 2020 | 537 | COVID pandemic year – counterintuitive decrease reflecting access barriers, not actual decrease in violence |
| 2021 | 696 | COVID shadow pandemic; returns to 2019 level as access barriers eased |

[Note: FPA case data series requires further clarification; figures across different FPA reporting documents show inconsistencies in baseline years and annual totals.]

Note: The COVID-19 year 2020 statistics may have been misread from available sources. Available sources indicate the DV case series showing 537 in 2020 may reflect access barriers. The FPA/UNFPA COVID-19 GBV report (2022) noted that reported cases dropped in early 2020 but increased sharply in late 2020 and 2021 as access to reporting improved. The Statistics Compendium §17.3.2 should be consulted for the authoritative full series.

**Interpreting the case series:** The dramatic increase from 19 cases in 2009 to 696 cases by 2019-2021 does not represent a 37-fold increase in actual DV. It primarily reflects:
1. Growing awareness of the FPA and its services among potential survivors
2. Increased social legitimacy of reporting DV as public awareness campaigns took effect
3. Improved recording and data systems as the FPA's capacity developed
4. Some real increase in certain categories of DV-adjacent behaviour documented following social stress periods

International evidence consistently indicates that increases in reported DV following introduction of specialised services and awareness campaigns reflect improved help-seeking, not actual increases in violence prevalence. The Fulu 2007 prevalence baseline (one in three women experiencing IPV) and the FPA case series are measuring different things: actual prevalence vs. official report rate.[^327]

### 5.28.5 FPA Counselling Services

Beyond case management, the FPA provides counselling services to DV survivors. These services include:

**Individual counselling:** Trauma-informed counselling for survivors of DV, provided by FPA counsellors. The focus is on processing the experience of violence, safety planning, and building the survivor's capacity to make informed decisions about her situation. The FPA explicitly avoids pathologising survivors' responses to violence – situating fear, trauma symptoms, and ambivalence about leaving as normal responses to abnormal circumstances.

**Children's counselling:** Children in DV-affected households are often direct witnesses to or secondary victims of violence. The FPA provides child-specific counselling support where capacity allows, in coordination with the Child and Family Protection Service (CFPS) for cases involving child welfare concerns.

**Group support:** The FPA has experimented with survivor support groups – creating a space for women with shared DV experience to support each other. These groups have strong international evidence as effective complements to individual counselling, particularly in building social support networks for isolated survivors. The capacity for group support programming is constrained by the same caseworker and counsellor capacity limitations affecting all FPA services.

**Referral to external counselling:** In cases where the FPA's own counselling capacity is insufficient – particularly for survivors with severe PTSD, suicidal ideation, or complex mental health needs – the FPA refers to the National Mental Health Centre (IGHM) in Malé. For outer atoll survivors, this referral pathway is effectively inaccessible.[^328]

### 5.28.6 The Shelter System: Capacity and Limitations

As of 2024-25, the Maldives has three ADB-funded DV shelters operational or in development:
- **Hithadhoo, Addu Atoll:** Serving the southernmost atoll region
- **Hulhumale':** Serving the Greater Malé region (the fastest-growing population centre)
- **Raa Atoll:** Serving northern atoll regions

These shelters provide:
- Emergency accommodation for survivors and their children
- In-shelter case management support
- Coordination with FPA, police, and courts
- Safety while protection order applications proceed
- Initial stabilisation before longer-term planning

**Shelter capacity limitations:**
- Three shelters serve 187 inhabited islands across 26 atolls – geographic reach is severely limited
- Shelter capacity (number of beds per shelter) is not published in available sources but is understood to be limited relative to need in even the atoll regions they serve
- Shelter placements are time-limited – survivors cannot remain indefinitely; the absence of long-term safe housing means that shelter exit often means returning to dangerous situations or to family whose support may be conditional
- Shelters in regional locations require survivors to travel from their island of residence – all the transport logistics documented in §5.17 apply

The UNICEF Social Sector Assessment (2022) found that the existing DV/GBV centre does not provide services tailored to survivors, and that residential services available through it are limited. On islands outside Male', DV victims have had to resort to temporary stays at children's shelters or other temporary accommodations organised ad hoc by Island Councils – arrangements that are neither designed for adult survivors nor equipped to provide the safety planning, legal support, or psychosocial services that DV cases require.[^gbv_unicef1]

**The FPA Transitional House (Malé):** In addition to the ADB shelters, the FPA operates a transitional facility in Malé for survivors in need of immediate safety. This facility provides shorter-term emergency accommodation while longer-term arrangements are made. Its capacity is similarly limited.[^329]

### 5.28.7 FPA-WDC Coordination: The Theory and Reality

The formal design of the outer atoll DV response envisions WDCs as the first point of contact for island women and as the channel through which cases are referred to the FPA. The FPA provides guidance and training to WDCs on DV recognition and referral. In theory, this creates a distributed first-response network that extends the FPA's reach to every inhabited island.

In practice, as documented in §5.10.3 and confirmed by the UNDP/MoSFD WDC Capacity Assessment (2022), the WDC network as a first-response channel is severely compromised by:
- WDC members' limited training – fewer than 40% received relevant formal training in two years prior to assessment
- WDC members' limited authority – no formal power to compel any action
- WDC members' personal risk – social consequences of facilitating DV disclosures in small communities
- Communication barriers – FPA contact from small islands may require travel to locations with reliable phone/internet

The gap between the designed function and actual function of WDCs as the outer island entry point for DV response is one of the system's critical failures. It means that for most outer island women, the de facto options are: navigate directly to the FPA by telephone (with privacy constraints), seek help from local police (with the capacity limitations documented in §5.17), or manage within family and community channels that may not support disclosure.[^330]

---


## 5.18 The Police FCPW: Between Mandate and Reality


### 5.29.1 The FCPW Structure and Mandate

The **Family and Child Protection Wing (FCPW)** is the specialised unit within the Maldives Police Service (MPS) mandated to handle domestic violence, child abuse, and sexual violence cases. The FCPW was established as a result of the DVPA (2012) requirement for a specialised police response to family violence, replacing earlier generalist police handling of DV cases.[^331]

The FCPW's mandate includes:
- Receiving DV reports and conducting initial criminal investigations
- Attending emergency DV callouts
- Referring cases to the FPA for civil (DVPA) case management while pursuing criminal investigation where relevant
- Facilitating protection order service on perpetrators
- Enforcing protection orders when violations are reported
- Investigating sexual violence allegations under the SOA
- Conducting child abuse investigations in coordination with CFPS

### 5.29.2 FCPW Staffing and Geographic Deployment

The FCPW is headquartered in **Malé** and has officers stationed at atoll police stations across the country. The staffing model and geographic deployment create significant capacity limitations:

**Malé FCPW:** The main FCPW unit in Malé handles the highest case volume and has the most experienced and trained officers. Officers receive specialised training in trauma-informed interviewing, evidence collection in DV cases, and sexual violence investigation.

**Atoll FCPW officers:** Each atoll has police stations with designated FCPW responsibility, but the number of officers per atoll varies and in smaller atolls may be a single officer with dual general policing responsibilities. Training levels outside Malé are documented as significantly lower than the Malé unit.[^332]

**The rotation problem:** Police service rotations – the standard assignment practice of regularly rotating officers between postings – mean that FCPW officers who receive specialised training are regularly transferred to other positions or islands, and their positions filled by officers who have not received FCPW-specific training. This rotation-capacity loss cycle is one of the most structurally problematic features of the specialist police response. The AIM Project has worked with MPS to develop training programmes that can be delivered more frequently to address rotation gaps, but the fundamental structural problem of police service rotation reducing specialised capacity remains.[^333]

**Gender composition:** The FCPW has a significantly higher proportion of female officers than general police units – a deliberate policy response to evidence that DV and sexual violence survivors are more likely to disclose comfortably to female officers. However, female officers are still a minority even within the FCPW, and survivor access to a female officer cannot always be guaranteed, particularly in outer atoll postings.[^334]

### 5.29.3 FCPW-FPA Interface: Coordination in Practice

The interface between the FCPW and the FPA is critical to the coherence of the overall DV response. When a DV case is reported to either agency, the following is supposed to happen:

1. The receiving agency (FPA or FCPW) informs the other of the report
2. The FPA handles the civil (DVPA protection order) pathway
3. The FCPW handles the criminal investigation pathway
4. Both agencies coordinate to ensure the survivor's safety needs and legal needs are simultaneously addressed without duplication, confusion, or conflicting advice

**In practice**, the coordination frequently fails:
- Information sharing between the two agencies depends on personal relationships and telephone communication rather than a systematic integrated database
- Survivors sometimes find themselves telling their story to multiple agencies without coordination – creating additional trauma of repeated disclosure
- Agencies sometimes give conflicting advice about what the survivor should do (pursue civil protection order vs. pursue criminal complaint vs. both)
- In outer atolls, the coordination challenge is compounded by the absence of both agencies from most locations – coordination happening via telephone between officers who may not know each other personally
- The AIM Project's information management system development is specifically designed to address this coordination failure, but as of 2024-25 is not fully operational[^335]

### 5.29.4 Police Response to Emergency DV Callouts

Emergency DV callouts – a survivor or bystander calling police during an acute violent incident – test the FCPW system most acutely. The documented record of emergency callout response in the Maldivian context includes:

**In Malé:** Response times are generally adequate for emergency services. FCPW or general police attend. The primary documented problem is decision-making on arrival – in the absence of clear mandatory arrest protocols, officers must make a judgment call about whether to arrest the perpetrator, remove the survivor, or attempt to mediate. Mediation as a response to ongoing DV is internationally recognised as counterproductive and potentially dangerous; but without mandatory arrest protocols, it remains in practice a common first response.

**In outer atolls:** Police stations may be unmanned after hours in smaller locations. Response times in emergencies may be measured in hours rather than minutes. The one-to-three officer postings that characterise smaller island police stations mean that a callout may leave the rest of the island without any police presence. Officers who are personally acquainted with the perpetrator face documented conflict of interest in making arrest decisions.[^336]

**The mediation default:** A concerning documented pattern in Maldivian police DV response is the use of **mediation** as the initial response to DV reports – attempting to resolve the situation through discussion between parties rather than treating the violence as a criminal matter. Mediation in DV cases is not neutral: it assumes equality between parties when a power asymmetry exists, may place the survivor at risk of further violence for having sought external intervention, and does not address the underlying dynamic. The DVPA does not mandate mediation and does not preclude criminal response; but the absence of mandatory arrest provisions and the cultural norm of privacy in family matters creates conditions in which mediation is the path of least resistance for officers who are not committed to treating DV as a criminal matter.[^337]

### 5.29.5 Sexual Violence Investigation Protocols

Sexual violence investigations by the FCPW face specific challenges documented in the UNFPA (2023) Human Rights and Gender Review of GBV Laws and the AIM Project Baseline Assessment (2022):

**Forensic evidence collection:** Forensic medical evidence is a critical component of sexual violence prosecution. The Maldives has limited forensic medical capacity – the primary forensic medical examinations occur at IGMH in Malé. For a survivor on an outer island, accessing a forensic examination typically requires travel to Malé – days after the assault, by which time evidence degradation is significant. The forensic chain of custody requirements (careful documentation of evidence collection, storage, and transfer) are also demanding for outer island police with limited training and equipment.

**Survivor interviewing:** Trauma-informed interviewing of sexual violence survivors – minimising re-traumatisation, using open-ended rather than leading questions, allowing the survivor to control the pace and level of detail – requires specific training. FCPW officers in Malé have received some training in this approach; outer atoll officers have received less. Documented cases of survivors being asked inappropriate, judgmental, or repetitive questions by officers are a significant barrier to subsequent prosecution.[^338]

**The reporting decision:** Survivors of sexual violence – particularly by strangers or acquaintances rather than intimate partners – must decide whether to report within a timeframe compatible with evidence collection. The barriers to this decision (fear of not being believed, fear of zina prosecution, shame, family pressure) mean that many survivors do not report until days, weeks, or months later – if at all. When they do report late, the forensic evidence gap weakens the subsequent investigation.[^339]

---


## 5.19 Gender and the Justice System: Judges, Lawyers, and Institutional Culture


### 5.40.1 Judicial Training and GBV Competence

The formal justice system's response to GBV – from the Magistrate Court in outer islands through the Family Court and Criminal Court to the High Court – is shaped not only by the legal framework but by the knowledge, attitudes, and institutional culture of judges, magistrates, and lawyers. Judicial training on GBV is documented as inadequate across the system.

**Current training situation:** The Judicial Service Commission (JSC) is responsible for judicial training and professional development. Training programmes relevant to GBV have been offered with support from the AIM Project and UNFPA, but coverage is incomplete:
- Family Court judges who regularly handle DVPA protection order cases have received some GBV-specific training
- Criminal Court judges handling sexual violence prosecutions have received less consistent training
- Magistrates in outer islands – who handle the vast majority of cases outside Malé – have received the least GBV-specific training of any judicial tier
- No mandatory minimum GBV training standard exists in the JSC's judicial qualification framework[^406]

**Judicial attitudes:** Survey and qualitative research data on Maldivian judges' attitudes toward DV and sexual violence is limited and not published. Indirect evidence from case outcomes – the low prosecution and conviction rates, the pattern of protection orders being denied or not issued – is consistent with a judicial culture that has not yet fully integrated a gender-sensitive approach to GBV cases, but causal attribution from case outcomes to judicial attitudes is not warranted without direct attitudinal data.[^407]

**The Small Bench problem:** The Family Court and Criminal Court operate with a relatively small number of judges. A single judge who holds gender-stereotyping attitudes will handle a disproportionate share of GBV cases – in a way that a larger court system would mitigate through random assignment. Specialised gender and GBV courts, or at minimum mandatory assignment protocols ensuring that GBV cases are assigned only to trained judges, would address this structural risk.

### 5.40.2 The Prosecutor General's Office and GBV Prosecution

The **Prosecutor General's (PG) Office** is the independent constitutional authority responsible for criminal prosecution decisions. Its decision-making on DV and sexual violence cases is the gateway between police investigation and court proceedings.

**Prosecution threshold:** The PG's Office applies a standard of "reasonable prospect of conviction" – analogous to the common law prosecution test – before committing to prosecution. In GBV cases, this standard is applied to evidence that is, by the nature of intimate violence, often limited to survivor testimony without corroboration. A prosecution culture that requires strong corroborating evidence before pursuing GBV cases effectively creates a higher de facto standard for GBV cases than the legal standard explicitly requires.[^408]

**PG-FCPW relationship:** The quality of police investigation determines what evidence the PG receives.
