Poorly documented investigations – incomplete evidence collection, traumatised survivor statements taken without trauma-informed protocols – produce thin case files that the PG is unlikely to proceed with. The relationship between FCPW investigation quality and PG prosecution rates creates a system-wide feedback loop: better investigations produce more prosecutions, which create more deterrence, which potentially reduce future perpetration. The current system is stuck in a negative feedback loop in which poor investigation quality contributes to low prosecution, which contributes to low deterrence.[^409]

**Survivor withdrawal:** The most frequently cited reason for case discontinuation at the prosecution stage is **survivor withdrawal** – the survivor recants her complaint or expresses unwillingness to proceed. This pattern is internationally documented in DV prosecution and reflects the economic dependency, fear, and family pressure that operate throughout the case journey. Some jurisdictions have responded with **evidence-based prosecution** approaches – developing cases based on all available evidence (medical records, 999 calls, police photographs, witness statements) that do not depend solely on survivor testimony, allowing prosecution to proceed even when the survivor withdraws. The Maldives' prosecution system does not consistently apply evidence-based approaches; survivor testimony remains the primary prosecution evidence in most cases.[^410]

### 5.40.3 The Legal Aid Gap for Survivors

Survivors who reach the criminal justice system as victims – requiring legal representation for their interests in criminal proceedings – face a significant legal representation gap. The Maldives does not have a comprehensive victim legal aid system for GBV cases:

- The FLC provides legal representation in Family Court proceedings but does not have capacity for criminal court representation
- Criminal court victims are formally represented through the prosecution (PG's Office) – but the PG represents the state's interest in prosecuting, not the individual victim's interest in compensation, information, or specific outcomes
- Private criminal law representation is unaffordable for most DV survivors
- The right to be informed about case progress, the outcome of prosecution decisions, and available remedies is not systematically implemented for DV victims[^411]

---


## 5.20 The Family Legal Clinic: Access to Justice in Practice


### 5.36.1 Mandate and Services

The **Family Legal Clinic (FLC)** is a legal aid organisation providing free legal advice and representation to women in family law matters who cannot afford private legal counsel. The FLC operates at the intersection of gender equality and access to justice – addressing the reality that the Family Court system, while nominally open to all, is in practice more accessible to those with legal representation and resources to sustain extended litigation.[^384]

FLC services include:
- **Free legal consultations:** Women can consult FLC lawyers about divorce proceedings, protection orders, custody disputes, maintenance claims, and inheritance matters
- **Court representation:** FLC lawyers represent clients in Family Court hearings – divorce, custody, and maintenance proceedings
- **Protection order assistance:** FLC supports women in preparing and filing DVPA protection order applications
- **Mahr claims:** Assistance with deferred mahr collection through the Family Court
- **Legal literacy workshops:** Community education on family law rights, focused particularly on women in outer atolls (through outreach programmes)

### 5.36.2 Caseload and Client Profile

The FLC's client base reflects the intersection of family law complexity and economic vulnerability. Based on FLC operational data and the Legal Framework chapter analysis:

- **Divorce:** The single largest category of FLC cases, reflecting the combination of high divorce rates and the complexity of navigating Family Court proceedings without legal representation
- **Maintenance:** Cases pursuing unpaid court-ordered maintenance are a significant second category – reflecting the MVR 2 million maintenance gap documented in §5.22
- **Custody:** Custody disputes, particularly involving children of multiple marriages and the hadhanah-wilayah split, are a consistent caseload component
- **DV protection orders:** FLC represents women in protection order proceedings, particularly the full hearing stage where representation is most critical
- **Inheritance:** Cases involving disputed inheritance where the Family Court's Islamic law application may not have been correctly applied, or where brothers have not implemented agreed inheritance shares[^385]

**Geographic reach:** The FLC is primarily Malé-based, with limited outer atoll capacity. Outreach legal literacy workshops have been conducted in selected atolls, but ongoing legal representation for outer island clients requires either the client to travel to Malé or case management by telephone – both with significant limitations for sustained representation.

**Funding:** The FLC's funding model is a documented vulnerability. Dependent on development partner grants (primarily international NGO support and government grants), the FLC has experienced funding gaps that threaten service continuity. A legal aid system for family law that depends on grant funding rather than sustained government appropriation is inherently fragile.[^386]

### 5.36.3 The Representation Gap

The FLC's case volume – while significant – represents a small fraction of the total need for family law legal representation among women who cannot afford private lawyers. Private lawyers in Malé charge fees that effectively make sustained Family Court litigation inaccessible for women without independent income. The most direct consequence is the power asymmetry in court proceedings:

- A woman with FLC representation faces a perpetrator with a private lawyer in roughly equal standing
- An unrepresented woman faces a perpetrator with a private lawyer in a deeply unequal position – the represented party can present evidence more effectively, cross-examine more effectively, and navigate procedural requirements that the unrepresented party may not know exist

The representation gap is particularly acute in cases where the economic stakes are highest: custody of children and maintenance are the two domains where legal outcome directly affects long-term wellbeing. A maintenance order that is too low because the woman did not know how to present her financial needs, or a custody arrangement that does not adequately protect children because the woman was unrepresented – these are the human consequences of the representation gap that the FLC is trying but insufficiently resourced to close.[^387]

---


## 5.21 The Legal Aid System and Access to Justice


### 5.71.1 The Right to Legal Representation

The right to legal representation in criminal and civil proceedings is a fundamental due process right under the 2008 Constitution. For DV survivors navigating both civil (DVPA protection order) and criminal (Penal Code prosecution) tracks simultaneously, the practical availability of legal representation is essential to whether the legal right is accessible.

**Legal aid system:** The Maldives' formal legal aid system is administered through the Attorney General's Office, which has a legal aid mandate for criminal defence (ensuring that accused persons have representation) that does not extend comprehensively to civil proceedings or to victims in criminal proceedings. The FLC addresses the gap for family law matters (divorce, custody, maintenance); but there is no systematic legal aid system for DV survivors navigating protection orders or criminal proceedings as complainants.[^488]

**Private legal profession:** Private lawyers in the Maldives are concentrated in Malé. Their fees are not publicly regulated; access to private legal counsel in family or criminal matters requires financial resources that most DV survivors – given the documented economic dependency – do not have. The gap between the legal system's formal accessibility and its practical accessibility through legal representation is significant.[^489]

**FLC capacity constraints:** The Family Legal Clinic's capacity limitations – documented in §5.36 – mean that it can serve a fraction of the women who need its services. FLC prioritisation of cases (taking the most complex or highest-risk cases when capacity is limited) means that women with "moderate" legal needs may not access FLC representation even when those needs are real and the outcome significant.

**Digital access to legal information:** An underexplored potential access improvement is digital legal information – providing women with online, accessible information about their legal rights (DVPA provisions, Family Act protections, SOA rights) in plain Dhivehi language. The FPA provides some information; Hope for Women provides some online content. But a comprehensive, accessible, government-backed digital legal rights resource for women does not exist in the Maldivian digital ecosystem.[^490]

---


## 5.22 The Employment Tribunal: GBV and Discrimination Cases


### 5.52.1 Tribunal Structure and Jurisdiction

The **Employment Tribunal** is the specialised administrative tribunal established under the Employment Act 2/2008 to adjudicate employment disputes including wage claims, wrongful termination, and – under the GEA – discrimination and harassment complaints. Its jurisdiction covers all employees in the formal sector; informal workers and domestic workers have limited access.

Relevant to gender equality, the Employment Tribunal can hear:
- Claims of gender discrimination in hiring, promotion, or termination
- Sexual harassment complaints under the GEA's employer liability provisions
- Pregnancy and maternity discrimination claims
- Equal pay disputes (subject to the wage data gaps documented in §5.32)

### 5.52.2 Case Experience: What the Data Shows

The Employment Tribunal's gender-related case volume is not systematically published in available public records. The GEA's passage in 2016 created the legal basis for new categories of claims; data on the number of gender discrimination and harassment complaints filed since 2016 would be essential for evaluating the GEA's practical impact on employment conditions. The CEDAW Committee's Seventh Periodic Report review will presumably address this data gap.

Indirect evidence from the Youth Vulnerability Study 2019 – which documented widespread experiences of interview questions about marital status and plans for children, dress requirements, and sexual harassment – suggests that the volume of potential complaints substantially exceeds the number of formal complaints. The gap between experienced discrimination and formal complaint is consistent with the broader pattern across the GBV system: legal remedies exist but access to them is limited by awareness, fear of retaliation, and trust in institutional response.[^445]

### 5.52.3 Employer Compliance with GEA Harassment Provisions

The GEA requires all employers to establish internal sexual harassment complaint procedures. There is no systematic monitoring of compliance with this requirement. Available assessment data suggests:
- Large government agencies have developed internal procedures, typically managed by HR departments with limited dedicated training
- Private sector employers – particularly small and medium enterprises – are largely non-compliant; the requirement is not actively enforced by any regulatory body
- Tourism sector employers (resorts) may have developed internal procedures as part of corporate compliance requirements linked to international hotel chain standards, but Maldivian regulatory oversight is not the primary driver of compliance[^446]

---


## 5.23 The Island Council (Atoll/Island) as a Gender Policy Actor


### 5.55.1 Decentralised Governance and Gender

The **Decentralisation Act** (as amended) established the current system of atoll and island councils as the primary local governance institution. The local council is the key policy actor at the island level for gender equality – or against it, depending on the composition and culture of each council. Understanding how island councils affect women's lives – and how women councillors are changing that effect – requires attention to the councils' actual decision-making authority and resource base.

**Council authorities relevant to gender:**
- Land use allocation decisions – including housing plots allocated to residents (with implications for women's housing security post-divorce)
- Local infrastructure decisions – placement of public facilities (water points, sanitation) that affect women's safety and mobility
- Local transport service oversight – relevance for women's access to health services, courts, and FPA
- Local social support programme administration – small councils may administer local social safety net provisions
- Recommendations on island development plans – affecting employment opportunities and women's economic participation[^455]

**Council authority limits:** Island councils have limited revenue and depend primarily on central government fiscal transfers. Major investment decisions are made at the national level; island councils have influence over smaller local matters. This means that even a fully gender-responsive island council with strong women's representation cannot substitute for national-level investment decisions about health infrastructure, education facilities, or employment generation.

### 5.55.2 How Women Councillors Are Changing Agenda

Despite the constraints, documented cases exist of women councillors successfully changing the agenda in ways that affect women's lives:

- **DV referral coordination:** Women councillors who have engaged with the WDC and FPA have, in some islands, established more consistent referral pathways for DV survivors – creating an informal system where a survivor can reach the women councillor as a trusted first contact who can help navigate the formal system
- **Health access advocacy:** Women councillors have in some atolls lobbied the atoll council for healthcare outreach visits – bringing health services to smaller islands rather than requiring travel to atoll capitals – which disproportionately benefits women with care responsibilities who cannot travel
- **Safe spaces:** Women councillors have in some islands identified and designated safe spaces – community buildings with basic facilities – that women experiencing DV can use temporarily while formal support is arranged
- **Public lighting and safety:** Women councillors in several islands have prioritised public space lighting as a safety measure – reducing the risk of sexual assault in poorly-lit public spaces, which is a documented concern for women in outer island communities[^456]

---


## 5.24 The Human Rights Commission of Maldives and Gender Policy


### 5.96.1 HRCM as a Gender Policy Actor

The **Human Rights Commission of Maldives (HRCM)** – established as an independent constitutional body with a mandate to protect and promote human rights – has been one of the most consistent institutional voices for gender equality in the Maldivian policy environment. Unlike the FPA (which is a service delivery agency) or the MoSFD (which is a government ministry with political accountability), the HRCM's independent status allows it to critique government performance on gender equality without political consequence.

**HRCM's gender-relevant work includes:**
- **Rights Side of Life Survey** – the most comprehensive periodic assessment of Maldivians' awareness and experience of their human rights, conducted in three editions (2007, 2013, 2019). The survey's gender findings – including the 23% women's rights awareness figure and the attitudes toward DV – are primary source data throughout this chapter.
- **Reports on corporal punishment** – the HRCM's 2022 report documenting that 85% of flogging sentences are against women, and its call for abolition of corporal punishment, is one of the most significant documented gender justice assessments by any Maldivian institution.
- **Disability and gender** – the HRCM's work on rights of persons with disabilities intersects with gender in documenting the double discrimination faced by women with disabilities.
- **Complaint investigation** – the HRCM investigates individual complaints of human rights violations including gender discrimination under the GEA, which creates a case-by-case accountability mechanism complementing the formal court system.

### 5.96.2 HRCM Independence and Effectiveness

The HRCM's effectiveness as a gender policy actor depends on its institutional independence – its ability to make findings and recommendations without political interference. This independence has been tested at various points in Maldivian political history:

- During the 2012-2013 political turmoil following the resignation of President Nasheed, the HRCM came under political pressure; its institutional independence was partially compromised in that period.
- The HRCM's recommendation for abolition of corporal punishment – politically sensitive given its Islamic jurisprudence basis – has been consistently rejected by government without substantive engagement, raising questions about whether HRCM recommendations on contentious gender-religion intersections have practical impact.
- The HRCM's reporting to international human rights bodies (including submissions to the UPR and alternative reports to treaty bodies) provides the most credible independent assessment of the government's human rights performance, including on gender equality.[^530]

---


## 5.25 The Children's Ombudsman and Gender


### 5.92.1 Overlap Between Child Protection and Gender Policy

The **Children's Ombudsman** – the independent authority for child rights advocacy established under the Child Rights Protection Act – has a mandate that significantly overlaps with gender policy:

- Girls who experience sexual abuse are simultaneously child protection cases (within the Ombudsman's mandate) and GBV cases (within the FPA's mandate)
- Girls who are subjected to FGM/C are gender policy cases (CEDAW, GEA) and child protection cases (Convention on the Rights of the Child, Children's Rights Protection Act)
- Girls married below 18 – or in de facto child marriages following the 2016 prohibition – are gender policy cases and child rights cases simultaneously
- Children who witness DV are both child protection cases and GBV-affected household members

The coordination between the Children's Ombudsman's office, the FPA, and the CFPS (Child and Family Protection Services) is a documented gap in the GBV response system – the same family may be subject to multiple concurrent case management processes without inter-agency coordination.[^524]

### 5.92.2 The CFPS-FPA Interface

The **Child and Family Protection Service (CFPS)** – the MoSFD agency responsible for child protection case management – operates in the same institutional space as the FPA (both under MoSFD) but with different mandates and client populations. In families where both DV and child abuse/neglect occur – which international research documents as heavily overlapping – both CFPS and FPA may be simultaneously involved.

The formal coordination protocol between CFPS and FPA requires each to notify the other when a case involves both an adult survivor and a child in the same household. In practice, this dual notification creates double case management load and risks either duplication (both agencies separately engaging the same family) or gaps (each assuming the other has covered specific elements). The integrated case management system that the AIM Project's GIMS is developing should address this coordination gap – but only if CFPS data is integrated into the system alongside FPA and FCPW data.[^525]

---


## 5.26 The Coordination Failure: Gender Policy in a Fragmented Architecture


### 5.107.1 The Structural Problem

The Action Plans Analysis chapter documented a fundamental structural problem in how the Maldives' action plans relate to gender: **the plans are designed as parallel vertical instruments for distinct population groups, but the populations they serve experience intersecting, compounding vulnerabilities that cannot be addressed through vertical delivery.**

The case of a woman with a disability experiencing domestic violence on an outer island – which appears in §5.103 of this chapter – is not a hypothetical. It illustrates a pattern that the action plan architecture structurally cannot address:

- She needs the NAPDI's healthcare access and justice access provisions
- She needs the DVPNS's protection order mechanisms and safe shelter access
- She needs the GEAP's economic empowerment strategies and legal empowerment
- She needs NSPA's disability allowance and potentially single parent allowance
- She needs health system mental health support (no plan covers this for her geography and intersection)
- She needs accessible housing (no plan covers this)

Each of these needs is addressed (partially) by a different plan, implemented by different agencies, coordinated (poorly) by different mechanisms, and funded (inadequately) from different budget lines. She encounters all of these systems through a single entry point: the local FCSC, staffed by a small number of workers responsible for child protection, domestic violence response, disability services, and social protection referrals across an entire atoll.[^552]

### 5.107.2 Plan-by-Plan Coordination Failures Affecting Gender

**GEAP and DVPNS:** These two instruments address adjacent problems – gender equality and domestic violence – but are implemented by different bodies (MoSFD GEAP Steering Committee vs. FPA-led DVPNS coordination) without explicit coordination mechanisms between them.

**GEAP and NAPDI:** The GEAP mentions women with disabilities as a vulnerable group requiring economic empowerment support. NAPDI includes gender-specific provisions. But the HRCM CRPD Shadow Report (2023) found that "feminist and women's rights discourses in Maldives do not adopt an intersectional lens and thus exclude women with disabilities in their advocacy." Even at the state level, the disability community is perceived as homogenous in terms of gender – meaning weak data collection on gendered implications and no targeted interventions at the intersection.

**GEAP and NAPVAC:** The GEAP addresses GBV elimination including violence against children; the NAPVAC addresses gender-equitable norms as part of its community strategy. Implementing bodies (FPA for DV; CFPS for child protection) operate through an MoU but without systematic case integration.

**GEAP and Ageing Policy:** Women constitute 69% of caretakers for bedridden elderly patients. The Ageing Policy acknowledges that "the caregiver burden is often solely on women" but provides awareness strategies rather than structural solutions. The GEAP does not include elderly caregivers as a target group for economic empowerment despite the scale of their unpaid contribution.

### 5.107.3 The Absence of a Supra-Plan Coordination Mechanism

There is **no cross-plan coordination mechanism** for gender policy. No inter-ministerial body is tasked with ensuring that the GEAP, DVPNS, NAPDI, Ageing Policy, and other plans are implemented in a coordinated manner, resolve conflicts, or address the gaps that fall between plans.

The SAP light chapters – which were intended to provide the overarching government planning framework within which sector plans would sit – were shelved before completion. The shelving means the Maldives currently lacks any unifying medium-term strategy for gender equality that sequences priorities, allocates resources across plans, and addresses the populations that fall between plans.

Individual plan coordination mechanisms are also weak:
- The GEAP's Gender Focal Point network remained incomplete as of 2024
- The DVPNS's coordination body (National Domestic Violence Coordinating Committee) meets periodically but has no enforcement authority over line agencies
- No mechanism exists for identifying and coordinating cases at the intersection of multiple plan responsibilities[^553]

---


# Part 4: Gender-Based Violence: Prevalence, Patterns, and Response


## 5.27 GBV Prevalence: What the Evidence Shows


### 5.4.1 National Prevalence – The Foundational Studies

**The Women's Health and Life Experiences Study (Fulu, 2007)** remains the foundational prevalence study for gender-based violence in the Maldives. Conducted with WHO multi-country study methodology by the Ministry of Gender and Family with Emma Fulu as lead researcher, the study established:

- Approximately **one in three Maldivian women** experienced physical and/or sexual violence from an intimate partner in their lifetime
- Childhood sexual abuse prevalence: **12.2% of women overall; 16.3% of women in Male'** – suggesting that urban environments, despite greater access to services, present specific risks
- One in four women experienced **intimate partner violence (IPV)** – physical, sexual, or psychological violence by a current or former partner

These figures – the first nationally representative data on gender-based violence in the Maldives – provided the evidence base for the DVPA 2012, the SOA 2014, and the GEA 2016.

**DHS 2016–17:** The Maldives Demographic and Health Survey 2016–17 updated violence against women data using a validated survey instrument. Key findings consistent with and updating the 2007 study:
- Women ever experiencing physical or sexual violence by partner: **~1 in 3**
- Physical violence in marriage: **~20%**
- Women experiencing violence who received legal assistance: only **3.7%** – confirming the near-total failure of the legal system to reach most survivors

No comprehensive national GBV prevalence study has been conducted since DHS 2016–17. The absence of updated national prevalence data beyond 2017 is a significant evidence gap, identified as a priority in the Statistics Compendium.

### 5.4.2 Administrative Data: FPA and MoSFD Case Statistics

Administrative data on GBV – derived from FPA case registrations, MoSFD case management records, and police reports – provides a partial but important picture of reported violence. Key caveats: administrative data captures only reported cases, and the evidence consistently shows that the vast majority of GBV in the Maldives goes unreported.

**FPA DV Case Time Series (2013–2024):**

| Year | Cases Received |
|------|---------------|
| 2013 | 19 |
| 2014 | 149 |
| 2015 | 438 |
| 2016 | 642 |
| 2017 | 539 |
| 2021 | 696 |
| 2022 (Jan–Sep) | 181 (FPA) + 67 (MPS) |
| 2024 | 537 |

*Source: Family Protection Authority Quarterly Statistics 2013–2024*[^43]

[Note: FPA case data series requires further clarification; figures across different FPA reporting documents show inconsistencies in baseline years and annual totals.]

The dramatic increase from 19 to 642 between 2013 and 2016 reflects primarily growing awareness of the DVPA and growing willingness to use the FPA mechanism – not a 30-fold increase in violence prevalence. The 2021 peak of 696 followed the COVID-19 lockdown period, during which reports of DV escalated globally as victims were confined with perpetrators with reduced access to support systems. The 2024 figure of 537 may reflect both improved case handling reducing repeat reports and capacity constraints discouraging new ones.

**Gender breakdown of complainants:** Women account for approximately **82% of DV reporters** to the FPA; men account for 18%. The 18% male share includes both male victims of domestic violence and male reporters reporting on behalf of female victims (family members, neighbours). Data on the sex of perpetrators indicates the overwhelmingly gendered nature of the phenomenon.

**MoSFD GBV case data:**
- 2021: 494 GBV cases received by MoGFSS (87% female victims, 13% male)
- January–September 2022: 378 cases (89% female, 11% male)

The high female share of GBV victims – 87–89% – is consistent across data sources and confirms the gendered character of GBV in the Maldivian context.

### 5.4.3 The Justice Pathway Attrition Problem

The gap between the number of DV/GBV cases reported and the number that result in prosecution and conviction is one of the defining features of the Maldivian GBV response failure. The AIM Project Baseline Assessment (UNDP/MoSFD, 2022) mapped this attrition at each stage of the justice pathway:

**Stage 1 – Prevalence to reporting:** DHS 2016–17 indicates approximately 1 in 3 women experience GBV in their lifetime. FPA annual cases in 2021 were 696. The Maldivian population includes approximately 120,000–130,000 adult women. Using a conservative lifetime prevalence of 33%, approximately 40,000 women have experienced GBV. FPA cases in 2021 represent approximately 1.7% of that population – confirming that the vast majority of women who have experienced GBV never contact the FPA.

**Stage 2 – FPA to police:** Not all FPA cases result in police reports. Some cases are addressed through civil protection orders without criminal justice involvement.

**Stage 3 – Police report to prosecution submission:** Of DV/GBV cases reported to police, approximately **3%** are submitted to the Prosecutor General's office. This 97% attrition at the police-to-prosecutor stage is the largest single point of failure in the justice pathway. The AIM Project identified multiple contributing factors: victim withdrawal (under social pressure, economic dependence, or security concerns); police recording cases as "civil matters" not requiring criminal investigation; insufficient evidence gathered during initial police response; and prosecutorial decision-making that declines cases on low-probability-of-conviction grounds.

**Stage 4 – Prosecution submission to charge:** Of cases submitted to the Prosecutor General, a further proportion is declined before charges are laid, on prosecutorial discretion grounds.

**Stage 5 – Charge to conviction:** The **conviction rate for DV/GBV cases that reach court is 16.7%** for the period 2016–2019. This low conviction rate reflects: the high evidentiary burden under Section 52 of the SOA; the recantation of victim testimony (under social pressure) during proceedings; judicial attitudes on GBV cases; and the absence of specialist GBV court procedures.

The cumulative result is near-zero accountability: from the estimated population of women who have experienced GBV, to final conviction of a perpetrator, the probability approximates zero under current system conditions. The AIM Project's entire programme logic is oriented toward improving this pathway.

### 5.4.4 Childhood Sexual Abuse

The **Women's Health and Life Experiences Study (Fulu, 2007)** documented childhood sexual abuse prevalence at:
- **12.2% of women overall** had experienced childhood sexual abuse
- **16.3% of women in Male'** – higher than the national average, suggesting that urban environments are not inherently safer for girls
- The **Global School-Based Student Health Survey (GSHS) 2014** provided additional school-based data on violence exposure among adolescents

These figures represent a significant child protection dimension to gender-based violence: girls are at substantial risk of sexual abuse in childhood, abuse that is documented to have lasting impacts on mental health, educational outcomes, and adult relationship patterns.

### 5.4.5 COVID-19 and the GBV "Shadow Pandemic"

The COVID-19 pandemic produced a "shadow pandemic" of domestic violence globally, and the Maldivian evidence is consistent with this pattern. Key data points:

- FPA case volumes peaked in **2021 at 696** – the highest annual figure in FPA history – following the lockdown periods of 2020
- The UNFPA-commissioned COVID-19 GBV analysis (conducted by the Institute of Research and Development) provided the most detailed examination of pandemic GBV dynamics, using case data from FPA, the Maldives Police Service, and the Prosecutor General's Office
- The **COVID-19 Income Support distribution** data revealed that only **15% of emergency relief loans went to women**, compared to approximately 74% to men – a figure that documented the structural exclusion of women from the formal financial response and increased economic dependence on male partners

The lockdown conditions in Male' – where population density already exceeds 65,000 people per square kilometre – placed families in extreme proximity with abusers in severely cramped conditions. The 2020 lockdown period in particular was associated with increased GBV reports globally and by FPA data in the Maldives.

---


## 5.28 The Maldives Study on Women's Health and Life Experiences (Fulu 2007): A Detailed Reading


### 5.46.1 Study Design and Methodology

The **Maldives Study on Women's Health and Life Experiences (WH&LE)** – commonly referred to as the Fulu study after lead researcher Emma Fulu – remains the foundational empirical reference for gender-based violence prevalence in the Maldives. Its methodology, findings, and limitations are essential for interpreting all subsequent GBV data.

**Methodology:** The study applied the **WHO multi-country study methodology** – a standardised, internationally validated approach to measuring intimate partner violence that had been applied in 10 countries prior to the Maldives study. Key features:

- **Nationally representative sample:** A two-stage cluster sampling design drawing from the 2000 Census sampling frame, with oversampling in Malé to allow separate urban analysis
- **Sample size:** Approximately 1,500 women interviewed across Malé and outer atolls
- **Interview method:** Face-to-face interview with a trained female interviewer, with specific privacy protocols to prevent the husband from being present during the interview
- **Standardised questionnaire:** The WHO conflict tactics scale adapted for the Maldivian context, with specific questions about physical, sexual, psychological, and economic violence; controlling behaviours; and sexual coercion
- **Childhood sexual abuse questions:** A specific module on childhood sexual abuse experiences using behaviourally specific questions (e.g., "Did anyone touch your private parts when you didn't want them to?") rather than the term "sexual abuse," which may not be understood[^427]

**Ethical framework:** The study applied WHO's ethical and safety guidelines for GBV research, including:
- Ensuring the interview was conducted in private
- Having a safety protocol for interviewers to follow if the woman appeared distressed
- Providing referral information for support services at the end of the interview
- Ensuring no record of study participation in the home

### 5.46.2 Core Findings Revisited

The Fulu study's core findings, contextualised within the broader evidence framework:

**IPV prevalence:** Approximately **one in three Maldivian women** had experienced physical and/or sexual violence from an intimate partner. This finding – that approximately 30-35% of ever-partnered women reported lifetime IPV – placed the Maldives at the higher end of international prevalence findings. Countries in the WHO multi-country study ranged from approximately 15% (urban Japan) to 71% (rural Ethiopia), with the Maldives at a level broadly comparable to Bangladesh and comparable South Asian contexts.[^428]

**Severity:** A significant proportion of women who experienced IPV reported severe forms – broken bones, burns, or being beaten unconscious. The presence of severe violence alongside the high prevalence suggests that the pattern is not confined to low-level violence that might be understood as "conflict"; it includes violence at the upper end of the severity spectrum.

**Psychological violence:** Psychological violence (humiliation, threats, surveillance) was even more prevalent than physical violence, reported by a majority of ever-partnered women in some sub-categories. This finding reinforces the DVPA's importance in covering psychological violence – and the challenge in addressing it given the evidentiary difficulties.

**Childhood sexual abuse:** **12.2% of women** reported experiencing childhood sexual abuse (penetrative or non-penetrative) nationally. For Malé specifically, **16.3%** reported CSA – a higher urban rate that may reflect higher actual prevalence (due to urbanisation dynamics, more complex household arrangements) or higher disclosure rates (due to greater awareness and privacy). This figure – approximately 1 in 8 Maldivian women having experienced childhood sexual abuse – is one of the most concerning findings in the study and has shaped child protection policy.[^429]

**Controlling behaviour:** Women reported high rates of controlling behaviour by intimate partners – including: telling her she couldn't meet friends (33%); limiting contact with family (20%); insisting on knowing where she was at all times (57%); jealousy and anger if she spoke to another man (50%). These controlling behaviours are the hallmark of **coercive control** – the pattern of domination that characterises abusive relationships beyond isolated violent incidents.

**Help-seeking:** A key finding was that most women who had experienced IPV had not sought help from formal sources (police, health services, NGOs). The primary reasons given: shame, fear of not being believed, the belief that the violence was normal or acceptable, and concern about the consequences for the family and children. This help-seeking data directly informed the design of the DVPA framework – establishing services that could reach women who were not self-presenting to authorities.

### 5.46.3 What the Fulu Study Doesn't Tell Us (18 Years Later)

The Fulu study was conducted in 2006-07. Eighteen years of social, legal, and economic change have substantially altered the context in which Maldivian women experience violence:

- The DVPA was enacted in 2012 – 5 years after the study
- The FPA was established and has processed thousands of cases
- The GEA was enacted in 2016
- Marital rape was criminalised in 2021
- The local council quota was implemented in 2021
- Digital technology and social media have transformed social life – including the nature of relationships, surveillance, and violence
- Urbanisation has accelerated – the Male' population has grown substantially

Whether these changes have affected actual IPV prevalence is unknown. A new national prevalence study is urgently needed to provide the evidence base for assessing whether the post-DVPA, post-GEA system has affected the situation of Maldivian women in practice – and to identify whether the nature of violence (forms, severity, help-seeking) has changed. The CEDAW Committee's call for a new prevalence study is well-founded.[^430]

---


## 5.29 Domestic Violence: The Complete Justice Pathway Analysis


### 5.5.1 From Disclosure to Protection Order

When a DV survivor in the Maldives discloses violence to the FPA or to any mandatory reporter (health worker, teacher, social worker), the formal pathway is designed as follows:

**Step 1:** Disclosure received by FPA caseworker (or referral from mandatory reporter to FPA)

**Step 2:** FPA conducts risk assessment using a standardised risk assessment tool; survivor is counselled on options including protection orders, refuge, and criminal complaint

**Step 3:** If survivor consents to legal action, FPA facilitates application to Family Court for protection order (or emergency protection order if immediate risk)

**Step 4:** Family Court issues temporary protection order (valid up to 21 days ex parte) or schedules full hearing for substantive protection order

**Step 5:** If violence constitutes criminal assault, FPA refers to police FCPW for criminal investigation

**Step 6:** Police FCPW investigates, prepares case file, and submits (or declines to submit) to Prosecutor General

**Step 7:** Prosecutor General reviews case file and decides whether to prosecute

**Step 8:** If prosecuted, case proceeds to Magistrate Court (for Penal Code assault) or Criminal Court

The gap between the formal design and operational reality is vast at almost every step. Police FCPW attitudes documented as discouraging formal complaints (Step 1 failure); risk assessments not conducted in most outer atoll cases (Step 2 failure); protection order applications not facilitated due to FPA geographic concentration in Male' (Step 3 failure); criminal referrals not made in most DV cases (Step 5 failure); and police submitting only 3% of referred cases for prosecution (Step 6 failure).

### 5.5.2 The Outer Atoll Gap

For a DV survivor on a small island in an outer atoll, the formal pathway above is largely inaccessible. The FPA's three caseworkers are in Male'. The Family Court – which issues protection orders – is in Male', with only magistrate courts and limited capacity in atolls. The specialist police FCPW is Male'-based. Legal aid is Male'-based.

In practice, DV response in outer atolls depends on whatever local capacity exists:
- **Island council members** (who may know the perpetrator personally and face community pressure not to intervene)
- **Local police** (who in the FPA Outer Atoll DV Response Assessment 2023 were found to rarely have specialised DV training)
- **WDC members** (volunteers without authority to compel action)
- **Atoll hospital staff** (frequently the first formal contact for survivors seeking medical care, but not systematically trained in DV identification, safety planning, or referral protocols despite the 2024 UNFPA clinical guidelines)

The CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report identifies atoll DV response as one of the most urgent capacity gaps requiring investment, specifically calling for deployment of DV caseworkers to atoll hospitals and regional centres.

### 5.5.3 Economic Dependence as a Structural Barrier

The economic dimensions of DV are as significant as the legal dimensions. A woman deciding whether to leave an abusive relationship faces economic consequences that may include:
- Loss of housing (particularly if the family home is owned or rented by the husband)
- Loss of income (if she has left employment due to marriage or children)
- Loss of child maintenance (imperfectly enforced even when awarded)
- Loss of economic support from a social network that may judge the decision to leave

These economic barriers are compounded by the specific economic structure of the Maldives. Housing in Male' consumes 37% of average household expenditure among renters – an extreme affordability burden that makes independent housing for a divorced or separated woman essentially unaffordable without sustained income. Social housing allocations do not prioritise DV survivors in a way that creates a reliable alternative.

The Single Parent Allowance (social protection cash transfer) provides some buffer, but at levels insufficient to cover rent in Male'. The FPA's safe house provides temporary refuge, but capacity is severely limited and time-limited stays do not address the medium-term housing need.

### 5.5.4 Social and Religious Barriers

Social norms in both Male' and outer atoll communities create barriers to reporting and to leaving abusive relationships that operate independently of legal frameworks:

**Family and community pressure:** In island communities where social reputation is paramount and community networks are close-knit, reporting domestic violence exposes the family to community scrutiny and potential stigma. Women have described being discouraged from reporting by their own family members – including parents and siblings – on grounds of family honour and community reputation.

**Religious framing:** DV has historically been framed by some religious voices through a lens that emphasises marital reconciliation, women's duty of obedience within marriage, and the use of Islamic mechanisms (*nushuz*, the concept of disobedient wife) that effectively place the burden of preventing marital violence on the wife's compliance. The MoSFD's DV Prevention Communication Strategy (2023) includes a component on working with the Ministry of Islamic Affairs to develop gender-sensitive religious messaging – recognising that transforming the religious framing of DV requires deliberate engagement with religious institutions, not just secular legal frameworks.

**Victim-blaming attitudes:** Survey data consistently documents substantial victim-blaming attitudes in the Maldivian population – the belief that women provoke or deserve domestic violence. This victim-blaming operates at the social level (affecting survivor decisions to report) and the institutional level (affecting police and judicial treatment of DV cases).

**Immigration of atoll women to Male':** Many women who leave abusive relationships in atolls migrate to Male' to escape – but find themselves in Male' without housing, income, family network, or access to legal services. Migration for safety creates a new set of vulnerabilities in the capital.

---


## 5.30 The DVPA in Practice: A Step-by-Step Case Analysis


### 5.21.1 Overview

The **Domestic Violence Prevention Act (Act 3/2012)** – commonly referred to as the DVPA – is the foundational legal instrument for addressing domestic violence in the Maldives. Its passage in 2012 was a significant step: for the first time, the state formally acknowledged that intimate partner violence and domestic abuse were legal matters requiring a public response, not merely private family disputes to be resolved within the household. Understanding the DVPA's practical operation – how it functions step-by-step from the first moment of disclosure to any ultimate legal outcome – is essential to assessing the extent to which it fulfils its protective function for survivors in the real conditions of Maldivian society.

The DVPA is a **civil law instrument**. This is a central jurisdictional fact about it. Acts of domestic violence that constitute criminal offences (assault, sexual assault) remain prosecutable under the Penal Code as criminal matters – with criminal courts, criminal evidentiary standards, and potential imprisonment as the outcome. The DVPA creates a parallel civil track: the primary remedy it provides is the **protection order**, a civil injunction that constrains the perpetrator's behaviour without involving criminal proceedings or requiring criminal proof standards. The civil-criminal distinction shapes everything about how the DVPA is used and what it can achieve.[^250]

### 5.21.2 Who Is Covered: The DVPA's Definitional Scope

The DVPA's coverage is defined by the relationship between perpetrator and victim. The Act covers violence perpetrated by:
- Spouses (current and former)
- Blood relatives
- Persons living in the same household
- Persons in dating or intimate relationships (not requiring formal marriage or cohabitation)
- Persons who share children (regardless of current relationship status)[^251]

This broad definition – extending beyond the marital relationship to former partners, dating partners, and family members – was deliberately designed to address the full spectrum of relationship contexts in which DV occurs. In the Maldivian context, this is particularly significant given the high divorce rate and pattern of serial relationships, meaning that a significant proportion of Maldivian adults have multiple current and former relationships within which DV dynamics may exist.

**Types of domestic violence** covered under the DVPA include:
- Physical violence (hitting, pushing, restraint, causing bodily harm)
- Sexual violence (within the relationship – now explicitly including marital rape following the 2021 amendment to the Sexual Offences Act, which is prosecuted separately under criminal law but also triggers DVPA civil protection)
- Psychological violence (threats, intimidation, verbal abuse, emotional manipulation)
- Economic abuse (controlling or restricting access to money, assets, employment)
- Social isolation (preventing contact with family and friends)
- Harassment and stalking (including by former partners post-separation)[^252]

The inclusion of psychological and economic abuse is significant, as these are harder to evidence than physical violence and represent dimensions of coercive control that can be severely harmful without ever involving a physical assault. In practice, the challenge is that psychological and economic abuse are also the dimensions most difficult to document and most likely to face judicial scepticism – meaning the broader definitional coverage has not fully translated into effective legal protection for survivors of non-physical forms of abuse.[^253]

### 5.21.3 Stage 1: Initial Disclosure

The DVPA process begins with a survivor's decision to disclose violence and seek help. This decision – seemingly straightforward as a matter of legal process – is actually the most demanding step for most survivors, involving a complex calculation of social, economic, and safety factors.

**Disclosure pathways** under the DVPA framework include:

- **Direct report to the Family Protection Authority (FPA):** The FPA is the designated first point of contact for DV under the DVPA framework. A survivor can contact the FPA by telephone, visit its Malé office, or – in theory – be referred to a WDC caseworker on their island who contacts the FPA on their behalf.[^254]

- **Report to police (FCPW):** The Family and Child Protection Wing of the Maldives Police Service (MPS) accepts DV reports and is required to refer cases to the FPA while also taking any necessary immediate safety measures. In acute emergency situations, police are the first responder; in non-emergency situations, FPA is the primary intake channel.[^255]

- **Report to a health facility:** Healthcare providers who identify DV in a clinical encounter – through injury patterns, disclosure, or clinical suspicion – are mandated reporters under the DVPA framework. The 2024 UNFPA National Guidelines for Healthcare Providers on GBV establish the clinical protocol for this pathway.[^256]

- **Self-referral to WDC or NGO:** Women who are unwilling to go directly to the FPA or police may first disclose to a WDC member or to Hope for Women, which can then facilitate a supported referral. This pathway is particularly significant for outer island women where WDCs represent the only locally accessible entry point.[^257]

**What happens at first disclosure:** When a survivor contacts the FPA, a trained caseworker conducts an initial intake interview. The intake covers:
- The nature and history of the violence
- Immediate safety assessment (Is she in immediate danger? Does she have somewhere safe to stay tonight?)
- Children in the household and their safety
- Whether she wishes to pursue a protection order
- Her economic circumstances and housing situation
- Her support network

The intake is conducted confidentially. FPA caseworkers are trained in trauma-informed interviewing – using open-ended questions, not expressing judgment, not pressuring decisions about what legal steps to take. The quality of this intake varies with caseworker skill and, critically, with the caseworker's caseload. The AIM Project Baseline Assessment (2022) documented that FPA caseworkers in Malé carry caseloads substantially above recommended levels, meaning intake interviews may be rushed and follow-up may be delayed.[^258]

**Barriers at the disclosure stage:** The evidence on DV disclosure barriers in the Maldivian context – drawn from the Fulu 2007 study, FPA case records, AIM Project assessment, and the CEDAW reporting – consistently identifies:

1. **Fear of not being believed:** Survivors who have been told by abusive partners that no one will believe them have internalised this fear. The historical pattern of police and Family Court responses that treated DV as a private matter reinforced this fear with actual evidence of non-response.

2. **Fear of consequences for children:** Many survivors fear that reporting DV will result in custody proceedings that could remove their children. The legal reality – that reporting is more likely to protect children than harm them – is not widely known.

3. **Economic dependency:** As documented in §5.9, women without independent income depend financially on the abusive partner. Reporting risks the loss of housing and income simultaneously.

4. **Social stigma:** In small island communities, reporting DV is known to the community almost immediately. The social consequences – being labelled a "troublemaker," being blamed for destroying the marriage, being excluded from social circles – are real and feared.

5. **Religious framing:** Some survivors have been told by family members, community leaders, or even religious figures that they must endure marital problems, that divorce is shameful, or that the violence is their own fault for not being a good wife. Religious framing of DV as a private marital matter is a documented barrier to disclosure in the HRCM Rights Side of Life Survey (2019).[^259]

6. **Geographic isolation:** Women on outer islands face the specific barrier that the FPA is in Malé, telephone contact may be limited by coverage or privacy concerns in shared housing, and visiting the FPA in person requires a journey that is expensive, logistically complex, and potentially visible to the abusive partner.

7. **Normalised perceptions of DV:** The UNICEF Social Sector Assessment (2022) identified normalised perceptions of domestic violence as a distinct barrier category, in which women and their families do not classify certain behaviours as violence because they are considered normal within the relationship or community context.[^259a]

8. **Fear of prolonged cohabitation during legal process:** Women who do report face the prospect of continuing to live with the perpetrator for the duration of what may be a multi-year legal process, given the limited shelter capacity and the absence of alternative housing arrangements documented in §5.28.6 above.[^259a]

### 5.21.4 Stage 2: FPA Casework and Safety Planning

Following intake, the FPA caseworker develops a **safety plan** with the survivor. Safety planning is a standard DV case management tool: a structured conversation about what the survivor can do to keep herself safe in the immediate future, before any legal protection is in place.

A Maldivian safety plan in practice might include:
- Identification of a trusted person to contact in emergency
- Securing copies of important documents (ID card, marriage certificate, children's birth certificates)
- Planning an exit route from the home if violence escalates
- Identifying a place to go – family home, shelter – in an emergency
- If on an outer island, planning the logistics of reaching a safer location
- Agreement on the FPA caseworker as the ongoing point of contact

The safety planning conversation is also where the caseworker assesses whether the survivor wants to pursue a protection order, and explains what the protection order process involves, what it can and cannot do, and what the timeframes are. Informed consent to the protection order process – including understanding that the perpetrator will be formally notified – is essential but may not always be adequately obtained in high-caseload, time-pressured conditions.[^260]

**Case management documentation:** The FPA case file created at intake is the primary record of the case going forward. This file documents the disclosure, the safety plan, referrals made, and any subsequent interactions. In theory, this file is accessible to other system actors (police, court) on a need-to-know basis. In practice, the AIM Project found that no integrated electronic case management system exists: each agency maintains its own records, and information sharing requires manual coordination between agencies, creating gaps and duplications in the case record.[^261]

### 5.21.5 Stage 3: Protection Order Application

The protection order is the DVPA's primary legal remedy. It is a civil court order that can:
- Prohibit the perpetrator from contacting the survivor
- Require the perpetrator to vacate the shared residence
- Establish no-contact requirements
- Prohibit the perpetrator from approaching the survivor's workplace, children's school, or other specified locations
- Grant temporary custody of children to the survivor

Protection orders are issued by the **Family Court** in Malé, or by the relevant **Magistrate Court** in outer atolls. The application process involves:

1. The survivor (or the FPA on her behalf) submits a protection order application to the court
2. The court schedules a hearing – in urgent cases within 24 hours; in standard cases within a legislatively specified timeframe
3. An **interim protection order** may be issued immediately without a hearing if the court is satisfied of immediate danger – this is an ex parte order (without the perpetrator being present)
4. A full protection order hearing is held with both parties present (or the perpetrator notified to appear)
5. The court issues, modifies, or denies the protection order
6. The order is served on the perpetrator by the police
7. Violation of a protection order is a criminal offence under the DVPA[^262]

**Interim orders in practice:** The interim protection order provision – allowing immediate protection without a full hearing – is critically important for acute danger situations. In practice, interim orders are underused in outer atolls because the Magistrate Court may have limited sessions, may not have a judge resident on the island (travelling circuit judges), or may not have staff who understand the interim order procedure. The result is that survivors in outer atolls in acute danger may wait days or weeks for any legal protection, even though the law provides for immediate response.[^263]

**The protection order hearing:** The full protection order hearing presents significant barriers for survivors:
- **Requirement to appear in court and face the perpetrator:** Many survivors, particularly those with ongoing fear of the perpetrator, find this requirement psychologically extremely difficult. Courts do not routinely provide protective measures such as screen arrangements, separate waiting areas, or video testimony (provisions that exist in domestic violence courts in other jurisdictions).
- **No mandatory legal representation:** Survivors who cannot afford a private lawyer and do not reach the Family Legal Clinic may appear unrepresented against a perpetrator who may have legal representation, creating a significant power asymmetry in the courtroom.
- **Evidentiary requirements:** While the DVPA establishes civil evidentiary standards (lower than criminal beyond reasonable doubt), survivors must still provide evidence of the violence – medical records, photographs, witness testimony. Collecting this evidence in advance of the hearing is challenging for survivors who have not been advised to document violence systematically.[^264]

**Protection orders in outer atolls:** The Magistrate Court system in outer atolls is the formal channel for protection orders outside Malé. In practice:
- Some atolls have a resident magistrate; others are served by circuit judges visiting on a schedule
- The application process may require the survivor to travel to the atoll capital
- In documented cases, the magistrate has been personally acquainted with the perpetrator or the perpetrator's family – raising concerns about impartiality in small island communities
- Post-order enforcement – ensuring the perpetrator complies – depends on the same small police force that may already have complicated relationships with the parties[^265]

### 5.21.6 Stage 4: Criminal Parallel Track

Separately from the DVPA protection order process, acts of domestic violence that constitute criminal offences are prosecutable under the Penal Code. The criminal pathway involves:
- Police investigation of the criminal complaint
- Referral to the Prosecutor General's Office for prosecution decision
- Prosecution in the Criminal Court
- Conviction and criminal sentencing (which may include imprisonment, fines, or community service)

The **parallel track problem** – managing both civil (DVPA protection order) and criminal (Penal Code prosecution) processes simultaneously – is a significant challenge for survivors and for system coordination. A survivor may have an active protection order case in the Family Court while simultaneously navigating a criminal complaint in the Criminal Court, requiring separate appearances, separate documentation, and coordination of testimony across both systems. The absence of integrated case management means these parallel processes are not systematically coordinated.[^266]

**The prosecution attrition story:** The justice pathway attrition documented in §5.4 is most acute at the criminal prosecution stage. Of the 696 DV cases reported to the FPA in 2020 – the highest annual figure on record – approximately **3%** reached prosecution. Of those prosecuted, approximately **16.7%** resulted in conviction. This means that in 2020, with 696 cases reported, roughly **21 cases were prosecuted** and roughly **3–4 convictions** were recorded. Given that the 696 FPA cases represent only a fraction of actual DV incidents, the ratio of actual incidents to criminal convictions is in the order of thousands to one.[^267]

The primary drivers of prosecution attrition identified in the AIM Project Baseline Assessment (2022) are:
1. **Case withdrawal:** Survivors withdraw complaints – due to economic dependency, reconciliation, fear, or family pressure – before prosecution can proceed
2. **Evidence insufficiency:** Police investigations do not produce evidence adequate for the Prosecutor General's prosecution threshold
3. **Prosecution discretion:** The PG's Office applies a probability-of-conviction standard that results in many domestic violence cases not meeting the threshold for prosecution
4. **Witness reluctance:** Witness testimony from family members, neighbours, or friends who observed violence is often withheld in the Maldivian social context

### 5.21.7 Stage 5: Post-Order Experience and Long-Term Outcomes

A protection order – if issued – does not end the case. The survivor must navigate the post-order period with an order that has real legal force but depends on police enforcement to be effective:

**Protection order violations:** Violations of DVPA protection orders are criminal offences. In theory, a perpetrator who violates a protection order should be immediately arrested and prosecuted for the violation. In practice:
- Violations are underreported – survivors may fear further violence if they report a violation
- Police response to reported violations varies; in outer islands, response capacity may be very limited
- Prosecution for violation adds another layer to an already complex case management burden

**Housing after the order:** A protection order that requires the perpetrator to vacate the shared residence creates an immediate housing problem – where does the perpetrator go? In a country where rental housing is extremely limited outside Malé, a man ordered to vacate may have nowhere to go, creating social pressure for compromise. Conversely, a survivor who must leave the shared home – particularly if it is the perpetrator's property – faces housing insecurity without a shelter placement.

**The reconciliation dynamic:** In a high-divorce, high-remarriage society like the Maldives, the dynamics of reconciliation are complex. Many DV survivors – under economic, family, and social pressure – reconcile with abusive partners. Reconciliation is their legal right and cannot be prohibited. However, reconciliation without the underlying violence being addressed means the protection order becomes effectively null, and the cycle of violence continues. The FPA's counselling services include couples counselling in some cases (a contested approach in DV policy internationally, as couples counselling in an abusive relationship can increase danger), but individual counselling for survivors is the primary ongoing support mechanism.[^268]

**Long-term outcomes:** Systematic long-term outcome data on DV cases in the Maldives – tracking what happened to survivors 1, 3, or 5 years after their FPA engagement – does not exist. The absence of longitudinal data makes it impossible to assess whether FPA intervention, protection orders, or criminal prosecution produce meaningful improvement in survivors' safety over time. This is itself a major data gap that the AIM Project's information management system component is partially designed to address.[^269]

---


## 5.31 Sexual Violence: Legal Framework, Prosecution, and Systemic Failures


### 5.6.1 Marital Rape: History, Criminalisation, and Enforcement

The history of marital rape law in the Maldives passes through three phases:

**Phase 1 – Legally invisible (pre-2014):** Before the Sexual Offences Act 2014, there was no statutory definition of rape in its contemporary form. Non-consensual sexual intercourse within marriage was effectively unaddressed by the criminal law – both because the concept of marital rape had no domestic legal recognition and because the evidentiary and social barriers to any sexual violence complaint were prohibitive.

**Phase 2 – Marital exception explicitly embedded (2014–2021):** The Sexual Offences Act 2014 provided a contemporary definition of rape based on consent. However, Section 130(b) of the Penal Code maintained a rebuttable presumption of consent within marriage, and the SOA as enacted contained provisions that, taken together, effectively excluded marital rape from the definition of rape. UNFPA's 2023 retrospective review described this as marital rape being "incompletely criminalised" during this period. The CEDAW Committee's 2021 Concluding Observations explicitly called for this gap to be closed.

**Phase 3 – Marital rape criminalised (2021–present):** The 2021 Amendment to the Sexual Offences Act removed the marital exception. Sexual intercourse without consent within marriage is now criminal rape under Maldivian law, aligning the statute with international standards. However, the 2021 amendment is only the beginning of addressing the problem: prosecutions under the amended provision remain rare. Women in marriages who are subjected to marital rape face all of the same barriers to reporting, investigation, and prosecution that apply to DV more broadly, plus additional barriers specific to sexual violence (including the Section 52 evidence requirements and the social and economic dependence of marriage).[^44]

**Enforcement barriers:**
- Victim reluctance to report sexual violence within marriage due to social stigma, fear of family reaction, economic dependence on the husband
- Police training on marital rape provisions is limited and inconsistent
- The absence of specialised Sexual Assault Referral Centre (SARC) services – with forensic nurse capacity, immediate evidence collection, and psychological support – means that the evidentiary pathway for prosecution is weak from the point of initial report
- Judicial unfamiliarity with the amended provision and absence of judicial training on sexual violence case management

### 5.6.2 Rape Outside Marriage: The Evidential Barrier

For rape outside marriage, the evidentiary framework of the SOA creates a high prosecution threshold. Section 52 specifies five categories of admissible evidence:
1. Testimony of the victim
2. Testimony of witnesses
3. Medical evidence
4. Forensic evidence
5. Confession of the accused

The combination of these requirements – particularly when the offence has occurred in private without witnesses – creates an evidentiary architecture where victim testimony alone is rarely considered sufficient for prosecution, medical evidence is only available if the survivor presents immediately for forensic examination (which many do not), forensic evidence degrades quickly and requires specialist collection capacity, and confessions are rare. Section 53, which permits courts to consider delayed reporting as a credibility factor, further disadvantages survivors who delay reporting due to shock, fear, or lack of trust in the system.

The **past sexual history of the complainant** is admissible under the current SOA framework – a provision inconsistent with international best practice in sexual violence prosecution (where "rape shield" laws typically exclude such evidence) and one that interacts badly with Maldivian social norms around female sexuality, where a survivor's sexual history is frequently used to cast doubt on the account of rape.

### 5.6.3 The Zina-Pregnancy Intersection and Its Effects on Rape Reporting

The most serious structural problem at the intersection of Islamic criminal law and sexual violence in the Maldives is the criminalisation of extramarital sexual intercourse (*zina*) and its interaction with pregnancy. Penal Code Section 411(d) provides 100 lashes for zina. Women constitute 85% of flogging sentence recipients – a gender disparity that reflects the inherently gendered evidentiary structure: pregnancy outside wedlock is itself direct evidence of zina for the woman, while men can only be convicted on witness testimony or confession.

For a woman who is raped and becomes pregnant, this creates an outcome so perverse that it borders on institutional re-victimisation: she may be prosecuted for zina on the basis of the pregnancy resulting from her rape. This outcome – documented by the HRCM in its 2022 report on corporal punishment – creates a powerful rational incentive for rape survivors not to report the crime, not to seek medical attention, and to conceal pregnancies. The CEDAW Committee in its 2021 Concluding Observations identified this as a priority concern (paragraph 51) requiring urgent legislative action.

### 5.6.4 Sexual Harassment in the Workplace

The **Prevention of Sexual Harassment Act 2014** requires employers to establish written anti-harassment policies and internal complaint mechanisms. The UNDP/NCTC Youth Vulnerability Study (2019) found that "women across the country complained about being asked about their marital status, their children, and their clothing in interviews, saying this was routine." Young women entering the workforce reported systematic workplace harassment that was treated as normal institutional behaviour.

The HRCM's monitoring of institutional compliance found that 43 institutions had established formal complaint mechanisms as required by law. The number of formal complaints received by those mechanisms: **zero**. This zero-complaint figure is not evidence of an absence of harassment but of an absence of confidence that formal complaints would be taken seriously, not harm the complainant's career, or produce any meaningful consequence for perpetrators. The formal architecture exists; the trust infrastructure to make it function does not.

Resort sector harassment is believed to be significantly underreported. Women workers in resorts – comprising only 3% of Maldivian female resort workers – face particular vulnerabilities related to geographic isolation (resorts on uninhabited islands away from family networks), 24-hour work environments, limited access to complaint mechanisms, and social stigma attached to women in hospitality settings. Cases of sexual harassment and assault in the resort sector face all the general evidence barriers plus the additional barrier of the resort operator's interest in minimising incidents that could damage reputation.

### 5.6.5 Online Sexual Exploitation and Digital Violence

The rapid penetration of smartphones and social media in the Maldives has created new vectors for sexual exploitation and gender-based violence:

**Image-based abuse:** The non-consensual sharing of intimate images ("revenge porn") is a documented phenomenon in the Maldivian context. Women who have shared intimate images within relationships have found those images distributed after relationship breakdown as a tool of harassment, blackmail, and reputational destruction. Legal provisions against this form of abuse exist under the Electronic Communications Act but are rarely invoked.

**Online harassment:** Women in public life – politicians, journalists, civil society advocates – face systematic online harassment, including threats of sexual violence, body-shaming, and coordinated campaigns to silence their public participation. The Maldivian Democracy Network's Women's Political Participation Study (2023) identified online harassment as a significant barrier preventing women from standing for elected office.

**Child sexual exploitation online (CSEO):** The FCSC (Family and Child Services Centre) case data documents increasing referrals involving online components – grooming through social media, solicitation of images from minors, and exploitation of children through digital platforms. While CSEO primarily affects children, it has specific gendered dimensions: girls are disproportionately targeted for image solicitation and sexual grooming.

**Trafficking via digital platforms:** Recruitment for sexual exploitation has shifted online, with trafficking networks using social media and messaging applications to identify and recruit vulnerable women and girls.

---


## 5.32 GBV in Specific Relationship Contexts


### 5.41.1 Violence Against Women in Dating Relationships

The DVPA's extended coverage to dating relationships – intimate partners who are not married or cohabiting – is an important legal development that reflects the reality of GBV in the Maldivian social context. With urbanisation, social media, and changing courtship patterns, dating violence has become an identifiable category of GBV that the FPA's case records include.

Dating violence characteristics in the Maldivian context include:
- **Digital control:** Monitoring of phone and social media, demanding access to accounts, sending threatening messages – forms of coercive control that are characteristic of intimate partner violence in digital-communication-mediated relationships
- **Physical violence in escalating relationships:** Dating violence often escalates in parallel with emotional intensity – violence occurring in the context of intense, rapidly escalating relationships
- **Difficulty using the DVPA:** Young women in dating relationships may be less aware that the DVPA covers their situation, or may not self-identify as domestic violence survivors when the relationship is not a marriage
- **Social complexity:** In a small, well-networked society, breaking up with a violent partner while still encountering them in social, educational, or professional contexts creates sustained risk[^412]

### 5.41.2 Violence in Same-Sex Relationships

Same-sex relationships are criminalised in the Maldives under the Penal Code's zina and sodomy provisions, reflecting Islamic law's prohibition of homosexual conduct. This criminalisation has direct and severe consequences for LGBTQ+ individuals who experience domestic violence: reporting DV to police or the FPA risks exposure as a same-sex relationship partner, triggering potential criminal prosecution and severe social consequences including family rejection and community ostracism.[^413]

The complete invisibility of same-sex DV in the formal GBV data system reflects the impossibility of disclosure in this legal environment. LGBTQ+ individuals – documented as present in Maldivian society by civil society research and by HRCM engagement with the issue – who experience intimate partner violence have no safe reporting pathway and are effectively excluded from the GBV response system. The FPA does not have a protocol for same-sex DV, and the DVPA's gender-neutral language could theoretically cover same-sex relationships, but the social and legal context makes this pathway inaccessible in practice.[^414]

### 5.41.3 Post-Separation Violence

The period immediately following separation or divorce is internationally documented as the highest-risk period for serious and lethal DV – perpetrators who lose control over a partner through separation may escalate violence as an attempt to reassert control. In the Maldivian context, this post-separation risk is amplified by:

- **Economic dependency:** Women who are financially dependent on estranged husbands are more likely to maintain contact that creates risk
- **Children as contact mechanism:** Custody and visitation arrangements require ongoing contact between parties, creating opportunities for confrontation
- **Housing proximity:** In small island communities, it may be impossible for separated parties to avoid encountering each other
- **Digital harassment:** Ex-partners who have access to phone numbers, social media accounts, and mutual social networks can maintain harassment and surveillance after physical separation
- **Family and community pressure for reconciliation:** The same family dynamics that discourage initial disclosure may pressure separated women to reconcile, returning them to the high-risk post-disclosure period[^415]

The DVPA protection order is the primary legal tool for managing post-separation risk. Its effectiveness in this context is limited by the enforcement challenges documented throughout this chapter. A woman with a protection order in a small island community where the perpetrator knows her daily movements, the police have limited enforcement capacity, and the community is pressuring reconciliation is in a very different practical risk situation than the legal document suggests.

### 5.41.4 Violence Associated with the Immigration of Male Workers

A specific and documentable pattern of DV in the Maldivian context involves male migrant workers – predominantly from Bangladesh and Nepal – who are employed in construction and other sectors in Malé and occasionally in married relationships with Maldivian women or with female migrant workers. The characteristics of DV in this context include:

- **Extreme financial dependency:** Female partners of migrant workers may be entirely dependent on the migrant worker's income, with no local family support network and no work permit of their own
- **Migrant worker stress:** Male migrant workers face documented occupational exploitation, wage theft, and poor living conditions – stressors that are associated with GBV risk in international literature on migrant labour and DV
- **Limited recourse:** Female partners of migrant workers who experience DV may be reluctant to report – knowing that reporting may result in the male partner's deportation (ending the household's income) or in their own deportation if their stay is conditional on the relationship
- **FPA language barriers:** The FPA operates primarily in Dhivehi; migrant women from Bangladesh, Sri Lanka, or Nepal may not communicate in Dhivehi, limiting their access to case management services[^416]

---


## 5.33 The GBV Response System: An Integrated Flowchart Analysis


### 5.48.1 From Incident to Outcome: The Full Case Journey

The following analysis traces the journey of a hypothetical domestic violence case through the Maldivian GBV response system from incident to final outcome – illustrating the attrition points and the decision nodes that determine whether any particular case reaches a meaningful legal outcome.

**Stage 0: The incident pool.** The Fulu 2007 study establishes a baseline DV prevalence of approximately one in three ever-partnered women.
