In a population of approximately 100,000 adult women, this implies roughly 30,000-35,000 women who have ever experienced IPV – many in ongoing abusive relationships. This is the denominator: the pool of actual violence incidents from which the official response system selects.

**Stage 1: The disclosure decision.** Of the estimated 30,000+ women who have ever experienced IPV, approximately 696 cases were reported to the FPA in 2020 – approximately 2% of the estimated total ever-IPV population. This disclosure rate is consistent with international DV disclosure literature (which typically estimates 20-30% of DV incidents are ever disclosed to anyone, and 10-15% are disclosed to official services). The Maldivian 2% rate reflects the specific disclosure barriers documented in this chapter.

**Stage 2: FPA intake.** Of the 696 cases reported, all receive FPA intake. However, intake quality varies with caseworker caseload. Cases may be closed at intake if the survivor decides not to proceed, if contact is lost, or if the situation is assessed as below threshold for continued engagement.

**Stage 3: Protection order application.** An unknown fraction of FPA cases proceed to protection order application. The AIM Project documents that this fraction is substantially below the intake number – many survivors do not want or cannot navigate the court process. Assume approximately 30-40% of FPA cases result in a protection order application, based on available attrition data.

**Stage 4: Protection order issuance.** Of applications filed, a further fraction are issued as protection orders – accounting for cases where the application is incomplete, the hearing is missed, or the court declines to issue the order. The protection order issuance rate from applications is not documented publicly but is assumed to be the majority of applications that reach a full hearing.

**Stage 5: Criminal investigation.** Parallel to the civil track, some FPA cases are referred to or directly reported to police. Police investigate. Of the 696 FPA cases, the 3% prosecution rate indicates approximately 21 cases proceeded to prosecution – implying approximately 21 police investigations resulted in charges being filed with the PG's Office and the PG agreeing to prosecute.

**Stage 6: Prosecution and conviction.** Of the approximately 21 prosecuted cases, approximately 3-4 resulted in conviction (16.7% conviction rate). This represents 3-4 criminal outcomes from 696 disclosures – roughly 0.5% of disclosed cases.

**Stage 7: Actual incident denominator.** If disclosed cases represent 2% of actual incidents, and 0.5% of disclosed cases result in criminal conviction, then approximately 1 in 10,000 actual DV incidents results in criminal conviction – a rate that provides no meaningful deterrence and minimal accountability.

This flowchart analysis – while using estimated figures at some stages – illustrates the structural problem: the GBV response system, as currently designed and resourced, converts approximately one in ten thousand actual incidents of DV into a criminal accountability outcome. Achieving meaningful accountability would require either dramatically reducing attrition at each stage (through better investigation, evidence-based prosecution, survivor support) or dramatically increasing the entry rate (through better disclosure systems and reduced barriers) – or both.[^436]

### 5.48.2 Entry Points That Could Expand Access

The flowchart reveals specific entry points where targeted intervention could most effectively reduce attrition:

**Stage 1→2 (disclosure rate):** Awareness campaigns, social norm change, and accessible reporting channels (including telephone and digital reporting that does not require physical travel) could increase the proportion of survivors who report. The more effective outer island WDC network envisioned by the DVPA would increase atoll disclosure significantly.

**Stage 2→3 (FPA to protection order):** Supported case management – with caseworkers actively supporting survivors through the protection order application rather than providing information only – could increase the protection order application rate. The FLC's legal support is important here.

**Stage 3→4 (application to order):** Expedited hearing procedures, interpreter support, video testimony, and separate waiting areas could reduce the barriers to survivors completing the protection order process.

**Stage 5→6 (investigation to prosecution):** Evidence-based prosecution approaches – developing cases using all available evidence, not solely survivor testimony – could increase prosecution rates independently of survivor withdrawal.

**Stage 6→7 (prosecution to conviction):** Judicial training in GBV case handling and application of the consent standard could improve conviction rates in cases that reach court.

---


## 5.34 The Shelter System: Operational Analysis and Expansion Needs


### 5.99.1 Three Shelters for 187 Islands

The operational architecture of the Maldivian DV shelter system – three ADB-funded shelters plus the FPA's transitional facility in Malé – can be precisely characterized by the geographic coverage it provides and does not provide:

**Shelter 1: Hithadhoo, Addu Atoll (southernmost)**
- Geographic catchment: Addu Atoll and the southern atolls (Gnaviyani/Fuvahmulah, Seenu/Addu)
- Population served: Addu Atoll has a significant population (Hithadhoo is one of the country's largest urban centres)
- Access: Women in remote islands of the southern atolls must travel to Hithadhoo to access the shelter

**Shelter 2: Hulhumale' (Greater Malé region)**
- Geographic catchment: Greater Malé region including Malé, Hulhumale', Villimalé
- Population served: The most densely populated area; this shelter addresses the capital region's shelter gap
- Access: Accessible from Malé by ferry; most accessible of the three shelters for the country's largest DV caseload

**Shelter 3: Raa Atoll**
- Geographic catchment: North-central atolls (Raa, Baa, Shaviyani)
- Population served: North-central atoll populations
- Access: Women in the northern atolls (Haa Alif, Haa Dhaal) must travel south to access the shelter

**Coverage gaps:** The three shelters provide geographic anchor points for three regions – greater south, greater Malé, and north-central. The following atoll regions have no shelter within accessible travel distance:
- Central atolls (Kaafu Atoll excluding Malé/Hulhumale', Alif Alif, Alif Dhaal, Vaavu)
- Central-eastern atolls (Faafu, Dhaalu, Thaa, Laamu)
- Far south (Gaafu Alif, Gaafu Dhaal, Fuvahmulah – Fuvahmulah is an outlier island with no atoll)

The gap is most acute in the central atolls – which include significant island populations – and in Laamu, whose vulnerability mapping specifically identified DV as a high-priority concern.

**Expansion plan:** The CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report (2025) and AIM Project documentation acknowledge the shelter gap and indicate plans for expansion. Whether specific new shelter locations have been funded and committed is not documented in available public sources as of early 2025.[^533]

### 5.99.2 Shelter Operations and Standards

DV shelter operations require more than physical infrastructure. International shelter standards (developed by UN Women and the NGO Women's Shelter Network) specify operational requirements including:

- **Safety and security:** Physical security measures preventing perpetrators from locating or entering the shelter; privacy of location; security protocols for residents
- **Resident services:** Basic accommodation (private rooms or dormitories), meals, children's facilities, counselling, legal advice, childcare
- **Case management:** Each resident assigned a caseworker who develops a safety and exit plan; coordination with FPA, courts, and FCPW
- **Exit planning:** Transition out of shelter to sustainable housing (the absence of which is the primary shelter limitation in the Maldivian context)
- **Children's services:** Dedicated space and programming for children in the shelter; liaison with schools; CFPS coordination where child welfare concerns exist

The ADB GESI Project's shelter component includes operational standards development alongside infrastructure. Whether the three shelters meet international operational standards consistently is not documented in available public sources; the AIM Project's system strengthening component addresses service quality but focuses primarily on FPA casework rather than shelter operations specifically.[^534]

---


## 5.35 The GBV Data System: Sources, Gaps, and Interpretive Challenges


### 5.32.1 Data Sources and Gaps

Understanding the GBV and gender inequality situation in the Maldives requires navigating a range of data sources with different methodologies, coverage, and purposes. No single data source is comprehensive; policy conclusions must be drawn from triangulated evidence across multiple sources, each with its own strengths and limitations.

**Primary data sources for gender policy analysis:**

| Source | Methodology | Reference Year | Strengths | Limitations |
|--------|-------------|---------------|-----------|-------------|
| Maldives DHS 2016-17 | Nationally representative survey | 2016-17 | Comprehensive; internationally comparable; covers DV, SRHR, nutrition, reproductive health | 8 years old; geographic coverage may not capture rapid urban-rural change |
| FPA Case Statistics | Administrative case registry | Annual (2009-present) | Time series; covers all FPA intakes | Reports only; not prevalence; methodology changes over time |
| Census 2022 | Full population enumeration | 2022 | Most current; covers all households | Limited on sensitive topics like DV |
| HIES 2016 | Household income survey | 2016 | Labour market; income; poverty | Outdated; limited on gender-specific questions |
| WHO STEP Survey 2021 | Health survey | 2021 | Mental health; NCD; some gender | Not designed primarily for gender |
| Fulu 2007 (WH&LE Study) | Dedicated GBV prevalence study | 2007 | Gold standard on DV/CSA prevalence | 18 years old |
| CEDAW Periodic Reports | Government self-report | 2025 (7th) | Official; comprehensive | Government framing; not independently verified |
| AIM Project Baseline (2022) | System assessment | 2022 | Operational detail; justice pathway | Not nationally representative |
| UNFPA/ADB assessments | Programme-linked assessments | Various | Thematic depth | Programme-linked; may have scope limitations |

### 5.32.2 The DV Prevalence Data Gap

The most significant data gap in Maldivian gender policy is the **absence of current national GBV prevalence data**. The Fulu 2007 study remains the only national population-based study of DV and intimate partner violence prevalence – it is now 18 years old. The social, economic, and legal environment in which Maldivian women experience GBV has changed substantially since 2007: the DVPA was enacted, the FPA established, the local council quota implemented, the GEA enacted, the marital rape criminalised. Whether these changes have affected actual DV prevalence (as distinct from reported rates) is unknown without a new prevalence study.

**What the FPA case series cannot tell us:** The FPA's annual case statistics are not a measure of prevalence. They measure the number of cases reported to the FPA – a function of awareness, accessibility, and willingness to report, as well as actual violence incidence. A system that has successfully increased help-seeking will show rising case numbers even if actual violence is declining. Conversely, a system that has become less accessible will show declining case numbers even as violence increases. The FPA case series must be interpreted as a help-seeking indicator, not a prevalence indicator.[^363]

**The CEDAW Committee's call:** The November 2021 Concluding Observations specifically called for a new national DV prevalence study to update the 2007 baseline. The Seventh Periodic Report (2025) does not indicate that such a study has been commissioned. This gap is critical for evidence-based policy: without current prevalence data, it is impossible to assess whether any intervention has changed the actual experience of violence for Maldivian women.

### 5.32.3 The Labour Market Data Gap

**Gender-disaggregated wage data** is not collected or published systematically by the National Bureau of Statistics. The most recent comprehensive household income and expenditure data (HIES 2016) is eight years old. The gender pay gap figure of 13-20% is drawn from ADB assessments rather than official national statistics, and its precise methodology is not specified in available sources.[^364]

The absence of systematic wage data means:
- Equal pay legislation, if enacted, would be impossible to enforce without wage data collection
- GRB processes that should account for wage inequality cannot be evidence-based
- CEDAW reporting on Article 11 (employment equality) cannot include systematic wage data
- Trends in the gender pay gap – whether narrowing or widening – cannot be tracked

### 5.32.4 The Property and Asset Ownership Gap

Women's property and land ownership is **not tracked** by any national data system. The National Bureau of Statistics does not publish data on gender-disaggregated property ownership, inheritance receipts, or household asset ownership. Land registration data, if gender-disaggregated, would provide this information; but the Land Registry has not published gender-disaggregated data.

This gap means that the cumulative impact of the inheritance half-share rule, the post-divorce housing insecurity pattern, and women's lower income and credit access on women's lifetime asset accumulation is unknown quantitatively. The gender wealth gap – which international evidence suggests may be larger than the income gap – is essentially invisible in Maldivian statistics.[^365]

### 5.32.5 Trafficking Data

Anti-trafficking data is particularly sparse. The primary sources are:
- US State Department TIP Reports (annual) – providing a qualitative assessment and tier rating
- International Organization for Migration (IOM) – providing some data on identified trafficking victims
- Police records – not systematically published

**What is known:** TIP Tier 2 Watch List status (consecutive years); dedicated hotline inoperable; zero government employees prosecuted; extremely low victim identification rate. **What is unknown:** The actual scale of trafficking (number of victims), the profile of victims by nationality, gender, and exploitation type, and the precise mechanisms through which trafficking occurs and remains undetected. The absence of an operational NRM means that data collection on trafficking is itself severely limited.[^366]

### 5.32.6 The Atoll Disaggregation Gap

National statistics mask enormous geographic variation in gender outcomes. The HIES and DHS provide some atoll-level disaggregation for large sample variables, but most gender-relevant statistics are not published at atoll level. This means:
- LFPR by atoll (which would show the outer atoll female LFPR to be far below the national 44%) is not published
- DV case rates by atoll (which would show geographic concentration and identify highest-need areas) are not published
- Educational outcome data by atoll and gender is available from the Ministry of Education but not routinely aggregated in gender policy reports

The Laamu Atoll Vulnerability Mapping represents a methodological model that could, if applied systematically across all atolls, provide the subnational geographic disaggregation needed for targeted social policy. The resource investment required for 26-atoll coverage is significant but necessary for evidence-based geographic targeting of limited resources.[^367]

---


## 5.36 Gender-Based Violence Prevention: Primary Prevention Strategies


### 5.67.1 Prevention Programmes

The Maldivian GBV response system is overwhelmingly oriented toward response – managing disclosed cases, providing legal protection, and pursuing prosecution. Prevention programming – addressing the conditions, norms, and social factors that produce GBV before it occurs – is a much smaller component of the overall investment.

Prevention is categorised in the international public health framework as:
- **Primary prevention:** Preventing violence from occurring in the first place (through social norm change, healthy relationship skills, economic empowerment)
- **Secondary prevention:** Identifying and responding to GBV early to prevent escalation (through screening, early intervention, safety planning)
- **Tertiary prevention:** Reducing the long-term impacts of GBV through comprehensive survivor support

The Maldivian system has the most developed tertiary response (FPA case management, shelter, court), a developing secondary response (healthcare provider guidelines, FCPW investigation capacity), and a limited primary prevention programme. Yet prevention is where the most cost-effective gains are achievable – addressing the causes of violence rather than managing its consequences.[^481]

### 5.67.2 What Primary Prevention Looks Like in the Maldivian Context

Effective primary prevention in the Maldivian context would include:

**Social norm change at community level:** The AIM Project's community engagement component is the primary vehicle for this, working through WDCs, mosques, schools, and community leaders to challenge the norms that enable DV (DV as private matter, victim-blaming, male authority over women). The evidence base for community norm change in SIDS contexts is limited – this is an area where the Maldives could contribute to the evidence base through systematic evaluation of AIM's community component.

**Healthy relationship skills in schools:** International evidence supports school-based programmes that teach young people about consent, healthy communication, and recognising abusive relationship patterns. Such programmes – when well-designed and culturally appropriate – have demonstrated reductions in adolescent relationship violence. The complete absence of this content from Maldivian schools (as documented in §5.13) is a missed prevention opportunity at the scale of the entire young population.

**Economic empowerment for women:** Women's economic independence is the strongest longitudinal predictor of reduced DV victimisation – not because economic independence causes violence reduction, but because it reduces the structural conditions (dependency, inability to leave) that maintain abusive relationships. Every investment in female LFPR, childcare infrastructure, equal pay, and outer island employment creation is simultaneously a DV prevention investment.

**Perpetrator-focused prevention:** Engaging men who are at elevated risk of perpetrating violence – through childhood trauma histories, substance abuse, and harmful masculinity norms – before violence occurs requires both community-level work (challenging norms) and individual-level intervention (mental health, substance abuse treatment, perpetrator-specific counselling). The Maldivian system has very limited dedicated perpetrator-focused prevention programming.[^482]

### 5.67.3 The RMNCAH Strategy as Prevention

The RMNCAH Strategy 2020-2025 includes prevention dimensions that, while not explicitly framed as GBV prevention, address important upstream determinants:

- **Adolescent sexual and reproductive health:** ASRH programming that improves young women's awareness and agency in sexual relationships reduces vulnerability to sexual coercion
- **Prenatal mental health:** Addressing mental health during pregnancy supports mothers' wellbeing and reduces the stress factors that can escalate to DV
- **Postnatal support:** Home visiting and postnatal care that identifies mothers under stress and provides early intervention prevents family crisis escalation

The integration of GBV prevention into RMNCAH programming – known as the "SASA!" or similar integrated SRHR-GBV model in international programming – is an evidence-based approach that has not yet been systematically applied in the Maldivian context.[^483]

---


## 5.37 The Progress Tracker: What Has Changed Since the DVPA


### 5.94.1 Before the DVPA (Pre-2012) vs. After

A before-after comparison of the gender policy framework – using 2012 (DVPA enactment) as the reference point – documents genuine but partial progress:

**What has changed:**
- DV is legally defined as a civil law violation with specific remedies (protection orders) – previously there was no civil law framework
- The FPA exists as a specialist agency with a caseload that reached 696 in a single year – previously no specialist agency existed
- Three DV shelters serve the country (limited but non-zero) – previously no shelters existed
- Women hold 33% of local council seats – previously they held 4%
- Marital rape is criminalised – previously it was not
- The Gender Equality Act creates positive obligations for the state and employers – previously no such obligations existed
- FGM/C prevalence has declined from 38% in the oldest cohort to 1% in the youngest

**What has not changed:**
- Female LFPR remains at 44% vs. 80% male – essentially unchanged from pre-2012 baselines
- Women's parliamentary representation has declined from 11.7% (2008) to 4.6% (2024)
- The DV prosecution rate at approximately 3% is not substantially higher than what can be inferred from pre-FPA police data
- The gender pay gap remains at 13-20% without systematic measurement or policy response
- Fewer than 4 formal daycare centres exist nationally – not substantially changed from pre-2012
- The CEDAW Article 16 reservation is unchanged since 1993
- FGM/C is not legally prohibited, and the legal status remains under review with no announced timeline[^528]

### 5.94.2 Measuring Progress on the Right Indicators

The progress assessment depends heavily on what indicators are used:

**Help-seeking indicators** (FPA case volume, protection order applications): Show large improvement – from 19 cases in 2009 to 696 in 2020-2021. This improvement reflects better services and awareness, not necessarily reduced violence.

**Accountability indicators** (prosecution rate, conviction rate, perpetrator programme access): Show minimal improvement. The 3% prosecution rate likely represents a similar proportion to what was achievable pre-DVPA for the small number of DV cases that reached the criminal system; the specialised DVPA framework has not substantially improved criminal accountability.

**Structural indicators** (LFPR gap, parliament representation, pay gap): Show mixed results – political representation improved at local level (quota) but declined at parliamentary level; LFPR gap unchanged; pay gap unchanged.

**Prevalence indicators** (actual DV incidence): Unknown – the absence of a new prevalence study since 2007 means we cannot assess whether any of these changes has reduced actual violence experienced by Maldivian women.

This indicator disaggregation is important for policy: the areas of clearest progress (help-seeking, local political representation) require different interventions than the areas of stagnation (accountability, economic participation, parliamentary representation, actual prevalence).[^529]

---


# Part 5: Female Genital Mutilation/Cutting


## 5.38 Female Genital Mutilation/Cutting (FGM/C): Prevalence, Practice, and Policy Response


### 5.7.1 Prevalence and Intergenerational Decline

Female genital mutilation/cutting (FGM/C) in the Maldives is confirmed by national survey data and represents a specific intersection of cultural-religious practice and gender-based harm. The **Maldives Demographic and Health Survey 2016–17** provided the most comprehensive available prevalence data:

- **Overall FGM/C prevalence in women aged 15–49:** 13%
- **Prevalence by age group:** The intergenerational decline is dramatic and statistically significant:

| Age Group | FGM/C Prevalence |
|-----------|-----------------|
| Women aged 45–49 | 38% |
| Women aged 40–44 | ~30% |
| Women aged 30–34 | ~15% |
| Women aged 20–24 | ~5% |
| Women aged 15–19 | 1% |

*Source: DHS 2016–17*[^45]

This intergenerational decline – from 38% in the oldest cohort to 1% in the youngest – demonstrates that FGM/C is being abandoned at the community level without formal legal prohibition. The norm change is real and substantial. However, 1% prevalence in the 15–19 cohort means that some girls are still being subjected to the practice, and without criminalisation, there is no formal deterrent and no systematic monitoring.

### 5.7.2 The Nature of Practice in the Maldives

FGM/C in the Maldives typically takes the form of a minor nick or incision classified as Type IV in the WHO taxonomy – a form less physically extensive than the more severe forms documented in parts of sub-Saharan Africa. This has been cited by some community and religious voices as a reason not to prioritise criminalisation, on grounds that the Maldivian practice is "different" from FGM/C elsewhere. The CEDAW Committee and international human rights bodies have rejected this distinction: any form of non-consensual alteration of girls' genitalia constitutes a violation of bodily integrity and the right to be free from violence, regardless of physical severity.

The practice in the Maldives is associated with religious norms – there is a strand of Islamic jurisprudence that characterises FGM/C as *sunnah* (recommended practice) or *makrumah* (an act of nobility) – though other Islamic jurisprudential positions do not support the practice. The intersection of religious authority and gender-based harm in the FGM/C context requires engagement with religious institutions and scholars, not merely legislative prohibition, to achieve durable norm change.

### 5.7.3 Legal Status and CEDAW Obligations

**FGM/C is not criminalised in the Maldives as of 2025.** No specific criminal law provision addresses FGM/C. Acts that cause physical harm to a child could potentially be prosecuted under general Penal Code assault provisions, but no such prosecution has been brought in relation to FGM/C.

The CEDAW Committee's 2021 Concluding Observations (CEDAW/C/MDV/CO/6, paragraph 39) called upon Maldives to explicitly criminalise FGM/C. This recommendation has been under review by MoSFD in consultation with the Ministry of Islamic Affairs. Public health campaigns have been conducted to discourage the practice. The CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report (2025) commits to completing the legislative review in the next reporting period.

### 5.7.4 Public Health and Community Response

Public health campaigns targeting FGM/C have been conducted through the Health Protection Agency and community health workers. The significant intergenerational decline in prevalence suggests that these campaigns, combined with broader education and social change, have been effective at the community level. However, the continued practice among a minority of families indicates that campaigns alone are insufficient, particularly in communities where FGM/C is framed as a religious obligation.

The FPA's mandate does not currently extend to FGM/C investigation or prevention – this is an area of programme gap given the reproductive and psychological health consequences of the practice. No dedicated FGM/C prevention programme with trained community workers exists.

---


## 5.39 FGM/C in the Maldives: A Detailed Analysis


### 5.47.1 Form, Prevalence, and Trend

Female genital mutilation/cutting (FGM/C) in the Maldives takes a specific form that is important to understand precisely given the WHO classification system and the associated health impact analysis. The Maldivian practice is categorised as **WHO Type IV** – "other harmful procedures to the female genitalia for non-medical purposes" – which in the Maldivian context involves the removal of the clitoral hood (prepuce) or a small cut to the clitoris without removal. This is distinct from the more extensive forms of FGM/C practised in parts of Africa and the Middle East (Types I-III, involving partial or complete clitoridectomy, excision, or infibulation).[^431]

The DHS 2016-17 provides the most comprehensive national data on FGM/C prevalence by age cohort:

| Age Cohort | FGM/C Prevalence | Interpretation |
|------------|-----------------|----------------|
| 45-49 years | 38% | Older generation; practice widespread in their childhood |
| 35-44 years | ~25-30% (interpolated) | Declining trend through this generation |
| 25-34 years | ~15-20% (interpolated) | Continued decline |
| 15-19 years | ~1% | Near-disappearing in youngest surveyed adult cohort [Note: The 13% FGM/C prevalence figure applies to all women aged 15-49; the 15-19 cohort-specific rate requires verification against DHS 2016-17 microdata.] |
| 5-9 years | 1% | Dramatic decline; practice near-disappearing in youngest cohort |

The declining trend – from 38% in the oldest cohort to 1% in the youngest – documents a generational shift away from the practice without any specific legislative intervention. The factors driving this natural decline include increased education and health awareness among mothers, reduced community pressure to conform, and the growing influence of international health discourse that frames FGM/C as harmful rather than as a religious obligation.[^432]

### 5.47.2 Islamic Jurisprudential Basis and Debate

FGM/C in the Maldives is practised on an Islamic jurisprudential basis – specifically citing a Hadith (attributed to the Prophet Muhammad) that is considered weak (*da'if*) or questionable by many Islamic scholars but has been invoked in several Islamic traditions as justifying the practice for women. The Shafi'i school – the dominant school in the Maldives – historically classified female circumcision as *sunnah* (recommended) rather than obligatory.

The Islamic scholars' debate on FGM/C is active and significant:
- **Opponents of the practice** within Islamic scholarship argue that the supporting Hadith is too weak to establish a recommendation; that the principle of *la darar wa la dirar* (no harm, no causing harm) militates against any practice causing physical harm; and that the practice is cultural rather than Quranic in origin
- **Proponents** argue that the Shafi'i school classification as sunnah creates an Islamic obligation; that the Maldivian form (Type IV) causes minimal harm; and that prohibiting it would constitute interference with religious practice

The government's position – not explicitly prohibiting FGM/C while acknowledging its presence and tracking its decline – reflects this jurisprudential ambiguity. The **CEDAW Committee** in its November 2021 Concluding Observations called on the Maldives to **explicitly prohibit FGM/C in law**, noting that the practice violates CEDAW provisions on discrimination affecting women's physical integrity and health. The CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report (2025) indicates the legal status remains under review without a commitment to prohibition.[^433]

### 5.47.3 Health Impacts of the Maldivian Form

The health impacts of Type IV FGM/C (the Maldivian form) are debated in the medical literature. The WHO classifies the practice as harmful regardless of form, and documents the following potential health consequences:

- **Immediate:** Pain, infection risk, bleeding at the time of the procedure
- **Short-term:** Wound infection if the procedure is conducted in non-sterile conditions
- **Long-term:** Potential impacts on sexual sensation and function; psychological trauma associated with the experience

The WHO's framework – that no form of FGM/C has health benefits; that all forms constitute harmful interference with a girl's or woman's physical integrity – is the international standard. The Maldivian government's engagement with this standard, however, has been shaped by the Islamic jurisprudential positioning of the practice, which frames any minimal-impact form as within the domain of religious practice rather than healthcare harm.[^434]

### 5.47.4 The Practice's Social Context

FGM/C in the Maldives is typically performed on infant girls – at birth or within the first weeks of life – by a traditional midwife or by a health worker. The decision is made by parents, with the mother playing the primary role in transmitting the practice across generations. The declining prevalence reflects primarily mothers from younger generations choosing not to have their daughters cut – a decision shaped by education, health awareness, and reduced community pressure.[^435]

The social dynamics of the decline are important for policy:
- The decline is happening **without prohibition** – suggesting that information and awareness campaigns, combined with organic generational change, are effective in reducing the practice even without legal coercion
- The 1% prevalence in the youngest age cohort suggests the practice is approaching disappearance through social change alone
- Legislative prohibition – if pursued – would need to be carefully designed to avoid driving the practice underground and potentially increasing harm (as documented in some other FGM/C contexts where prohibition drove practice from trained personnel to untrained practitioners)

---


# Part 6: Sexual and Reproductive Health


## 5.40 Sexual and Reproductive Health Rights


### 5.8.1 Overview and Policy Framework

Sexual and reproductive health rights (SRHR) occupy a contested space in Maldivian policy – acknowledged in international commitments, partially addressed through health system programming, but constrained by Islamic law provisions, conservative social norms, and persistent gaps in service access across the atoll geography. The foundational policy instrument is the **Reproductive, Maternal, Newborn, Child and Adolescent Health (RMNCAH) Strategy 2020–2025**, developed by the Ministry of Health with WHO and UNFPA technical support, which provides a structured framework for improving maternal and newborn health, family planning, and adolescent sexual and reproductive health (ASRH) services across the 187 inhabited islands.[^46]

UNFPA is the lead UN agency for SRHR programming in the Maldives. Its partnership with the Ministry of Health includes the **National Guidelines for Healthcare Providers on Gender-Based Violence** (2024, second edition), which integrate SRHR components including referral pathways for survivors of sexual violence, post-exposure prophylaxis, and documentation of GBV-related reproductive health presentations. UNFPA also supports the training of island health workers in SRHR counselling, though coverage across outer atolls remains incomplete.[^47]

The Gender Equality Act 18/2016 prohibits discrimination in access to health services on grounds of gender and disability, creating a formal legal basis for challenging unequal reproductive health service provision. In practice, however, geographic isolation, social stigma, and the absence of trained providers on smaller islands mean that women's access to reproductive health services is substantially determined by island of residence rather than legal entitlement.[^48]

### 5.8.2 Maternal Mortality: Trajectory, COVID Spike, and Current Position

Maternal mortality in the Maldives follows a trajectory of dramatic improvement over three decades, interrupted by a sharp reversal in 2021 associated with the COVID-19 pandemic, and subsequent partial recovery. The trajectory is as follows:

| Year | Maternal Mortality Ratio (per 100,000 live births) | Source |
|------|----------------------------------------------------|--------|
| 1990 | ~450 | WHO/UNICEF Estimates |
| 2000 | 120 | WHO/UNICEF Estimates |
| 2006 | 69 | NDMC/MoH |
| 2012 | 49 | World Bank WDI/WHO |
| 2017 | 25 | DHS 2016-17 |
| 2019 | 29 | MoH Annual Health Statistics |
| 2021 | 60 | MoH Annual Health Statistics 2021 |
| 2022 | 34 | MoH Annual Health Statistics 2022 |

The reduction from 450 per 100,000 in 1990 to 49 per 100,000 in 2012 represents one of the most significant public health achievements in the country's history – a 89% reduction in 22 years, driven by skilled birth attendance, facility delivery, and emergency obstetric care expansion. By 2012, the Maldives had effectively achieved Millennium Development Goal 5 on maternal health.[^49]

The **2021 spike to 60 per 100,000** is attributed to multiple COVID-19 pandemic effects: disruption of routine antenatal care (ANC) services, reduced willingness of women to attend health facilities during lockdown periods, delayed emergency obstetric referrals due to restricted inter-island transport, and healthcare worker redeployment away from maternal health services. The Ministry of Health's internal analysis identified **haemorrhage, hypertensive disorders, and infection** as the leading direct causes across the pandemic period. The spike brought the Maldives back to pre-2010 maternal mortality levels in a single year, and serves as a critical reminder of how quickly gains can be reversed when health system stress disrupts continuity of care for pregnant women.[^50]

Recovery to 34 per 100,000 in 2022 represents a return below the 2012 level of 49, though the volatility across 2019–2022 highlights the fragility of these gains in a small population. The RMNCAH Strategy 2020–2025 targets further reduction, with specific investments in emergency obstetric care (EmOC) capacity in regional hospitals and Community Health Workers (CHW) skills for complication recognition on smaller islands.[^51]

### 5.8.3 Antenatal Care, Skilled Birth Attendance, and Caesarean Section

Aggregate coverage statistics for obstetric care are among the highest in South Asia, reflecting the country's success in building a basic health infrastructure across islands:

- **ANC coverage (at least 1 visit):** 97% (DHS 2016-17)[^52]
- **ANC coverage (4+ visits):** 82% (DHS 2016-17)[^53]
- **Facility births:** 95% (DHS 2016-17)[^54]
- **Skilled birth attendance:** 95% (DHS 2016-17)[^55]
- **Caesarean section rate:** 32% (Health Systems chapter §11.X, citing MoH 2022)[^56]

The 32% caesarean section rate substantially exceeds the WHO recommended range of 10–15%, indicating over-medicalisation in facility births, concentrated primarily at Indira Gandhi Memorial Hospital (IGMH) and private hospitals in Malé. Women in outer atolls, by contrast, face the opposite problem: emergency obstetric access is inadequate. Atoll hospitals have variable capacity for EmOC, and women in labour on smaller islands face a referral pathway that – even in optimal conditions – involves a speedboat transfer to the nearest atoll hospital. In adverse weather, this pathway is dangerous or impossible. The combination of high aggregate statistics masking geographic extremes is a defining feature of Maldivian reproductive health.[^57]

### 5.8.4 Contraception: Prevalence, Unmet Need, and Barriers

The Maldives Demographic and Health Survey 2016-17 provides the most recent nationally representative data on contraceptive use:

- **Modern contraceptive prevalence rate (mCPR):** 27% among married women of reproductive age[^58]
- **Total CPR (any method):** 34.7%[^59]
- **Unmet need for family planning:** 29%[^60]

The 29% unmet need – meaning nearly one in three married women who want to avoid or delay pregnancy cannot access effective contraception – is one of the highest in the South Asian region and represents a substantial gap between stated reproductive intentions and actual service access. Causes of unmet need documented in the DHS and UNFPA assessments include:

**Supply-side barriers:**
- Inconsistent availability of contraceptive supplies on smaller islands, where health posts may run out of stock for weeks at a time
- Absence of trained family planning counsellors on islands below atoll hospital level
- Injectable contraceptives and implants requiring trained providers unavailable at many island level health posts
- Limited pharmacy services in outer atolls for oral contraceptive restocking

**Demand-side barriers:**
- Husband opposition or lack of spousal communication about family planning – documented in DHS as a significant barrier particularly in socially conservative atolls
- Religious concerns about specific methods (IUDs, implants) perceived as potentially abortifacient by some community members, though no formal fatwa prohibits their use
- Stigma associated with family planning visits – women report reluctance to be seen attending family planning services in small island communities where privacy is difficult
- Limited women's autonomy in reproductive decision-making where spousal or family pressure to have more children is significant
- Low awareness of long-acting reversible contraception (LARC) options among women on smaller islands[^61]

The gap between the high ANC coverage rate (97%) and the low mCPR (27%) is striking and suggests that the health system's capacity to provide services during pregnancy substantially exceeds its capacity to support inter-pregnancy reproductive planning. UNFPA's programming under the RMNCAH Strategy specifically targets this gap through CHW capacity building and social behavioural change communication.[^62]

### 5.8.5 Abortion: Legal Position, Penal Code Conflict, and Reproductive Rights Implications

The legal position on abortion in the Maldives has become more restrictive over time, creating a significant tension with women's reproductive rights obligations under CEDAW Article 12. The current position under the **Penal Code** (Act 9/2014 and subsequent amendments) is that abortion is permitted **only to save the mother's life** – the most restrictive position available under international standards and more restrictive than the pre-2014 position.[^63]

The earlier position, under pre-2014 guidelines and previous Penal Code provisions, had permitted abortion in additional circumstances including cases of foetal abnormality incompatible with life and, in practice, cases of rape. The 2014 Penal Code consolidated and effectively narrowed the exceptions, removing the prior ambiguity that had allowed broader interpretation. The current prohibition means that women who become pregnant as a result of rape or incest – including those in the highly documented DV context – have no legal pathway to abortion except in the very narrow circumstance of life-threatening physical risk to the mother.

There is one important partial exception: the **Thalassaemia Control Act 4/2012** permits termination of a thalassaemia-affected pregnancy within **120 days of gestation** following mandatory premarital genetic screening and prenatal diagnosis. This exception – motivated by the extremely high thalassaemia carrier rate (18% of the Maldivian population) and the severe health consequences of Thalassaemia Major – creates a pragmatic carve-out that is not grounded in a broader reproductive rights framework, but rather in a disease-specific public health rationale. The Thalassaemia provision has been interpreted by some health practitioners as opening a limited pathway in other severe foetal abnormality situations, though this interpretation has no clear legal basis.[^64]

The **CEDAW Committee** in its November 2021 Concluding Observations (CEDAW/C/MDV/CO/6) expressed concern about the restrictive abortion law and called on the Maldives to decriminalise abortion in cases of rape, incest, serious foetal abnormality, and where the pregnancy poses a serious risk to the woman's physical or mental health. The Government's Seventh Periodic Report (2025) does not indicate any plans to amend the Penal Code on abortion.[^65]

The practical consequences of the restrictive law are difficult to document given the underground nature of unsafe abortions. Healthcare providers, civil society organisations, and UNFPA field staff have documented that women in unwanted pregnancies – including rape survivors – do access abortion through informal channels, with associated risks of unsafe procedures, complications that must be treated at government health facilities under cover of miscarriage diagnoses, and the threat of criminal prosecution for both the woman and any healthcare provider involved. The **zina law** – which provides for 100 lashes for sexual intercourse outside marriage under Penal Code Section 411(d) – creates a further deterrent: a woman who seeks post-rape healthcare risks disclosure of the pregnancy and potential prosecution for zina if the rape cannot be proved (which, as documented in §5.6, is structurally difficult given evidence requirements).[^66]

### 5.8.6 Adolescent Sexual and Reproductive Health

The RMNCAH Strategy 2020–2025 includes an adolescent sexual and reproductive health (ASRH) component, acknowledging that Maldivian adolescents – particularly girls – face significant SRHR vulnerabilities. Key documented issues include:

**Early marriage and adolescent pregnancy:** Despite the legal minimum marriage age of 18 (Family Act 2000, as amended), the DHS 2016-17 found that **2.2% of married women were married before age 18**, with the proportion higher in outer atolls than in Malé. Adolescent girls who marry young typically withdraw from education – Ministry of Education data shows girls as a significant proportion of education dropouts in contexts involving early marriage or pregnancy – and face higher risks of maternal complications due to physical immaturity.[^67]

**SRH curriculum gaps:** As documented in the Education chapter (§10.X) and confirmed by the Youth Vulnerability Study 2019, the school curriculum includes basic human reproduction biology but explicitly excludes content on consent, contraception, healthy relationships, GBV recognition, or safe disclosure mechanisms. Teachers are generally not trained in ASRH and avoid the topic beyond the mandated biology content. This gap leaves adolescents – including survivors of childhood sexual abuse – without information or referral pathways.[^68]

**Adolescent mental health and SRH intersection:** The WHO STEP Survey 2021 documented 16.2% of young women aged 15-24 reporting moderate to severe depression, with GBV victimisation a significant co-occurring factor. Adolescent girls who have experienced sexual abuse (documented at 12.2% nationally, Fulu 2007) show significantly elevated rates of mental health distress, early sexual debut, and subsequent sexual risk-taking. The absence of integrated SRH-mental health services for adolescent girls leaves this vulnerability largely unaddressed by the current service architecture.[^69]

### 5.8.7 Women's Nutritional Status

The nutritional profile of Maldivian women documented in the DHS 2016-17 reveals several dimensions of vulnerability with direct reproductive health implications:

- **Short stature (<145 cm):** 12% of women – associated with obstetric complications including obstructed labour and increased caesarean necessity[^70]
- **Underweight (BMI <18.5):** 4.6% – associated with adverse birth outcomes and maternal anaemia[^71]
- **Anaemia:** 15.1% of women of reproductive age – a direct driver of maternal mortality risk[^72]
- **Zinc deficiency:** 27% – with implications for immune function and pregnancy outcomes[^73]
- **Iodine insufficiency:** 27% – with implications for foetal neurological development[^74]

Cultural norms documented in the Health Systems chapter (§11.X) note that **outdoor exercise is socially discouraged for women** in many island communities, particularly in more conservative atolls, limiting physical activity options and potentially contributing to weight-related health issues including gestational diabetes risk. The RMNCAH Strategy includes nutritional supplementation provisions – iron/folate supplementation during ANC visits – but addressing the broader determinants of women's nutritional status requires intersectoral action beyond the health system.[^75]

### 5.8.8 Thalassaemia: A Gender-Relevant Genetic Health Crisis

With an **18% carrier rate** – among the highest globally – thalassaemia is a pervasive genetic health concern with significant gender dimensions. The national response is structured around the Thalassaemia Control Act 4/2012, which mandates:

- **Premarital genetic screening** for thalassaemia carrier status for all couples before marriage registration
- **Prenatal diagnosis** for couples where both partners are carriers (25% risk of a Thalassaemia Major child)
- **Termination option** within the first 120 days of gestation for confirmed Thalassaemia Major foetuses – the sole explicit legal abortion provision in Maldivian law

The burden of the thalassaemia management falls disproportionately on women, both as carriers and as primary caregivers for affected children. Women who carry Thalassaemia Major children – who require lifelong blood transfusions and chelation therapy – face enormous caregiving burdens with limited state support. The gendered dimension of caregiving has not been systematically addressed in thalassaemia policy, which focuses on disease prevention and clinical management rather than the social and economic consequences for mothers and families.[^76]

---


## 5.41 The Health System's Gender Dimensions: Clinical Practice and Service Gaps


### 5.53.1 GBV Identification in Healthcare Settings

Healthcare providers – doctors, nurses, and health post workers – are on the front lines of GBV identification and response. Women who are experiencing DV or sexual violence may present to health facilities with:
- **Physical injuries** that may or may not be attributed to the actual cause
- **Somatic presentations:** Unexplained chronic pain, headaches, gastrointestinal complaints, gynaecological complaints – all documented in GBV research as common presentations of DV-related psychological distress
- **Mental health presentations:** Depression, anxiety, suicidal ideation – presenting to primary care settings that may not systematically screen for GBV as an underlying cause
- **Reproductive health presentations:** Unintended pregnancy, STI diagnosis, repeated presenting for contraceptive services – contexts in which GBV may be an underlying factor
- **Antenatal care attendance:** Pregnancy, including unwanted pregnancy, is a context in which GBV is particularly prevalent; ANC settings are therefore a key potential identification point

The **UNFPA National Guidelines for Healthcare Providers on GBV (2024, 2nd edition)** provide the clinical protocol framework for how healthcare providers should respond when they identify or suspect GBV. The guidelines cover:
- Safe enquiry – how to ask about GBV without jeopardising safety (not asking in front of the partner)
- First response – immediate support, confidentiality, documentation
- Clinical management – treatment of injuries, forensic evidence collection where relevant
- Safety assessment – risk assessment using standardised tools
- Referral pathways – to FPA, FCPW, shelter, FLC, mental health services[^447]

### 5.53.2 Training Coverage and Healthcare Worker Capacity

The guidelines are only as useful as the healthcare workers who are trained to apply them. Training coverage is documented as incomplete:
- IGMH Malé has the highest coverage of GBV-trained healthcare workers, reflecting the concentration of training resources in the capital
- Regional hospital staff have received some training, with AIM Project support extending to atoll-level health settings
- Island health post workers – the first point of contact for most Maldivians – have the lowest training coverage

The specific challenges at the island health post level:
- Health posts are often staffed by a single nurse or health worker with broad responsibilities and no supervision for GBV response specifically
- Privacy is structurally difficult – small health post buildings in small island communities mean that a woman presenting with unexplained injuries is visible to others in the community
- The health worker may be personally acquainted with both the survivor and the perpetrator, creating conflict of interest pressures
- Referral pathways from island health posts to FPA and FCPW require telephone communication and the survivor's own willingness to engage with those agencies[^448]

### 5.53.3 Mandatory Reporting: The Healthcare Provider's Obligation

Healthcare providers who identify GBV are **mandatory reporters** under the DVPA framework – they are legally required to report to the FPA or police. The mandatory reporting obligation creates a tension with survivor confidentiality:
- A survivor who discloses GBV to a healthcare provider does not automatically consent to that information being shared with other agencies
- Mandatory reporting without the survivor's knowledge can breach her trust in the health system and deter future disclosure
- In contexts where reporting may precipitate retaliation (perpetrator learning of the report through a small-community information network), mandatory reporting without safety planning may increase immediate risk

The healthcare guidelines attempt to navigate this tension by emphasising safety planning before any mandatory report, informing the survivor of the reporting obligation, and coordinating with FPA before reporting in non-emergency situations. In practice, the training required to navigate this tension ethically and effectively requires more sustained clinical GBV education than most healthcare workers have received.[^449]

### 5.53.4 Reproductive Healthcare and GBV Intersection

The reproductive healthcare system – ANC, postnatal care, family planning services – is a critical point of contact for women experiencing GBV, for several documented reasons:

- **Pregnancy and GBV:** DV prevalence is documented to increase during pregnancy in international research. The theory that pregnancy is protected from violence is not supported by evidence; pregnancy may exacerbate DV through economic stress and changing relationship dynamics. ANC visits, which reach 97% of pregnant women in the Maldives, represent a unique regular contact opportunity for GBV identification.

- **Contraception and GBV:** The high unmet need for contraception (29%) intersects with GBV through **reproductive coercion** – a form of intimate partner violence in which a partner controls reproductive decisions through sabotaging contraceptive use, refusing to use condoms, or forcing unwanted pregnancies. Reproductive coercion is not systematically documented in Maldivian data but is well-documented internationally and likely relevant in the Maldivian context given the coercive control patterns identified in the Fulu study.

- **STI diagnosis:** A woman presenting with an STI in a context of reported fidelity may be experiencing sexual violence. Clinical protocols that screen for DV in STI consultations are not yet systematically applied in Maldivian settings.[^450]

---


## 5.42 Women's Physical Health Beyond Reproduction: A Neglected Domain


### 5.82.1 Non-Communicable Diseases and Gender

Non-communicable diseases (NCDs) – cardiovascular disease, diabetes, cancer, chronic respiratory disease – are the leading causes of death in the Maldives, as in most middle-income countries. The gender dimensions of NCDs in the Maldivian context have not been adequately addressed in available policy documents:

**Cardiovascular disease:** The WHO STEP Survey 2021 provides some gender-disaggregated NCD data. Women's cardiovascular risk factors may differ from men's in ways not captured by standard NCD screening: psychosocial stress from DV, economic stress, and care burden are documented cardiovascular risk factors that are female-concentrated; the protective effect of regular outdoor physical activity is reduced by the social norms restricting women's outdoor exercise.

**Diabetes:** The STEP Survey documents diabetes prevalence by gender. Women with gestational diabetes are at elevated risk of type 2 diabetes – and the high caesarean section rate (32%) may be partially associated with gestational diabetes that has not been adequately managed during pregnancy.

**Cancer:** Cervical cancer is a preventable and detectable cancer that has disproportionate impact on women. Human papillomavirus (HPV) vaccination – now part of expanded immunisation programmes in comparable countries – is not documented as routinely available in the Maldives. Breast cancer screening programmes targeting women over 40 are limited to major urban facilities. The outer island gap in cancer screening is documented as significant.[^512]

**Tobacco and substance use:** While tobacco and substance use are documented as predominantly male patterns, women's tobacco use – and the passive smoking exposure from male household members – is a health risk that gender-disaggregated health data should track but currently inadequately captures.

### 5.82.2 Occupational Health and Gender

Women's occupational health in the Maldives has specific features that reflect the sectors of female concentration:

**Teaching:** Teaching is associated with specific occupational health risks – voice disorders from sustained vocal use; stress and burnout from overcrowded classrooms and administrative demands; exposure to childhood illness. The feminised teaching workforce faces these risks disproportionately.

**Nursing and healthcare:** Healthcare workers face occupational exposure risks (infectious disease, musculoskeletal injury from patient handling) and psychosocial risks (exposure to trauma, burnout). Women in nursing face these risks predominantly. The intersection of nursing occupational health and GBV (healthcare workers who themselves experience DV) is inadequately addressed in available occupational health frameworks.

**Domestic work:** Women employed as domestic workers – both Maldivian women and migrant domestic workers – face occupational health risks from cleaning chemicals, physical domestic labour, and the psychosocial stress of isolated working environments. No specific occupational health framework covers domestic workers in the Maldives.

**Fishing-adjacent work:** Fish processing – a traditional female economic activity in coastal communities – involves cold, wet working conditions; exposure to allergens from fish processing; and repetitive motion injuries from traditional processing techniques. These occupational health risks of women's traditional economic activities are essentially unaddressed in occupational health policy.[^513]

---


## 5.43 Community Health Workers and Gender: The Island Level


### 5.91.1 The CHW Network as a Gender Resource

The **Community Health Worker (CHW)** network – trained volunteer or lightly paid health workers operating at the island level below the formal health post – is a critical but undervalued resource for gender health programming. CHWs in the Maldivian context include:

- **Community health workers** trained in basic health promotion and referral
- **Traditional birth attendants** (diminishing in number as facility births have become near-universal)
- **Family planning educators** supported by UNFPA and Ministry of Health programming

The gender relevance of the CHW network:
- CHWs are predominantly women – reflecting the gender dynamics of community care work
- They are trusted members of the local community, often better positioned to have sensitive health conversations with women than formal health facility staff
- They are the front-line for GBV identification in health contexts on islands without a trained GBV healthcare provider

**RMNCAH Strategy CHW role:** The RMNCAH Strategy 2020-2025 includes CHW capacity building for maternal health, family planning counselling, and referral. The integration of GBV identification and referral into CHW training – following the UNFPA healthcare guidelines – would extend the GBV-aware health response to islands without health post staff trained in GBV.[^523]

### 5.91.2 The Pharmacy as a Gender Service Point

Pharmacies on larger islands serve as informal health advice points for women who prefer not to register a formal health visit. Women seeking emergency contraception, treating reproductive tract infections, or looking for information about family planning may approach a pharmacist rather than a health facility. The regulatory framework for pharmacy services – and the training of pharmacists in SRHR counselling and GBV referral – is an under-exploited intervention point for extending gender health services.

Pharmacist training in recognising signs of DV (a woman who frequently presents for treatment of injuries; a woman who appears afraid or is accompanied by a controlling partner), in providing emergency contraception information, and in referring to the FPA telephone line could extend the GBV-aware health network at relatively low cost.

---


# Part 7: Women's Economic Participation and Poverty


## 5.44 Women's Economic Participation


### 5.9.1 The Labour Force Participation Gap: Scale, Causes, and Persistence

The most quantifiable dimension of gender inequality in the Maldives is the **labour force participation rate (LFPR) gap**: female LFPR at **44%** versus male LFPR at **80%** (Household Income and Expenditure Survey 2016; reconfirmed by National Bureau of Statistics 2019). This gap of 36 percentage points is one of the widest in South Asia, and represents a structural feature of the Maldivian economy that has proved resistant to the educational gains women have made over the same period.[^77]

The gender LFPR gap is particularly striking because it occurs in a context where women's educational attainment substantially exceeds men's at tertiary level (59–64% of higher education students are female, Ministry of Higher Education 2023). The "education paradox" – high female education, low female labour force participation – reflects the extent to which participation is determined by structural barriers beyond individual capability or credential.

Factors documented in the HIES 2016, ADB assessments, and CEDAW reporting as driving the LFPR gap include:

**1. Childcare infrastructure deficit.** The Maldives has **fewer than 4 formal daycare centres** across the entire country (CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report 2025). In the absence of affordable, accessible childcare, the default assumption – reinforced by social norms – is that mothers will be the primary caregivers of young children, taking them out of formal employment. Women with young children who wish to remain in employment face a choice between private (expensive) childcare arrangements unavailable in most islands, reliance on extended family (geographically constrained by the atoll dispersion), or withdrawal from the labour market.[^78]

**2. Geographic restriction.** Many formal sector jobs are concentrated in Malé, tourism resorts, or atoll capitals. Women on smaller islands face extremely limited formal employment options. The traditional female productive activities – fish processing, weaving, handicraft production – have declined in economic value and market integration. Fishing is socially and practically male-dominated. Women's employment in outer atolls is thus confined to government services (particularly teaching and nursing), the very limited local commercial sector, and unpaid care and domestic work.[^79]

**3. Tourism sector exclusion.** The resort sector, which constitutes the largest share of formal private employment in the Maldives, has historically employed very few Maldivian women: only **3% of resort employees were Maldivian women** in available data (Housing and Poverty chapter §12.X). This reflects a combination of: resort geographic isolation (one-island resorts requiring staff to live away from home, incompatible with women's family care roles and social supervision norms); industry recruitment concentrated in male-dominated technical roles (boat captains, water sports, maintenance); and social norms that have historically discouraged women from the mixed-gender, international-facing resort environment. Government workforce Maldivianisation policies have increased overall Maldivian resort employment, but the gender composition has changed only slowly.[^80]

**4. Occupational segregation and sectoral concentration.** Government employs **43% of Maldivian women workers** compared with 38% of male workers (HIES 2016). While government employment provides relative job security, it also reflects the channelling of educated women toward the public sector – particularly education and health – while private sector economic opportunities remain relatively inaccessible. Career guidance in schools reinforces this: girls are steered toward teaching, nursing, and social work; boys toward fishing, tourism, and technical trades.[^81]

**5. Social norms and family pressure.** Across income levels, social norms position women primarily as mothers and wives, with employment permissible but secondary to household responsibilities. Research documented in the Cross-Cutting Issues chapter finds that women who choose employment over full-time caregiving may face social criticism and family pressure. In some conservative communities, husbands' permission for wives to work is an informal expectation. The Youth Vulnerability Study 2019 documented that women in job interviews are routinely asked about marital status, plans to have children, and dress requirements – reflecting employer expectations that women's employment commitments are contingent on family circumstances in ways that men's are not.[^82]

### 5.9.2 NEET Rates and Youth Economic Exclusion

The **Not in Employment, Education, or Training (NEET)** indicator captures a critical dimension of young women's economic exclusion. CEDAW reporting data indicates a **42.7% NEET rate among young women in outer atolls** – nearly double the NEET rate for young urban women and substantially higher than the male NEET rate in the same geographic cohort. This indicator reflects the compound effect of geographic isolation, limited job market opportunities, early marriage and pregnancy withdrawal from education, and the absence of vocational training accessible from smaller islands.[^83]

NEET status among young women represents a particularly significant social policy failure because it concentrates across the same populations already identified as most economically vulnerable: young women in outer atolls who have completed secondary education but cannot access tertiary education or formal employment compatible with their family situation. The long-term economic consequences – lifetime earnings foregone, dependency on husbands or family, heightened vulnerability to poverty after divorce – are severe and disproportionately affect subsequent generations.

### 5.9.3 The Gender Pay Gap

The gender pay gap in the Maldives is not systematically measured, representing a **critical data gap** noted both by the CEDAW Committee in its November 2021 Concluding Observations and by the Statistics Compendium (Part XVII, §17.3). Available indirect evidence suggests a significant gap:

- **ADB assessments** of the Maldivian labour market indicate women earn **13–20% less** than men in comparable formal sector positions[^84]
- Women are concentrated in lower-paid sectors and positions even within mixed-gender sectors
- **Career progression** for women in government – the largest single employer of women – is constrained by inadequate childcare and the expectation that women will manage career interruptions for care responsibilities
- The **skills mismatch** documented by the Housing and Poverty chapter (§12.X) further compounds the gap: 15% of women are overqualified for the low-skill jobs available to them (particularly in outer atolls), while 44% lack skills for higher-paying jobs in growing sectors (particularly technology and tourism management)

The National Bureau of Statistics does not publish gender-disaggregated wage data on a routine basis. The CEDAW Committee has specifically called for disaggregated labour market data collection as a priority recommendation.[^85]

### 5.9.4 COVID-19 Economic Impact: The Gendered Recession

The COVID-19 pandemic had a disproportionate economic impact on women, reversing some of the slow gains made in the preceding decade:

- Women were **twice as likely as men to stop work** during the pandemic (Housing and Poverty chapter §12.X, citing NBS COVID Rapid Assessment 2020)[^86]
- **22% of female manufacturing workers** stopped working during the pandemic, compared with **8% of male manufacturing workers** in the same sector[^87]
- Women were more likely to be in informal or part-time employment with no social protection coverage, leaving them without income support when work stopped
- The increase in caregiving demands during school closures fell predominantly on women
- GBV case reports to the FPA increased from **539 in 2019 to 696 in 2021** – a **29% increase** – during the same period [Note: FPA quarterly statistics attribute the 696 figure to 2021 rather than 2020; the year requires clarification with FPA.] that women's economic independence was most severely compromised, consistent with international evidence on the relationship between economic stress, COVID lockdowns, and intimate partner violence escalation[^88]

Post-pandemic recovery has been uneven, with women's employment recovery lagging men's in most documented sectors. The tourism sector's recovery (which has been rapid) has not translated into proportionate increases in female tourism employment.

### 5.9.5 SDFC Women's Lending and Access to Credit

The **Small and Medium Enterprise Development Finance Corporation (SDFC)** administers government credit programmes for small businesses. CEDAW reporting indicates that **approximately 20% of SDFC loans** went to women entrepreneurs (CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report 2025, Article 13 section). This represents significant underrepresentation relative to women's population share and their documented appetite for entrepreneurship.[^89]

Barriers to women's credit access include:
- **Collateral requirements:** Women's lower property ownership rates (women's land and property ownership is not systematically tracked but is known to be lower than men's, reflecting inheritance patterns and post-divorce property allocation) reduce ability to meet collateral requirements for larger loans
- **Business registration:** Women in informal businesses are less likely to have the formal registration documentation required for SDFC access
- **Financial literacy:** UNFPA and ADB assessments note lower financial literacy among women entrepreneurs in outer atolls
- **Social barriers:** In some communities, women's independent business ownership may be socially discouraged or require husband's support

The **ADB MSME Project** – a parallel initiative to the GESI Project – specifically targets credit access for women entrepreneurs through credit lines with limited collateral requirements, and provides business development support to women-owned micro and small enterprises. The ADB's gender assessments found significant unmet demand among women for business financing that the SDFC's standard product suite does not reach.[^90]

The **Strengthening Social and Financial Support Systems for Women project** (SAILS) includes targeted employment services for women jobseekers, gender-responsive employment service provision through the Employment Service Centres network, and linkages between women's skills training programmes and employment opportunities in identified sectors. SAILS is designed to address the pipeline problem: even where jobs exist, women may lack the specific vocational skills or job-search skills to access them.[^91]

### 5.9.6 Maternity Protection and Unpaid Care Work

**Maternity leave** under the Employment Act 2/2008 provides **60 days paid maternity leave** for formal sector workers, and **14 days for informal or probationary workers**. The provision applies in the formal sector; informal workers – a significant proportion of women's employment – have no equivalent protection. **No paternity leave** is mandated under law (CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report 2025), meaning the assumption that newborn care is the mother's primary responsibility is written into employment law.[^92]

The **unpaid care burden** falls dramatically more on women than men. DHS 2016-17 data indicates women spend approximately **19 hours per week** on unpaid domestic and care work, compared with **8.1 hours for men** – a gap of nearly 11 hours per week. This represents time that is not available for education, formal employment, civic participation, or leisure. The unpaid care burden both reflects and reinforces women's economic disadvantage: women reduce paid work hours (or withdraw from the labour market entirely) to meet care obligations that the social system assigns to them, which then reduces their lifetime earnings, pension accumulation, and economic independence.[^93]

The near-total absence of formal childcare infrastructure is the most documented structural enabler of the unpaid care burden. With fewer than 4 formal daycare centres nationally, and no subsidised community childcare in outer atolls, the childcare gap is both a SRHR issue (affecting women's ability to space pregnancies) and a labour market issue (confining mothers to the home in the years most critical for career development).

### 5.9.7 Gender-Responsive Budgeting

The Gender Equality Act 18/2016 mandates **Gender-Responsive Budgeting (GRB)** across government ministries – a legislative innovation that, if implemented, would systematically embed gender analysis into the national budget cycle. The Ministry of Finance and the National Planning Commission are the lead agencies for GRB implementation.[^94]

The practical record is mixed. MoSFD's GRB Implementation Report 2024 indicates that **fewer than 40% of government ministries** complied with GRB guidelines in the most recent budget cycle – meaning that the majority of ministries continue to allocate resources without systematic analysis of gender-differentiated impacts. The CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report (2025) acknowledges this compliance gap and notes that GRB capacity within ministries remains limited despite periodic training.[^95]

GRB is particularly significant for gender economic empowerment because it creates an institutional mechanism for ensuring that national budget decisions – on education subsidies, transportation, health services, childcare – are analysed through the lens of their differential impact on women and men. Its under-implementation is thus not merely a procedural compliance failure but a missed opportunity to align fiscal policy with gender equality commitments across government.

---


## 5.45 Gender Inequality in Specific Economic Sectors


### 5.30.1 Tourism: The Largest Sector's Gender Exclusion

The tourism sector is the Maldives' largest economic sector by GDP share and formal employment. Understanding why Maldivian women are largely absent from this sector is essential to any analysis of women's economic participation.

**The resort-island model:** Maldivian resorts are built on uninhabited islands leased to resort operators. Staff live and work on the resort island full-time, typically on rotation schedules (one month on, one week off, or similar). This live-in model is the primary structural barrier to Maldivian women's resort employment: living away from family and community – on a mixed-gender, international staff island – is socially unacceptable in many Maldivian communities, particularly for unmarried women or women with children.

**Recruitment patterns:** Resort operators recruit predominantly through informal networks, with Maldivian male recruits channelled through fishing community and atoll connections. Women are rarely part of these networks for the technical and operational roles that dominate resort staffing (boat captains, diving instructors, maintenance, water sports). Administrative and food service roles where women might be employed require the same live-in commitment that creates the access barrier.

**The 3% figure in context:** The figure that only 3% of resort employees were Maldivian women (Housing and Poverty chapter §12.X) represents the intersection of structural access barriers (geography, live-in model), social norm constraints (unsupervised mixed-gender living), and recruitment network exclusion. Closing this gap would require: resort operators offering Maldivian-specific employment models that allow non-live-in or day-commute work from nearby inhabited islands; social norm change around women's work in resort environments; and deliberate Maldivian women's recruitment and training pipelines.[^340]

**Day tripper tourism and guesthouse sector:** The growth of the guesthouse tourism sector on inhabited islands – following the 2009 liberalisation allowing tourists to stay on inhabited (not just resort) islands – has created new formal sector employment opportunities for Maldivian women on their home islands, without the live-in barrier. Guesthouse employment in cooking, housekeeping, reception, and tour guiding has increased women's tourism employment, though the aggregate numbers remain limited by the relatively small scale of the guesthouse sector compared with resort tourism.[^341]

**Gender and the 'Maldivianisation' policy:** The government's tourism Maldivianisation policy – requiring resort operators to meet minimum Maldivian employee quotas – has increased total Maldivian employment in resorts but has not been accompanied by gender-disaggregated targets. The policy effectively increases male Maldivian resort employment without addressing the gendered access barriers that exclude most Maldivian women. Gender-specific Maldivianisation targets or quotas would be required to change the employment gender composition.[^342]

### 5.30.2 Fishing: The Livelihood Gender Boundary

Fishing – historically the primary livelihood of most Maldivian families and the cultural foundation of Maldivian male identity – is socially and practically male-dominated. The *dhoni* (traditional fishing vessel) and the reef fishing practices around it are associated with masculine identity, physical strength, and sea-going skill. Women have historically participated in the shore-side of the fishing economy (fish processing, drying, trading) rather than sea fishing itself.[^343]

The economic transformation of the fishing sector has had mixed gender implications:
- Industrial and commercial fishing, managed through fishing companies and export markets, has concentrated economic value in the offshore fishing sector dominated by male labour
- Traditional shore-side fish processing (previously a significant female economic activity) has declined in relative economic value as fresh/frozen fish export markets have grown and dried/smoked fish markets have contracted
- Women's traditional economic role in fish processing – the *mas hiki* of dried tuna – has been partially displaced, reducing a traditional source of women's economic income without equivalent replacement opportunities in the commercial fishing economy
- Fishing cooperatives, which manage collective fishing resources and marketing, are male-dominated institutions that exclude women from governance of an economic resource that has historically shaped entire island economies[^344]

The ADB ARISE Project's gender component specifically identifies women's exclusion from fishing governance as an economic equity issue with climate dimensions – as fishing resource management decisions (regulated by Fishermen's Associations and Island Councils) increasingly involve climate adaptation choices that affect both male and female members of fishing communities.

### 5.30.3 Government and Public Services: The Female Concentration
