Government employment is the largest formal employment sector for Maldivian women – 43% of employed women work in government (HIES 2016). This concentration reflects the channelling of educated women toward teaching, nursing, and social services, and the relative accessibility of public sector employment through formal application processes rather than the informal male-dominated networks that dominate private sector recruitment.[^345]

Within government employment, gender is not equally distributed across levels:
- **Service delivery roles** (teachers, nurses, social workers, administrative staff) have high female concentrations
- **Policy and management roles** (senior civil service, ministry leadership, department heads) are male-dominated
- **Technical and regulatory roles** (engineering, fisheries management, financial regulation) remain predominantly male

The **Civil Service Commission** does not publish gender-disaggregated data on salary levels and career progression by grade and department – a data gap that makes it impossible to track the gender pay gap within government or assess promotion equity. The CEDAW Committee has called for this data to be collected and published.[^346]

**Teaching:** The feminisation of the teaching profession is marked. Women constitute the majority of primary and secondary school teachers, with female concentration increasing at lower school levels. The profession's perceived lower status relative to other professional roles reflects and reinforces the gendered channelling toward teaching, while the teaching workforce's own gender dynamics are rarely examined as a gender equality issue in themselves.[^347]

**Nursing and health services:** Nursing is similarly feminised. The nursing workforce is predominantly female; medical doctor and specialist roles are more gender-balanced at junior levels but male-dominated at senior and specialisation levels. The gender dynamics of the health workforce interact with the health system's capacity to respond to women's health needs – including GBV-related health presentations – through the provider's own awareness and training.[^348]

### 5.30.4 Agriculture, Handicrafts, and the Informal Economy

A significant proportion of women's economic activity in the Maldives – particularly in outer atolls – occurs in the informal economy, outside the formal employment and statistical measurement systems. Informal economic activities with high female participation include:

**Home-based food production and trade:** Women produce and sell food from home – cooked foods, baked goods, traditional Maldivian foods – through informal markets, social networks, and increasingly through social media. This activity is economically significant for household income but largely invisible in formal labour statistics, contributing to the undercount of women's economic participation in LFPR figures.

**Handicrafts and weaving:** Traditional Maldivian weaving (*thundu kunaa* – lacquerware and mat weaving) has been predominantly a female skill. The handicraft sector has both cultural and economic dimensions; government programmes have intermittently supported women handicraft producers to access markets, though the sector remains small in aggregate economic terms.

**Home-based childcare:** Women who provide informal childcare for neighbours' or family members' children – enabling those women to participate in the formal labour market – are providing an economic service that is unremunerated and statistically invisible, but which is a significant component of the childcare infrastructure that the formal sector has not provided.

**Agricultural cultivation:** On islands with sufficient land (particularly in southern atolls), women participate in cultivation of vegetables, coconut, and breadfruit for household consumption and local sale. This activity has declined as supply chain integration and import of food commodities has made local cultivation less economically competitive, but it remains a component of household food security in some communities.[^349]

The invisibility of informal economic activity in official statistics systematically understates women's economic contribution to Maldivian households and communities. It also means that informal workers – predominantly women – have no access to social protection systems (pension contributions, employment injury insurance) linked to formal employment.

---


## 5.46 The Economic Cost of Gender Inequality


### 5.33.1 Framing Gender Inequality as an Economic Loss

A dimension of the gender equality argument that has growing traction in development policy discourse is the **economic cost** of gender inequality – the GDP and productivity foregone as a result of women's exclusion from full economic participation. While this framing is not the primary ethical argument for gender equality, it is politically significant in contexts (like the Maldives) where economic development is a central policy priority.

### 5.33.2 The LFPR Gap: Direct Economic Cost

The 36-percentage-point LFPR gap between men (80%) and women (44%) represents a direct economic cost: the productive capacity of working-age women who are not in formal employment. A rough estimate of this cost:

- If female LFPR in the Maldives were raised to the level of male LFPR (80%), approximately 36% more women of working age would enter formal employment
- The female working age population (ages 15-64) is approximately 100,000-110,000 (rough Census 2022 estimate)
- 36% of this population = approximately 36,000-40,000 additional women in formal employment
- At the average formal sector wage, this represents tens of millions of MVR in additional GDP annually

This is a conservative estimate that does not account for the productivity effects of women entering higher-value employment if structural barriers (childcare, education-job matching, sector access) were addressed. The McKinsey Global Institute has estimated that closing gender gaps in economic participation could add 12% to GDP in South Asia; a Maldives-specific estimate does not exist but the order of magnitude would be comparable.[^368]

### 5.33.3 The Cost of GBV

The economic cost of domestic violence includes:
- **Healthcare costs:** Medical treatment of DV-related injuries across health facilities
- **Criminal justice costs:** Police investigation, prosecution, court processing of DV cases
- **Lost productivity:** Women who reduce work hours, miss work, or leave employment due to DV-related physical or psychological harm
- **Social welfare costs:** FPA services, shelter provision, child welfare services for DV-affected families
- **Long-term costs:** Intergenerational effects – children growing up in DV-affected households have documented negative effects on educational outcomes, mental health, and adult economic productivity

No comprehensive economic cost-of-DV analysis has been conducted for the Maldives. The WHO's global estimates suggest domestic violence costs economies between 1.2% and 3.7% of GDP annually; applied to the Maldives' approximately USD 6 billion GDP, this would represent USD 72-222 million per year in economic costs – a figure that substantially exceeds the total budget of the FPA, AIM Project, and related GBV response programmes combined. The economic case for GBV prevention investment is, on these estimates, compelling.[^369]

### 5.33.4 The Childcare Infrastructure Gap as Economic Constraint

The absence of adequate childcare infrastructure is simultaneously a gender equality failure and an economic efficiency failure. The macroeconomic modelling of childcare investment – conducted for several comparable contexts – consistently finds that childcare investment:
- Pays for itself through the tax revenue from women who enter or remain in formal employment
- Produces additional developmental benefits for children that translate into higher lifetime earnings and productivity
- Reduces the social welfare costs associated with NEET women and children in poverty

The Maldives currently invests extremely little in formal childcare infrastructure (fewer than 4 centres nationally). A systematic investment in publicly funded childcare – calibrated to the atoll geography through a mix of large-island centres and subsidised home-based childcare networks – would represent one of the highest-return social investments available in the Maldivian policy portfolio. The CEDAW Committee's call for childcare infrastructure investment is not merely a gender equality recommendation; it is an economic development recommendation.[^370]

---


## 5.47 Women and the Ocean Economy: Emerging Gender Dimensions


### 5.59.1 The Blue Economy and Gender

The Maldives is developing a "**Blue Economy**" strategy – leveraging its Exclusive Economic Zone (EEZ) of approximately 900,000 km² for sustainable economic development through fisheries, marine biotechnology, desalination, ocean energy, and sustainable tourism. The gender dimensions of the Blue Economy are emerging policy terrain that the existing gender policy literature does not adequately address.

**Fisheries:** As documented in §5.30, the fisheries sector is male-dominated in ocean-going activities. Blue Economy development of the fisheries sector – particularly aquaculture expansion and sustainable fisheries certification – creates new economic opportunities that require deliberate gender inclusion to avoid simply reinforcing the existing male monopoly. Aquaculture development may actually be more accessible to women than ocean fishing, since aquaculture infrastructure can be island-based, potentially compatible with care responsibilities and not requiring the sea-going participation that has historically excluded women.

**Marine biotechnology:** An emerging sector with no existing employment tradition – therefore potentially more accessible to women entering with relevant educational qualifications than sectors with entrenched male-only traditions.

**Eco-tourism and marine conservation:** Maldivian eco-tourism and marine conservation work (coral restoration, marine protected areas management) has a significant female presence internationally. Positioning Maldivian women in this growing sector – particularly in community-based marine conservation management – could create sustainable employment compatible with island location.[^462]

### 5.59.2 Tourism's Gender Potential Reconsidered

Beyond the resort sector's gender exclusion, the tourism economy has gender-inclusive dimensions that policy has not fully leveraged:

**Cultural tourism:** Maldivian cultural tourism – crafts, traditional music, culinary traditions, historical sites – has higher female participation potential than ocean-based tourism. Women who are the primary practitioners of traditional crafts and cooking could be primary beneficiaries of cultural tourism development if supported with marketing, quality standards, and tourist connection infrastructure.

**Community homestay tourism:** The guesthouse sector's growth has created homestay opportunities on inhabited islands. Women who manage the household are natural potential operators of small guesthouses – an extension of domestic skills into a formal economic activity. Policy support for women guesthouse operators through training, licensing facilitation, and microfinance access could substantially expand women's tourism income.

**Online craft and food markets:** Digital platforms have created opportunities for Maldivian women producing handicrafts and food products to reach domestic urban and international markets without physical presence in tourist areas. Platform-based commerce is accessible from outer islands in a way that resort employment is not. Government investment in women's digital commerce skills – product photography, online platform use, digital payment systems – could convert existing productive capacity into market-connected income.[^463]

---


## 5.48 The National Employment Service: Women's Access


### 5.90.1 Employment Service Centres

The **Employment Service Centres (ESCs)** – operated by the Ministry of Economic Development (and supported by the ADB SAILS project) – provide job placement services, skills matching, and employment counselling for Maldivian jobseekers. The gender dimensions of ESC services are documented in the SAILS project documentation:

**Women's use of ESCs:** Women who use the ESCs include recently educated graduates seeking their first job, women returning to the labour market after caregiving breaks, and women in outer atolls with limited local employment options seeking urban or remote employment opportunities. The SAILS project specifically targets women as an underserved jobseeker demographic and develops gender-responsive service protocols.

**ESC outer island access:** ESCs are concentrated in Malé and atoll capitals. Women in smaller islands who cannot travel to ESC locations face the same geographic access barrier that limits access to FPA, courts, and health services. Virtual ESC services – delivered through telephone or online platforms – are a partial solution being developed through SAILS.

**Skills mismatch support:** The ESC's skills-matching function is particularly relevant for women given the documented skills mismatch (15% overqualified, 44% underqualified). ESCs that can identify women whose qualifications exceed local job requirements and connect them to remote work or retraining opportunities – or identify women whose skills gaps prevent access to available jobs and provide retraining support – could substantially improve women's labour market outcomes.[^522]

---


## 5.49 Housing, Poverty, and Women's Economic Security


### 5.14.1 Female-Headed Households: Prevalence and Poverty Profile

Female-headed households constitute **42.7% of all households** in the Maldives (HIES 2016) – an extraordinarily high proportion internationally and one directly connected to the high divorce rate and pattern of serial marriage. When a marriage dissolves, women typically take primary custody of children but often lose access to the marital home; many divorced women return to parents or siblings, creating the female-headed household structures captured in census data.[^154]

Female-headed households have a poverty rate of **6%** compared with **5% for male-headed households** – a small but statistically meaningful difference that reflects the economic disadvantage divorce imposes on women in a context of limited formal employment options, unpaid maintenance, and family law property provisions that may not adequately compensate for the disparity.[^155]

The high proportion of female-headed households reflects the interaction of several features specific to the Maldivian context:
- High divorce rate (5.52 per 1,000 in 2024) meaning many women experience household headship following divorce
- Pattern of divorced women with children forming independent households rather than immediately remarrying
- Male labour migration – particularly to resort islands and Male' – leaving wives as de facto household heads on home islands
- Deaths from sea-related occupational risks disproportionately affecting male workers, leaving widows as household heads[^156]

### 5.14.2 Housing Insecurity After Divorce

A critical and under-documented dimension of post-divorce vulnerability for Maldivian women is **housing insecurity**. In the traditional matrilocal system, women had residential stability post-divorce because they remained in the family island home while the husband left. Urbanisation has substantially eroded this pattern: in Malé and growing island towns, housing is market-provided (rental or owner-occupied), not family-held.[^157]

Upon divorce in urban settings:
- A divorced woman who was renting with her husband must either secure her own rental accommodation (at market rates on a single income, often with children) or return to her home island
- A divorced woman who was living in the husband's rented accommodation must vacate, as she has no legal claim to the tenancy
- Where the couple owned a home, the husband typically retains ownership or occupancy, as women's property rights in the marital home are governed by Islamic inheritance and property principles that privilege male ownership
- The MVR 2 million in unclaimed court-ordered maintenance represents the gap between maintenance orders issued by the Family Court and maintenance actually received – leaving many single mothers without the income to cover housing costs[^158]

For survivors of domestic violence, housing insecurity is additionally complex: leaving an abusive partner means leaving the shared home, and without a shelter placement, the survivor may have nowhere to go. The three ADB-funded shelters (Addu/Hithadhoo, Hulhumale', Raa Atoll) and the FPA's transitional housing provision in Malé address only a small fraction of need, and provide time-limited accommodation with no pathway to sustainable independent housing.[^159]

### 5.14.3 Single Mothers: The Intersection of Multiple Vulnerabilities

Single mothers – women raising children without a co-resident partner – face the intersection of multiple identified vulnerabilities:
- **Economic:** Single income (often lower than equivalent male wage) supporting children without reliable maintenance
- **Childcare:** Full childcare responsibility without a partner, in a country with fewer than 4 formal daycare centres, constraining employment options
- **Social:** Social stigma associated with divorce and single parenthood varying by community, but consistently present as a constraint on social participation
- **Housing:** Housing insecurity as documented above, particularly in urban settings
- **Geographic:** Isolation for single mothers on outer islands, where there may be no state services, no WDC support, and no shelter access[^160]

The Laamu Atoll Vulnerability Mapping (cited in the Housing and Poverty chapter, §12.X) specifically identified **single mothers** as one of the most vulnerable population groups in the atoll, with compounded needs across income support, childcare, housing, and GBV protection. The mapping is one of the few systematic subnational analyses of this intersection, and its findings are likely generalisable to similar outer atoll contexts.[^161]

### 5.14.4 Women's Property and Asset Ownership

The CEDAW Committee and Statistics Compendium both identify women's land and property ownership as a **critical data gap** in the Maldivian statistical system. No routine national data collection captures gender-disaggregated property ownership, inheritance receipts, or asset accumulation.[^162]

What is known from indirect sources:
- Islamic inheritance law (daughters receiving half the share of sons) creates a structural gender wealth gap in property accumulation across generations
- Land on islands is traditionally controlled by male household heads; women's access to land is mediated through family relationships rather than independent legal rights in many outer island contexts
- In the absence of property ownership, divorced women have limited collateral for business loans or housing security
- The gender pay gap (13-20% in formal sector), combined with lower labour force participation, means women accumulate lower savings and pension entitlements throughout working life

The consequence is a compounding of gender inequality: lower inherited assets, lower wages, lower employment rates, and the financial consequences of divorce collectively produce a gender wealth gap that is likely significant but unmeasured.[^163]

### 5.14.5 Skills Mismatch and Its Gendered Dimensions

The Housing and Poverty chapter (§12.X) documents a structural skills mismatch in the Maldivian economy with gendered dimensions:
- **15% of women** in the labour market are **overqualified** for the jobs available to them – particularly in outer atolls where formal employment options are limited to a few government positions[^164]
- **44% of women** are **underqualified** for the growing high-skill jobs available in urban and tourism-adjacent settings – reflecting historical channelling away from technical education[^165]

The skills mismatch creates a double bind: educated women in outer atolls cannot access jobs that match their qualifications; women without technical skills cannot access the growing economy's highest-wage opportunities. The SAILS project and ADB ARISE project both target aspects of this mismatch – vocational retraining, gender-responsive employment services – but the scale of intervention is not commensurate with the structural problem.

## 5.50 The Gender Dimensions of Poverty: A Detailed Analysis


### 5.73.1 Poverty Measurement and Gender

The Maldives' poverty measurement – using the national poverty line and the HIES 2016 data – documents a 4.5% national poverty rate with female-headed households at 6% and male-headed households at 5%. This relatively small differential in percentage points masks a larger absolute story: given that female-headed households are 42.7% of all households, a 6% poverty rate in that group means a substantial absolute number of women and children in poverty.

**The HIES methodology:** The HIES measures poverty at the household level – a household is classified as poor if its per capita income or consumption falls below the poverty line. Gender-disaggregated poverty measurement requires going beyond household-level poverty to individual-level poverty, accounting for intrahousehold distribution of resources. In a household where resources are unequally distributed between a male household head and other household members, household-level poverty measurement may understate women's individual poverty.

**Intra-household resource distribution:** Research on intra-household resource distribution in the Maldivian context is limited. International evidence consistently finds that households with female control over resources have better nutrition, health, and education outcomes for children – suggesting that the gender of the resource controller matters for poverty impact. In Maldivian households where men control income and distribute it unequally, female household members may experience poverty conditions that are invisible to household-level poverty measurement.[^495]

### 5.73.2 The Poverty-Violence-Poverty Cycle

The bidirectional relationship between poverty and DV creates a cyclical dynamic that is extremely difficult to interrupt:

**Poverty → DV:** Economic stress is a documented proximate trigger for DV escalation. Households in poverty experience ongoing financial conflict, resource competition, and the stress of material deprivation that creates conditions for violence. Women in poverty have fewer economic exit options – no independent income, no savings, no alternative housing – making the power imbalance in the household more extreme.

**DV → Poverty:** Violence in the household has direct economic consequences for women: inability to maintain employment due to injury, trauma, and time in legal proceedings; loss of housing when leaving an abusive relationship; financial abuse including the abusive partner controlling or taking income; and the costs of legal proceedings (court fees, transport, childcare) that consume limited resources.

**Breaking the cycle:** Single interventions – income support, or DV response, but not both – cannot break a cycle with two entry points. The most effective anti-poverty programmes in international DV research are those that combine economic empowerment (income generation, savings groups, financial literacy) with social norm change (addressing violence-enabling norms) rather than treating poverty and violence as separate policy domains.[^496]

### 5.73.3 Women and the Subsistence Economy

For a significant proportion of outer island women, survival does not depend on formal employment or government transfers but on the **subsistence economy** – household food production, fishing-adjacent activities, informal exchange, and family support networks. This subsistence-level economy is substantially invisible to formal statistics but represents real economic activity with significant gender dimensions.

**Coconut cultivation and home gardens:** Many Maldivian islands have traditional home garden cultivation of coconuts, breadfruit, vegetables, and herbs. This cultivation – primarily managed by women – produces food for household consumption and, when production exceeds household needs, for exchange or sale. Climate change is affecting island freshwater availability and coastal flooding, directly threatening this subsistence production base – with disproportionate consequences for women who depend on it.[^497]

**Fish sharing networks:** In fishing communities, fish sharing networks – informal distribution of catch among households based on community solidarity norms – provide food security that is partially independent of household income. Women's position in these networks varies: some are active participants; others receive fish through their husbands' participation. Climate impacts on reef fish populations threaten this food security component.

**Informal credit and savings:** *Bodufenvalin* and similar informal savings and credit circles – where members make regular contributions and periodically receive the pooled funds – are a traditional Maldivian financial instrument with significant female participation. These informal credit mechanisms provide access to lump sums (for emergency expenses, productive investments, or home repairs) that formal banking does not provide to women without collateral or credit history. The economic empowerment literature identifies these savings circle mechanisms as an underutilised asset for women's financial inclusion.

---


## 5.51 Housing and Women's Spatial Safety


### 5.65.1 Housing Policy and Gender

Maldivian housing policy – including the government's housing construction and allocation programmes, the Hulhumale' development, and the island housing infrastructure – has significant gender dimensions that have been inadequately addressed in mainstream housing policy discourse.

**The Madhanee Mahala (Government Housing) programme:** The government's subsidised housing programmes for low-income Maldivians – including the Hulhumale' Social Housing scheme and similar island-level programmes – allocate housing units through a prioritisation process. The extent to which this process applies gender-sensitive criteria – prioritising female-headed households, DV survivors, or single mothers – is not documented in available public records. Housing allocation decisions that default to male household heads in dual-parent households replicate patterns of male property control.[^476]

**Post-divorce housing:** As documented in §5.14, post-divorce housing insecurity is one of the most severe material consequences of divorce for women in urban settings. Government housing policy has not developed specific instruments for recently divorced women – short-term bridge housing, priority allocation in housing programmes, or security deposit assistance for independent rental. The gap between the high divorce rate and the housing policy non-response to post-divorce female housing insecurity is stark.

**Hulhumale' development:** Hulhumale' – built on reclaimed land adjacent to Malé – is the fastest-growing population centre in the Maldives, housing primarily working-age adults and families. Its social services infrastructure is not yet commensurate with its population: there is limited FPA presence, limited police FCPW capacity, and no shelter in the Hulhumale' area (the ADB-funded shelter is in Hulhumale' city as a separate facility). As Hulhumale's population grows, its GBV response infrastructure needs to scale proportionately – but the investment has lagged population growth.[^477]

### 5.65.2 Women's Mobility and Spatial Safety

Women's use of public space – particularly in Malé, Hulhumale', and larger island towns – is shaped by safety concerns and social norms that limit freedom of movement:

- **Night-time mobility:** Women report avoiding public spaces at night in Malé due to safety concerns, including documented incidents of street harassment and sexual assault. The concentration of drug use and associated crime in certain Male' areas creates legitimate safety concerns for women's public movement.

- **Fishing harbour and waterfront areas:** Traditionally male-dominated spaces – fishing harbours, boat yards, construction sites – that adjoin public areas create environments where women passing through may experience harassment or feel unwelcome.

- **Public transport:** The male-dominated culture of public ferries and speedboats in outer island contexts creates environments in which women travelling alone may experience unwanted contact or harassment. Women's ability to use inter-island transport safely is directly relevant to their access to services (FPA, courts, health) in the outer atoll context.

**Safe space design:** Urban planning for gender safety – well-lit streets, emergency call points, accessible public bathrooms, women-only spaces in public facilities – has not been systematically integrated into Maldivian urban planning. The Hulhumale' planning process, which had a clean-slate opportunity to design a gender-safe city, did not systematically apply gender safety principles.[^478]

---


## 5.52 Social Protection and Women: The NSPA and Aasandha


### 5.56.1 The Social Protection Architecture

The Maldivian social protection system encompasses:
- **Aasandha (National Health Insurance):** Universal health coverage providing free healthcare at government health facilities for all Maldivians
- **National Social Protection Agency (NSPA) poverty assistance:** Income support for vulnerable households below the poverty line
- **Old Age Basic Pension (OABP):** Universal non-contributory pension for all Maldivians above age 65
- **Contributory pension scheme:** Managed by the Maldives Pension Office for formal sector employees
- **Disability benefits:** Income support for persons with disabilities meeting eligibility criteria

### 5.56.2 Gender Dimensions of Aasandha

Aasandha's universal coverage is a significant gender equality achievement: women access government health services (including ANC, postnatal care, and reproductive health services) without cost at point of service. This removes a major financial barrier to healthcare access that many comparable-income countries face.

**Limitations:**
- Aasandha covers government health facilities; private healthcare (including the private hospitals that provide specialised reproductive health services) requires out-of-pocket payment or supplementary insurance
- The quality and availability of services covered by Aasandha varies dramatically by island location – outer island health posts provide basic services; specialist care requires referral and travel to Malé or regional hospitals
- Mental health services covered by Aasandha are limited – the National Mental Health Centre at IGMH is covered, but GBV-specific counselling, postnatal mental health support, and trauma-informed therapy are not consistently available through the Aasandha system[^457]

### 5.56.3 NSPA Poverty Assistance: Gender Access

The NSPA's poverty assistance programme provides income support to households below the national poverty line. Gender dimensions of NSPA access:

**Female-headed households:** Female-headed households (42.7% of all households) are disproportionately represented in poverty (6% poverty rate vs. 5% for male-headed households). Female household heads who meet the poverty eligibility criteria should be prioritising NSPA assistance. The extent to which the NSPA's case-finding and registration processes reach women who are isolated, in abusive situations, or in outer islands without regular contact with government social workers is not systematically documented.

**DV survivors' access:** Domestic violence survivors who have left abusive households – including women who have relocated to shelters or to family homes – may face administrative barriers to NSPA registration if their residential address is uncertain, if their national ID has been withheld by an abusive partner, or if the NSPA's eligibility criteria are tied to household composition at a specific address.

**Conditional cash transfers:** The Maldives does not operate a conditional cash transfer (CCT) programme of the kind common in South Asian social protection systems (Bangladesh's stipend for secondary girls' education, India's MGNREGS). The absence of CCT instruments means the social protection system has no mechanism for specifically incentivising girls' education retention or discouraging child marriage through financial transfers.

### 5.56.4 Pension System Gender Gap

The contributory pension scheme managed by the Maldives Pension Office accumulates employer and employee contributions over a working career, with the accumulated balance determining the retirement pension benefit. The structural features of the pension system interact with the gender employment gap:

- Women's lower formal sector LFPR (44%) means fewer women accumulate contributory pension entitlements
- Women's lower wages (13-20% below men's in comparable positions) mean lower contribution amounts even for women in formal employment
- Career interruptions for caregiving (maternity leave, reduced hours) reduce pension accumulation
- Women's shorter working careers (more likely to withdraw from employment around family formation) produce lower accumulated balances

The consequence is a **gender pension gap** that compounds over retirement years: women with lower pensions are more dependent on the universal OABP (a flat rate, regardless of prior contributions), family support, or insufficient retirement income. The OABP's flat rate provides a floor – no Maldivian over 65 receives zero retirement income – but the gap between the OABP level and the income needed for a comfortable retirement is filled differently for men (through higher contributory pension balances) than for women.[^458]

---


## 5.53 Social Protection for Women: Design, Gaps, and COVID Failures


### 5.111.1 The Single Parent Allowance: Gender Profile and Design Barriers

The **Single Parent Allowance** administered by NSPA is the primary social protection instrument specifically addressing gender-related household vulnerability. Its eligibility criteria:

- The applicant must be widowed, divorced, or unmarried, with sole or primary custody of at least one child under 18
- If divorced, at least three months must have elapsed since the divorce
- A spouse whose partner is serving a prison sentence of six months or more is also eligible
- Income must fall below a specified threshold

The three-month post-divorce waiting period before eligibility is a significant design flaw: the period immediately following divorce is typically the most acute vulnerability moment, when a woman has lost household income, may have lost housing, and has the highest immediate needs. Requiring three months to elapse before the first benefit payment begins forces women to navigate this crisis period without income support.[^565]

**Documentation and application barriers:** Single parent allowance requires documentary proof of divorce (a court document), custody arrangement, and income status. For women on outer islands, obtaining and submitting documentation requires interaction with island council officials, Family Court records, and NSPA administrative systems – processes that may be inaccessible for women with low literacy, limited digital access, or childcare responsibilities during working hours. The CFW recommendations call for "fast-track procedures for single parent allowance and emergency in-kind support for newly separated women" (R27) as a year-one quick win – acknowledging that the current process is too slow for the pace of need.

### 5.111.2 COVID-19: The Gender-Blind Emergency Response

The initial COVID-19 emergency response did not adequately account for the gendered nature of the economic shock. The government's own assessment acknowledged this as a design failure. Specifically:

- **Increased unpaid care burden on women:** School closures transferred childcare responsibility to parents; the gender distribution of this responsibility fell overwhelmingly on women. The tourism sector shutdown removed the male breadwinner income from many households, with women absorbing both the income loss (through reduced own income) and the increased care demand simultaneously.

- **Vulnerability of female-headed households:** The 42.7% female-headed household rate (Census 2022) meant that a large proportion of Maldivian households had no male income-earner to absorb the tourism sector shock. Income support schemes that were designed around formal sector employment excluded many female-headed households operating in the informal economy.

- **GBV spike:** The lockdown period documented a spike in GBV cases. In Malé, where the strict lockdown was most severe, women were confined with their abusers with limited escape options. UNFPA provided dignity kits to women in shelters during the lockdown, but the shelter capacity at the time was inadequate for the volume of need.

- **Gender-blind income replacement:** The Emergency Income Support Allowance paid during the lockdown did not disaggregate eligibility by gender or household type, potentially missing female-headed households whose informal income sources were not captured in formal employment records.[^566]

### 5.111.3 Aasandha and Women's Health

The Aasandha universal health insurance scheme nominally covers all Maldivians regardless of gender. The gender dimension of Aasandha coverage failures includes:

- **Reproductive health:** The 29% unmet need for contraception suggests that Aasandha's coverage of contraceptive services is inadequate in practice – either through supply-side gaps (contraceptives not stocked at island health posts), knowledge gaps (women not knowing contraceptive services are covered), or access gaps (women unable to access pharmacies or clinics privately).

- **Infertility treatment:** The SAP light chapter on Women and Gender identified access to maternity and infertility treatment through Aasandha as a medium-term priority – indicating that current coverage is inadequate.

- **Out-of-pocket burden:** The 49% out-of-pocket health expenditure nationally suggests that Aasandha's nominal coverage does not translate into free access at the point of care. Women's reproductive health needs – which are frequent and often non-acute – are particularly susceptible to this out-of-pocket barrier, as visits for contraception, prenatal care, or postnatal monitoring may be deprioritised when cost is a constraint.[^567]

---


# Part 8: Political Representation and Civic Participation


## 5.54 Political Representation and Civic Participation


### 5.10.1 Parliamentary Representation: Historical Pattern and Current Position

Women's representation in the People's Majlis (Parliament) has been and remains among the lowest in the world. The trajectory since the 2008 constitutional transition reflects episodic gains without sustained structural progress:

| Election Year | Women MPs | Total Seats | % Women | Notes |
|---------------|-----------|-------------|---------|-------|
| 2008 | 9 | 77 | 11.7% | Post-constitution first election |
| 2009 | 6 | 77 | 7.8% | Decline in first regular election |
| 2014 | 5 | 85 | 5.9% | Further decline |
| 2019 | 4 | 87 | 4.6% | Near-historic low |
| 2024 | 4 | 87 | 4.6% | No change |

The 2024 Parliament figure of **4 women out of 87 seats (4.6%)** is confirmed by the CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report (2025). Earlier drafts of this encyclopedia incorrectly cited 3/93; the correct figures are 4/87.[^96]

The Maldives consistently ranks in the bottom decile globally for women's parliamentary representation. The IPU's 2024 ranking placed the Maldives at approximately 175th out of 185 countries measured – behind most of the world, including all comparator South Asian and small island developing states. Sri Lanka (5.3%), the closest comparator, is marginally higher.[^97]

Barriers to women's candidacy and election identified in research by the Maldivian Democracy Network (MDN), the Human Rights Commission, and international election observers include:

- **Campaign financing:** Parliamentary campaigns require substantial personal financing (estimated MVR 200,000–500,000 per campaign). Women's lower lifetime earnings and limited access to business financing leave them less able to finance campaigns independently.[^98]
- **Party structures:** Major political parties do not have internal gender quotas for candidate selection. Nomination decisions are made by party leadership structures dominated by men, and evidence from MDN assessments indicates women candidates are more likely to be placed in unwinnable constituencies.[^99]
- **Social norms and religious framing:** A sustained strand of public discourse argues that women's leadership is inconsistent with Islamic principles – drawing on Hadith literature. While this view is not official government policy, it is openly expressed by religious scholars with public influence and creates a hostile discursive environment for women seeking elected office.[^100]
- **Time and care constraints:** The Majlis schedule (including late-night sessions) is incompatible with the primary care responsibilities that fall on women. Women parliamentarians have cited the absence of childcare at the People's Majlis as a practical barrier to full participation.[^101]
- **Harassment:** Women in political life face higher rates of online harassment and personal intimidation than male politicians. MDN has documented coordinated social media campaigns against women candidates, including gender-based content targeting their dress, marital status, and family background.[^102]

### 5.10.2 Local Council Quota: A Structural Success Story

In contrast to the parliamentary record, the introduction of a **33% gender quota** for local council elections represents the most significant structural intervention in Maldivian political history for women's representation:

- **Pre-quota (2017):** Women held approximately **4%** of local council seats nationally – roughly equivalent to the parliamentary figure[^103]
- **Post-quota (2021 elections):** Women's local council representation rose to approximately **33%** – an increase of nearly 30 percentage points in a single election cycle[^104]

The quota was legislated through amendments to the Decentralisation Act and applied for the first time in the 2021 local council elections. All atolls and islands were required to ensure at least one-third of council seats were held by women. The transformation was immediate and dramatic. For the first time, thousands of women across 270 inhabited islands gained formal political representation and a constituency for their concerns within local governance.

The quota's impact has not been uniform. Research on WDC and council effectiveness by UNDP/MoSFD (2022) documents that newly elected women councillors face:
- **Exclusion from council meetings** by dominant male members unwilling to recognise their equal standing
- **No dedicated budget** in many islands for gender equality programming that women councillors might wish to pursue
- **Advisory framing:** women councillors describe being treated as advisors rather than decision-makers, particularly on traditionally male domains (fisheries, infrastructure)
- **Training gaps:** fewer than 40% of WDC members had received formal training in the two years prior to assessment

Despite these implementation challenges, the quota represents proof of concept that structural intervention can rapidly transform the composition of representative bodies. Women councillors, even in constrained circumstances, have raised issues of domestic violence, health service access, and women's safety that had previously been absent from local council agendas.[^105]

### 5.10.3 Women's Development Committees: Mandate, Practice, and Reality

The **Women's Development Committees (WDCs)** are statutory bodies established on each inhabited island under the Decentralisation Act, tasked with overseeing women's development at island level. There are WDCs on all 270 inhabited islands, making them the most geographically extensive gender-focused institutional presence in the Maldives.

In practice, the WDC mandate is substantially unrealised. The Transparency Maldives (2015) and IFES (2015) assessments, and the more recent UNDP/MoSFD (2022) WDC Capacity Assessment, consistently document that WDCs:

- **Have no independent budget:** WDCs receive no dedicated budget allocation and must apply to island councils for any funding. Island councils (which have their own resource constraints) routinely deprioritise WDC requests. This makes it impossible for WDCs to carry out even minimal programming – organising community awareness events or supporting DV survivors – without personal financial contributions from members.[^106]
- **Are excluded from formal governance:** Despite being statutory bodies, WDC recommendations are not binding on island councils or government agencies. The WDC role is formally advisory, and in practice WDC assessments find their advice is routinely not acted upon.[^107]
- **Have advisory-only DV functions:** In DV cases, WDCs have a referral role – connecting survivors to FPA and police – but no legal power to compel any action. Their effectiveness in this role depends on members' personal relationships with survivors and with local police, rather than on any formal authority.[^108]
- **Face capacity constraints:** Fewer than 40% of WDC members received formal training relevant to their mandate in the two years prior to the 2022 assessment. Members are unpaid volunteers with varying levels of understanding of the laws and services they are meant to navigate.[^109]
- **Face social pressure:** In small island communities, WDC members who are known to have referred a DV case or supported a woman leaving an abusive marriage face community criticism and, in some documented cases, social ostracism. The social cost of activism on gender issues in small communities limits WDC members' ability to fulfil their mandate effectively.[^110]

The WDC gap is particularly significant for DV response in outer atolls, where the WDC may be the only gender-focused institutional presence. A WDC that cannot access the FPA (which requires telecommunications and the woman's willingness to escalate), cannot compel police action, and cannot offer the survivor any safe housing or financial support is a formal structure that may create an appearance of coverage without delivering effective protection.

### 5.10.4 Cabinet and Judiciary Representation

Women's representation in senior executive and judicial positions has been similarly limited. Key documented data points include:

- **Cabinet/ministerial positions:** Women have served as ministers in several portfolios, including Health, Education, and Social Development, across different administrations. Representation has fluctuated between administrations without a consistent upward trend. The Muizzu government formed in November 2023 initially included a small number of women in Cabinet.[^111]
- **Civil service senior grades:** The civil service employs a higher proportion of women than the private sector, but women are concentrated in service delivery roles rather than senior management and policy positions. Women's representation in Grade 7 (senior civil servant) and above is disproportionately low relative to their overall civil service share.[^112]
- **Judiciary:** The Supreme Court has had no female Justice in its history. Lower courts have a small number of female judges, concentrated primarily in the Family Court – a pattern that both reflects and reinforces the social assignment of family matters as a female domain while keeping women excluded from the highest-prestige legal positions.[^113]
- **HRCM Rights Awareness:** The HRCM Rights Side of Life Survey (2019) found that only **23% of Maldivian women** reported being aware of all their civil and political rights – a significant rights literacy gap that affects women's ability to advocate for their own political participation and that of other women.[^114]

---


## 5.55 Women's Political Participation: The Post-Quota Reality


### 5.54.1 Women Councillors: Experience and Challenges

The dramatic increase in women's local council representation – from approximately 4% to 33% following the 2021 elections – produced a generation of women councillors across all atolls and islands. Understanding their experience in the council chambers is essential for assessing whether structural representation translates into substantive policy influence.

**UNDP/MoSFD field documentation (2022)** provides the most systematic available picture of women councillors' experience. Key findings:

**Inclusion in meetings:** A documented pattern exists of newly elected women councillors being marginalised in council proceedings – excluded from agenda-setting, interrupted or dismissed when speaking, and treated as consultants rather than decision-makers with equal authority. This marginalisation is most acute on islands where the pre-existing male-dominated council culture is entrenched and where no women had previously served.

**Policy influence:** Women councillors who have successfully established their authority report that they have raised issues that were previously absent from council agendas: domestic violence prevention, women's health services access, safe public spaces, and girls' educational challenges. These contributions represent the democratic dividend of representative inclusion – different perspectives producing different policy attention.

**Physical and social safety:** Women councillors in some communities report harassment – both in council settings (dismissive treatment, gender-based comments) and in the community (social pressure from community members who oppose their service, in some documented cases). The social cost of sustained political participation as a woman is higher than for men, particularly in more conservative communities.

**Support from national level:** The quota was imposed from the national level without adequate accompanying support infrastructure – mentoring for new councillors, training in local governance procedures, and protection from harassment. The UNDP/MoSFD capacity programme has partly addressed this gap through post-election training, but the support architecture is not yet commensurate with the transformation of local council composition.[^451]

### 5.54.2 The Quota's Institutional Design

The local council gender quota is a **reserved seat** model – a specified minimum number of seats is reserved for women, with the quota applied at the atoll and island level. The design means:
- In each council, at least one-third of seats are filled by the women who receive the highest vote counts among female candidates
- If fewer women than the minimum quota seek election, the quota may not be fully achieved
- The quota does not cap women's representation – women can win more than the minimum one-third if they receive sufficient votes

This design – requiring women to win seats through competitive elections rather than appointment – has the advantage of maintaining democratic legitimacy (elected women have won actual votes) while guaranteeing minimum representation. It differs from the purely proportional list quota model (which allocates seats on party lists) and the appointment model (where a quota is filled by appointment to reserved seats without competitive election).[^452]

**Replicability at parliamentary level:** The success of the reserved seat model at local level is the strongest argument for applying the same model at parliamentary level. The CEDAW Committee's call for temporary special measures at parliamentary level (para. 24-26 of the November 2021 Concluding Observations) is consistent with international evidence that reserved seats produce faster and more durable representation gains than voluntary candidate list quotas.

The political obstacle – that quota opponents in Parliament would need to vote for a quota that directly limits their own pool of potential seats – is the primary barrier. Comparable quota adoptions internationally have required either executive authority (presidential or royal decree) or sustained civil society and development partner pressure over multiple election cycles. The Maldivian trajectory on parliamentary quota will depend on political dynamics that are not currently favourable but could change with the right coalition.[^453]

### 5.54.3 Women in the Civil Service: Representation Without Authority

The civil service employs a high proportion of women – particularly in service delivery roles – but gender gaps in senior civil service representation remain significant. The Civil Service Commission has not published grade-disaggregated gender data for all ministries in any recent publicly available report; the following characterisation is based on sectoral observation and the limited data available in CEDAW reporting:

**Health sector:** Women dominate the nursing and health post workforce; are well-represented in health administration; and are represented at medical officer level but less so at specialist and department head level. The Ministry of Health's own senior management is more gender-balanced than most ministries.

**Education sector:** Women dominate primary school teaching; are near-parity at secondary level; and are underrepresented at school principal and Ministry leadership levels. The feminisation of teaching combined with male-dominated school administration reflects a gendered hierarchy that is common internationally but worth noting in the Maldivian context.

**Finance and economic ministries:** Women are underrepresented in the Ministry of Finance, Ministry of Economic Development, and related policy ministries – the ministries with most fiscal authority and policy impact. Gender inequality in these ministries directly affects the quality of GRB implementation and the gender analysis embedded in the national budget.[^454]

---


## 5.56 Looking Ahead: The 2028 Parliamentary Elections and Gender


### 5.85.1 The Electoral Window

The next People's Majlis elections are scheduled for 2028 – the next major political moment at which women's parliamentary representation could change. The trajectory since the 2008 democratic transition has been consistently poor: 11.7% in 2008, declining to 4.6% by 2024. Without structural intervention, the 2028 elections are unlikely to produce a meaningful improvement.

**Structural options for 2028:**
1. **Reserved seats quota:** Amendment to the Elections Act to reserve a minimum percentage (33%, following the local council model) of parliamentary seats for women, implemented through a parallel ballot or reserved constituency system
2. **Candidate list quota:** Requirement for political parties to nominate at least 30-40% women candidates – weaker than reserved seats; dependent on parties nominating women in winnable seats
3. **Public financing incentive:** Link public campaign financing to parties' achievement of gender-balanced candidate slates – creating financial incentives for parties to nominate women
4. **No structural change:** Reliance on organic improvement through awareness, women's candidacy encouragement, and campaign finance support – historically produced no meaningful improvement

The CEDAW Committee's call for temporary special measures at parliamentary level (Concluding Observations 2021, para. 26) specifically endorses structural options 1 or 2. The political coalition required to pass any of these options through the current Parliament – which is itself only 4.6% female – requires male parliamentarians to vote for changes that alter their constituency pool. The political economy is challenging but not unprecedented: comparable quota adoptions elsewhere have succeeded through a combination of executive support, international pressure, and civil society momentum.

**The window:** The 2026-2027 period – in which pre-election political positioning occurs – is the window for building the coalition and legislative momentum for a parliamentary quota before 2028. Civil society advocacy, development partner support for the policy case (evidence from local council quota success), and engagement with political parties' women's wings are the strategic tools available.

### 5.85.2 The CEDAW Review Cycle and Policy Leverage

The Government's Seventh Periodic Report (2025) will be reviewed by the CEDAW Committee in the 2025-2026 session cycle. The Concluding Observations from that review will provide the next formal international accountability moment for Maldivian gender policy.

**Key issues likely to appear in 2025-2026 Concluding Observations (based on 2021 Concluding Observations and Seventh Report content):**
- Continued concern about parliamentary representation and call for quota
- Progress assessment on AIM Project and GBV response system improvement
- Updated call on abortion law reform
- Progress on childcare infrastructure gap
- FGM/C legal prohibition
- Article 16 reservation withdrawal
- New call for updated national GBV prevalence study
- Trafficking NRM operationalisation

The Maldivian government's response to these anticipated recommendations – whether it commits to parliamentary quota legislation before 2028, whether it announces childcare infrastructure investment, whether it engages meaningfully on Article 16 – will signal the trajectory of gender equality policy through the next political cycle.

---


# Part 9: Marriage, Family Law, and Divorce


## 5.57 Marriage, Family Law, and Divorce


### 5.11.1 The Maldivian Divorce Rate: Scale and Social Consequences

The Maldives' divorce rate is one of the most distinctive features of its social fabric. The country held the **Guinness World Record** for the highest national divorce rate in 2002, at **10.97 per 1,000 population**. Despite sustained family law reform, the rate remains extraordinary: **5.52 per 1,000 in 2024** (Directorate of Judicial Administration), still among the world's highest nationally recorded rates.[^115]

Census 2022 data adds nuance: among currently divorced adults, **women substantially outnumber men** – divorced women exceed divorced men by **42.86%** (Statistics Compendium §17.3.1). This asymmetry reflects the gendered experience of divorce in the Maldivian context: men can remarry more quickly and face fewer social consequences from divorce than women, meaning divorced men are more likely to already be remarried by census time (and thus counted as married rather than divorced), while divorced women remain in that status longer or do not remarry.[^116]

UNDP's 2011 research noted that a "**typical Maldivian woman marries three times by age 30**" – a striking encapsulation of the pattern of serial marriage and divorce that characterises Maldivian family life, particularly for younger women. The Directorate of Judicial Administration (DJA) 2019 statistics found that **58% of female divorcees were aged 26–39**, with combined divorce incidence rates of approximately 70% for the 18–39 age cohort.[^117]

### 5.11.2 Causes of Divorce: Ahmed and Riyaz 2024 Analysis

The most recent systematic analysis of divorce causes in the Maldives is provided by **Ahmed and Riyaz (2024)**, who examined recorded divorce cases to identify the nine most commonly documented causes. These include:

1. **Incompatibility and personality conflicts** – the most commonly cited category, reflecting the difficulty of sustaining marriages contracted under social and family pressure in a context of changing expectations
2. **Infidelity** – documented in both male and female-initiated divorces
3. **Domestic violence** – divorce as an escape mechanism from abusive marriages (the intersection between DV and divorce is significant: many DV survivors access divorce as their primary protection mechanism rather than the DVPA protection order system)
4. **Financial problems and economic stress** – including male unemployment, inadequate maintenance payments, and economic disagreements
5. **Substance abuse (by the male partner)** – widely documented as a trigger for both DV and relationship breakdown
6. **Long-distance separation** – a consequence of the atoll geography, migration for work, and the resort employment model
7. **Family interference** – in-law involvement in marital decision-making
8. **Child-related conflicts** – including disagreements about children from prior marriages
9. **Sexual dissatisfaction** – including within abusive or coercive relationships[^118]

The diversity of causes reflects the multiple stresses on Maldivian marriages: economic pressure, geographic separation, substance abuse, and the intersection of changing gender expectations with persistent social norms. Importantly, **domestic violence as a cause of divorce** (category 3) suggests that for many women, the formal divorce process is the closest available approximation to an escape route from abuse – a pattern consistent with the evidence on DV case attrition documented in §5.5 and the justice pathway analysis.

### 5.11.3 Child Marriage: Data and Dynamics

The Family Act 2000 sets the minimum marriage age at **18 years**. A 2016 amendment removed the previously existing provision allowing under-18 marriages with Family Court approval in exceptional circumstances. Despite this legal change, the DHS 2016-17 found **2.2% of married women had married before age 18** – a figure that, given the small total population, translates to hundreds of girls and that is likely higher in outer atolls where enforcement is more difficult.[^119]

Child marriage in the Maldivian context occurs primarily in circumstances of pregnancy – the social pressure to formalise a premarital pregnancy through marriage is intense in conservative island communities where unmarried pregnancy is highly stigmatised and potentially subject to zina provisions. Girls who are married young typically withdraw from school (as documented in the Education chapter), enter an asymmetric marital relationship with an older partner, and face higher obstetric risks if they become pregnant during physical adolescence.[^120]

The interaction between child marriage and GBV is bidirectional: girls who experience childhood sexual abuse (12.2% nationally, Fulu 2007) are at elevated risk of early marriage as a family response to the abuse or to resultant pregnancy; and girls who marry young enter situations of reduced education, limited economic options, and power asymmetry that increase vulnerability to intimate partner violence.

### 5.11.4 Polygamy: Legal Framework and Practice

Under the Family Act 2000, polygamy is permitted with Family Court approval and subject to conditions including the wife's consent and the husband's demonstrated ability to maintain multiple households equitably. In practice, the court approval requirement has not eliminated polygamy – the Family Court grants a significant number of polygamy approvals annually. CEDAW reporting documents approximately **165 polygamy approvals in 2020 and 157 in 2021** (CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report 2025).[^121]

The CEDAW Committee has repeatedly called for the abolition of polygamy as incompatible with CEDAW Article 16 on equality in marriage. The Government's position is that the Family Court approval requirement provides an adequate safeguard, and that polygamy as a religious right cannot be legislated away. The Committee's November 2021 Concluding Observations reiterated this concern without change in the government's position.[^122]

Women in polygamous marriages face compounded vulnerability. Research on the social consequences of polygamy in the Maldivian context documents: reduced household resources as maintenance obligations are divided; social stigmatisation associated with the status of second or subsequent wife; heightened emotional distress; and, in documented cases, use of the polygamy threat as a form of coercive control within abusive relationships.[^123]

### 5.11.5 Prenuptial Agreements: Near-Total Absence

Despite the frequency of divorce and the predictable economic consequences for women, the use of prenuptial agreements remains extremely rare. Family Court records indicate that **only approximately 8 prenuptial agreements** were registered in the most recent year of available data – a figure that reflects near-total social non-use of an available legal tool.[^124]

The extreme rarity of prenuptial agreements is significant given the documented pattern of divorced women losing access to housing, facing uncertainty about maintenance, and experiencing significant economic harm from divorce. It reflects:
- Social stigma: raising financial terms before marriage is seen as mercenary or expressing distrust
- Religious framing: the *mahr* (dower) payment is understood as the primary financial protection for women in Islamic marriage, reducing perceived need for contractual supplements
- Low awareness: most Maldivian women – including educated women – are not aware of their right to negotiate prenuptial terms
- Limited legal advice access: the Family Law Centre offers some guidance, but prenuptial agreement preparation is not a routine service

The consequence is that most divorcing women face financial negotiations without any prior contractual framework, relying on maintenance orders that are chronically unpaid (**MVR 2 million in unclaimed maintenance** documented in Family Court records) and custody arrangements that favour male authority in practice despite the Family Act's neutral custody principles.[^125]

### 5.11.6 Inheritance Law: Structural Gender Inequality

Under Islamic inheritance law as applied in the Maldives, **daughters receive half the inheritance share of sons**. This provision is derived from the Quran (Sura An-Nisa: 11) and is applied through the Family Court's inheritance division. The Government's CEDAW reservation on Article 16 effectively insulates this provision from CEDAW challenge.[^126]

The practical consequence is that women accumulate less inherited wealth than men across generations, compounding the gender wealth gap from education and labour market sources. For women in outer atolls whose families own agricultural land, fishery rights, or property – the primary forms of wealth available on smaller islands – the half-share inheritance rule means women's property base is structurally smaller than that of their brothers from the same family.[^127]

Women's land and property ownership is **not systematically tracked** by the National Bureau of Statistics – a data gap noted in the Statistics Compendium (§17.3). The absence of data makes it impossible to assess the full extent of the inheritance gap's accumulation over generations or to monitor trends. The CEDAW Committee has called for gender-disaggregated property and land ownership data collection.[^128]

---


## 5.58 Child Custody, Maintenance, and the Post-Divorce Family Law Reality


### 5.22.1 Custody Framework Under the Family Act

The **Family Act 2000** governs child custody following divorce or separation. The Act establishes a framework that is nominally gender-neutral – custody decisions are to be made in the best interests of the child – but in practice incorporates Islamic jurisprudence principles that create a gendered custody trajectory.[^270]

Under the Family Act's custody provisions:
- **Hadhanah** (physical custody, or day-to-day care): awarded to the mother for children under age 7 (boys) or until puberty (girls), as a default reflecting Islamic jurisprudence's traditional assignment of early childhood care to mothers
- **Wilayah** (guardianship, legal decision-making authority): retained by the father regardless of who holds physical custody – covering decisions about education, travel, major health decisions, and other legal matters
- Children above the traditional threshold ages may be assigned to either parent based on best interests analysis
- The court may override the default custody assignment if the mother is found unfit – a determination that, in practice, requires documented evidence of harm to the child[^271]

The hadhanah-wilayah split creates a common practical problem: the mother has physical custody and bears the daily care burden, but the father retains legal authority to make major decisions about the child's life. In an acrimonious post-divorce relationship, this split can be weaponised – a father may refuse to sign documents for a child's passport, school enrolment abroad, or major medical procedure, effectively holding the mother and child hostage to his cooperation without any court-compelled obligation to act in the child's interest.[^272]

### 5.22.2 Custody in Practice: The Court Process

A custody application in the Family Court involves:
1. Filing a custody application (which may be made simultaneously with or separate from a divorce application)
2. Family Court social worker investigation – in theory, including visits to both parents' homes to assess child welfare
3. Judicial hearing with both parents present (or with legal representation)
4. Court order specifying custody arrangement, visitation schedule, and maintenance obligation
5. Enforcement through subsequent court action if the order is not followed

**Access and enforcement problems:**
- The Family Court in Malé has a significant case backlog; custody proceedings can take months to years
- Outer atoll families must either travel to Malé for Family Court proceedings or be served by circuit court arrangements that may not include dedicated family court capacity
- Enforcement of custody orders – particularly visitation arrangements – is the primary source of ongoing post-divorce Family Court litigation, and enforcement mechanisms are weak
- Women who breach visitation orders by preventing a father's court-ordered access face the same weak enforcement that women face when seeking enforcement of maintenance payments – the system is structurally inconsistent in which party's rights it effectively protects[^273]

### 5.22.3 Maintenance: The Gap Between Order and Reality

Child maintenance – financial support from the non-custodial parent – is central to the economic welfare of divorced women and their children. The Family Act provides for maintenance orders to be issued by the Family Court, specifying the amount and payment schedule.[^274]

The practical record of maintenance payment in the Maldives is deeply troubling:
- Family Court records document approximately **MVR 2 million in unpaid, court-ordered maintenance** – representing maintenance that has been legally ordered but not paid
- No automatic enforcement mechanism (such as wage garnishment directly from the payer's employer) exists under current law
- Enforcement of maintenance orders requires the surviving party to file a separate enforcement application with the Family Court – adding another layer of legal process to a system already difficult to navigate
- In small island communities, informal pressure may be more effective than formal legal enforcement for some cases, but informal pressure may also operate against the woman (pressure to accept a smaller amount to avoid conflict)
- Men who leave the Maldives for resort employment – often in locations without fixed addresses or predictable income – become de facto impossible to enforce maintenance orders against, even if technically subject to the order[^275]

The MVR 2 million figure represents documented unpaid maintenance – the visible portion of a problem that is likely substantially larger. Women who give up attempting to enforce maintenance orders, or who never pursue them in the first place due to the system's complexity and their low expectations of success, are not captured in this statistic. The practical consequence for single mothers is reliance on family support, personal labour market earnings (constrained by childcare gaps), or poverty – with the formal maintenance system providing only partial and unreliable compensation for the financial consequences of divorce.

### 5.22.4 The Mahr: Islamic Dower and Its Practical Function

Under Islamic marriage law applied in the Maldives, a **mahr** (dower) is a mandatory gift from the husband to the wife that is agreed at marriage – either paid immediately at the time of marriage (*mahr muajjal*, prompt mahr) or deferred until divorce or the husband's death (*mahr muakhkhar*, deferred mahr). The mahr is the wife's individual property and cannot be used without her consent.[^276]

The mahr functions, in theory, as a form of financial protection for women in the event of divorce – a guaranteed payment that compensates for the economic disruption. In the Maldivian context:
- Mahr amounts are often symbolic rather than substantive – small amounts agreed at the time of marriage that do not reflect the actual economic consequences of divorce
- The deferred mahr is the form most commonly used in the Maldives; it becomes due upon divorce or the husband's death
- Collection of the deferred mahr through the Family Court is a legal right but in practice faces the same enforcement challenges as maintenance – the husband must have assets or income against which the debt can be enforced
- The social norm of setting small mahr amounts – and the social pressure on women not to insist on larger amounts as this might appear mercenary – means the mahr provides less economic protection than its theoretical function would suggest

The Family Legal Clinic's client data provides evidence that mahr disputes are a significant component of post-divorce family court litigation, with women frequently unable to collect deferred mahr even when courts find the obligation valid.[^277]

---


## 5.59 Child Marriage and Adolescent Girl Vulnerability: A Detailed Analysis


### 5.72.1 Child Marriage Dynamics

The 2.2% of married women who were married before age 18 (DHS 2016-17) represents a small but significant population of girls whose educational and developmental trajectories were altered by early marriage. Understanding the dynamics of child marriage in the Maldivian context requires attention to the specific social and legal mechanisms through which it occurs.

**Pregnancy-driven marriage:** The most commonly documented pathway to child marriage in the Maldivian context is pregnancy – when an unmarried girl under 18 becomes pregnant, the social and religious pressure to formalise the relationship through marriage is intense. The pregnancy may result from consensual sexual activity or from coercion/abuse; the marriage solution is applied regardless of the nature of the sexual relationship. The 2016 amendment to the Family Act removed the judicial exception for under-18 marriages – but girls who become pregnant under 18 still face intense family pressure to either marry (now impossible for under-18s to register) or to manage the stigma of unmarried pregnancy, which may include informal cohabitation that provides social cover without legal protection.

**The 2016 amendment's consequences:** By removing the judicial exception without simultaneously addressing the social dynamics that produced child marriage, the 2016 amendment may have shifted child marriage from registered (and therefore statistically visible) to informal cohabitation (and therefore statistically invisible). Girls who are effectively in child marriages without legal registration have no access to Family Court protections – no maintenance orders, no formal custody rights – if the relationship breaks down.[^491]

### 5.72.2 Early Marriage and Educational Consequences

A girl who marries at 16 or 17 – even if the marriage is not registered – typically exits the education system at the point of marriage. The intersection of marriage and educational dropout is documented in the Education chapter (§10.X) and represents a specific form of gendered educational harm: girls who marry young are disproportionately the ones who do not complete secondary school and do not access higher education, perpetuating the educational disparity between outer atoll young women and their urban peers.[^492]

**The re-entry gap:** Unlike some countries that have legal and institutional provisions for pregnant students and recently married girls to continue or re-enter education, the Maldives does not have a systematic re-entry mechanism. A girl who leaves school for marriage – whether at 16 or 20 – faces no supported pathway back. This makes the educational cost of early marriage effectively permanent.[^493]

### 5.72.3 Adolescent Girls and Sexual Violence

As documented throughout this chapter, adolescent girls are at elevated risk of sexual violence in the Maldivian context:
- **12.2% nationally** reported childhood sexual abuse (Fulu 2007)
- The age cohort 5-15 is the period of highest documented sexual abuse incidence in international research and consistent with Maldivian field evidence
- **Family-based sexual abuse** (by fathers, brothers, uncles, family friends) is the most commonly documented category in many jurisdictions – and is the most difficult to disclose given that the home is the site of the abuse and the abuser is the family member on whom the child is dependent

The interaction between childhood sexual abuse, early pregnancy, and the child marriage dynamics above creates a cycle in which a girl who is abused by a family member may become pregnant, may be pressured into marriage (with the abuser or with another man to conceal the origin of the pregnancy), and exits the education system without the support or information to understand what happened to her as abuse or to access accountability.

The CRC Periodic Report Draft (Part XVIII-B) and Child Protection chapter (Part IV) address this in detail from the child protection perspective; the gender dimension is the disproportionate impact on girls and the way gender norms (female sexual purity, family honour) compound both the abuse and the non-disclosure.[^494]

---


## 5.60 Child Sexual Exploitation and Trafficking: Gender Dimensions


### 5.113.1 Commercial Sexual Exploitation of Children

Commercial sexual exploitation of children (CSEC) is criminalised under the Sexual Offences Act (2014) as amended. The documented scale:

- **291 incidents of child commercial sexual exploitation** investigated by MPS in one reporting year
- The true prevalence is believed significantly higher given documented barriers to reporting
- The tourism industry creates demand for commercial sexual services with documented risk of child sexual exploitation
- Online platforms have become a mechanism for solicitation and exploitation

The gender dimension of CSEC: while both boys and girls are vulnerable, the demand-side structure of commercial sexual exploitation in the tourism context is gendered – demand is predominantly from male clients for female children. Documentation of specific gender distribution within CSEC cases is limited by data system gaps.[^570]

**Service gap:** Specialist trauma-informed services for child CSEC survivors are available primarily in Malé through a combination of CFPS counselling, IGMH psychiatric support, and NGO services. Children in outer islands who have experienced CSEC have very limited access to specialist support.
