A **National Child Sexual Abuse Response Protocol** is being developed by MoSFD with UNICEF support, which will establish clear multi-agency procedures for identification, immediate support, criminal investigation, and longer-term survivor services.

### 5.113.2 Child Trafficking and Gender

MoGFSS data identified **21 suspected child trafficking cases** referred to or identified by the Ministry in the reporting period, of which a significant proportion did not result in formal investigation.

The documented vulnerability pathways with gender dimensions:
- Domestic workers (many migrant) at risk of labour exploitation and trafficking – this pathway disproportionately affects women and girls who enter domestic service arrangements that can become exploitative
- Tourism industry creating demand for commercial sexual services with documented risk of child sexual exploitation – again gender-differentiated by demand structure
- Children accompanying trafficked parents in situations of labour exploitation – in practice, children accompanying mothers in exploitative domestic service situations

The **Fiyavathi Rehabilitation Centre** serves as the rehabilitation facility for female juvenile offenders but also houses girls in need of protective care "who cannot be managed in community settings." This dual function – combining offenders and non-offending vulnerable girls in the same facility – raises fundamental child rights concerns. Girls who have been trafficked or commercially exploited may be placed in a facility designed for juvenile offenders, experiencing a system response that conflates victim status with offender status.[^571]

### 5.113.3 Child Marriage: Legal Framework and Gaps

Prior to 2016, there was no statutory minimum age of marriage in the Maldives. The Family Act (Law No. 4/2000) permitted marriage from puberty with the consent of a *wali* and Family Court registration, effectively allowing marriage of girls as young as 12 or 13.

The **Family Law Regulation (2016)** established 18 years as the minimum age of marriage. However, the regulation retained **a judicial exception allowing marriage below 18 with court approval in circumstances deemed appropriate**. This exception has been widely criticised by the HRCM, civil society, and international treaty bodies as undermining the minimum age standard in practice.

The Child Rights Protection Act (2019) reinforces 18 as the minimum age but does not explicitly eliminate the judicial exception in the Family Law framework. Data from civil registration records indicates that some marriages of persons under 18 continue to be registered each year, though numbers have declined since 2016.

The DHS 2016-17 documented a 2.2% prevalence of child marriage (marriage before age 18). The gender dimension: child marriage is overwhelmingly a practice affecting girls, not boys – the risk of "marriage before 18" refers almost entirely to girls married to adult men, often with family-arranged or family-pressured consent.[^572]

---


# Part 10: Gender, Religion, Culture, and Social Norms


## 5.61 Gender, Religion, and Islam: The Normative Terrain


### 5.23.1 Islam as Both Constraint and Resource

Any substantive analysis of gender policy in the Maldives must engage with the role of Islam – not as a monolithic force for gender inequality, but as a complex normative terrain within which both constraints on and resources for women's equality coexist. Islamic jurisprudence as applied in the Maldives reflects specific historical interpretive traditions (predominantly the Shafi'i school of Sunni Islam) that have shaped family law, social norms, and state legitimacy – but the Islamic tradition contains substantial internal diversity on questions of gender that is rarely surfaced in public discourse.

The **2008 Constitution** establishes Islam as the state religion and makes Islamic jurisprudence a source of law. The constitution's rights provisions – including non-discrimination and equality – are qualified by consistency with Islam, meaning that rights claims must navigate the Islamic framework rather than superseding it. This constitutional structure has been central to debates about gender equality reform: advocates have had to argue within an Islamic framework, and reform proposals inconsistent with dominant interpretations of Islamic law face not just political opposition but constitutional challenge.[^278]

### 5.23.2 The Conservative Turn: 2008 to Present

Maldivian society has undergone a documented increase in religious conservatism since the democratic transition of 2008. The political competition of the first multi-party period included significant contestation over the country's Islamic identity, with opposition parties and religious groups arguing that the reform era was secularising the Maldives. The result was a pattern in which political parties – including those nominally committed to gender equality – were reluctant to advocate positions that could be characterised as un-Islamic, particularly on women's rights issues.[^279]

Visible manifestations of the conservative turn include:
- **Increased hijab wearing:** Survey data and observation document a substantial increase in hijab adoption among Maldivian women, particularly younger women, since 2010. The social environment in many communities has shifted from hijab as a minority practice to hijab as the social expectation, with unveiled women increasingly experiencing social pressure.
- **Niqab adoption:** A smaller but visible and growing proportion of Maldivian women wear the niqab (face covering). This practice was historically extremely rare in the Maldives and represents adoption of Gulf-influenced Islamic practice that is not part of traditional Maldivian religious culture.
- **Restrictions on mixed-gender social spaces:** Some communities have attempted to enforce gender segregation in social spaces – beaches, cafes, social gatherings – that were historically mixed-gender.
- **Opposition to gender equality legislation:** The GEA (2016) faced parliamentary opposition on the grounds that it was inconsistent with Islamic principles. The opposition was overcome, but the controversy illustrates the political reality in which gender equality legislation must operate.[^280]

The conservative turn is not uniform across the population. Urban, educated, and younger women hold diverse views on the relationship between Islam and gender equality – including a significant strand of Islamic feminist thought that argues for gender equality within an Islamic framework. The public space for this discourse has, however, been constrained by the political dynamics that treat any gender equality advocacy as potentially un-Islamic.

### 5.23.3 Islamic Feminist Perspectives

Within the Maldivian intellectual and civil society space, a strand of Islamic feminism – arguing for women's rights within and from Islamic tradition rather than against it – has developed, though with limited public visibility compared to more conservative religious voices. Key arguments in this tradition as applied to the Maldivian context include:

**On DV:** Islamic jurisprudence explicitly prohibits harm (*darar*) to spouses. The Prophet Muhammad's documented condemnation of wife-beating and his instruction that "the best of you are those who are best to their wives" provides a Quranic and Hadith basis for condemning domestic violence that should be more prominent in religious discourse. Islamic feminist advocates have argued that the DVPA and GBV laws are consistent with Islamic principles of protecting people from harm – not in conflict with them.[^281]

**On women's testimony:** The Quranic verse (2:282) specifying two female witnesses as equivalent to one male witness in certain contractual contexts – often invoked to justify restrictions on women's testimony in GBV cases – is subject to scholarly debate about its contextual scope. Many Islamic scholars, including prominent scholars in the Maliki and Shafi'i traditions, argue that this provision is limited to its specific original context (financial contracts) and does not extend to other legal matters including criminal evidence. This interpretation, which supports full gender equality in testimony, has not become dominant in Maldivian Islamic discourse but is legally and theologically credible.[^282]

**On polygamy:** Islamic jurisprudence's permission of polygamy (limited to four wives with conditions of equal treatment) is subject to significant scholarly debate about the conditions under which it is permissible. The Quranic verse (4:3) specifying that husbands must treat wives equally – and the subsequent verse (4:129) acknowledging that equal treatment between wives is impossible – has been interpreted by scholars including Jamal Badawi, Zainah Anwar, and the Musawah network as fundamentally restricting the conditions for permissible polygamy to near-impossibility in practice. This interpretation supports legislative restriction of polygamy within an Islamic framework.[^283]

**On inheritance:** The inheritance half-share provision is one of the most robustly grounded in Quranic text (4:11). Islamic feminist engagement with this provision typically acknowledges the Quranic basis while arguing for contextual interpretation – noting that the inheritance provision historically complemented the obligation of male family members to financially support female relatives, and that in a context where women are major breadwinners and financial contributors, the application of the half-share without the corresponding full male maintenance obligation creates an unjust outcome that cannot have been the Quran's intent. Morocco's (limited) inheritance reforms and Tunisia's (contested) attempts represent the regional boundary of this debate.[^284]

### 5.23.4 Ministry of Islamic Affairs and Religious Discourse on Gender

The **Ministry of Islamic Affairs (MIA)** is constitutionally responsible for Islamic affairs governance, including the regulation of mosques, the licensing of religious scholars, and the production of official Islamic guidance. The MIA has not been a consistent voice for gender equality; in several documented instances, MIA statements or associated religious scholars have taken public positions that reinforce conservative gender norms – including statements opposing women's leadership in some contexts and reinforcing traditional family role expectations.

The **Supreme Council for Islamic Affairs** – the government's highest advisory body on Islamic matters – has taken a conservative position on the specific question of CEDAW's Article 16 reservation, consistently supporting retention of the reservation as necessary to protect Islamic family law. This institutional position has been a significant political constraint on legislative reform of family law.[^285]

The relationship between the gender equality policy agenda and the religious institutional environment is one of the most delicate and consequential features of Maldivian social policy. Successful reform in comparable Muslim-majority contexts (Morocco, Indonesia, Tunisia) has typically required strategic engagement with religious authorities – identifying Islamic legal justifications for reform, securing fatwas or religious opinions from credible scholars supporting specific changes – rather than secular imposition. The Maldivian gender equality movement has made limited inroads into this strategic religious engagement, which remains one of the most important untapped levers for advancing reform.[^286]

---


## 5.62 Religious Institutions and Gender: The Mosque Network


### 5.74.1 The Mosque as a Gender Policy Actor

The mosque network – approximately one mosque per inhabited island, with larger islands having multiple mosques – is the most pervasive institutional presence in Maldivian society. Friday sermons, religious education, and the social authority of Imams shape community norms in ways that no government agency or NGO can match in outer island communities. Understanding the mosque's role in gender policy is essential for understanding both the barriers to and the opportunities for norm change.

**Imams and DV:** The Friday sermon (*khutbah*) is the primary vehicle for normative communication at community level. Imams who include content on family violence – whether framing it as acceptable (through misapplication of Quranic verses on marital authority) or unacceptable (through the prophetic tradition on treating women well) – have direct norm influence that community awareness campaigns cannot replicate.

The Ministry of Islamic Affairs provides guidelines and content support for Friday sermons. Including DV prevention content – framed within an Islamic rights-of-wives framework – in the national sermon guidance system would reach every inhabited island through the Imam network at a scale that no programme can match. This has not been systematically attempted as of 2024-25.[^498]

**Religious education and gender norms:** The *dhars* (religious education sessions) held in mosques and *edhuruge* (religious schools) are the primary contexts for religious learning, particularly in outer islands where formal schooling may not include substantial Islamic studies. The content of this religious education – including any content on gender relations, family authority, and women's status – shapes the normative framework that community members bring to their family lives.

**Progressive Islamic scholarship:** A minority but growing strand of progressive Islamic scholarship is beginning to reach Maldivian audiences through digital platforms – YouTube lectures, social media content, and WhatsApp-distributed audio – from scholars like Sheikh Khalid Abou El Fadl, Amina Wadud, and Khaled Abou El Fadl who engage Islamic jurisprudence on gender equality from within the tradition. This scholarly content reaches primarily the educated urban audience rather than outer island communities; but it is beginning to create a counter-discourse that challenges the conservative gender norms dominant in mainstream Maldivian Islamic discourse.[^499]

### 5.74.2 The Raabithaa (Youth Religious Groups)

Youth-focused Islamic organisations – particularly the *Adhaalath Party*-affiliated religious youth network and mosque-based youth religious groups – are influential in shaping young Maldivian men's religious identity and, through it, their understanding of gender relations. These organisations are typically male-dominated in leadership; their gender discourse tends toward conservative framing of male authority and female domesticity.

Engaging these organisations as potential gender equality allies – through presenting Islamic jurisprudential evidence for women's rights and human dignity – is a harder but potentially higher-impact strategy than engaging exclusively through secular civil society channels. The experience of Indonesian Islamic organisations (particularly Fatayat NU's gender equality work within a mass Muslim youth organisation) provides a relevant model.[^500]

---


## 5.63 Social Norms, Media, and the Cultural Architecture of Gender Inequality


### 5.24.1 The Role of Social Norms

Social norms – the shared expectations within a community about appropriate behaviour – are the cultural architecture within which laws operate, and frequently determine whether legal rights are exercised in practice. In the Maldivian context, a set of deeply embedded social norms shapes gender equality outcomes in ways that no legislative reform alone can fully address:

**Norm 1: DV is a private family matter.** Despite 12 years of the DVPA and sustained public awareness campaigns, the dominant norm in many communities continues to frame domestic violence as a private matter between husband and wife to be resolved within the family, not disclosed to the state. This norm is reinforced by community leaders (including some religious authorities), by family pressure on survivors to keep abuse private, and by survivors' own internalisation of the expectation that "washing dirty linen in public" is shameful. The norm means that the vast majority of DV incidents are never reported – with all the downstream consequences for accountability that are documented in the justice pathway analysis.[^287]

**Norm 2: Women's behaviour is responsible for violence.** Victim-blaming norms – suggesting that women who are abused brought the violence upon themselves through their own failure to comply with expectations – are documented across all the major Maldivian surveys on DV attitudes. The HRCM Rights Side of Life Survey (2019) found that significant proportions of both men and women attribute some responsibility for domestic violence to the victim's behaviour. This norm both discourages disclosure and shapes police and court responses – officers or judges who hold victim-blaming beliefs are less likely to take DV seriously as a legal matter.[^288]

**Norm 3: Divorce is shameful for women.** Despite the Maldives' world-record divorce rate, the social consequences of divorce fall more heavily on women than on men. Divorced women are frequently stigmatised, particularly in outer island communities; divorced women who have not remarried are viewed with suspicion; and women who initiate divorce (even from abusive relationships) may be characterised as defiant or selfish. This norm operates as a barrier to exit: even when a woman recognises that leaving is necessary for her safety, the social costs of doing so – stigma, family disapproval, loss of social standing – are a real disincentive.[^289]

**Norm 4: Women's proper role is domestic.** Despite women's educational majority at tertiary level, the dominant social norm continues to position women's primary role as wife and mother, with employment as acceptable but secondary. This norm is reinforced through family expectations, school career guidance (as documented in §5.13), media representations, and religious discourse. It operates as a structural constraint on women's economic participation: women who prioritise career over family face social criticism; and employers who hold these norms are less likely to offer women advancement.[^290]

**Norm 5: Women's bodies are subject to community regulation.** Dress codes, mobility restrictions, and social supervision of women's public behaviour are documented as significant constraints on women's freedom in many outer island communities. Women who are seen as dressing immodestly, moving freely in public spaces, or engaging in social activities without appropriate supervision are subject to community censure. These norms limit women's economic participation (restricting access to outdoor employment, fitness, and public space), social engagement, and political participation.[^291]

### 5.24.2 Media Representation and Gender

Maldivian media – including television, radio, and the rapidly growing digital media sector – plays a significant role in both reflecting and shaping social norms on gender. Key dimensions of the media-gender relationship include:

**Television and entertainment media:** Maldivian television drama, which is highly popular, typically represents women primarily in domestic roles – as wives, mothers, and daughters dealing with family conflicts. Working women are sometimes represented but typically within feminised occupations (teachers, nurses, office workers). Storylines exploring DV have appeared in Maldivian drama but tend toward narrative resolution through reconciliation rather than survivor empowerment or state accountability – reinforcing the private-matter norm.[^292]

**News media coverage of GBV:** Media coverage of DV and sexual violence cases is governed by complex tensions between public interest, victim privacy, and the Maldives' small-community social environment where naming victims in sexual violence cases could effectively destroy their social lives. The result is that most GBV cases receive no media coverage at all, limiting the visibility of the problem and reducing public pressure for systemic accountability. High-profile cases that do receive coverage tend to be atypical – involving unusual circumstances or high-profile perpetrators – rather than the typical DV case.[^293]

**Social media and digital discourse:** Social media – particularly Facebook (widely used among Maldivians) and Instagram – has become a significant terrain for gender discourse, both progressive and conservative. Women's rights advocates and survivors have used social media platforms to raise awareness, share experiences, and create solidarity that is not possible through formal channels in a small-community context. Simultaneously, social media has become a vector for online harassment and gender-based abuse of women (particularly public figures), and for the spread of conservative religious content that reinforces gender hierarchy norms.[^294]

**The #MeToo moment in the Maldives:** A Maldivian iteration of the #MeToo movement emerged on social media approximately 2020-21, with women using anonymous and named accounts to share experiences of sexual harassment and assault – including in professional and educational settings. The movement created significant public discourse but did not result in any formal accountability proceedings, illustrating both the potential and the limitations of social media-based gender rights advocacy in the Maldivian context.[^295]

### 5.24.3 Attitudes Surveys: What Maldivians Believe About Gender

Systematic attitude survey data on gender norms in the Maldives is limited but includes several important data points:

**HRCM Rights Side of Life Survey (2019):** The survey found that:
- **23% of women** knew all their civil and political rights – meaning 77% were unaware of the full scope of their legal rights
- Significant proportions of both men and women held attitudes justifying DV under some circumstances (specific percentages not reported in available sources but documented as significant)
- Younger and more educated respondents showed more gender-equitable attitudes, suggesting generational change over time but not yet change adequate to drive structural norm shifts[^296]

**DHS 2016-17 DV attitudes module:** The DHS includes a standard question set on justification of wife-beating. For the Maldives, the DHS found non-trivial proportions of women – and presumably higher proportions of men – justifying wife-beating in specified circumstances (e.g., wife goes out without telling husband, wife burns food, wife refuses sex). The normalisation of attitude toward DV reflected in these responses is consistent with the disclosure barriers documented in the GBV data.[^297]

**AIM Project community surveys (2022):** The AIM Project baseline included community attitude surveys as part of its assessment. The surveys found that awareness of DVPA provisions was low – many respondents were unaware that DV was covered by specific legislation – and that attitudes toward DV as a private matter remained prevalent. Community awareness campaigns are a core component of AIM's intervention strategy in response to these findings.[^298]

---


## 5.64 Journalism and Gender Reporting


### 5.78.1 Media Coverage of GBV

The quality of media coverage of GBV – how violence against women is reported, framed, and discussed in the news media – is a significant influence on public awareness, survivor willingness to disclose, and political pressure for systemic reform. In the Maldivian media context:

**Sensational vs. structural reporting:** The available evidence (MDN media monitoring; FPA observations) suggests that Maldivian media coverage of GBV, when it occurs, tends toward sensational reporting of specific high-profile cases rather than structural analysis of the system's failures. A rape case that results in acquittal is covered as a news event without analysis of why the acquittal occurred or what evidentiary failures produced it. A DV death is covered as a tragedy without context on the justice pathway attrition that preceded it.

**Survivor identification risk:** In a small, closely networked society, any media identification of a DV survivor – even without a full name – may effectively identify her to her community. The consequent self-censorship by survivors who might otherwise speak publicly, and the media caution about covering GBV cases, results in very limited authentic survivor voice in the public media discourse.

**Privacy law tensions:** Maldivian privacy law creates tensions for GBV journalism. Reporting on court proceedings – which are public – may be restricted by provisions that protect individuals' privacy in sensitive cases. The balance between accountability journalism (reporting on the justice system's performance in GBV cases) and survivor privacy is a live editorial challenge.[^505]

### 5.78.2 Social Media as Counter-Public

As documented in §5.24, social media has emerged as the primary counter-public space for gender discourse that is not adequately served by mainstream media:

- Anonymous and named accounts sharing DV survivor experiences create peer validation and awareness that the formal media cannot or will not provide
- Feminist commentary on gender policy debates – often from younger, educated women – provides analysis missing from mainstream news
- Coordinated advocacy campaigns (petition drives, awareness hashtags) mobilise public opinion on specific policy issues

The limitations of social media as a counter-public include: the same community-network surveillance that constrains offline disclosure can operate through social media (a survivor sharing her story anonymously may be identified through the details of her account); online harassment of women who speak publicly about gender issues is a documented deterrent to public voice; and the social media discourse is not reliably connected to the formal policy process in ways that translate engagement into institutional change.[^506]

---


## 5.65 Honour, Shame, and the Cultural Architecture of GBV Silence


### 5.38.1 Shame as a Social Control Mechanism

In the Maldivian social context, as in many South Asian and Islamic cultural contexts, **shame (sharm)** – and its corollary concept of **honour (izzath)** – functions as a powerful social control mechanism that operates specifically and disproportionately through women's bodies, behaviour, and relationships. The honour-shame dynamic is not an abstract cultural observation; it has direct and documented consequences for GBV disclosure, help-seeking, and access to justice.

The core logic of the honour-shame system in the Maldivian context is: **family honour is substantially located in women's sexual behaviour and marital status**. A woman who is known to have been sexually assaulted brings shame on her family; a woman who is known to have been in an abusive marriage brings shame by publicising the failure of the marriage; a woman who leaves an abusive husband is shamed for breaking up the family. In each case, the social consequence of the violence – or of seeking help for the violence – falls on the woman and her family, not on the perpetrator.[^394]

### 5.38.2 The Practical Consequences

The honour-shame framework has specific documented consequences:

**Non-disclosure:** Women who experience sexual violence frequently do not disclose – not only because of the formal legal barriers (zina risk) but because disclosure means the family knowing, which means shame. The family may respond to the disclosure by pressuring the woman to be silent, to refuse medical care, or to "resolve" the matter within the family (in cases of family-based sexual abuse) rather than involving external authorities.

**Opposition to leaving abusive marriages:** Family members who know about an abusive marriage may advise or pressure the woman to stay – not out of cruelty, but out of genuine belief that divorce is worse than the abuse (in terms of the woman's social future), and that the family's social standing will suffer if the marriage fails. The advice to "tolerate" (*yemey*) abuse is documented in the Maldivian DV research literature as a common family response to disclosure.

**Post-disclosure social consequences:** Women who do report violence – to police, FPA, or courts – may experience community backlash: being labelled as troublemakers who "destroyed a family," being blamed for the violence, or being excluded from social networks. In small island communities where social relationships are the primary social security mechanism, exclusion has real material consequences beyond the emotional harm.

**Perpetrator protection:** The honour logic extends to protecting perpetrators from outside judgment – a family that has a violent member does not want this known outside the family, creating pressure from the perpetrator's own family on the survivor to withdraw complaints, drop protection orders, and reconcile. The perpetrator's family may have much more social capital in the island community than the survivor, making their pressure effective.[^395]

### 5.38.3 Religious Framing of Shame

The honour-shame system is frequently articulated in religious language – framing disclosure of marital problems as inappropriate, as exposing one's husband's faults (which some religious advisors argue is discouraged in Islam), or as failure to seek intra-family reconciliation before external intervention. This religious framing makes the social pressure to silence harder to challenge: a woman who insists on reporting DV despite family pressure must confront both the social consequences and the religious framing that positions her as acting against Islamic norms.

The counter-narrative – that Islam explicitly prohibits harm to spouses and family members, that seeking protection from harm is consistent with Islamic principles, and that state intervention in domestic violence is comparable to any other state intervention to prevent harm – has been articulated by progressive Islamic scholars and by the FPA in its awareness campaigns. But this counter-narrative has not displaced the dominant shame-silence narrative in many communities, particularly in more conservative outer atolls.[^396]

### 5.38.4 Implications for Intervention Design

The honour-shame framing has direct implications for GBV intervention design:

- **Awareness campaigns** that frame DV as shameful for the perpetrator (rather than for the victim) work with the honour framework rather than against it: if the community shame attached to DV shifts from the survivor to the perpetrator, disclosure becomes less costly for survivors and more costly for perpetrators
- **Community leader engagement** – particularly religious leaders who can reframe Islamic guidance on family violence – is essential for norm change in communities where religious authority carries the most weight
- **Survivor support networks** – either within trusted family circles or through survivor peer support groups – can provide the social support that family withdrawal creates when a woman discloses and seeks help
- **Legal aid provision** that is confidential and does not require the survivor to navigate public court processes alone reduces the public exposure component of shame[^397]

---


## 5.66 Men, Masculinity, and Gender Equality in the Maldives


### 5.37.1 The Role of Men in GBV

Any comprehensive gender policy analysis must address the role of men – both as the primary perpetrators of GBV and as potential allies in prevention. The dominant framings of GBV policy in the Maldives – and internationally – focus primarily on women as survivors and service recipients, which is appropriate given that the immediate need is survivor protection. But prevention requires addressing the behaviours and beliefs of perpetrators and potential perpetrators, which means engaging men directly.

**Perpetrator profile:** Maldivian DV research does not provide a systematic profile of perpetrators in terms of demographic characteristics, socioeconomic background, or other variables. International research consistently finds that DV cuts across income, education, and social status, with no single "perpetrator profile" – meaning prevention strategies must address broad social norms rather than targeting a specific demographic group. Maldivian data that is available points to associations between DV perpetration and: male substance abuse (documented as a DV trigger in case records), young age of marriage (power asymmetry in younger marriages), economic stress (as a proximate trigger), and childhood exposure to DV (the intergenerational cycle documented internationally).[^388]

**Harmful masculinity norms:** Research on masculinity in Maldivian society is limited but consistent with regional patterns. Key harmful masculinity norms documented include:
- Expectation that men should be the primary financial provider – creating shame and stress when this role is threatened by unemployment or economic crisis
- Association of male authority in the household with legitimate control over wives' behaviour – creating a cultural framework in which controlling behaviour toward partners is normalised
- Social permission for men to engage in substance use, infidelity, and some degree of relationship volatility as normal male behaviour
- Stigma against men who seek help for relationship problems, mental health difficulties, or the consequences of their own childhood trauma[^389]

### 5.37.2 Perpetrator Intervention Programmes

The Maldivian GBV response system as of 2024-25 does not include systematic **perpetrator intervention programmes** (PIPs) – structured programmes designed to change the behaviour and attitudes of men who have perpetrated DV. PIPs are a recognised component of comprehensive DV response in many jurisdictions, operating on the premise that criminal sanctions alone are insufficient and that sustained behaviour change requires therapeutic and psychoeducational intervention.[^390]

The absence of PIPs in the Maldivian system means that perpetrators who are prosecuted (the 3% who reach prosecution) and convicted face criminal sanctions without systematic follow-up intervention; perpetrators who are not prosecuted (the overwhelming majority) receive no intervention at all. The FPA's counselling services are survivor-focused; perpetrators are not a client group.

**Arguments for and against PIPs in the Maldivian context:**
- **For:** Long-term DV reduction requires changing perpetrator behaviour, not only protecting survivors; PIPs provide a structured intervention that criminal justice does not
- **Against:** PIPs can be misused to rehabilitate perpetrators prematurely, reducing the urgency of criminal accountability; in a resource-constrained system, investment in perpetrators may divert from survivor services; cultural context of couple counselling (which PIPs approach from a perpetrator angle) is sensitive in the Maldivian Islamic framework

The AIM Project has included community-level male engagement as a prevention component – engaging men through community education and male champion programmes rather than clinical perpetrator intervention. This lighter-touch approach is less evidence-based as a behaviour change intervention for men already perpetrating violence, but is appropriate as a primary prevention strategy.[^391]

### 5.37.3 Men as Gender Equality Allies

A growing body of international evidence demonstrates that men's engagement as allies – rather than merely as objects of behaviour change – is essential for sustainable gender equality progress. Male allies in the Maldivian context can play roles including:

- **Political champions:** Male parliamentarians and ministers who publicly advocate for gender equality legislation (DVPA, GEA, potential parliamentary quota) provide legitimacy that cannot be provided by women alone in a political context where religious concerns about women's leadership circulate
- **Community norm setters:** Men in community leadership positions – island mayors, mosque leaders, community elders – who model gender-equitable attitudes and call out DV-enabling norms create community permission for norm change
- **Workplace leaders:** Male employers and managers who implement equal pay, harassment complaint mechanisms, and maternity protection demonstrate that gender equality is compatible with business success
- **Family-level allies:** Fathers, brothers, and husbands who support women's education, employment, and exit from abusive relationships provide the family-level social support that enables individual women to make changes that collective norm change has not yet made automatic[^392]

The AIM Project's male engagement component, the MDN's men's rights literacy programming, and the FPA's community awareness campaigns all include male audience targeting. The evidence on what works in male engagement for DV prevention specifically – from international programmes like Promundo's Programme H, Sonke Gender Justice (South Africa), and Caring Dads (Australia) – has not been systematically adapted for the Maldivian cultural context, representing an opportunity for evidence-based programming design.[^393]

---


## 5.67 The Intergenerational Transmission of Gender Inequality


### 5.83.1 How Inequality Reproduces Across Generations

One of the most important – and most difficult to address – dimensions of gender inequality is its intergenerational transmission: the ways in which inequality in one generation creates conditions that reproduce inequality in the next. Understanding these transmission mechanisms is essential for designing interventions that break cycles rather than merely treating symptoms.

**DV intergenerational cycle:** Children who grow up in households with domestic violence have elevated risk of both experiencing DV as victims (girls) and perpetrating DV as adults (boys). The mechanism is both psychological (normalisation of violence as a conflict resolution tool) and biological (childhood trauma affecting stress response systems). The Fulu 2007 study documented exposure to DV in childhood as a significant risk factor for adult DV in the Maldivian context.[^514]

**Educational transmission:** A mother's educational level is one of the strongest predictors of her children's educational outcomes across all societies. In the Maldivian context, this creates a positive feedback loop from the expansion of girls' education in the 1970s-1990s to higher educational aspirations and outcomes for their daughters – and a potential negative feedback loop where women who leave school early due to early marriage or pregnancy have daughters who face similar pressures.

**Economic transmission:** A mother's economic position – her income, her property ownership, her financial independence – affects her children's material circumstances, educational opportunities, and exposure to poverty. Women in poverty raise children in poverty; breaking the economic gender gap therefore has intergenerational multiplier effects.

**Norm transmission:** Gender norms – what it means to be a man or woman, what behaviour is acceptable in relationships – are primarily transmitted through families. Parents who model equitable relationships transmit those norms to children; parents who model unequal or violent relationships transmit those norms. Social norm change programming that reaches parents therefore has potential intergenerational impact.

### 5.83.2 Targeting Intergenerational Intervention Points

The most effective intergenerational interventions are those that simultaneously address multiple transmission pathways. In the Maldivian context:

**Early childhood development:** High-quality early childhood education and development programmes for children from DV-affected households provide protective factors against the normalisation of violence. They also provide a neutral, supported environment for young children during the period when they are most vulnerable to trauma from home circumstances. The current ECD infrastructure in the Maldives is extremely limited.

**School-based norm change:** The school system, if it incorporated comprehensive gender equality and consent education (as the CEDAW Committee has recommended), would be the largest-scale intergenerational norm change vehicle available. Reaching the entire school-age cohort with healthy relationships content would shift the normative baseline for the next adult generation.

**Economic empowerment of mothers:** Economic support to mothers – through childcare that enables employment, through maintenance enforcement that provides income, through vocational training that creates skills – has documented intergenerational effects on children's outcomes.[^515]

---


# Part 11: Women's Mental Health and Education


## 5.68 Women's Mental Health


### 5.12.1 Gender Patterns in Mental Health: Depression and Self-Harm

Mental health data from the Maldives reveals striking gender differentials that underscore the psychological costs of gender inequality, GBV victimisation, and social constraint. The **WHO STEP Survey 2021** (cited in the Mental Health chapter, §9.X) found that:

- **33.5% of women** reported depression symptoms, compared with **9.6% of men** – a gender ratio of more than 3:1[^129]
- Young women aged 15–24: **16.2% reported moderate to severe depression** – the highest rate of any age-gender subgroup[^130]

The MoSFD's 2024 administrative data on self-harm presentations adds a further dimension:
- **211 documented self-harm cases** in 2024[^131]
- **74% of self-harm cases were female**[^132]

This pattern – women substantially more likely to internalise psychological distress through depression and self-harm, while men are more likely to externalise through substance abuse – is consistent with international mental health literature and has direct implications for the design of crisis services. Services oriented around visible, externalised crisis presentations (violence, intoxication) systematically underserve the hidden crisis of women's internalised distress.[^133]

### 5.12.2 GBV as a Mental Health Driver

The intersection of GBV victimisation and mental health is strongly documented internationally and is consistent with Maldivian field evidence. GBV survivors experience elevated rates of:
- **Post-traumatic stress disorder (PTSD):** Maldivian research and international evidence suggest a significant proportion of DV and sexual violence survivors meet PTSD diagnostic criteria, though national epidemiological data is not available[^134]
- **Depression and anxiety disorders:** Documented in Maldivian clinical settings by the FPA caseworker network and Health Services through screening protocols embedded in the 2024 National GBV Healthcare Guidelines[^135]
- **Suicidal ideation and self-harm:** The self-harm gender gap (74% female) is consistent with elevated rates among GBV survivors documented internationally[^136]
- **Somatic presentations:** GBV-related psychological distress often presents at health facilities through physical complaints – unexplained pain, gastrointestinal symptoms, headache – and healthcare providers who are not trained in GBV identification may treat the somatic symptom without addressing the underlying cause[^137]

UNFPA's National Guidelines for Healthcare Providers on GBV (2024, second edition) includes a mental health screening component for women presenting with GBV-related injuries or disclosures, with referral pathways to the FPA counselling services and, in Malé, to the National Mental Health Centre (Indhira Gandhi Memorial Hospital). The outer atoll gap in this referral pathway – where no NMHC equivalent exists – means that GBV survivors with mental health needs in most of the country have no access to specialist mental health care.

### 5.12.3 Postnatal Mental Health

Postnatal depression and postnatal mental health more broadly represent a significant gap in the Maldivian health system's gender-responsive services. The RMNCAH Strategy 2020–2025 acknowledges postnatal care as a priority but does not include a specific postnatal mental health protocol. Available data on postnatal depression prevalence in the Maldives is limited; no national screening programme exists.[^138]

Given the documented rates of depression among Maldivian women of reproductive age (33.5% overall), and the additional stresses of the atoll environment – geographic isolation from family support, domestic violence in a significant proportion of households, financial stress – the absence of postnatal mental health services represents a gap with real consequences for mothers and infant outcomes. International evidence consistently finds that untreated postnatal depression affects child development, breastfeeding, and maternal-infant bonding, making it both a mental health and a child welfare issue.

### 5.12.4 Stigma and Barriers to Mental Health Access

The Mental Health chapter (§9.X) documents **four types of mental health stigma** in the Maldivian context that affect access:
1. **Supernatural stigma:** Mental illness attributed to possession or supernatural causes, leading families to seek religious intervention rather than clinical care
2. **Moral stigma:** Mental illness attributed to personal weakness or insufficient faith
3. **Social stigma:** Fear of community judgment if mental health treatment-seeking becomes known
4. **Institutional stigma:** Distrust of the formal mental health system, particularly in communities with limited prior experience of mental health services[^139]

For women, these stigmas interact with the gendered dynamics of disclosure. A woman who discloses depression associated with marital violence risks her husband being informed (violating confidentiality expectations), community judgment about her marriage, and family pressure not to jeopardise the relationship. The social barriers to women's mental health service access thus mirror and compound the social barriers to GBV disclosure documented in §5.5.[^140]

### 5.12.5 Substance Abuse: Indirect Gender Pathways

Substance abuse in the Maldives is documented as **94% male** in the population admitted to treatment services (Mental Health chapter §9.X, citing NSDC data). This apparent gender gap – male substance abusers substantially outnumbering female – conceals an important indirect gender pathway: women are the primary secondary victims of male substance abuse in the domestic sphere.[^141]

Research documented in the Cross-Cutting Issues chapter links male substance abuse to domestic violence escalation – alcohol and drug intoxication are documented triggers for acute DV incidents in Maldivian case records, consistent with international evidence. The gendered impact of substance abuse thus falls on women not primarily through their own use, but through violence perpetrated by using partners. This intersection is underexplored in both the mental health and GBV response systems, which tend to operate as separate services.[^142]

---


## 5.69 Education and Gender


### 5.13.1 The Reverse Gender Gap: Achievement Without Economic Parity

The Maldives presents one of the most striking "reverse gender gaps" in South Asia at the tertiary education level: **59–64% of students in higher education are female** (Ministry of Higher Education 2023). At secondary level, the dropout rate is **88% male** – meaning almost all students who leave school before completion are boys, not girls. Girls in the Maldives now substantially outperform boys in educational persistence and credential attainment.[^143]

This represents a profound historical inversion. The first formal school for girls opened in 1944 (Aminiya School) – two decades after the first boys' secondary school. Within approximately 50 years, girls went from being excluded from secondary and tertiary education to constituting the majority of university students.

The educational achievement, however, has not translated proportionately into economic equality – demonstrating that educational attainment is a necessary but insufficient condition for gender economic empowerment in a context where structural labour market barriers, social norms, and institutional failures prevent educational gains from flowing through to economic outcomes. Women's LFPR (44%) remains 36 percentage points below men's (80%) despite women's tertiary education majority, and women's wages remain 13–20% below men's in comparable positions.[^144]

### 5.13.2 Early Marriage, Pregnancy, and Girls' Educational Dropout

While the aggregate statistics show women's educational advantage, this masks a pattern of educational attrition at the secondary level linked to early marriage and pregnancy, particularly in outer atolls. Girls who become pregnant – whether through consensual or coerced sex – typically withdraw from school due to a combination of:
- Social stigma making continued school attendance impractical
- School administration policies that effectively exclude visibly pregnant students
- Family decisions prioritising marriage over continued education[^145]

The absence of re-entry pathways for girls who leave school due to pregnancy is a significant policy gap. In most high-income countries with comparable social policy frameworks, school re-entry after pregnancy is legally protected and supported. In the Maldives, there is no formal re-entry mechanism, and girls who leave school for marriage or pregnancy typically do not return to formal education.[^146]

### 5.13.3 Sexual and Reproductive Health Education: Curriculum Gaps

The school curriculum's treatment of sexual and reproductive health is a critical gap with documented consequences for adolescent health outcomes and GBV vulnerability. The Youth Vulnerability Study 2019, Education chapter analysis (§10.X), and UNFPA assessments consistently find that:

- **Basic biology only:** SRH education is confined to reproductive anatomy and processes – the biological facts of reproduction – without content on consent, healthy relationships, contraception, or STI prevention
- **No consent or GBV content:** Students receive no instruction on what constitutes abuse, consent to sexual activity, recognition of warning signs, or safe disclosure mechanisms
- **Teacher discomfort:** Teachers responsible for biology units containing reproductive content typically minimise or skip this content due to discomfort, lack of training, and anticipated parental and community criticism
- **No specialist ASRH educators:** There are no dedicated sexual health educators in Maldivian schools; SRH content is nominally delivered by regular science teachers who receive no specific training[^147]

The practical consequences are significant. Adolescent survivors of childhood sexual abuse (12.2% of women nationally) do not receive information through school channels that would help them identify their experience as abuse, understand available options, or access support services. Girls who become sexually active – whether through consensual experience, coercion, or abuse – receive no information about contraception or STI prevention through formal education channels.[^148]

The CEDAW Committee's November 2021 Concluding Observations specifically called for comprehensive sexuality education to be incorporated into the school curriculum, with content covering consent, contraception, and healthy relationships. The Government's Seventh Periodic Report (2025) does not indicate implementation of this recommendation.[^149]

### 5.13.4 Career Guidance and Occupational Stereotyping

The Education chapter (§10.X) documents that career guidance in Maldivian schools systematically channels girls toward traditionally feminised occupations – teaching, nursing, social work – and boys toward traditionally masculine occupations – fishing, tourism, construction, and technical trades. This occurs both explicitly, through career counsellor advice that reflects societal gender role expectations, and implicitly, through the social environment that makes certain career choices easier or harder to pursue by gender.[^150]

The effect is to reproduce occupational segregation through the education system. Girls who show aptitude and interest in technology, engineering, or business are not routinely encouraged to pursue these paths; boys who show interest in caregiving or health professions face social discouragement. The consequence is a TVET and higher education enrollment pattern that shows women concentrated in education, social science, and health programmes while men concentrate in engineering, fisheries, and technical trades.[^151]

**TVET (Technical and Vocational Education and Training)** data illustrates the segregation directly: males substantially outnumber females in technical programmes, including those with growing labour market demand and associated higher wages. The ADB ARISE Project specifically targets this gap with gender-inclusive training methodologies and efforts to increase women's participation in non-traditional vocational areas including solar PV technology.[^152]

### 5.13.5 Higher Education Access: Geography and Equity

While aggregate higher education numbers favour women, the geographic distribution of higher education access creates inequities by island location. Higher education institutions are concentrated in Malé, with limited presence in outer atolls. Women from outer atolls who wish to pursue higher education face:
- The same inter-island transport costs and logistical challenges that affect all outer atoll residents
- Additional social barriers: moving to Malé unaccompanied by family is more socially complex for women than for men in conservative communities
- The need to leave island-level family support structures, including childcare arrangements, that allow mothers on their home islands to manage care responsibilities while working or studying[^153]

The Maldives National University (MNU) has expanded its distance learning and digital education provision, which partially addresses geographic access barriers. Women are significant users of distance learning provision – consistent with their need for flexible study options compatible with care responsibilities – but digital connectivity gaps on outer islands limit the effectiveness of online education as an equity solution.

---


## 5.70 The Maldives National University: Gender in Higher Education


### 5.89.1 MNU's Gender Profile

The **Maldives National University (MNU)** is the country's largest higher education institution, established from the merger of several technical institutes in 2011. MNU's student population reflects the national gender pattern in higher education: female students constitute approximately 60% or more of total enrollment across most faculties.

**Faculty-level gender distribution:**
- **Faculty of Education:** Heavily female (reflecting the feminisation of teaching as a profession); approximately 70-75% female students in teacher education programmes
- **Faculty of Health Sciences:** Female majority in nursing programmes; more balanced in health management; male-dominated in some technical health fields
- **Faculty of Hospitality and Tourism Studies:** Male-majority reflecting the tourism sector's gender dynamics and the aspiration to resort employment that is socially problematic for women
- **Faculty of Engineering, Science and Technology:** Male-majority; female participation increasing but still minority
- **Faculty of Arts, Law and Social Sciences:** Female majority
- **Faculty of Islamic Studies:** Historical male dominance; the only faculty where significant gender imbalance favouring men exists in the Muslim world context

**Distance and flexible learning:** MNU's distance learning programmes have significantly higher female enrollment than on-campus programmes, consistent with the finding that flexible, non-residential education options are more accessible to women managing care responsibilities and geographic constraints.[^521]

### 5.89.2 Gender in MNU Research

MNU's research output includes studies on Maldivian social issues, some of which address gender – including research on divorce, family law, and social development. Whether MNU has established a dedicated gender research centre or programme – which would provide an institutional base for the gender data research that is chronically underfunded in the Maldives – is not documented in available public sources. Establishing a Centre for Gender Studies at MNU would provide an institutional home for the Maldivian-specific Islamic feminist scholarship, gender data analysis, and policy research that the gender equality movement needs but currently lacks.

---


## 5.71 The Digital Gender Divide and Technology Access


### 5.75.1 Internet and Mobile Penetration

The Maldives has high internet and mobile phone penetration – one of the highest in South Asia. The 2022 Census and ICT sector data indicate near-universal mobile phone ownership among adults, with internet access available through mobile data across most inhabited islands. Social media use – particularly Facebook, TikTok, and WhatsApp – is pervasive across demographic groups.

This digital connectivity has significant gender dimensions:

**Access:** Women and men have roughly similar rates of mobile phone ownership and internet access nationally. The gender digital divide in the Maldives is less about access than about use – how people use digital platforms, and whether digital access translates into economic, educational, or social opportunity.

**Economic opportunity:** Digital connectivity creates potential for women in outer islands to access economic opportunities (online commerce, digital freelancing, remote employment) that the geographic constraints of their island make impossible in the physical economy. The realisation of this potential requires digital skills training and digital payment system access that are not yet available at adequate scale.[^501]

**Social media and GBV:** As documented in §5.16, digital platforms are both a source of expanded connectivity and a vector for gender-based violence – online harassment, non-consensual image sharing, and stalking. Women who are public figures face coordinated digital harassment; private women face targeted harassment from ex-partners. The legal and institutional framework for addressing digital GBV lags the technology.

### 5.75.2 The WhatsApp Factor in GBV Response

An under-examined dimension of digital connectivity and GBV response is the use of **WhatsApp and messaging apps** as informal support channels for DV survivors:

- Women who cannot make a telephone call to the FPA in private can send a WhatsApp message from a private moment
- Women who are connected to Hope for Women or other NGO networks through Facebook or WhatsApp can reach support without the visibility of a telephone call or physical visit
- Documentation of violence – photographs of injuries, screenshots of threatening messages – can be sent digitally before approaching the formal system

These informal digital pathways are not systematically documented or supported by the formal GBV system but represent an adaptive use of available technology by survivors navigating a limited formal system. Formalising a WhatsApp-based reporting channel for the FPA – with privacy protocols and trained case managers – could substantially increase the accessibility of first-contact support.

---


# Part 12: Women in the Criminal Justice System


## 5.72 Women in the Criminal Justice System: Victims, Defendants, and the Zina Law


### 5.25.1 Women as GBV Victims in Criminal Courts

When DV or sexual violence progresses to the criminal justice track – the small minority of cases in which police investigation and prosecution follow – survivors navigate a criminal court system with structural features that compound the barriers to effective access to justice:

**Physical court environment:** The Criminal Court in Malé is a formal adversarial environment in which survivors must appear publicly, potentially face cross-examination by the perpetrator's defence counsel, and present their evidence before a judge who may have limited specific training in trauma-informed approaches to GBV cases. No screen arrangements, video testimony provisions, or dedicated GBV support workers are routinely provided in the Maldivian criminal court.[^299]

**Evidence requirements in sexual violence cases:** The SOA provisions governing sexual violence require corroborating physical evidence in practice – while the legal evidentiary standard is not formally different from other criminal cases, the evidentiary culture means that cases without medical evidence documenting injury face significantly lower rates of prosecution. The forensic examination requirement – detailed in §5.6 – means that survivors who did not present to a health facility immediately after assault typically cannot meet the evidence threshold.[^300]

**The credibility assessment:** A survivor's credibility is assessed across multiple dimensions in the criminal court process – consistency of her account, demeanour, absence of apparent motive to fabricate, and the specific assessment of whether she reported promptly and why she may have delayed. Delay in reporting is a documented feature of most sexual violence cases, driven by the exact same barriers (fear, shame, confusion) that research consistently identifies. Courts that treat delay as evidence of fabrication are applying a norm that is inconsistent with the psychosocial reality of GBV disclosure.[^301]

**Cross-examination trauma:** In Maldivian criminal proceedings, as in most adversarial systems, defence counsel has the right to cross-examine prosecution witnesses including the victim/survivor. In GBV cases, this cross-examination can be re-traumatising – revisiting the details of violence, challenging the survivor's account, and implicitly or explicitly questioning her motives or character. No special provisions restricting the nature of cross-examination in GBV cases exist in Maldivian criminal procedure – the rape shield provisions that many jurisdictions have enacted (preventing reference to a survivor's sexual history) do not have a clear equivalent in Maldivian law.[^302]

### 5.25.2 Women as Criminal Defendants: The Zina Law

One of the most acute gender justice issues in Maldivian criminal law is the **zina provision** of the Penal Code: sexual intercourse outside marriage is criminalised for both parties, with a prescribed punishment of **100 lashes** by flogging. The gendered application of this provision is the most significant documented human rights concern in Maldivian criminal law.

**Gendered evidence burden:** Proving zina under Islamic criminal law (*hadd* standard) requires either confession or the testimony of four male eyewitnesses to the act of intercourse. This impossibly high standard means that zina prosecutions based on direct evidence are essentially non-existent. Instead, zina is inferred – and the woman's pregnancy outside marriage is the primary form of circumstantial evidence used in practice. A pregnant unmarried woman cannot conceal the evidence of sexual intercourse and may be prosecuted for zina on the basis of her pregnancy; a man who is not pregnant has no equivalent visible evidence and is rarely prosecuted without confession.[^303]

**The rape-zina trap:** The intersection of the zina law with the sexual violence response creates a documented trap for rape survivors. A woman who reports rape:
- Must prove rape through the standard evidence requirements (medical evidence, four witnesses, or confession) – which is structurally difficult
- If rape cannot be proved but pregnancy results, may be prosecuted for zina – using the pregnancy as evidence of intercourse outside marriage
- Cannot rely on the rape allegation as a defence to zina without meeting the rape's own high evidence standard

The result is that survivors of sexual violence who become pregnant face potential prosecution – a documented deterrent to rape reporting. The HRCM's 2022 report on corporal punishment documented that **85% of flogging sentences imposed in the Maldives were against women** – a figure that directly reflects the gendered evidentiary asymmetry of the zina provision. The CEDAW Committee described this pattern in its November 2021 Concluding Observations (para. 51) as a serious violation of women's rights and called for repeal of the zina flogging provision.[^304]

**Implementation in practice:** The flogging sentence for zina – 100 lashes – is publicly implemented in the Maldives. A 2015 international controversy arose when video of a flogging was shared internationally, generating significant diplomatic and media attention. The government defended the practice as a requirement of Islamic law. The HRCM has consistently recommended the abolition of corporal punishment, including flogging, as incompatible with constitutional rights provisions and international human rights law.[^305]

**The constitutional tension:** The 2008 Constitution both guarantees rights (including protection from torture and cruel treatment) and establishes Islamic jurisprudence as a source of law. The Constitutional Court has not resolved the tension between these provisions, leaving the flogging sentence for zina in a legal limbo where it is simultaneously constitutional (as a Shariah requirement) and arguable unconstitutional (as a form of cruel treatment) – a tension that the political system has not been willing to resolve through legislative action.[^306]

### 5.25.3 Corporal Punishment Provisions

The zina flogging is one component of a broader set of corporal punishment provisions with documented gender dimensions. Other relevant Penal Code provisions include:

**Alcohol and substance use:** The consumption of alcohol is criminalised for Maldivians under Islamic law, with flogging as a prescribed punishment. Women convicted of alcohol consumption receive the same formal sentence as men, though enforcement patterns and social consequences differ.

**Apostasy and religious offences:** Religious offences under Maldivian law have particular gender dimensions – women who speak publicly about leaving Islam or questioning religious doctrine face both formal legal risk and social violence that is gendered in its intensity.

**Reforms under discussion:** The HRCM's recommendation for abolition of corporal punishment has been consistently rejected by the government on Islamic grounds. No political party has made repeal of flogging a policy commitment in any Maldivian election. International pressure – from the UN human rights system, the European Union, and bilateral partners – has not generated domestic political movement on this issue. The most realistic reform pathway identified in gender policy advocacy is through Islamic jurisprudential channels – seeking religious opinions that the hadd flogging standard's impossibly high evidentiary requirements effectively make the punishment inapplicable in the modern context – rather than secular abolitionism.[^307]

---


## 5.73 Trafficking, Online Exploitation, and Violence Beyond the Household


### 5.16.1 Human Trafficking: Legal Framework and Implementation Record

The **Anti-Human Trafficking Act 12/2013** established the Maldives' foundational anti-trafficking legislation, creating trafficking-specific criminal offences and mandating a National Referral Mechanism (NRM) for trafficking victim identification, protection, and assistance. The law defines trafficking in persons broadly to include recruitment, transportation, transfer, harbouring, and receipt of persons through coercion, deception, or abuse of power for exploitation – including sexual exploitation, forced labour, and organ removal.[^183]

As of 2025, implementation of the Anti-Trafficking Act falls well short of the legal framework's intentions:

- The **US State Department Trafficking in Persons (TIP) Report** has consistently placed the Maldives on the **Tier 2 Watch List** – indicating significant violations of the minimum standards for anti-trafficking action combined with insufficient progress – for multiple consecutive years[^184]
- A **dedicated anti-trafficking hotline** – mandated under the NRM framework – has been documented as **inoperable** in multiple assessment periods[^185]
- **Zero government employees have been prosecuted** for trafficking-related offences, despite documented evidence of trafficking facilitated through government-issued documents and processes[^186]
- Victim identification rates remain extremely low – trafficking victims, particularly in the domestic work sector, are more likely to be deported as irregular migrants than identified as trafficking victims and provided with protection[^187]

### 5.16.2 Domestic Workers: The Hidden Trafficking Population

The trafficking population of greatest relevance to gender policy is **domestic workers** – predominantly women from Sri Lanka and Bangladesh employed in private households throughout the Maldives. The Anti-Trafficking Act's definition covers domestic servitude (a form of forced labour), and the private household context creates particular enforcement challenges:

- Domestic workers are employed in private homes, which creates privacy barriers to inspection and monitoring
- Many domestic workers enter the Maldives with valid work documents but experience exploitation after arrival – debt bondage, confiscation of passports, restriction of movement, and non-payment of wages – that constitutes trafficking but is difficult to identify from the outside[^188]
- Cultural norms around privacy of the domestic sphere – reinforced by the same social dynamics that limit DV disclosure – make domestic workers reluctant to report exploitation
- The absence of an operational victim identification mechanism means that domestic workers who do reach the attention of authorities are typically treated as immigration cases rather than trafficking victims[^189]

The domestic worker population in the Maldives is substantial and has grown as Malé's dual-income households increasingly employ live-in domestic workers. No comprehensive census of domestic worker numbers exists; estimates suggest several thousand workers, predominantly South Asian women.[^190]

### 5.16.3 Sexual Trafficking and Exploitation

Sexual exploitation – both of Maldivian women and of foreign women trafficked into the country – is documented but difficult to quantify given the underground nature of sex work (which is criminalised) and the limited victim identification system:

- Maldivian women, particularly in situations of economic vulnerability, may be exploited in informal commercial sexual transactions, often without clear victim identification given the criminalisation of all forms of sex work[^191]
- Resort islands – where large numbers of foreign staff work in relative isolation with limited oversight – have been identified in field assessments as environments where sexual exploitation of staff may occur with limited accountability[^192]
- Online platforms have expanded pathways for sexual exploitation, including recruitment of Maldivian women and girls through social media for commercial sexual services, particularly in Malé[^193]

The intersection of trafficking and the GBV response system is weak. The FPA does not have a designated trafficking victim support protocol separate from its general GBV response, and the Criminal Court processes for trafficking cases are at an early stage of development. The AIM Project's Baseline Assessment (2022) identifies trafficking as an area where legal reform has significantly outpaced operational capacity.[^194]

### 5.16.4 Online Exploitation and Digital Gender-Based Violence

Online and digital forms of gender-based violence represent a growing and inadequately addressed dimension of GBV:

**Non-consensual intimate image sharing (NCIIS/revenge porn):** The sharing of intimate images or videos without consent – often by former partners following relationship breakdown – has been documented in FPA case records and in civil society reports. The Sexual Offences Act provides limited coverage for some forms of this conduct, but a dedicated digital GBV legal framework does not exist.[^195]

**Online harassment and stalking:** Women who are public figures (politicians, journalists, civil society leaders) face coordinated online harassment campaigns documented by MDN. Private individuals, particularly survivors of DV who publicly share their experiences, also face online harassment. The police FCPW does not have dedicated capacity for cybercrime intersecting with GBV, and cases are typically handled under the general cybercrime framework (which exists but has low prosecution rates).[^196]

**Recruitment for exploitation:** Social media platforms are used to recruit young women – including minors – for sexual exploitation. The combination of a small, well-networked society (where offline and online social networks heavily overlap) and limited platform-level content moderation of Dhivehi-language content creates a specific vulnerability.[^197]

**Dating application risks:** The rapid growth of dating application use in Malé creates contexts for intimate partner violence and sexual exploitation that traditional social control mechanisms – family oversight, island community supervision – cannot address. Young women meeting men through dating applications have been documented in case records of the FCPW and FPA as victims of sexual assault and coercion.[^198]

The CEDAW Committee's November 2021 Concluding Observations included online and digital violence as an area of concern. The Government's response has focused primarily on general cybercrime law rather than gender-specific digital GBV provisions.[^199]

### 5.16.5 Workplace Sexual Harassment

The **Employment Act 2/2008** and the **Gender Equality Act 18/2016** both prohibit workplace discrimination and harassment on grounds of gender. The GEA provides the more specific framework, defining sexual harassment and requiring employers to establish internal harassment complaint procedures.[^200]

The practical implementation of workplace sexual harassment protections is weak:
- Most employers – particularly in the private sector and in outer islands – have not established internal harassment complaint mechanisms as required by the GEA
- Women who report workplace harassment risk retaliation, particularly in a small and highly networked economy where blacklisting is a real risk
- The Employment Tribunal's capacity to handle harassment complaints is limited; cases documented in the Cross-Cutting Issues chapter indicate multi-year delays
- Cultural norms frame much workplace sexual harassment as routine interpersonal behaviour rather than a disciplinable offence, reducing both reporting and sanction expectations[^201]

The tourism sector presents specific workplace harassment vulnerabilities: women employed in resorts (though very few are Maldivian) work in settings with power asymmetries between management (often expatriate, male) and service staff (often young women), limited union presence, and social isolation that discourages complaint. The AIM Project's GBV at work component addresses public sector workplaces but does not extend to the resort sector.[^202]

---


# Part 13: Geographic Inequality and Atoll Women


## 5.74 Atoll Women and Geographic Inequality


### 5.17.1 The Atoll Geography as a Gender Justice Issue

The Maldives consists of 187 inhabited islands spread across 26 atolls and 750 kilometres of ocean. This geography is not gender-neutral. Services, infrastructure, employment, and justice are concentrated in Malé and a small number of regional centres; women on smaller and more remote islands face compounding geographic barriers to accessing the rights and services to which they are legally entitled. The lived experience of gender inequality differs substantially between a woman in Malé and a woman on a remote outer island – and even the national statistics that document gender inequality typically mask this internal geographic variation.[^203]

The atoll geography interacts with gender inequality through several specific mechanisms:

**Service access:** FPA case management services, Family Courts, Police FCPW, and the Hulhumale' and Hithadhoo shelters are all centralised in specific locations. A woman on a remote island who needs protection from a violent partner cannot walk to a shelter – she must make a multi-stage journey (motorised dinghy to atoll capital, possible air or ferry transfer to Malé or shelter location) that may take days, cost thousands of Maldivian rufiyaa, and require leaving her children behind.[^204]

**Justice access:** Filing a DV protection order, pursuing criminal charges for sexual violence, or applying to the Family Court for divorce requires physical presence or legal representation in Malé (or the relevant island court). Remote island women either travel at significant personal cost or rely on telephone or digital mediation that the courts may not fully accommodate.[^205]

**Surveillance and control:** Paradoxically, small island communities can both protect women (through extended family solidarity and community knowledge of warning signs) and constrain them (through the same community knowledge used to enforce social norms against disclosure, leaving abusive relationships, or pursuing non-traditional economic activities). A woman who reports DV on a small island reports it to the same community that will also see her at the fishing dock, the mosque, and the school pick-up.[^206]

**Male out-migration:** In atolls where male labour migration to resorts or Malé is high, women may be de facto heads of household with expanded responsibility but limited formal authority, economic resources, or access to services. The labour migration dynamic creates households where women manage complex social and economic situations with inadequate support.[^207]

### 5.17.2 Outer Atoll DV Response: The Infrastructure Gap

The DV response infrastructure outside Malé and the three ADB-funded shelter locations (Hithadhoo/Addu, Hulhumale', Raa Atoll) is severely limited. The infrastructure gap in outer atoll DV response includes:

**No shelter:** The vast majority of inhabited islands have no safe accommodation for DV survivors other than returning to family – which may not be safe or available, particularly for women whose families do not support them leaving the abusive relationship.[^208]

**Police capacity:** Outer island police stations have one to three officers who serve multiple functions (civil order, traffic, investigation). These officers may be personally known to the perpetrator, may share social relationships with the perpetrator's family, and receive no sustained specialised training in DV response. Transfer away from large islands means officers with FCPW training may leave shortly after completion of training.[^209]

**Court access:** Family Courts serving outer atolls have limited session frequency and backlogs. Protection orders – which require judicial issuance – may take weeks to obtain on islands served by visiting judges. Interim protection is theoretically available under DVPA provisions but rarely used in outer atoll contexts.[^210]

**WDC limitations:** As documented in §5.10.3, WDCs on outer islands have no budget, no legal authority, and limited training. Their effectiveness as a DV response asset depends entirely on individual member commitment and informal relationships.[^211]
