**Transport as barrier and risk:** Even when a DV survivor decides to leave, the logistics of safe departure from a small island are complex – speedboat or ferry schedules are limited, departures are visible to the community (including the perpetrator), and the journey to the nearest shelter may be dangerous if the perpetrator is aware.[^212]

### 5.17.3 Laamu Atoll: A Case Study in Intersecting Vulnerabilities

The Laamu Atoll Vulnerability Mapping – cited in multiple chapters of this encyclopedia – provides one of the most detailed subnational analyses of intersecting gender, climate, and economic vulnerabilities. Laamu Atoll is instructive precisely because it is not exceptional: it represents conditions found across many outer atolls, and the mapping methodology is transferable to other geographic contexts.

Key findings from the Laamu mapping with gender dimensions include:
- **Single mothers** identified as the highest-vulnerability household type, combining income insecurity, full childcare responsibility, housing instability, and DV risk
- **Climate-DV intersection:** Livelihood stress from coral bleaching (affecting reef fishing and tourism in the atoll) documented as a DV risk amplifier through economic stress mechanisms
- **Service desert:** The map revealed that for most islands in the atoll, the combination of no shelter, no specialist police, no FPA caseworker, and no WDC with any resources created a comprehensive service vacuum for DV survivors
- **Geographic compound:** Remote location within the atoll meant that even the limited services in the atoll capital (Fonadhoo) were inaccessible without significant cost and logistical effort for women on smaller islands[^213]

The Laamu mapping's value lies in its granularity – identifying specific islands where specific service gaps create acute vulnerability. This level of analysis has not been systematically conducted across all 26 atolls, representing a significant gap in the evidence base for geographically targeted social policy interventions.

### 5.17.4 Urbanisation and Its Gender Dimensions

The long-term demographic trend toward urbanisation – concentration of population in Malé, Hulhumale', and a small number of growing regional centres – has gender-differentiated consequences:

**Positive dimensions:**
- Urban women have access to employment options, services, and social networks unavailable on small islands
- Urban anonymity provides a degree of protection against the community surveillance that constrains women's choices on small islands
- Services – FPA, shelters, health facilities with specialist capacity, courts – are physically accessible in Malé without the multi-day journey required from remote islands
- Economic independence is more achievable in Malé's more diverse economy

**Negative dimensions:**
- Rapid urbanisation without adequate affordable housing, infrastructure, or social services creates urban poverty that affects women disproportionately as primary caregivers
- Migration to Malé of women escaping abusive relationships may place them in urban settings without social support networks, affordable housing, or formal employment
- Hulhumale' – the planned city built on reclaimed land north of Malé – is home to a growing population but has limited social services infrastructure relative to its population size, a gap particularly affecting women and children[^214]

---


## 5.75 A Day in the Life: The Geographic Reality of Gender Inequality


### 5.70.1 The Outer Island Woman's Reality

Abstract statistics – LFPR of 44%, 3% prosecution rate, 29% contraceptive unmet need – acquire meaning when situated in the lived experience of women in specific contexts. This section attempts to trace what gender inequality looks like concretely for women in different positions in Maldivian society.

**The outer island married woman with children:** A 28-year-old woman on a remote island in Raa Atoll with two children and a husband employed at a nearby resort island (visiting home once a month) lives a life defined by geographic isolation and economic constraint. Her island has a health post staffed by a single nurse. The atoll capital is 45 minutes by speedboat – further, more expensive, and less accessible in rough weather. She has no formal employment (there are no formal sector jobs compatible with her childcare situation on her island). She produces and sells small amounts of food through social networks. Her income is entirely dependent on her husband's remittance.

If her husband becomes violent on his monthly visits, her options are: the island health post (one nurse, no DV training, limited privacy); the WDC member (her neighbour, who is sympathetic but has no resources and fears social consequences of becoming involved); or calling the FPA (which requires telephone privacy she doesn't have in a small house shared with in-laws, and which leads to a case management process that is physically remote). The shelter in Raa Atoll capital is 45 minutes and several hundred rufiyaa away – and requires leaving her children. The Family Court is in Malé.

This woman's situation is not exceptional; it describes the structural reality for a significant proportion of Maldivian women.

**The Malé professional woman:** A 32-year-old woman in Malé with a university degree and a government position in healthcare has a fundamentally different set of options. She knows about the FPA because it is physically near her workplace. She has access to Hope for Women's services through social media and can attend their community events. She has some economic independence – enough to rent a room if she needed to leave a relationship. She can reach the Family Court without a multi-day journey. Her social network includes women in similar positions who know the GBV system.

She also faces barriers – marriage pressure, job interview questions about her marital status, career progression limits, and a social environment that expects her to prioritise family over career. But the barriers are qualitatively different from those facing the outer island woman: they are social and normative rather than geographic and material.

**The recently divorced single mother:** A 34-year-old divorced woman with three children who has left an abusive marriage in Malé is navigating: family court proceedings for custody and maintenance (with or without FLC representation); the search for affordable housing without a joint income or any guaranteed maintenance income; the challenge of childcare that would allow her to work; and the social consequences of divorce in her community. The MVR 2 million in unclaimed maintenance represents, in individual terms, months of unpaid childcare support from her children's father. Her situation is the economic consequence of the gender inequality documented throughout this chapter translated into daily material struggle.

### 5.70.2 The Male Perpetrator's Position

Understanding gender inequality also requires attending to the position of men who perpetrate violence – not to excuse or minimise, but to understand the structural conditions that produce DV perpetration and how they might be changed:

A 35-year-old man who grew up in a household with DV (which a significant proportion of Maldivian children have, given the prevalence data) has normalised patterns of conflict resolution that include intimidation and physical force. He may have substance use issues – documented as highly male-skewed and as a proximate DV trigger. He may experience economic stress from unemployment or underemployment – particularly if his identity is structured around provider masculinity that his actual economic situation cannot support. He has never encountered a programme challenging his understanding of relationship dynamics; the school he attended did not cover consent or healthy relationships; the mosque he attends frames marital authority in ways that he interprets as supporting his control over his wife; and the criminal justice system has never meaningfully imposed a consequence for his violent behaviour.

This man is the typical DV perpetrator in the Maldivian data – and the system that has been built around him (inadequate prosecution, no perpetrator intervention, limited deterrence) has done nothing substantive to interrupt his pattern. The gender equality system is heavily weighted toward survivor support and almost entirely absent on perpetrator accountability and behaviour change.[^487]

---


## 5.76 The Intersection of Gender and Ethnicity/Regional Identity


### 5.66.1 Maldivian Social Diversity

The Maldives is sometimes characterised as an ethnically homogeneous society – a single Maldivian people with a shared Islamic culture and Dhivehi language. In practice, significant regional variation in social norms, economic conditions, and gender relations exists across the atolls, with documented consequences for gender equality outcomes.

**Regional variation in DV norms:** Outer atoll communities – particularly in the more conservative southern atolls – are documented in field research as having more restrictive gender norms, higher rates of DV tolerance, and stronger community pressure against disclosure than urban Malé. This variation is not ethnic in origin (the population is ethnically Maldivian throughout) but reflects the interaction of geography (isolation, limited media exposure), economic conditions (higher poverty rates, dependence on fishing), and religious practice patterns (more conservative religious observance in some atoll communities).[^479]

**The Addu Atoll Factor:** Addu Atoll (Hithadhoo and surrounding islands, the southernmost atoll) is a distinctive regional community with a history of greater exposure to British colonial influence (as a WWII and Cold War British military base), which produced somewhat different cultural patterns – including somewhat higher rates of English language use and familiarity with international norms. Whether Addu Atoll's gender norms differ systematically from other outer atolls is not systematically documented, but it is notable that the ADB GESI shelter programme placed one of its three shelters in Hithadhoo/Addu – reflecting both the atoll's population size and the assessment that a shelter could be effectively operated there.

**The Malé vs outer atoll divide:** The most significant social division in the Maldivian gender policy context is not ethnic or communal but geographic – between Malé and the outer atolls. Women in Malé have access to the FPA, courts, shelters, NGO services, healthcare, and employment options that are simply not available to women on smaller islands. This geographic inequality in gender equality outcomes is the defining structural feature of the Maldivian situation, as documented throughout this chapter.[^480]

---


# Part 14: Climate, COVID-19, and Substance Abuse


## 5.77 Gender and Climate Vulnerability


### 5.15.1 The Climate-Gender Nexus in Small Island Developing States

The Maldives is categorised by the IPCC as one of the world's most climate-vulnerable nations: 80% of the country's land area lies less than one metre above sea level, and all inhabited islands are exposed to sea level rise, storm surge, coral bleaching, and intensifying weather events. Gender intersects with climate vulnerability across multiple dimensions – women and men experience climate impacts differently, due to differences in economic resources, social roles, mobility, political voice, and underlying vulnerability.[^166]

International frameworks – including the Paris Agreement (2015), the Sendai Framework for Disaster Risk Reduction (2015–2030), and the Kunming-Montreal Global Biodiversity Framework (2022) – now incorporate gender mainstreaming as a requirement for climate action. The Maldives, as a signatory to the Paris Agreement and active voice in the AOSIS (Alliance of Small Island States) coalition, is both a subject of international climate policy and a participant in shaping it. The gender dimension of this engagement has received growing attention in domestic policy frameworks since 2021.[^167]

### 5.15.2 Gender-Responsive Climate Financing Strategy 2023

The most significant domestic policy development linking gender and climate is the **Gender-Responsive Climate Financing Strategy (GRFS) 2023**, developed by the Ministry of Environment, Climate Change and Technology with UNFPA technical support and international climate financing input. The GRFS represents the Maldives' formal commitment to ensuring that climate finance – from Green Climate Fund (GCF), Adaptation Fund, and bilateral climate finance sources – is allocated in ways that address gender inequality rather than reinforce it.[^168]

The GRFS 2023 has three pillars and **16 strategies**:

**Pillar 1: Increasing women's participation in climate decision-making**
- Strategies include mandatory gender balance targets for climate committee membership
- Local island climate resilience committees required to include women (mirroring local council quota approach)
- Women's perspectives formally incorporated into National Adaptation Plans (NAPs)

**Pillar 2: Gender-responsive climate finance allocation**
- Gender impact screening required for all major climate finance proposals submitted to GCF and other international mechanisms
- Targeting of adaptation finance toward gender-differentiated vulnerability – particularly female-headed households and women in low-lying outer islands
- Accessible climate finance mechanisms for women's livelihood adaptation – small grants, microfinance for climate-resilient income diversification

**Pillar 3: Gender data and monitoring**
- The **Gender Monitoring Framework 2023** accompanying the GRFS establishes gender-disaggregated climate indicators for monitoring across all 16 strategies
- Baseline data collection on women's climate vulnerability by island and atoll
- Periodic gender-disaggregated reporting to UNFCCC on climate adaptation outcomes[^169]

The GRFS represents a significant policy commitment, but implementation is in early stages as of 2025. Funding for the 16 strategies depends substantially on international climate finance access, which requires successful GCF project approval and development partner engagement. The gender indicators in the Monitoring Framework represent a potential data system that, if sustained, would substantially improve the evidence base on gender-climate intersections.[^170]

### 5.15.3 The ARISE Project: Gender in Climate Resilience Programming

The **Atoll Resilience and Island-level Sustainability Enhancement (ARISE) project** is the primary operational vehicle for integrating gender into climate resilience programming at the island level. ARISE includes a **Gender Action Plan** – a requirement of its development partner financing – that specifies:

- Gender-balanced participation in island resilience planning processes
- Targeted skills training for women in climate-resilient income diversification
- Women's inclusion in **solar photovoltaic (PV) technology** training – historically a male-dominated technical domain – as part of climate energy transition programming[^171]

The ARISE gender finding on solar PV is particularly notable: women are substantially underrepresented in the technical workforce managing and maintaining renewable energy systems. As the Maldives transitions from diesel generators to solar PV on outer islands – a core component of the climate strategy – the energy sector workforce is at risk of remaining male-dominated if active gender inclusion measures are not taken. The ARISE Gender Action Plan's solar PV component attempts to address this by providing training and certification pathways accessible to women on programme islands.[^172]

ADB's gender assessment of ARISE noted that even where women participate in climate resilience activities, they tend to be assigned to social roles (community awareness, care of community assets) while men are assigned to technical roles (installation, maintenance, engineering). Breaking this pattern requires deliberate role assignment and mentorship support, which the ARISE Gender Action Plan attempts to provide.[^173]

### 5.15.4 Climate as a Driver of Domestic Violence

An under-examined intersection in Maldivian gender policy is the pathway from **climate stress → economic stress → intimate partner violence escalation**. Evidence from international climate-GBV research, and from Maldivian field documentation in the Climate Vulnerability chapter (§14.X) and Cross-Cutting Issues chapter (§15.X), suggests several mechanisms:

**Livelihood stress:** Climate events – coral bleaching disrupting reef fish populations, storm surge damaging agricultural land, flooding destroying home gardens – reduce household income. Economic stress is a documented trigger for DV escalation in the Maldivian case literature and consistent with international DV economics research.[^174]

**Displacement stress:** When climate events require temporary or permanent displacement from a damaged island, household stress intensifies. Post-displacement living arrangements (crowded evacuation centres, displacement to Malé) concentrate households in confined spaces with limited privacy, reduced social support networks, and heightened economic anxiety – conditions associated internationally with DV risk.[^175]

**Male coping behaviours:** Climate-related livelihood loss among men in fishing communities – where masculinity is closely tied to productive capacity – is associated with increased substance abuse and, subsequently, DV. The Laamu Atoll Vulnerability Mapping documented the intersection of climate livelihood stress and DV risk as a specific compound vulnerability in that atoll.[^176]

**Women excluded from adaptation response:** Where women are excluded from island disaster management committees and climate resilience planning bodies, adaptation measures may not address women's specific needs – in evacuation procedures, shelter arrangements, and livelihood support – making women more exposed to harm from climate events and reducing their agency in the recovery process.[^177]

### 5.15.5 Women's Exclusion from Climate Governance

Despite the GRFS 2023 commitment to gender inclusion, the practical exclusion of women from climate governance bodies at island level has been consistently documented:

- **Island disaster management committees** have historically been male-dominated, with women's exclusion documented in field assessments across multiple atolls[^178]
- **Island development committees** (which make decisions about land use, sea walls, and island infrastructure with climate resilience implications) similarly reflect the broader pattern of women's exclusion from formal local decision-making[^179]
- **Fishermen's organisations** – relevant to climate adaptation in fishing communities – are by social convention and in some cases by formal rule male-only, excluding women from participation in marine resource adaptation decisions even where women participate in fish processing and trading[^180]

The local council quota (33% after 2021 elections) is beginning to change this at the council level. Women councillors have, in documented cases, raised climate adaptation issues – sea wall placement affecting women's mobility, evacuation shelter conditions – that male-dominated councils had not prioritised. But the broader governance ecosystem of informal island decision-making bodies remains largely unaffected by the formal quota.[^181]

### 5.15.6 Female-Headed Households and Climate Disproportionality

Female-headed households face disproportionate climate impacts through several compounding mechanisms:

- **Housing quality:** Female-headed households have lower average incomes and lower rates of homeownership, translating into greater likelihood of living in structurally vulnerable housing (older materials, lower elevation, inadequate storm drainage)
- **Limited insurance:** Climate risk insurance is essentially absent for most Maldivian households; but female-headed households' lower asset base means storm or flood damage has relatively greater financial impact
- **Reduced social support:** Female-headed households – particularly in the outer atolls – may have reduced access to the informal male-coded networks (fishing cooperatives, community labour sharing) that facilitate recovery after climate events
- **Geographic concentration:** Female-headed households in outer atolls, where climate vulnerability is highest and climate adaptation resources are scarcest, face the compound of geographic climate exposure and gender-related economic vulnerability[^182]

---


## 5.78 Climate-Gender Integration: The Paris Agreement and Gender Justice


### 5.98.1 The Enhanced Lima Work Programme on Gender

The **UNFCCC Enhanced Lima Work Programme on Gender and its Gender Action Plan (2019-2024)** – which the Maldives, as a Paris Agreement signatory, is obligated to engage with – provides the global framework within which the GRFS 2023 sits. Key provisions of the UNFCCC Gender Action Plan that have specific Maldivian relevance:

- **Capacity building for gender-responsive NDCs (Nationally Determined Contributions):** The Maldives' NDC should include gender-disaggregated vulnerability and adaptation analysis. The GRFS 2023 was developed partly to provide this analytical foundation.
- **Gender-balanced representation in national climate delegations:** AOSIS delegations have historically been male-dominated; the UNFCCC gender action plan calls for gender-balanced technical and political representation in climate negotiations.
- **Access to climate finance with gender equality conditions:** GCF projects must meet gender performance standards; the GRFS positions the Maldives to apply for gender-transformative GCF financing.

**The Maldives' climate gender record:** The Maldives has been a vocal advocate for climate justice in international forums as a frontline SIDS. The gender dimension of this advocacy – explicitly linking women's climate vulnerability (female-headed households in low-lying areas, women's exclusion from climate governance, the climate-DV pathway) to the loss and damage and adaptation finance negotiations – has been less consistently articulated but is increasingly relevant as the GRFS 2023 provides a domestic policy foundation.[^532]

---


## 5.79 The COVID-19 Pandemic as a Gender Policy Crisis


### 5.39.1 The Shadow Pandemic

The COVID-19 pandemic (2020-2022) produced what international GBV researchers and UN Women termed a "**shadow pandemic**" – a surge in domestic violence and gender inequality running parallel to the public health crisis. The Maldivian experience of this shadow pandemic is documented across several data sources and represents one of the most significant short-term shocks to gender policy in recent history.[^398]

The mechanisms through which COVID-19 intensified DV are well-documented internationally and consistent with the Maldivian evidence:
- **Lockdowns** forced survivors and perpetrators into shared spaces without the social structures (extended family visits, community activities, school attendance) that normally create some protection through disruption of isolation
- **Economic stress** – including job losses disproportionate to women, as documented in §5.9 – intensified household financial tensions associated with DV escalation
- **Loss of access to services** – FPA office access constrained, shelter capacity limited by health protocols, courts operating at reduced capacity – reduced survivors' ability to seek help when violence escalated
- **Perpetrator isolation** – perpetrators who lost income, social standing, and mobility also experienced stress that, combined with the close-quarters lockdown context, drove violence escalation
- **Children at home** – school closures increased caregiving demands on women, reducing their ability to reach out for help while increasing household stress that perpetrators converted to violence[^399]

### 5.39.2 Maldivian Data on COVID-GBV

The specific Maldivian data on COVID-GBV includes:

**FPA case series:** The case statistics during the pandemic period – 696 (2019), 537 (2020), 696 (2021) – require careful interpretation. The 2020 decline likely reflects reduced access to reporting services (lockdowns preventing FPA office visits; telephone lines overwhelmed) rather than reduced actual violence. The 2021 return to the 2019 peak, as access improved, supports this interpretation. UNFPA's COVID-19 GBV analysis for the Maldives documented a **29% increase in GBV case reports** comparing 2019 to 2020 data in some reporting categories – a figure that contrasts with the FPA's overall intake figure, suggesting the counting methodology requires careful attention.[^400]

**Women's labour market impacts:** Women were twice as likely as men to stop working during the pandemic (NBS COVID Rapid Assessment 2020); 22% of female manufacturing workers stopped compared to 8% of males in the same sector. The loss of income increased women's economic dependency on partners at exactly the moment when household stress was highest.[^401]

**Health service disruption:** ANC attendance – while high at 97% in the pre-pandemic baseline – was disrupted during peak lockdown periods. The MMR spike to 60 per 100,000 in 2021 reflects health service disruption effects extending into the period of delayed care-seeking. Contraceptive supply chains were disrupted, contributing to reports of increased unintended pregnancies.[^402]

**Mental health deterioration:** The Mental Health chapter (§9.X) documents that the pandemic period saw significant increases in reported mental health presentations, with women's presentations disproportionately affected. The self-harm data – 74% female in the MoSFD 2024 statistics – reflects post-pandemic patterns in which pandemic-era mental health deterioration has persisted.[^403]

### 5.39.3 COVID Policy Response and Gender

The Maldivian government's COVID-19 policy response – while not gender-specific in its primary health and economic management – had gendered dimensions:

**School closures and caregiving:** The closure of schools from March 2020 placed full caregiving responsibility on mothers in dual-income households. No emergency childcare provision was established. The implicit assumption that women would absorb the caregiving burden – consistent with the broader unpaid care work patterns documented in §5.9 – shaped both the design and impact of school closure policy.

**Economic support measures:** COVID economic support packages – including employment support, small business assistance, and income transfers – were not gender-disaggregated in design. The extent to which support reached women in informal employment and women who had stopped working (rather than been formally laid off) is unclear from available data.

**DV response adaptation:** The FPA and FPA-partnered shelter services implemented some adaptive measures during the pandemic – phone-based case management, extended shelter hours, coordination with police for emergency callouts. These adaptations were commendable but demonstrated the fragility of a system that had not systematically prepared for scenarios in which physical office access was constrained.[^404]

### 5.39.4 Post-COVID Recovery and Gender

The post-COVID recovery trajectory (2022-2024) has been uneven from a gender perspective:

- The tourism sector's rapid recovery – the primary engine of GDP recovery – has not significantly increased female tourism employment, as the structural barriers documented in §5.30 remained unchanged
- Women's labour force participation recovery has lagged men's in available data
- The MMR recovery (34 per 100,000 in 2022, down from the 60 peak in 2021) has returned below the 2012 level of 49, though the volatility across these years highlights the fragility of maternal health gains in a small population
- Mental health presentations, particularly for young women, remain elevated above pre-pandemic baselines in available MoSFD data
- The FPA case volume returning to pre-pandemic levels suggests that the shadow pandemic's DV surge has not abated through post-pandemic recovery – access to reporting services recovered to pre-pandemic levels, not violence levels declining[^405]

---


## 5.80 COVID-19 Economic Response: The Gender Finance Gap


### 5.117.1 The COVID-19 Lending Discrimination

The Legal Framework chapter's documentation of COVID-19 financial relief records a stark gender disparity that has not been prominently featured in post-pandemic gender analysis:

- **COVID-19 relief loans went 15% to women versus 74% to men**
- Only 3 out of 7 commercial loan schemes had special provisions for women
- **Only 20% of SDFC (Small and Medium Enterprise Development Finance Corporation) approved loans went to female-based businesses**

These figures reflect the intersection of gender and the formal financial system: women are less likely to hold the collateral, credit history, or formal business registration that loan eligibility criteria typically require. Where COVID-19 relief was channelled through the existing financial system rather than social protection mechanisms, the existing system's gender biases were amplified.

The contrast with the Single Parent Allowance – which targets women – and the Aasandha scheme – which is universal – illustrates a pattern: targeted women's social protection reaches women, but credit and economic recovery programmes that operate through market mechanisms default to male beneficiaries unless gender-specific provisions are mandated.[^582]

### 5.117.2 The Employment Gender Gap: Legal Framework Provisions

The Employment Act (2008) prohibits gender-based discrimination in employment, covering:
- Equal pay for equal work
- Prohibition of discrimination in hiring, promotion, and termination
- Maternity leave provisions

However, the Legal Framework chapter documents the gap between these provisions and outcomes:
- Gender pay gap of 13–20% (ADB estimate)
- Women concentrated in government employment (43% of women workers in government sector vs. lower representation in private sector and resort industry)
- Resort industry employment of Maldivian women at approximately 3% of the resort workforce – a structural exclusion sustained by accommodation requirements, alcohol service, and industry norms despite the Employment Act's non-discrimination provisions

The Employment Act's equal pay provisions are largely unenforceable in practice: there is no systematic pay transparency requirement, no gender pay gap reporting mandate, and no regulatory body with the capacity to conduct sector-wide pay audits. Enforcement depends on individual complaint, and the 3.7% rate of women receiving any legal assistance in violence cases suggests that legal complaint pathways are equally underutilised for employment discrimination.[^583]

---


## 5.81 The Substance Abuse-GBV Nexus: A Detailed Analysis


### 5.64.1 Substance Use Patterns and Gender

The Maldives has a significant substance abuse problem, primarily among young men. The **National Substance Dependency Centre (NSDC)** treatment admission data documents that **94% of persons receiving substance abuse treatment are male**. The primary substances of concern are:

- **Heroin and opioids:** A well-documented heroin addiction problem in Malé, with supply chain connections documented in the 2000s-2010s. Opioid addiction among young male Maldivians has been associated with both criminal activity (to finance addiction) and family breakdown.

- **Cannabis:** Widespread recreational use documented across demographic groups; legal status criminalised under the Penal Code.

- **Alcohol:** Alcohol is prohibited for Maldivian Muslims under Islamic law; criminalised for Maldivians but available in resort islands for non-Maldivian tourists. Maldivian alcohol consumption occurs in violation of the prohibition and is associated with young men who have exposure to resort employment or international travel.

- **Prescription drugs and novel psychoactive substances:** A growing category in Maldivian substance abuse, including benzodiazepines and synthetic cannabinoids (increasingly detected in the Maldivian context).[^473]

### 5.64.2 The Causal Pathway to GBV

Substance abuse by male partners is one of the most consistently documented proximate triggers for domestic violence escalation – in Maldivian case records, in survivor testimonies documented through the FPA and Hope for Women, and in the broader South Asian DV literature. The causal pathway operates through multiple mechanisms:

**Acute intoxication:** Alcohol and stimulant intoxication lowers inhibition and impairs judgment, facilitating violent behaviour that the individual might not engage in when sober. Acute intoxication is documented as a trigger for serious physical assault in Maldivian DV case records.

**Withdrawal and craving:** The periods between substance use – particularly during withdrawal – are associated with irritability, aggression, and emotional volatility that can precipitate DV incidents.

**Economic stress from addiction:** Addiction imposes significant financial costs – on the addicted individual and the household. Financial stress associated with funding addiction is documented as a proximate trigger for DV in the context of household arguments about money.

**Social isolation and relationship deterioration:** Addiction progressively damages relationships, trust, and communication. The relationship deterioration associated with long-term addiction creates conditions in which violence risk is elevated – particularly if the addicted partner perceives the non-addicted partner as judgmental, threatening, or a source of shame.

**Childhood trauma linkage:** A significant proportion of male substance abusers have childhood histories of trauma – including exposure to DV in their families of origin. The intergenerational cycle of violence (witnessed DV → childhood trauma → substance use → adult DV perpetration) is relevant to the Maldivian context, where the Fulu 2007 study documented high rates of childhood DV exposure.[^474]

### 5.64.3 The Treatment and GBV System Disconnect

A significant problem in the Maldivian GBV response system is the near-complete separation between the substance abuse treatment system (NSDC-based) and the DV response system (FPA-based). In cases where substance abuse is a driver of DV, effective intervention ideally requires simultaneous engagement with both dimensions – treating the addiction as part of the DV response, or at minimum coordinating between the two systems. In the current architecture:

- The NSDC focuses on addiction treatment without a dedicated DV co-occurrence protocol
- The FPA focuses on survivor case management without a standard protocol for cases where the perpetrator is known to be substance-dependent
- Courts that order DV perpetrators to treatment typically order anger management (where it exists) rather than substance abuse treatment
- The children of substance-abusing DV perpetrators receive no systematic co-response from child protection services

The AIM Project's coordination mandate includes the health sector, and the NSDC is nominally within scope as a coordination partner. Whether the AIM Project's implementation has created effective FPA-NSDC coordination is not documented in available public sources.[^475]

---


## 5.82 Women in the Drug Policy System: Invisibility and Inadequate Services


### 5.110.1 The Data Invisibility of Female Drug Use

The Maldives' drug policy system is built almost entirely around male drug users and male offenders. **No systematic data on female drug use exists** – making women essentially invisible in drug policy design, treatment planning, and resource allocation.

Female drug use is believed to be significantly underreported due to:
- Stronger social stigma for women – drug use carries compounded shame for women in a context where gender norms are already constraining
- Fewer pathways to detection – women are less likely to be stopped and searched by police, less likely to be caught in drug raids, and less likely to enter the criminal justice pipeline
- The near-total absence of gender-sensitive treatment services – there is no pathway into treatment that a woman would find accessible, private, and culturally appropriate

The 2010 World Bank evaluation of the drug treatment system noted that there was **no access to detoxification services for females** – a fundamental gap that left women with substance use disorders without any treatment entry point.[^562]

### 5.110.2 Gendered Impacts of Drug Use

While women are less visible in official data, they are not unaffected by the drug crisis. The documented gender impacts include:

**Women as secondary victims of male drug use:** The AIM Project Baseline Assessment (2022) documented the GBV-substance abuse nexus – male substance use is a significant driver of domestic violence, with intoxication documented as a factor in physical violence by intimate partners. Women bear the economic and care burden of households where a male partner is incarcerated for drug offences (47% of prisoners are drug offenders) or in treatment.

**Women in sex trade to fund drug use:** The drug policy literature documents sex trade involvement among substance users, with both males and females engaging in transactional sex to fund drug use. Women in sex trade are at elevated risk of STIs, violence, and trafficking.

**Women as drug couriers:** The tourism industry's role in drug supply chains has been documented, with women sometimes used as couriers due to assumptions about reduced police scrutiny.

**Treatment access:** The Drug Rehabilitation Centre at Dhoonidhoo accommodated "a dormitory of up to 20 women" – a small capacity relative to the overall male treatment capacity, reflecting the system's design orientation. No gender-sensitive aftercare services exist.[^563]

### 5.110.3 Policy Implications

The absence of gender-disaggregated drug data is not a minor statistical gap – it means that policy decisions about treatment capacity, prevention programmes, and outreach are made without knowing how drug use patterns differ between men and women, what drives women's use, what barriers they face to treatment, and what they need to achieve recovery. A drug policy designed for male users will systematically fail female users.

The GEAP's economic empowerment strategies for vulnerable women include "addressing drug dependency among women" as a medium-term direction (noted in the SAP light chapter on Women and Gender). No operational programme currently exists to implement this commitment. The NDA does not have a gender desk or gender focal point. The 14-year-old baseline (2011-12 drug prevalence survey) does not disaggregate by sex in a way that enables gender analysis.[^564]

---


# Part 15: Intersectionality and Vulnerable Sub-Populations


## 5.83 Vulnerable Sub-Populations: Intersectional Analysis


### 5.31.1 Women with Disabilities

Women with disabilities face intersecting discrimination – the compound effect of gender inequality and disability discrimination – that the CRPD Periodic Report Draft (Part XVIII-A) addresses in the disability domain and that this chapter must address from the gender policy perspective.

**LFPR for women with disabilities:** The CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report (2025) documents a female labour force participation rate of **28%** for women with disabilities – compared with 44% for women overall and 80% for men overall. This represents a triple disadvantage: women with disabilities have substantially lower participation than men with disabilities, and substantially lower participation than women without disabilities.[^350]

**Access to GBV services:** Women with disabilities who experience domestic violence face additional barriers to accessing DV response services:
- Physical accessibility of FPA offices, shelters, and courts may not accommodate women with mobility impairments
- Communication accessibility (sign language interpretation for Deaf women, alternative format information for women with visual impairments) is not systematically provided
- Social isolation that can accompany disability – particularly in outer island communities – may increase both DV risk and barriers to disclosure
- The assumption that women with disabilities are asexual (reducing recognition of their SRHR) or that violence against them is less serious contributes to underreporting[^351]

**Reproductive rights:** Women with disabilities face specific SRHR vulnerabilities:
- Documented cases of women with intellectual disabilities being sterilised without adequate informed consent – a form of reproductive violence
- Limited access to contraceptive services adapted to disability needs
- Obstetric care that may not be accessible or disability-inclusive

**Economic vulnerability:** Women with disabilities have lower educational attainment, lower employment, and lower income than women without disabilities, compounding dependency and reducing capacity to leave abusive situations. The SDFC's women's credit programmes do not appear to have disability-specific provisions.[^352]

### 5.31.2 Migrant Women

Migrant women working in the Maldives – primarily from Sri Lanka and Bangladesh, predominantly employed in domestic service and some formal sector roles – represent a significant and specifically vulnerable population within the gender policy domain.

**Scale and profile:** No authoritative census of migrant workers by gender exists in available public data. Estimates derived from work permit statistics and migrant labour research indicate that female migrant workers number in the thousands, with domestic workers constituting the largest category. Sri Lankan and Bangladeshi women predominate; some women from Nepal, the Philippines, and other South Asian countries also work in the Maldives.[^353]

**Legal status and protection:** Migrant workers in the Maldives are governed by work permit regulations administered by the Ministry of Economic Development. Legal protections include:
- The Employment Act (which nominally applies to all workers regardless of nationality)
- Immigration Act provisions governing work permit conditions

In practice, enforcement of Employment Act protections for migrant workers – particularly domestic workers in private households – is extremely limited. The private household context removes the ordinary labour inspection mechanisms that apply to formal workplaces.[^354]

**Domestic worker vulnerability:** As documented in §5.16, domestic workers face elevated trafficking and exploitation risk. Specific documented vulnerabilities include:
- Passport confiscation (illegal under Maldivian law but widely practiced)
- Non-payment or underpayment of wages
- Restriction of movement
- Absence of a weekly day of rest
- Verbal, physical, and sexual abuse by employers
- No access to healthcare (health insurance coverage is not routinely provided to domestic workers)
- Language barriers limiting ability to seek help or navigate legal systems[^355]

**DV in migrant worker households:** Migrant women in intimate relationships with Maldivian men – whether registered marriages or informal partnerships – face the same DV vulnerabilities as Maldivian women, with additional barriers: limited language skills, no family support network, potential immigration status precarity if reporting requires attention from authorities, and limited familiarity with the Maldivian GBV response system. The FPA does not have dedicated services for migrant women, and the language of service provision is primarily Dhivehi.

### 5.31.3 Women in Poverty

The poverty-gender intersection is documented across this chapter: female-headed households have higher poverty rates; women in outer atolls face NEET rates of 42.7%; divorced women face housing insecurity; single mothers face compounded vulnerabilities. A dedicated focus on women in poverty as a sub-population highlights several additional dimensions:

**Social protection access:** The Social Protection System (Aasandha health coverage, the National Social Protection Agency's poverty assistance programme, and pension provisions) nominally covers all Maldivians. Women in poverty, however, may face barriers to accessing social protection:
- Administrative barriers: registration requirements that assume literacy, formal address documentation, and ability to travel to administrative offices
- Knowledge barriers: low awareness of specific entitlements, particularly among outer island women
- The Madhanee Mahala (housing programme) allocation that prioritises male household heads in practice

**Poverty traps:** The economic constraints on poor women create poverty traps that are harder to escape than those facing poor men: limited childcare prevents employment; limited employment maintains poverty; poverty prevents childcare investment; and the cycle repeats. Breaking these traps requires simultaneous interventions across childcare, employment, training, and income support – the type of integrated programming that rarely occurs within sector-based service delivery.[^356]

**Poverty and GBV:** The evidence on poverty-GBV interaction is bidirectional: poverty increases DV risk (economic stress, power dynamics, reduced exit options) and DV increases poverty risk (economic abuse, job loss following DV trauma, costs of legal proceedings). Women who are both poor and DV survivors face compound barriers that no single intervention can adequately address.[^357]

### 5.31.4 Older Women and Elderly Gender Inequality

Women aged 60 and above face gender inequality patterns specific to their life stage and cohort:

**Pension access:** The Maldives Pension Office provides a universal non-contributory old-age allowance and a contributory occupational pension. Women's lower lifetime formal sector employment means lower contributory pension accumulation; women whose economic activity was in the informal sector or as unpaid caregivers have no contributory pension entitlement at all. The universal allowance provides a floor, but the gender gap in contributory pension accumulation reflects and extends the lifetime economic inequality documented throughout this chapter.[^358]

**Social isolation:** Older women – particularly widows and divorced women who have not remarried – may face significant social isolation in both urban and outer island settings. Extended family structures that historically provided older women with social roles (as grandmothers, household advisors) have been disrupted by urbanisation and nuclear household formation. The Elderly chapter (Part VII) documents this in the elderly domain; the gender dimension is that older women's social isolation tends to be more acute than older men's, reflecting the gendered social networks that both support and constrain women across the life course.[^359]

**Late-life health access:** Older women's health needs include reproductive health cessation (menopause), bone density and osteoporosis concerns associated with low calcium intake and limited outdoor activity, and the long-term health consequences of childbearing (particularly in the cohort of women who gave birth in settings without skilled birth attendance). The health system's attention to older women's specific health needs is limited beyond the basic primary care framework.[^360]

**Domestic violence in older age:** DV against older women – both by intimate partners and by adult children – is a recognised but underdocumented phenomenon. The FPA case registry does not systematically track survivor age in a way that allows analysis of DV against older women as a specific category. The elderly DV problem likely overlaps with elder abuse covered in the Elderly chapter, but from the FPA's perspective it is not systematically addressed as a distinct category.[^361]

### 5.31.5 Young Women and Girls: A Life Course Perspective

Young women and girls (ages 10-24) face a specific constellation of gender inequality that the ASRH and education data document, but that benefits from a life course framing:

**Early adolescence (10-14):** The period of puberty and early secondary school involves the physical changes that social norms begin to regulate more aggressively – dress codes, mobility restrictions, social supervision. Girls who experience childhood sexual abuse (estimated 12.2% nationally) are typically in this age cohort at the time of most severe abuse. School-based safety mechanisms, mandatory teacher reporting, and SRH education are the primary protection tools – all of which are inadequate in the Maldivian system.

**Mid-adolescence (15-19):** The period of risk for early marriage, early pregnancy, and education dropout for girls (even as boys in the same cohort are more likely to drop out without those specific triggers). Secondary school completion is critical for girls in this cohort; the absence of re-entry pathways after pregnancy or marriage dropout permanently affects their lifetime economic prospects.

**Early adulthood (20-24):** Young women in this cohort who have remained in education face the transition to a labour market with documented discrimination (interview questions about marital status, NEET rates, LFPR gap). Those who have married early may already be experiencing DV (FPA data shows young married women as a significant proportion of DV survivor caseloads). Those who have had children face the childcare gap that limits employment options.

The SDG 5 target on ending harmful practices affecting young women – child marriage, FGM/C – and the CRC's provisions for adolescent girls together provide the international framework for addressing this life course phase. Implementation through the school system, health system, and community-level WDC network is the primary channel – all of which have documented gaps in Maldivian practice.[^362]

---


## 5.84 The Intersection of Gender with Other Protected Characteristics


### 5.103.1 Gender and Age: A Life Course Overview

Gender inequality intersects with age in ways that produce distinctly different gender experiences at different life stages:

**Childhood (0-11):** Girls and boys experience broadly similar treatment in the earliest years; differentiation increases with puberty. FGM/C affects girls primarily in infancy. CSA affects both boys and girls but has documented female majority in Maldivian data.

**Adolescence (12-18):** Girls face intensifying dress and mobility constraints; risk of early marriage/cohabitation; risk of CSA and sexual coercion; educational attrition from early marriage or pregnancy. Boys face elevated dropout risk from different causes (lack of engagement, need to contribute income).

**Young adulthood (18-25):** Women face labour market barriers including interview discrimination and childcare gaps; NEET rates peak for outer atoll young women. Men may face higher substance abuse risk in this cohort.

**Prime working age (25-45):** DV peaks in this age range for women (documented in FPA caseload age distribution); economic dependency compounds with care responsibilities; political participation opportunities emerge through council quotas.

**Mature working age (45-60):** Women approaching retirement face pension gap accumulated over a working life with lower wages and care interruptions; health needs include menopause (inadequately addressed by health system) and chronic disease prevention.

**Older age (60+):** Gender pension gap materialises; social isolation risk for divorced or widowed women; health system inadequately addresses older women's specific needs; elder abuse risk intersects with gender.[^539]

### 5.103.2 Gender and Disability: The Double Discrimination

As documented in §5.31.1, women with disabilities face intersecting discrimination – the compound of gender inequality and disability discrimination. The CRPD (Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities) provides the international framework for disability rights; the CEDAW provides the framework for gender equality. Their intersection – when both discrimination grounds operate simultaneously – produces outcomes worse than either alone.

**Specific intersections:**
- Women with physical disabilities face barriers to FPA and shelter access (physical accessibility)
- Women with intellectual disabilities face elevated GBV risk (exploitation) and inadequate protection systems
- Women with sensory disabilities (Deaf, blind) face communication barriers in all stages of the GBV response system
- Women with mental health conditions face both elevated GBV vulnerability and barriers to disclosure (credibility concerns, medication management, mental health service access)

The CRPD Periodic Report Draft (Part XVIII-A) addresses disability rights comprehensively. This chapter has addressed the gender-disability intersection specifically at §5.31.1. The two frameworks together provide the analytical basis for intersectional programming that addresses both disability and gender simultaneously.[^540]

### 5.103.3 Gender and Poverty: Intersectional Programming

The gender-poverty intersection – documented extensively in §5.73 – demands intersectional programming approaches that address both dimensions simultaneously rather than treating them as separate problems with separate interventions. The most effective international models for addressing gender-poverty intersection:

**Cash transfers with social norms components:** Conditional or unconditional cash transfers that provide direct income support to poor women, combined with social norm change programming (healthy relationships, women's empowerment) that addresses the violence risk that poverty compounds. Bangladesh's BRAC programme is the most documented model.

**Women's savings groups with embedded protection:** Savings groups (similar to traditional Maldivian *bodufenvalin*) enhanced with GBV awareness content, referral mechanisms, and peer support networks – so that women have both economic assets and protection knowledge simultaneously.

**Integrated social protection:** Social protection packages that combine income support, health coverage (Aasandha), housing assistance, and childcare in a single household support framework – rather than requiring vulnerable women to navigate separate systems for each need.

The Maldivian NSPA's current approach is income support-focused without systematic integration with GBV services, childcare, or housing. An integrated package approach – piloted in the highest-vulnerability outer atolls – could demonstrate the additional impact of integrated vs. siloed social protection for gender equality outcomes.[^541]

---


## 5.85 Women with Disabilities: From Structural Invisibility to Dual Marginalisation


### 5.108.1 Demographic Reality

The 2022 Census, using the Washington Group Short Set methodology, identified **24,401 persons with disabilities** in the Maldives – **55% of whom are women** (13,456 women; 10,945 men). This means women constitute the majority of the disabled population, yet disability policy has historically been designed around a gender-neutral (effectively male) default.

The gender ratio in the National Disability Registry (NDR) differs markedly from the Census: the NDR shows **58% men and 42% women** – the reverse of the Census gender split. This discrepancy indicates that women face greater barriers to registration than men. The Hameed et al. (2022) study confirmed that registration coverage is lowest among women (alongside older adults, those in Malé, and those with sensory impairments), suggesting that the administrative systems intended to identify and support women with disabilities are least effective at reaching them.[^554]

### 5.108.2 Pre-2019 Structural Invisibility

Before 2019, there was no policy or legislative instrument specifically addressing the intersectional situation of women with disabilities. Disability policy treated persons with disabilities as a homogeneous group, while gender policy did not integrate disability dimensions. The CRPD Periodic Report Draft (2025) acknowledged directly: **"Women with disabilities were effectively invisible in both systems."**

Research and anecdotal evidence confirmed that women with disabilities faced compounded disadvantage:
- Higher rates of exclusion from education and employment
- Greater dependence on family caregivers (predominantly female family members providing care without formal support)
- Elevated vulnerability to GBV with reduced access to protection services – shelters not physically accessible, FPA lacking disability-responsive case management, sign language interpretation not available at FPA intake points
- Institutional barriers to accessing justice – courts conducting proceedings without interpreters, legal documents inaccessible

The 2022 Census confirmed the demographic reality of women's majority status in the disabled population. The CRPD Committee, in its August 2025 review, explicitly noted the "significant absence of strategies, policies, and disaggregated data specifically addressing women with disabilities" and identified this as a structural gap requiring attention.[^555]

### 5.108.3 Economic Marginalisation: The Compounded Labour Market Exclusion

Census 2022 data shows stark economic disparities at the gender-disability intersection:
- Labour force participation: **41% for men with disabilities, 28% for women with disabilities** – a 13 percentage point gap within the disabled population, added on top of the general gender LFPR gap
- Economic opportunities allocated to persons with disabilities have largely benefited men, with no gender quotas for women within disability employment schemes
- Women with disabilities are not encouraged to live independently, typically relying on families, which perpetuates economic dependency and vulnerability

Family members have documented patterns of obstructing women with disabilities from employment, "especially for jobs outside the island." Some FGD participants reported "resigning from jobs because their husbands were unhappy with them working following pregnancy." This intersection of disability with the broader gender dynamic of male control over women's economic participation is not addressed in either the GEAP or the NAPDI.[^556]

A significant 2024 development: the government introduced a **five-year financial scheme of MVR 100 million to support women entrepreneurs, with 25% of allocations reserved for women with disabilities**. This is the first specific economic inclusion measure targeting women with disabilities as a distinct group and represents a meaningful policy departure from the gender-neutral disability policy of the previous period.

### 5.108.4 Violence Against Women and Girls with Disabilities

The intersection of gender and disability in relation to violence is among the most serious documented in the Maldivian context. The CRPD Periodic Report Draft (2025) recorded that **the prevalence of sexual violence against children with disabilities was found to be significantly high at 60%, with a large majority of survivors being girls with disabilities**.

The risk factors are structural:
- Women with disabilities are "more vulnerable to violence (including sexual violence) and exploitation" due to dependence on caregivers
- FPA shelter facilities are not physically accessible to women with sensory or physical impairments
- Sign language interpretation is not systematically available at FPA intake points, making disclosure by Deaf women practically impossible in the standard intake process
- Protection orders – the primary safety mechanism – are inaccessible when survivors cannot communicate in standard formats
- The justice system has documented cases of court proceedings involving children with disabilities being conducted "without interpreters"

The absence of accessible reporting mechanisms is not incidental – it is a structural feature of a response system designed without reference to the disability dimensions of GBV. The CEDAW Committee has urged the Maldives to submit information on women with disabilities and ensure equal access to GBV services, but the gap between this recommendation and operational practice remains substantial.[^557]

### 5.108.5 The Caregiving Burden: Women as the Invisible Support System

A cross-cutting finding across disability, elderly, and child protection is the gendered nature of informal caregiving. The 2024 UNPRPD assessment observed that **primary support persons for persons with disabilities are mostly women**. For the elderly, **69% of caretakers for bedridden elderly patients are women**. For children with disabilities in education, mothers are the primary advocates navigating an inaccessible system.

Women caregivers require flexible workplace policies, respite services, and psychosocial support – but no formal support systems exist for family caregivers in the Maldives. The Ageing Policy draft (2024) identified caregiver support as a critical gap and called for development of respite care and caregiver training, but provides awareness strategies rather than structural solutions.

The economic cost of this unpaid care burden falls entirely on women. The GEAP's acknowledgment that parental leave schemes and shared care responsibilities are needed is a recognition of this pattern; the absence of any childcare infrastructure (fewer than 4 formal daycare centres nationally) means that the "sharing" of care remains theoretical as long as care services do not exist.[^558]

---


## 5.86 Elderly Women: Pension Gap, Caregiving, and Invisible Ageing


### 5.109.1 Demographic Profile of Elderly Women

Women constitute **46.1% of the population aged 60+** and **48.1% of those aged 80+**. Life expectancy at birth is 75 for women and 73 for men – a two-year female longevity advantage that means women will constitute a growing share of the oldest and most care-dependent elderly cohort as the population ages.

The demographic transformation is rapid: total fertility has fallen from 6.4 children per woman in 1990 to 1.7 by 2022 (below replacement). This means the cohort of potential family caregivers (daughters, daughters-in-law) is shrinking relative to the elderly population that depends on them – accelerating the collision between female caregiving expectations and female labour market participation.[^559]

### 5.109.2 The Pension System and Older Women

The Old-Age Basic Pension of MVR 5,000/month (2024) provides near-universal coverage for Maldivians aged 65+, regardless of gender, employment history, or contribution record. This universality is critically important for older women, because **99.1% of women's employment is in the informal sector** (National Ageing Policy situational analysis, HIES 2019). Informal employment generates no contributory pension entitlements – meaning that without the universal basic pension, virtually no older woman would have any income in old age.

The pension adequacy problem documented in §5.57 is gender-differentiated:
- Older women are less likely to have supplementary income sources (savings, investment, formal pension) beyond the basic pension
- Older widowed women who lived as economic dependents of their husbands may have no savings or assets of their own
- Older divorced women face the accumulated effect of career interruptions, lower wages, and lost maintenance payments – all converging on pension-age poverty

At MVR 5,000/month, the pension covers basic needs in outer islands more adequately than in Malé, where rent alone can consume most of the payment. The gender dimension of this geographic pension adequacy gap has not been systematically analysed.[^560]

### 5.109.3 Women as the Caregiving Infrastructure

The Ageing Policy (2024 draft) explicitly acknowledged the gendered nature of elderly care: "the caregiver burden is often solely on women." The quantitative confirmation: **69% of the 67 patients receiving home-care visits in Greater Malé are cared for by women**. Outside Greater Malé – where no formal home care programme exists – the proportion is even higher, as all care falls on informal (predominantly female) family members.

Taj and Suzana (2023) documented the 69% female caretaker figure as part of analysis of the MoSFD home care programme. The day care facility in Addu Atoll was established explicitly to "ease caregiver burden 'which is mostly on women.'"

As more women enter formal employment, the traditional assumption that daughters or daughters-in-law will be available full-time to care for elderly parents becomes untenable. The government's MIPAA statement acknowledged this tension but offered no structural policy response. The conflict between caregiving obligations and employment is a gender equality issue that the GEAP, NAPDI, and Ageing Policy each partially acknowledge but none addresses with binding programmatic commitments.[^561]

---


# Part 16: The Muizzu Administration and Current Government Policy


## 5.87 The Muizzu Administration (2023-present): Gender Policy Under the Current Government


### 5.34.1 Political Context

President Mohamed Muizzu assumed office in November 2023, marking a political transition from the Ibrahim Mohamed Solih administration (2018-2023). The Muizzu administration's gender policy commitments and their early implementation record represent the most current context for understanding the political economy of gender equality in the Maldives.

The Muizzu administration entered office with a political base including more conservative constituencies than the Solih administration, and with a regional geopolitical reorientation toward a stronger China relationship and a more distanced relationship with India. The political implications for gender policy are complex: the administration has maintained formal commitments to CEDAW compliance and SDG 5, while governing through a political coalition that includes parties with more conservative social policy positions.[^371]

### 5.34.2 Early Gender Policy Record

As of early 2025, the Muizzu administration's gender policy record includes:

**Continuity commitments:**
- The AIM Project (EU-UNDP-MoSFD) has continued under the administration, with ADB GESI Project and SAILS Project similarly continuing
- The CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report has been submitted (2025), demonstrating continued engagement with the treaty body process
- MoSFD has continued operating the FPA and associated services

**Areas of concern documented in civil society and international assessment:**
- The GRFS 2023 implementation is at early stages; it is unclear whether the current administration's environmental and economic priorities will sustain investment in gender-responsive climate financing
- The childcare gap remains unaddressed – no new childcare infrastructure investment has been announced
- No parliamentary gender quota proposal has emerged from the administration
- The CEDAW reservation on Article 16 remains unchanged
- Family law reform toward greater gender equality has not been publicly prioritised[^372]

**Cabinet composition:** The initial Muizzu Cabinet included a small number of women in ministerial positions, at a proportion broadly consistent with prior administrations – below parity but not at the extreme exclusion that would represent a regression. Specific portfolio assignments are documented in government records.

### 5.34.3 The Political Economy of Future Reform

The political economy analysis of future gender equality reform in the Maldives under any administration must account for:

**Electoral incentives:** Women constitute approximately half the voting population. Gender equality policies – particularly those addressing DV, which affects a large proportion of women – have potential electoral support. However, the linkage between women voters' preferences and electoral reward for gender equality policy is mediated by political party structures, candidate access, and the religious framing of specific policy debates.

**Coalition constraints:** Any government that relies on more conservative coalition partners faces constraints on social policy reform, particularly on family law matters with Islamic jurisprudence dimensions. Reform is more achievable when it can be framed as consistent with Islamic values (as with DV prevention) than when it requires explicit departure from Islamic family law norms (as with inheritance equality).

**Development partner leverage:** Development partner financing – EU through AIM, ADB through GESI/SAILS/ARISE, UNFPA through RMNCAH – provides a degree of leverage for gender policy continuity across administrations. Projects that require government co-financing commitments and implementation partner relationships create institutional continuity even through political transitions.

**Civil society persistence:** As documented in §5.19.7, Maldivian civil society organisations – Hope for Women, MDN, FLC – have maintained gender equality advocacy across multiple administrations. Their institutional persistence represents the most reliable engine of reform pressure through the long term.

# Part 17: Action Plans, Strategic Frameworks, and Donor Programmes


## 5.88 Action Plans, Strategic Frameworks, and Policy Coherence


### 5.27.1 National Gender Equality Action Plans

The Maldives has produced a series of gender equality action plans across successive political administrations. The pattern – frequent plan production with limited implementation continuity – is a documented feature of gender policy in many developing country contexts and is particularly acute in the Maldivian system given the high frequency of political change.[^317]

Key gender-relevant strategic frameworks as of 2024-25:

**National Gender Equality Policy (2019-2023):** Developed under the Solih administration, this policy established the overarching framework for gender equality programming aligned with SDG 5 commitments. The policy included commitments on GBV response, women's economic empowerment, political participation, and SRHR. Implementation progress was mixed – some components (local council quota, AIM Project, ADB GESI) advanced, others (GRB compliance, childcare infrastructure, CEDAW reservation withdrawal) did not.[^318]

**GBV Response Framework (2020-2024):** A specific multi-agency framework for GBV response developed with AIM Project support, establishing coordination protocols between FPA, police, health, and courts. The framework was developed but the integrated information management system that would make it operationally coherent has not been fully implemented. [^319]

**RMNCAH Strategy 2020-2025:** The Reproductive, Maternal, Newborn, Child and Adolescent Health Strategy provides the health sector's gender-relevant operational framework, covering maternal health services, SRHR, and adolescent health. Partially implemented with UNFPA support.[^320]

**Gender-Responsive Climate Financing Strategy 2023:** The GRFS, discussed in §5.15, is the most recent major gender strategic framework. Its implementation is in early stages.

### 5.27.2 SDG 5 and Gender Policy Coherence

The **Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) – particularly Goal 5 (Gender Equality)** – provide the international monitoring framework against which Maldivian gender policy is assessed. Maldives' SDG reporting shows:

- **SDG 5.1:** Legal frameworks for gender equality – GEA 2016 in place; CEDAW reservation an outstanding gap
- **SDG 5.2:** Violence against women – data improving (FPA statistics) but implementation gap severe
- **SDG 5.3:** Harmful practices – FGM/C declining; child marriage at 2.2%; polygamy ongoing
- **SDG 5.4:** Unpaid care and domestic work – 19:8.1 hours ratio; no policy response
- **SDG 5.5:** Women's political participation – local council 33% (positive); parliament 4.6% (severe gap)
- **SDG 5.6:** SRHR – high obstetric coverage; contraceptive unmet need 29%; abortion law restrictive
- **SDG 5.a:** Economic rights and land – data gap; inheritance law inequality
- **SDG 5.b:** Technology and women – growing access; digital GBV concerns
- **SDG 5.c:** Policy and legislation – GRB <40% compliance; implementation gap persists[^321]

The SDG framework provides a monitoring structure, but monitoring alone does not drive implementation. The challenge is converting SDG reporting commitments into budget allocations, institutional capacities, and accountability mechanisms that produce actual change in women's lived experiences.

### 5.27.3 Policy Coherence Across Sectors

A recurring finding across the evidence reviewed in this chapter is **policy incoherence** – situations where policies in different sectors work at cross-purposes with gender equality:

- The **Penal Code's zina provision** works against the **DVPA's** goal of encouraging rape disclosure
- The **abortion law's** restriction works against the **RMNCAH Strategy's** maternal health goals
- The **childcare infrastructure gap** works against the **Gender Equality Act's** prohibition on employment discrimination
- The **Family Act's** maintenance enforcement weakness undermines the **DVPA's** goal of enabling survivors to leave abusive relationships
- **Tourism sector employment policies** that focus on Maldivianisation numbers rather than gender composition work against women's economic empowerment goals[^322]

Achieving policy coherence – ensuring that sector policies align with and reinforce rather than undermine gender equality commitments – requires cross-sectoral coordination at senior government level. The Cabinet-level Gender Equality Coordination Mechanism established under the GEA provides the formal institutional space for this coordination, but its operation has been limited in practice. The MoSFD as the gender focal ministry has limited authority over other ministries' policies, meaning that inter-ministerial policy coherence depends on political commitment at the highest level rather than on institutional enforcement.[^323]

## 5.89 National Action Plans: Gender in the Development Planning System


### 5.63.1 The Strategic Planning Architecture

Maldivian national development planning is structured through medium-term strategic frameworks – the **Strategic Action Plan (SAP)** – that establish national development priorities for multi-year periods. Gender equality has been incorporated into the SAP architecture with varying levels of prominence across different administrations.

**SAP 2019-2023 (Solih administration):** Gender equality was a cross-cutting priority, with specific targets on:
- GBV case management system development (AIM Project)
- Women's political participation (quota for local councils)
- Women's economic empowerment (SAILS, SDFC women's lending)
- SRHR service access improvement

The SAP provides the medium-term framework within which gender-specific action plans (the National Gender Equality Action Plan, the GBV Response Framework) sit. Coordination between the SAP and sectoral action plans is managed by the National Planning Commission.[^470]

**SAP 2023-2027 (Muizzu administration):** The current SAP has been developed under the Muizzu administration. The extent to which gender equality features as a cross-cutting priority – and whether the specific programme commitments of the previous SAP are continued or restructured – is documented in CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report references and government policy announcements. The administration's formal commitments to ongoing development partner gender programmes (AIM, ADB GESI, UNFPA) suggest policy continuity at the operational level even if the rhetorical priority on gender equality has shifted.

### 5.63.2 SDG Localisation and Gender

The Maldives' **Voluntary National Review (VNR)** reports to the UN High-Level Political Forum provide the most comprehensive government self-assessment of progress toward the SDGs, including SDG 5 (Gender Equality). The most recent VNR available (2022) documents:

- **SDG 5.1 (legal frameworks):** GEA in place; CEDAW reservation acknowledged as gap
- **SDG 5.2 (violence against women):** FPA case system operational; prosecution gap documented
- **SDG 5.3 (harmful practices):** FGM/C declining; child marriage data available
- **SDG 5.5 (political participation):** Local council quota success highlighted; parliament gap acknowledged
- **SDG 5.6 (SRHR):** Positive ANC/facility birth statistics; contraception gap acknowledged

The VNR documents notable progress (local council quota, ANC coverage) while acknowledging persistent gaps (prosecution, parliamentary representation, LFPR). The SDG monitoring framework provides a public international accountability structure that complements CEDAW – both point in the same direction but through different mechanisms.[^471]

### 5.63.3 The National Policy on the Prevention of Violence Against Women and Children

A specific National Policy on the Prevention of Violence Against Women and Children – developed in the 2020-2022 period with UNICEF and UNFPA support – provides the government's stated inter-agency framework for GBV prevention programming. Key elements:

- Multi-agency coordination structure with FPA as lead
- Community prevention programming standards
- Referral pathway protocols across FPA, FCPW, health, courts, and WDCs
- Data collection standards for GBV case monitoring

The policy's implementation has been tracked through the AIM Project monitoring framework. As of 2024-25, the multi-agency coordination structure it envisages is partially operational – regular inter-agency meetings occur in Malé – but the information system integration that would make coordination effective across 187 islands does not yet function.[^472]

---


## 5.90 The Gender Equality Action Plan 2022-2026: Full Operational Profile


### 5.105.1 Statutory Basis and Architecture

The **National Gender Equality Action Plan (GEAP) 2022–2026** is the primary operational instrument for gender equality in the Maldives. Unlike other action plans in the social policy architecture, it is a *statutory requirement* – Article 42 of the Gender Equality Act 2016 mandates the development and maintenance of such a plan. This gives the GEAP legal grounding that other plans lack, making it the only gender policy instrument with enforceable institutional backing.

The GEAP derives from and operationalises both the GEA 2016 and the National Gender Equality Policy (2019), translating them into strategies across five policy goals. Its implementation is coordinated by MoSFD as lead agency, with Gender Focal Points established across 25+ implementing agencies. The GEAP's ambition is breadth: it assigns responsibilities across virtually every government ministry, independent institution, and service agency in the country.[^542]

### 5.105.2 The Five Policy Goals: Full Detail

**Policy Goal 1: Leadership and Governance**

Four strategies under this goal address women's political participation:
1. Creating public awareness to increase women's participation in political leadership at all levels
2. Recognising shared parental responsibilities as a precondition for women's political participation
3. Reviewing electoral laws to identify and remove structural barriers to women's political candidacy and leadership
4. Adopting affirmative action measures to achieve equal representation in government bodies
