The strategy list includes both supply-side (awareness, parental leave) and demand-side (quota/affirmative action) approaches, but the GEAP relies primarily on awareness campaigns and electoral law review – neither of which has historically been sufficient to move the needle on women's political representation without mandatory quotas at the parliamentary level. The local council quota (33%) was achieved through a legislative mandate, not through the awareness strategies the GEAP emphasises.[^543]

**Policy Goal 2: Economic Empowerment**

Four strategies address women's economic participation:
1. Supporting programmes for economic empowerment of *vulnerable* women specifically – women with disabilities, elderly women, single mothers, and GBV survivors – recognising that economic vulnerability concentrates in these groups
2. Addressing gender stereotyping and social norms that constrain women's economic choices, including stereotypes that confine women to caring roles and discourage them from technical or traditionally male-dominated occupations
3. Reviewing employment legislation and parental leave schemes to ensure they support working mothers and equitable sharing of care responsibilities
4. Enforcing existing economic empowerment schemes across all economic sectors, with specific attention to sectors from which women are structurally excluded (notably the resort industry)

The explicit inclusion of women with disabilities, elderly women, and GBV survivors in economic empowerment strategies reflects an intersectional approach – recognising that the most economically excluded women are not simply "women" but women at the intersection of multiple disadvantages. However, the strategy does not specify mechanisms for reaching these groups, and as of 2025, no specific programme targets women at the intersection of disability and gender or elderly women.[^544]

**Policy Goal 3: Institutional Gender Mainstreaming**

Seven strategies under this goal constitute the infrastructure through which all other GEAP goals are to be pursued:
1. Updating the MoSFD mandate document to reflect the full scope of gender equality responsibilities
2. Establishing inter-agency mechanisms for the Maldivian Gender Equality Model (MGEM) – the operational framework for gender mainstreaming across government
3. Reviewing all legislation for compliance with CEDAW and the Gender Equality Act, identifying provisions that discriminate directly or indirectly against women
4. Incorporating gender equality into the national curriculum – so that the next generation's attitudes toward gender are shaped through formal education, not just community awareness campaigns
5. Introducing gender-responsive budgeting (GRB) across all ministries, with the Ministry of Finance as the mechanism through which gender perspectives are integrated into the annual budget cycle
6. Integrating gender training into civil service appraisal – making gender equality competency a performance evaluation criterion, not just a training exercise
7. Revising national data collection for sex and age disaggregation – so that all government statistical products enable gender analysis

These seven strategies are mutually reinforcing: without sex-disaggregated data (Strategy 7), GRB (Strategy 5) cannot be designed; without GRB implementation, gender equality commitments remain aspirational; without MGEM (Strategy 2) and appraisal integration (Strategy 6), focal points lack the institutional authority to drive change. As of the SAP light chapter assessment (2024), the Gender Focal Point network remained "incomplete" nearly six years after the GEA was passed – meaning the coordinating architecture for all seven strategies was still being constructed.[^545]

**Policy Goal 4: Elimination of Gender-Based Violence**

Three strategies address the GBV response system:
1. Strengthening institutional and legislative frameworks for GBV and DV response, aligned with the 3rd Domestic Violence Prevention Strategy (2023–2027)
2. Creating support mechanisms for universal Sexual and Reproductive Health Rights (SRHR) education, addressing the knowledge gap documented in community-level assessments
3. Strengthening the health sector's response to GBV – training, protocols, and screening – aligned with the Domestic Violence Prevention Strategy

The GEAP's GBV strategies are notably institutional in character: they focus on strengthening the systems that respond to GBV rather than preventing it at its root causes (social norms, economic dependency, housing constraints). This institutional focus is appropriate but insufficient: prevention requires social norm change that must be driven through the mechanisms in Goals 1, 2, and 3 – which in turn require the institutional infrastructure in Goal 3 that itself remains under construction.[^546]

**Policy Goal 5: Access to Justice**

Three strategies address women's access to legal remedies:
1. Creating awareness for legal empowerment of women – addressing the documented finding (Shakir, 2025) that recently divorced women in Greater Malé were unaware of their legal rights before or during marriage
2. Strengthening protection order mechanisms for GBV victims – addressing the documented failures in protection order enforcement, including eight-month evidence collection delays, breach with no consequences, and perpetrators locating victims through informal networks
3. Ensuring equal participation of men and women as employees and decision-makers in the justice sector – addressing the structural gender imbalance in the judiciary and legal profession

The protection order strategy is the most operationally critical: protection orders issued under the DVPA 2012 are the primary immediate safety tool for DV survivors, but their utility depends entirely on enforcement – which the vulnerability mapping research documented as the weakest link in the entire GBV response chain.[^547]

### 5.105.3 GEAP Evidence Cross-Reference

| GEAP Goal | What the Evidence Shows | Does the Goal Address the Right Problem? | Critical Evidence Gaps |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Leadership and Governance** | Maldives ranked 101st of 136 countries for political empowerment (2013); WDCs assessed as ineffective (Transparency Maldives, 2015); 4.6% women in Parliament (2024) | Partially – awareness campaigns and electoral law review are insufficient; quota legislation is the proven mechanism | No updated political participation data post-2013; no assessment of barriers at local government level specific to women |
| **Economic Empowerment** | 44% female LFPR vs 80% male; 59% of informal sector employment is female; 36 percentage point LFPR gap; women concentrated in lower-paid government employment | Partially – addresses stereotyping and legislation but not the absence of childcare infrastructure or resort sector exclusion | No time-use survey data; no cost-benefit analysis of childcare provision; no sectoral breakdown of employer discrimination |
| **Institutional Mainstreaming** | GRB not yet implemented across government; gender focal point network incomplete as of 2024; terms of reference for updating gender training manual issued only in October 2022 | Yes – mainstreaming is the right mechanism; execution has been slow | No assessment of gender focal point effectiveness; no gender budget analysis of current government spending by ministry |
| **GBV Elimination** | 1-in-3 women IPV lifetime prevalence (Fulu 2007, 18 years old); FPA cases rose from 19 (2013) to 696 (2020/2021); 3% prosecution rate; protection orders breached with no consequences | Yes – but strategies are institutional (systems strengthening) rather than structural (housing, economic independence for survivors, social norms) | No updated prevalence survey since 2007; no data on DV during COVID-19 at national level; no data on economic abuse |
| **Access to Justice** | Recently divorced women unaware of legal rights before or during marriage; protection order enforcement effectively absent in outer islands; burdensome evidence requirements for prosecution | Yes – but does not address Islamic family law intersection, which is the structural root of women's legal disadvantage; no plan addresses CEDAW Art. 16 reservation removal | No data on protection order applications, grants, and enforcement; no systematic legal aid availability assessment |

### 5.105.4 GEAP Indicator Monitoring Assessment

The GEAP's monitoring framework has significant weaknesses. Many indicators are process-oriented (number of trainings conducted) rather than outcome-oriented (reduction in IPV prevalence). The following baselines are available:

| Indicator | Baseline | Source | Year |
|---|---|---|---|
| IPV prevalence (lifetime) | 1 in 3 women | DHS 2016-17 | 2017 |
| Physical violence in marriage | ~20% | DHS 2016-17 | 2017 |
| Women in Parliament | 4.6% (4/87) | Elections Commission | 2024 |
| Women in island councils (post-quota) | Minimum 33% | Decentralisation Act | 2021 |
| FPA cases annually | ~600 | FPA database | 2022 |
| Gender-responsive budgeting | No systematic implementation | ADB GESI (2025) | 2025 |
| DV prosecution rate | ~3% | PGO data | 2022 |
| DV conviction rate | ~16.7% of prosecutions | PGO/Criminal Court | 2022 |

**Missing data:** No updated IPV prevalence survey since 2016-17 DHS; no technology-facilitated abuse prevalence data; no quantified economic abuse data; no tracking of protection order breach rates or enforcement timelines; no gender-disaggregated budget analysis at ministry level; no data on legal aid access rates for women in family law matters.[^548]

---


## 5.91 Development Partners and Donor Programmes


### 5.19.1 Overview of Gender-Relevant Development Partner Engagement

The Maldives' gender equality and GBV response programming is substantially dependent on development partner financing and technical capacity. The primary partners with active gender programmes as of 2024-25 are summarised below.

### 5.19.2 United Nations Population Fund (UNFPA)

UNFPA is the lead UN agency for gender, SRHR, and GBV programming in the Maldives. UNFPA's contribution to gender policy includes:

**Research and evidence:**
- **Women's Health and Life Experiences Study (2007)** – the Fulu study that established the baseline evidence on GBV prevalence (one in three women experiencing IPV; 12.2% CSA nationally)
- **Human Rights and Gender Review of GBV Laws in the Maldives (2023)** – comprehensive legal analysis of the GBV legislative framework's compliance with international standards
- Technical support to CEDAW reporting across multiple review cycles

**Programme:**
- **National Guidelines for Healthcare Providers on GBV (2024, 2nd edition)** – the primary clinical protocol for GBV identification and response in the health system
- **RMNCAH Strategy 2020-2025** – technical support for reproductive health policy and service development
- **Gender-Responsive Climate Financing Strategy (2023)** – technical support for GRFS development

**Current programming:**
- UNFPA Country Programme aligned with 2022-2026 UNSDCF
- Focus areas: SRHR, ending GBV, and strengthening gender data systems[^233]

### 5.19.3 Asian Development Bank (ADB)

ADB has been the primary multilateral development bank providing GBV infrastructure investment. Key programmes:

**ADB Strengthening Gender Inclusive Initiatives (GESI) Project (Project 54373-001):**
- Financing for three DV shelters: Addu/Hithadhoo, Hulhumale', Raa Atoll
- Gender-responsive budgeting (GRB) technical assistance to Ministry of Finance
- DV response capacity building for FPA, police FCPW, and health sector
- GBV information management system development

**ADB ARISE Project:**
- Climate resilience programming with Gender Action Plan
- Solar PV and non-traditional vocational training for women
- Gender-responsive island resilience planning support

**ADB SAILS Project:**
- Gender-responsive employment services through Employment Service Centres
- Targeted support for women jobseekers
- Skills matching for women in non-traditional sectors

**ADB MSME Project:**
- Credit lines for women entrepreneurs with limited collateral requirements
- Business development support for women-owned micro and small enterprises[^234]

### 5.19.4 European Union (EU)

The EU is the primary bilateral donor for the **AIM Project (Accountability and Integrity Matters project)**:

- **Joint EU-UNDP-MoSFD initiative** focused on GBV response system strengthening
- Covers: justice pathway capacity building, FPA operational development, police FCPW training, and community-level GBV awareness
- AIM Project Baseline Assessment (2022) provided the most comprehensive contemporary analysis of the GBV response system's attrition data, operational gaps, and capacity deficits
- Funding cycle aligned with EU-Maldives development partnership framework[^235]

### 5.19.5 United Nations Development Programme (UNDP)

UNDP's gender engagement in the Maldives is primarily through:
- **AIM Project** (co-implementing with EU and MoSFD)
- **Local governance gender mainstreaming support** – technical assistance to island councils and WDCs following the 2021 local council quota elections
- **WDC Capacity Assessment (2022)** – key evidence document on WDC implementation gaps
- UNDP SDG monitoring support – gender indicators across SDG 5 (gender equality) and intersecting goals[^236]

### 5.19.6 Other Development Partners

- **World Bank:** Supports health system strengthening including maternal health components (RMNCAH Strategy financing); education system reform including gender equity analysis
- **UNICEF:** Child protection programming with gender dimensions – girl children's protection from sexual abuse, child marriage, and trafficking; support for CRPD and CRC reporting
- **UN Women:** Engagement on women's economic empowerment and political participation; technical support for gender quota implementation monitoring
- **Australia DFAT / New Zealand MFAT:** Regional SIDS-focused development partnership with gender equity components; support for Maldives' UPR and CEDAW follow-up
- **Japan (JICA):** Health system support with maternal health components; fisheries sector development (historically male-focused but increasingly including gender equity commitments)[^237]

### 5.19.7 NGO and Civil Society: The Domestic Gender Ecosystem

Domestic civil society organisations play roles that complement and sometimes substitute for government services in the gender equality space:

**Hope for Women (Dhivehi: Aadheys):** The oldest and most established domestic women's rights NGO. Provides direct services to DV survivors including counselling, legal aid navigation, and emergency support; runs public awareness campaigns; and engages in policy advocacy. Hope for Women has sustained programming through multiple political administrations, including periods when government support was hostile.[^238]

**Maldivian Democracy Network (MDN):** Civil liberties and democracy organisation with a significant gender programme – monitoring women's political participation, documenting online harassment, and publishing research on gender equality indicators. MDN's Women in Politics reports are primary source documents for parliamentary and local council representation data.[^239]

**Family Legal Clinic (FLC):** Provides legal advice and representation to women in family law matters – divorce, custody, maintenance – who cannot afford private legal counsel. FLC's client data provides granular insight into the family law system's practical operation from the survivor's perspective, including maintenance enforcement failures and custody process documentation.[^240]

**Transparency Maldives:** Good governance organisation whose 2015 WDC assessment remains a foundational document on WDC capacity and the reality of women's formal local participation.[^241]

---


## 5.92 International Funding and the Gender Programme Financing Architecture


### 5.49.1 The Aid Dependency Question

A distinctive feature of the Maldivian gender equality and GBV response system is its substantial dependence on international development finance. The AIM Project (EU funding), ADB GESI Project, UNFPA Country Programme, and World Bank health components collectively finance a large proportion of the gender-related programme activity that the government itself does not adequately fund. This aid dependency raises important questions about sustainability, alignment with national priorities, and institutional capacity building versus external provision of services.

**Dependency risks:**
- Programme discontinuity when funding cycles end – FPA capacity built through AIM Project support may not be sustained if AIM funding ends without government budget absorption
- Priority distortion – programmes that reflect donor priorities rather than domestically identified needs
- Institutional shallowness – capacity that exists in project-specific staff rather than civil service positions with long-term career paths
- Coordination costs – managing multiple development partners with different priorities, reporting requirements, and institutional cultures absorbs management time that could otherwise go to service delivery

**Benefits:**
- Additional resources beyond government budget capacity
- Technical expertise not available domestically
- International accountability linkage – development partner monitoring creates pressure for performance
- Political protection – internationally funded programmes have some insulation from domestic political volatility[^437]

### 5.49.2 The UNSDCF 2022-2026 Framework

The **UN Sustainable Development Cooperation Framework (UNSDCF) 2022-2026** provides the overarching coordination mechanism for UN agency engagement in the Maldives. Gender equality is a cross-cutting priority across the UNSDCF's three pillars:

1. **Sustainable economic development** – gender economic empowerment, women's entrepreneurship
2. **Human capital and social development** – gender-responsive health and education, GBV response
3. **Climate action and environmental sustainability** – gender-responsive climate policy

UNFPA, UNDP, UNICEF, UN Women, WHO, and IOM are the primary UN agencies with gender-relevant programming under the UNSDCF. The UNSDCF's gender coordination mechanism – the UN Gender Theme Group – provides inter-agency coordination to avoid duplication and align programming with national gender policy frameworks.[^438]

### 5.49.3 The Green Climate Fund Opportunity

The **Green Climate Fund (GCF)** is the primary international climate finance mechanism under the Paris Agreement, with significant resources available for SIDS adaptation and mitigation. The Maldives' Gender-Responsive Climate Financing Strategy (2023) is designed in part to position the country to access GCF resources through gender-responsive project design.

**GCF gender requirements:** Since 2017, all GCF projects are required to meet minimum gender performance standards, including gender analysis in project design, gender action plans, and gender-disaggregated monitoring. GCF's Gender Policy also provides a "gender-responsive" to "gender-transformative" project design spectrum, with higher transformation requiring more substantive integration of gender equality as a goal (not merely a compliance requirement).

The Maldives' National Designated Authority for GCF is the Ministry of Environment, Climate Change and Technology. If the Ministry successfully submits gender-transformative project proposals, GCF could provide significant climate adaptation finance that simultaneously addresses gender equality – specifically in the areas of women's climate resilience, climate-affected livelihoods, and women's participation in island-level adaptation decision-making.[^439]

---


## 5.93 Donor Coordination and Gender Funding


### 5.77.1 Coordination Mechanisms

Multiple development partners with active gender programmes in the Maldives require coordination to avoid duplication, ensure alignment with national priorities, and maximise the collective impact of available resources. Coordination mechanisms include:

**UN Gender Theme Group (GTG):** The inter-agency body coordinating UN system gender programming under the UNSDCF 2022-2026. The GTG meets regularly and coordinates positions on CEDAW reporting, SDG 5 monitoring, and joint programming (e.g., AIM Project where UNDP participates alongside EU).

**MoSFD coordination role:** MoSFD is the government's coordination focal point for development partner gender programmes. It manages the AIM Project directly (as implementing partner); coordinates with ADB on GESI, SAILS, and ARISE; and provides government inputs to UNFPA and UNICEF gender programmes. MoSFD's capacity to coordinate multiple simultaneous development partner programmes is itself a capacity constraint.

**HACT framework:** The UN system's Harmonized Approach to Cash Transfers (HACT) framework standardises financial management across UN agencies, reducing the administrative burden on government counterparts in managing multiple UN programme financial flows. Its application to gender programmes reduces (but does not eliminate) the coordination transaction costs.[^503]

### 5.77.2 Financial Sustainability Gap

The most critical question for the sustainability of the Maldivian gender programme architecture is: what happens when development partner funding ends? Key programmes with defined funding cycles:

- **AIM Project (EU-UNDP):** The funding cycle duration is documented in the project agreement. At the end of the AIM cycle, the FPA capacity built through AIM, the GIMS system, and the community awareness infrastructure all require government budget absorption to continue. Without a budget absorption plan committing government funds to sustain AIM-built capacity, the institutional improvement is at risk of reversal.

- **ADB GESI Project:** Similarly, the three shelters funded by ADB require operational funding beyond the construction and initial equipment investment. Government operational budget allocation for shelter management, staffing, and maintenance is required for the shelters to continue functioning beyond the ADB project cycle.

- **UNFPA Country Programme:** UNFPA Country Programme cycles align with the UNSDCF 2022-2026 framework. UNFPA contributions – clinical GBV guidelines, RMNCAH strategy support, CEDAW reporting technical assistance – are complementary to government capacity but do not substitute for it.

The sustainability question is not hypothetical – there are documented cases from other development contexts in which significant donor-funded gender programme investments collapsed when funding ended because the government had not developed domestic budget commitments to sustain them. The Maldivian government's budget constraint is real (a small island economy with significant development finance dependence), but the sustainability planning is a necessary complement to the programme investment.[^504]

---


## 5.94 The AIM Project: A Detailed Programme Analysis


### 5.61.1 Background and Objectives

The **Accountability and Integrity Matters (AIM) Project** is the most significant ongoing GBV response investment in the Maldives. Implemented jointly by the **European Union, UNDP, and the Ministry of Social and Family Development (MoSFD)**, the AIM Project represents a substantial multi-year commitment to strengthening the institutional capacity of the Maldivian GBV response system – from first disclosure through to justice outcomes.

The AIM Project emerged from the recognition – shared across the EU, UNDP, and MoSFD – that the problem in the Maldivian GBV system is not primarily legislative: by 2020, the DVPA, SOA, and GEA provided an adequate legal framework. The problem is operational: the institutions mandated to implement this framework lack the capacity, coordination, and information systems to function at the scale required. AIM is a capacity and system development project, not a legislative reform project.[^464]

**AIM Objectives (as documented in project documentation and UNDP project page):**
1. Strengthen the FPA's operational capacity for case management, safety planning, and survivor support
2. Improve FCPW officers' capacity for trauma-informed investigation and evidence collection
3. Enhance judicial and PG capacity for GBV case handling and prosecution
4. Develop an integrated GBV information management system (GIMS) connecting the FPA, FCPW, and courts
5. Build community awareness and norm change – addressing the social norms that discourage disclosure and enable perpetrator impunity
6. Strengthen WDC capacity as the outer island first-response mechanism

### 5.61.2 The Baseline Assessment: What AIM Found

The AIM Project Baseline Assessment (2022) is the most comprehensive independent assessment of the Maldivian GBV response system available. Its findings, cited extensively throughout this chapter, include:

**Justice pathway attrition:** The baseline documented the full justice pathway from FPA disclosure to criminal conviction, establishing the 3% prosecution and 16.7% conviction rates that are now the primary accountability reference points for assessing system performance.

**FPA capacity:** The baseline found FPA caseworkers carrying caseloads substantially above recommended levels, intake processes that were sometimes rushed, and follow-up that was inconsistent – particularly for outer atoll cases handled remotely.

**FCPW capacity:** Training levels in outer islands significantly below Malé FCPW; rotation creating persistent capacity loss; mediation as default response; interview techniques not consistently trauma-informed.

**Court capacity:** Limited GBV-specific judicial training; protection order procedures not consistently understood by outer island magistrates; no video testimony or protective arrangements for survivors appearing in court.

**Coordination:** No integrated information system; case management coordination by telephone and personal relationship; survivors required to repeat their story to multiple agencies without coordination.

**Community awareness:** DVPA awareness low in community surveys; DV as private matter norm prevalent; religious framing discouraging disclosure documented in community settings.

**WDC function:** <40% training in two years; no budget; advisory-only; social pressure limiting effectiveness.[^465]

### 5.61.3 AIM Interventions: What the Programme Is Doing

Against this baseline, AIM's interventions include:

**FPA capacity building:**
- Structured training programme for FPA caseworkers in trauma-informed case management, safety planning, and multi-agency coordination
- Development of standard case management procedures and documentation tools
- Supervision and mentoring structure to support caseworker professional development
- Tele-case management protocols for outer atoll engagement

**FCPW training:**
- Training for FCPW officers in trauma-informed survivor interviewing, evidence collection, and case documentation
- Model investigation procedures for DV and sexual violence cases
- Advocacy within MPS for rotation protection for trained FCPW officers

**Judicial and PG capacity:**
- Judicial training workshops on GBV case handling, consent law application, and survivor credibility assessment
- Training for PG staff on evidence-based prosecution approaches
- Development of model court procedures for GBV cases

**GIMS development:**
- Technical design and development of an integrated Gender-based violence Information Management System connecting FPA, FCPW, Family Court, Criminal Court, and – eventually – the shelter system
- Data standards development to allow consistent recording and reporting across agencies
- Dashboard development for aggregate data monitoring

**Community engagement:**
- Community awareness campaigns in partnership with WDCs, mosques, schools, and media
- Male engagement component – targeting men through community sports, faith networks, and workplace programmes
- Social and behaviour change communication (SBCC) on DV norms and help-seeking

**WDC strengthening:**
- Training programme for WDC members on DV recognition, DVPA provisions, and referral protocols
- Resource materials for WDCs in Dhivehi language
- Linking WDCs more effectively into the FPA referral system[^466]

### 5.61.4 Implementation Status and Challenges

As of the most recent publicly available AIM Project documentation (2024), key implementation status points include:

- FPA training has been delivered to a significant proportion of Malé-based caseworkers; outer atoll coverage is in progress
- FCPW training has been delivered in Malé and several atoll settings; rotation continues to create knowledge loss
- GIMS development is underway but not yet operationally live across all agencies – a multi-year technical challenge
- Community awareness campaigns have been launched; assessment of normative impact is ongoing
- WDC training has been delivered across multiple atolls with positive uptake from engaged members

**Implementation challenges:**
- Institutional resistance to information sharing – agencies protective of their data for institutional reasons
- Staff turnover in both FPA and FCPW creates a training replacement treadmill
- The GBV Information Management System requires sustained government commitment for operation after AIM Project funding ends – the sustainability plan is not yet determined
- Community norm change is slow relative to the project cycle – the social norms driving DV silence are generational, not amenable to single-cycle intervention

### 5.61.5 AIM Project Data Implications

The GIMS, when fully operational, will significantly improve Maldivian gender data availability. Current data gaps that GIMS is designed to address:
- Tracking case journeys from FPA intake through court outcome in a single system
- Generating atoll-level and island-level DV response data
- Enabling longitudinal outcome tracking (what happened to survivors 1 year after FPA engagement)
- Providing disaggregated data on survivor demographics (age, employment status, number of children, location)

The gap between current administrative data and what a functioning GIMS would provide illustrates the extent to which the current data system under-describes the real situation of GBV in the Maldives.[^467]

---


## 5.95 Specific Sectoral Programme Profiles: Implementation Detail


### 5.101.1 The ADB GESI Project (54373-001): Full Programme Analysis

The **Asian Development Bank Strengthening Gender Inclusive Initiatives Project** (Project number 54373-001) is the most significant single capital investment in the Maldivian GBV response infrastructure. The full project profile:

**Financing:** ADB grant and loan combination; total project value approximately USD 15-20 million (specific figures per ADB project database)

**Executing agency:** Ministry of Gender, Family and Social Services (MoGFSS)/MoSFD

**Key components:**
1. **Shelter construction and equipping:** Three DV shelters at Hithadhoo/Addu, Hulhumale', and Raa Atoll – physical infrastructure including safe rooms, counselling spaces, children's areas, case management offices, and security systems
2. **GBV response capacity building:** Training for FPA caseworkers, FCPW officers, and health sector providers in GBV response; development of standard procedures and protocols
3. **GRB technical assistance:** Support to Ministry of Finance and line ministries for Gender Responsive Budgeting implementation – curriculum development, training delivery, monitoring system development
4. **Data and information systems:** GBV case management database development; gender-disaggregated data collection protocols; reporting templates for FPA statistical publication
5. **Community awareness:** Public information campaigns on GBV and the DVPA; community engagement through WDCs and local partners

**Project timeline:** 2021-2026 (approximate; exact dates per ADB project documentation)

**Geographic coverage:** National, with shelter infrastructure at three specific locations and capacity building across all atolls

**Project database entry:** Available at https://www.adb.org/projects/54373-001/main[^535]

### 5.101.2 The SAILS Project: Employment Services for Women

The **Strengthening Access and Inclusiveness of Labour Market Support (SAILS) Project** – also ADB-financed – targets women's access to employment services specifically. SAILS operates through the Employment Service Centre network and includes:

**Gender-responsive ESC services:**
- Women's jobseeker advisory services – dedicated counsellors for women returning to the labour market, women with care responsibilities, and women with skills mismatches
- Job matching that considers care constraints – identifying employers offering flexible hours, part-time positions, or work-from-home options compatible with care responsibilities
- Skills gap analysis – identifying the specific skills that prevent women from accessing available jobs and connecting them to training programmes that can close those gaps
- Employer engagement – working with employers to understand the business case for gender-inclusive hiring and flexible working arrangements

**Outer island components:**
- Virtual ESC services – telephone and online advisory services for women in atolls without ESC physical presence
- Mobile outreach – periodic visits to atoll centres to provide intensive job market assistance to women who cannot travel to Malé

**Connection to vocational training:**
- Referral pathways from ESC to TVET programmes – including the ADB ARISE project's non-traditional vocational training for women
- Recognition of prior learning – assessing women's informal skills (traditional crafts, food production, home-based childcare) for formal credential pathways[^536]

### 5.101.3 The ARISE Project Gender Action Plan: Operational Details

The **ARISE (Atoll Resilience and Island Sustainability Enhancement) Project** Gender Action Plan goes beyond standard gender mainstreaming compliance to include specific operational commitments:

**Women's participation quotas in ARISE activities:**
- Minimum 40% women in all ARISE-supported community resilience planning processes
- Minimum 40% women in all training activities where technically feasible
- Gender-balanced community resilience committees as a condition of island-level ARISE investment

**Solar PV gender inclusion programme:**
- Specific recruitment of women for solar PV installation and maintenance training
- Training curriculum adapted to be accessible to women without prior technical background
- Job placement support for women completing solar PV certification – connecting them to installation and maintenance contracts on ARISE project islands

**Women's alternative livelihoods:**
- Identification and support for climate-resilient income diversification options for women – including eco-tourism, aquaculture, and value-added food production
- Market linkage support – connecting women's climate-resilient enterprises to urban and export markets
- Credit access support – connecting women entrepreneurs to ADB MSME project microfinance for climate-resilient livelihood investments

**Gender-disaggregated monitoring:**
- ARISE project monitoring framework includes gender-disaggregated indicators for all key outputs
- Annual gender assessment by independent evaluator
- Results reported to ADB and UNFCCC as part of climate finance gender performance requirements[^537]

---


# Part 18: International Frameworks, CEDAW, and Comparisons


## 5.96 CEDAW Compliance and Treaty Body Engagement


### 5.18.1 CEDAW Ratification, Reservations, and Reporting History

The **Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Discrimination Against Women (CEDAW)** was ratified by the Maldives on **1 July 1993** – a date confirmed by the UN Treaty Collection and the Government's own Seventh Periodic Report. The Maldives entered two specific reservations at ratification:

**Reservation on Article 7(a):** The right of women to vote and stand for election – interpreted to exclude women from certain religious advisory bodies constitutionally reserved for men (specifically the Islamic advisory council structures that require a male quorum under constitutional religious governance provisions).

**Reservation on Article 16:** Equality in marriage, family relations, divorce, inheritance, and custody – where the Maldives reserves the right to apply Islamic Shariah law rather than CEDAW standards. This reservation has the broadest practical impact, as it insulates from CEDAW challenge the inheritance half-share rule, polygamy, unilateral male divorce rights, and other Islamic family law provisions that structurally disadvantage women.[^215]

The CEDAW Committee has repeatedly called for withdrawal of both reservations, characterising the Article 16 reservation as incompatible with CEDAW's object and purpose and therefore legally impermissible under VCLT Article 19. The Government has consistently declined to withdraw the reservations, arguing that they reflect constitutional religious commitments that cannot be modified through ordinary legislation.[^216]

**Reporting history:**
- First Periodic Report: submitted significantly late, reviewed 2007
- Combined Second-Fifth Periodic Report: reviewed 2015
- Sixth Periodic Report: reviewed November 2021; Concluding Observations CEDAW/C/MDV/CO/6 issued
- Seventh Periodic Report: submitted 2025 (forthcoming review)[^217]

### 5.18.2 CEDAW Committee Concluding Observations: November 2021

The CEDAW Committee's November 2021 **Concluding Observations (CEDAW/C/MDV/CO/6)** represent the most comprehensive international assessment of Maldivian gender policy. Key areas of concern and recommendations (organised by CEDAW Article) include:

**Article 2 (Non-discrimination):** Concerns about the Islamic Shariah reservation; recommendation to review all legislation for CEDAW compliance; concern about absence of effective remedies for discrimination[^218]

**Article 5 (Gender stereotypes):** Concern about stereotypes entrenching gender roles, religious justifications for inequality, negative portrayals in media; recommendation for comprehensive national strategy to eliminate stereotypes[^219]

**Article 6 (Trafficking):** Concern about Tier 2 Watch List status; non-functional trafficking hotline; zero prosecutions of trafficking facilitators; recommendation for operationalise NRM and trafficking victim identification[^220]

**Article 7 (Political participation):** Concern about 4.6% female parliamentary representation; recommendation for temporary special measures (quotas) at parliamentary level, following the successful local council model[^221]

**Article 10 (Education):** Concern about SRH education gaps; early marriage and girls' dropout; recommendation for comprehensive sexuality education across school levels[^222]

**Article 11 (Employment):** Concern about LFPR gap, gender pay gap, NEET rates, childcare deficit; recommendation for childcare infrastructure investment, gender pay gap data collection, equal pay legislation[^223]

**Article 12 (Health):** Concern about restrictive abortion law; contraceptive access gaps; MMR; recommendation for decriminalisation of abortion in rape, incest, severe foetal abnormality, and mental health risk cases[^224]

**Article 13 (Economic and social benefits):** Concern about women's limited access to credit and entrepreneurship support; recommendation for targeted business development support for women[^225]

**Article 14 (Rural/island women):** Concern about geographic inequality in service access; WDC capacity limitations; recommendation for targeted investment in atoll women's service access[^226]

**Article 15 (Equality before the law):** Concern about women's limited legal literacy; inadequate legal aid; recommendation for expanded legal aid and rights education[^227]

**Article 16 (Marriage and family):** Concern about polygamy, inheritance inequality, child marriage, maintenance enforcement gap, prenuptial information access; recommendation for family law reform toward gender equality; reservation withdrawal[^228]

### 5.18.3 Government Response: Seventh Periodic Report 2025

The Government's **Seventh Periodic Report (CEDAW, 2025)** provides the most recent authoritative government account of gender policy status. Key disclosures in the report include:

- **CEDAW ratification confirmed:** 1 July 1993 (with unchanged reservations)
- **GEA implementation:** Gender Equality Act 18/2016 acknowledged as the primary legislative instrument; GRB compliance acknowledged as incomplete (<40% of ministries)
- **AIM Project:** Joint EU-UNDP-MoSFD initiative acknowledged as primary GBV response investment
- **Parliament:** 4/87 seats (4.6%) confirmed; no quota proposed at parliamentary level
- **Local councils:** 33% post-quota achievement acknowledged
- **FGM/C:** Declining trend confirmed (38% age 45-49, ~1% age 15-19, 1% age 5-9); legal status under review [Note: The 13% FGM/C prevalence figure applies to all women aged 15-49; the 15-19 cohort-specific rate requires verification against DHS 2016-17 microdata.]
- **Abortion:** No plans to amend Penal Code restrictions
- **SDFC:** 20% loans to women
- **Paternity leave:** Absence acknowledged; no legislative change announced
- **WDCs:** Capacity gaps acknowledged; training programme pledged[^229]

The Seventh Periodic Report reflects a pattern consistent across Maldivian CEDAW reporting: acknowledged legal frameworks, acknowledged implementation gaps, modest programmatic responses, and no withdrawal of reservations. The Committee's review of the Seventh Periodic Report will determine whether this cycle produces stronger recommendations than the 2021 Concluding Observations.

### 5.18.4 Other Treaty Body Engagement

Maldivian gender policy commitments are monitored not only through CEDAW but through several intersecting treaty body reporting processes:

**CRC (Convention on the Rights of the Child):** CRC reporting addresses girl children's rights – specifically child marriage, sexual abuse, corporal punishment, and education access. The CRC Periodic Report Draft (Part XVIII-B of this encyclopedia) addresses CSEA (child sexual exploitation and abuse), FGM/C as affecting girl children, and education access for girls including early marriage dropout.[^230]

**CRPD (Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities):** The CRPD Periodic Report Draft (Part XVIII-A) documents that women with disabilities face intersecting discrimination – LFPR for women with disabilities is documented at **28%** (CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report 2025), substantially lower than the general female LFPR of 44%. Women with disabilities face barriers to accessing GBV services and reproductive health care that compound the general barriers documented throughout this chapter.[^231]

**Universal Periodic Review (UPR):** The Maldives has undergone three cycles of the UPR process. Gender equality and GBV have featured prominently in recommendations from reviewing states across all three cycles. The government's acceptance or rejection of recommendations, and its follow-up reporting, provides an additional accountability mechanism complementing CEDAW.[^232]

---


## 5.97 The CEDAW Reporting Process: A Governance Tool


### 5.58.1 How CEDAW Reporting Works in Practice

The CEDAW periodic reporting process – through which the Maldivian government submits a report to the CEDAW Committee, which then reviews it, hears from civil society, and issues Concluding Observations – functions as one of the primary gender policy governance mechanisms available in the Maldivian system. Understanding how it works in practice, and what its limitations are, is important for assessing its contribution to gender equality progress.

**The reporting cycle:**
1. The government prepares a Periodic Report documenting policy developments and compliance with each CEDAW article
2. Civil society organisations submit "shadow reports" or alternative reports providing independent assessment of the government's report
3. A pre-session working group of CEDAW Committee experts reviews the reports and develops a "list of issues" – questions the government must answer
4. The government submits responses to the list of issues
5. At the formal CEDAW session, government representatives engage in a dialogue with Committee experts
6. The Committee issues Concluding Observations – a formal assessment with specific recommendations
7. The government is expected to implement the recommendations before the next reporting cycle

**MoSFD's role:** The Ministry of Social and Family Development (MoSFD) is the focal ministry for CEDAW reporting, coordinating inputs from all other relevant ministries (Health, Education, Justice, Finance, etc.). The quality and completeness of the Periodic Report depend on the effectiveness of MoSFD's coordination with other ministries – and on the political will of those ministries to disclose gaps and failures rather than presenting an optimistic picture.[^459]

### 5.58.2 Civil Society Shadow Reporting

The parallel civil society shadow reporting process is critical for providing independent verification of the government's account. In the Maldivian context, shadow reports have been submitted by:
- Hope for Women (Aadheys) – focusing on GBV response implementation gaps
- Maldivian Democracy Network (MDN) – focusing on political participation and democratic rights
- Human Rights Commission of Maldives (HRCM) – the national human rights institution's independent assessment

Shadow reports in previous cycles have documented discrepancies between the government's positive framing of implementation progress and field evidence of continued gaps – particularly on FPA caseworker capacity, outer atoll shelter availability, and prosecution rates. This accountability function of civil society CEDAW engagement is significant but depends on civil society organisations having the capacity and funding to prepare high-quality shadow reports.[^460]

### 5.58.3 Translating Concluding Observations into Policy

The critical – and frequently failing – step in the CEDAW process is the translation of Committee recommendations into actual policy change. The pattern across Maldivian CEDAW cycles documents:

- **Recommendations accepted:** The government formally accepts most Concluding Observations and commits to implementing them
- **Implementation plans:** Some recommendations are incorporated into action plans (Gender Equality Action Plan, SDG implementation plans)
- **Budget allocation:** The gap between accepted recommendations and budget allocation is significant – recommendations are accepted without the resources needed to implement them
- **Follow-up reporting:** In subsequent periodic reports, many recommendations from prior cycles reappear as "outstanding" because implementation has not occurred

The CEDAW Committee's "list of issues prior to reporting" in the current cycle (leading to Seventh Periodic Report review) specifically asked about progress on outstanding recommendations from 2015 and 2021 – including the childcare gap, the pregnancy termination restriction, the trafficking hotline, and the parliamentary quota. The government's responses, embedded in the Seventh Periodic Report (2025), acknowledge most gaps without providing substantive new policy commitments.[^461]

---


## 5.98 The Universal Periodic Review: Gender Recommendations


### 5.97.1 UPR Process and Gender

The **Universal Periodic Review (UPR)** – conducted by the UN Human Rights Council – reviews every UN member state's human rights record in a peer review process. The Maldives has been reviewed three times (2010, 2015, 2020). Gender equality has been a consistent theme across all three reviews.

**2020 Third Cycle UPR recommendations (selected gender-relevant):**
- Multiple reviewing states called for withdrawal of the CEDAW Article 16 reservation
- Calls for the legal prohibition of FGM/C
- Recommendations on women's parliamentary representation (quota)
- Recommendations on the restrictive abortion law
- Calls for effective implementation of the DVPA, including outer island service access
- Anti-trafficking recommendations including hotline operationalisation and NRM implementation

The Maldives' UPR response – accepting some recommendations, noting others (which is UPR language for partial acceptance), and rejecting a few – follows the standard diplomatic pattern. Tracking which recommendations have been implemented between cycles provides an accountability mechanism alongside CEDAW.

**Key pattern:** The Maldives consistently accepts recommendations framed in terms of implementation of existing commitments (strengthen DV services, improve GBV data collection, train justice sector officials) and consistently rejects or notes recommendations requiring changes to Islamic family law (Article 16 reservation, inheritance equality, abortion law, polygamy restriction). This pattern reflects the genuine constitutional constraint that Islamic family law provisions have – any government that withdraws the CEDAW reservation or legislates against Islamic family law norms faces constitutional challenge.[^531]

---


## 5.99 International Obligation Architecture: What No Action Plan Covers


### 5.106.1 Mapping CEDAW Against Existing Action Plans

The CEDAW provides a comprehensive framework for gender equality obligations. The following mapping (derived from the Action Plans Analysis chapter's bidirectional obligation mapping) shows which CEDAW obligations are addressed by current action plans and which remain uncovered:

| CEDAW Article | Which Plans Address It | Coverage Assessment | **GAPS** |
|---|---|---|---|
| Arts. 1–5 (discrimination, stereotypes) | GEAP (primary); DVPNS | Moderate | Structural discrimination in Islamic family law explicitly excluded by reservation |
| Art. 6 (trafficking and exploitation) | DVPNS (partial) | Weak | No active anti-trafficking action plan; hotline non-operational; NRM not implemented |
| Art. 7 (political participation) | GEAP Goal 1 | Weak | No parliamentary quota exists; awareness strategy insufficient |
| Art. 10 (education) | GEAP Goal 3 (gender in curriculum) | Partial | No standalone education gender action plan |
| Art. 11 (employment) | GEAP Goal 2 | Partial | Resort exclusion, childcare gap, pay gap not specifically addressed in any plan |
| Art. 12 (health) | RMNCAH Strategy (2020–2025) | Moderate | RMNCAH expires 2025; no successor plan committed |
| Art. 13 (economic benefits) | GEAP Goal 2; NSPA programmes | Partial | No gender analysis of NSPA coverage rates; single parent allowance design barriers not addressed |
| **Art. 14 (rural women)** | **No plan** | **Absent** | **The distinct circumstances of women in outer atolls – geographic isolation, limited service access, weaker enforcement of legal rights – are not specifically addressed by any current action plan** |
| Arts. 15–16 (equality in law, marriage) | **CEDAW reservation means excluded** | **Absent** | **The reservation on Art. 16 means Islamic family law provisions – covering marriage, divorce, custody, inheritance, and maintenance – are explicitly outside CEDAW obligations. No plan addresses reservation removal** |

### 5.106.2 The Article 16 Reservation: The Structural Gap

The Maldives' reservation on CEDAW Article 16 – covering equality in marriage and family life – is the most consequential single constraint on gender equality in the country. By maintaining this reservation, the government has explicitly excluded from its CEDAW obligations the areas of law where women face the most immediate and severe disadvantage:

- **Marriage:** The bride's guardian (*wali veriyaa*) requirement for marriage registration under the Family Act (Law No. 4/2000) has no equivalent for men. A woman cannot legally marry without a male guardian's involvement.
- **Divorce:** Unilateral divorce rights (talaq) are available to men without court involvement; women's unilateral divorce (khul') typically requires returning the mahr and potential court approval. Women have been fined for out-of-court divorces they did not initiate.
- **Child custody:** Islamic jurisprudence (hadhana) provides mothers custody of young children; fathers custody at older ages. The court's discretion within this framework has been documented to discriminate against women who remarry or are perceived as insufficiently compliant with Islamic gender norms.
- **Maintenance:** Men are legally obligated to provide maintenance to ex-wives during the post-divorce waiting period (iddah) and to children; enforcement mechanisms are weak, with MVR 2 million in unpaid maintenance documented by DJA.
- **Inheritance:** Islamic inheritance law provides women half the share of male heirs. This provision is constitutionally grounded and no action plan has addressed it.

Multiple reviewing states at the 2020 UPR called for withdrawal of the Art. 16 reservation. The government's standard response – that the reservation is required by constitutional provisions making Islam the state religion – reflects a genuine constitutional constraint, but this constraint has never been tested through a constitutional challenge.[^549]

### 5.106.3 Rural Women: The Missing Framework

CEDAW Article 14 requires states to take account of the particular problems faced by rural women. In the Maldivian context, "rural women" translates to women in the outer atolls – approximately 40% of the female population living on islands where:

- FCSCs serve entire atolls with minimal staffing
- FPA has no operational presence; DV cases must be navigated without trained caseworkers
- Protection orders are effectively unenforceable (no police presence on many islands to execute or monitor)
- Shelters are inaccessible – the nearest ADB-funded shelter may require multi-day travel
- Economic opportunities are severely constrained
- Women's Development Committees (WDCs) are the only institutional gender mechanism present
- Healthcare – including SRHR services – is limited to primary care facilities

No current action plan specifically addresses this concentration of disadvantage. The GEAP does not include an outer atoll-specific strategy. The DVPNS 3rd (2023–2027) does not specify how its protective mechanisms will be made accessible to women in islands without FCSC presence. The RMNCAH Strategy addresses maternal health but not the geographic access barriers that make mortality risk higher in the atolls.[^550]

### 5.106.4 FGM/C: The Obligation No Plan Addresses

Both the CRC and CEDAW frameworks require the Maldives to take steps toward eliminating FGM/C. The Action Plans Analysis chapter identified FGM/C elimination as one of six obligations for which **no action plan exists**. The DHS 2016-17 documented:

- 13% prevalence among women aged 15–49
- 38% prevalence in the oldest age cohort (45–49) – showing dramatic generational decline
- 1% in the youngest measured cohort (5–9) – showing the practice has not fully stopped

Despite the dramatic generational decline, the legal status of FGM/C remains ambiguous: it is not explicitly prohibited by any Maldivian law, though it may fall under general criminal prohibitions on harm. The government's CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report (2025) acknowledged this gap. The GEAP does not include a specific strategy on FGM/C. The NAPVAC does not include FGM/C in its definition of violence against children. No timeline has been set for legal prohibition.[^551]

---


## 5.100 International Comparative Analysis: The Maldives Among SIDS and Muslim-Majority States


### 5.26.1 Comparative Context

The Maldives occupies an unusual position in comparative gender analysis: a small island developing state (SIDS) with South Asian cultural influences, a Muslim-majority population with Gulf-influenced Islamic practice, and a democratic constitutional order committed in principle to gender equality. Understanding how the Maldives compares with similar states is essential for identifying realistic policy options and learning from what has and has not worked in comparable contexts.

### 5.26.2 Comparison with South Asian SIDS

**Sri Lanka:** The closest geographic comparator with a SIDS profile and mixed South Asian cultural heritage. Sri Lanka's female LFPR (36%) is lower than the Maldives' 44%, reflecting shared regional constraints, but Sri Lanka has achieved substantially higher women's parliamentary representation (5.3% vs 4.6%). Sri Lanka has had a female Prime Minister (Sirimavo Bandaranaike, the world's first, elected 1960) and female President (Chandrika Kumaratunga), demonstrating that executive-level female political leadership is achievable in the regional context. Sri Lanka's DV legislation (Prevention of Domestic Violence Act, 2005) predates the Maldives' DVPA and provides some comparative lessons.[^308]

**Maldives vs Sri Lanka LFPR comparison:** Despite Maldivian women's substantially higher educational attainment (tertiary majority vs minority in Sri Lanka), the LFPR gap in the Maldives is smaller than Sri Lanka's – suggesting that the atoll geography, which concentrates population on islands without formal economy, may create LFPR constraints more severe than Sri Lanka's geography.[^309]

### 5.26.3 Comparison with Muslim-Majority Comparators

**Morocco (MENA comparator):** Morocco's 2004 Mudawwana (Family Code) reform is widely cited as the most successful family law reform in a Muslim-majority country in the modern period. The reform – achieved after years of civil society advocacy and with the explicit endorsement of King Mohammed VI on Islamic grounds – eliminated husband's unilateral right to polygamy (requiring judicial approval and the first wife's knowledge), raised the marriage age, created shared marital assets, reformed custody and inheritance provisions, and established judicial divorce on no-fault grounds. Morocco's reform succeeded because it was framed within Islamic jurisprudence rather than against it, secured royal religious endorsement, and had sustained civil society backing.[^310]

The Maldivian lesson from Morocco is the importance of Islamic jurisprudential framing of family law reform and the role of executive political commitment. Morocco's reform had the King's public endorsement as Commander of the Faithful – equivalent political authority in the Maldivian context would require the President's active Islamic-grounded advocacy.

**Indonesia (Southeast Asian comparator):** Indonesia's approach to gender equality within an Islamic framework is instructive for the Maldives. Indonesian Islamic feminist organisations – particularly Fatayat NU (Nahdlatul Ulama women's wing) – have developed an influential intellectual tradition arguing for gender equality from within Islamic jurisprudence. Indonesia's DV law (Law No. 23 of 2004 on Elimination of Domestic Violence) preceded the Maldives' DVPA by 8 years and includes criminal penalties alongside civil protection orders. Indonesia's experience suggests that large-scale Islamic organisations can be allies for gender equality when intellectually engaged rather than politically challenged.[^311]

**Tunisia (progressive reform comparator):** Tunisia represents the most advanced state of family law reform in the Arab Muslim world. The 1956 Code du Statut Personnel abolished polygamy entirely, established divorce by mutual consent through the courts, and prohibited repudiation (*talaq*). Tunisia's 2017 proposed inheritance equality law – although contested and not fully enacted – demonstrates that Quranic inheritance provisions can be subject to legislative debate even in an Islamic constitutional framework. Tunisia's trajectory also demonstrates that rapid reform without broad social consensus can create backlash – a cautionary note for reform strategies.[^312]

**Bangladesh (South Asian Muslim comparator):** Bangladesh's experience with GBV legislation and institutional development – including the National Women's Policy (2011 and subsequent revisions), the Multi-Sectoral Programme on Violence Against Women, and the One-Stop Crisis Centres model – provides lessons on integrated GBV response in a resource-constrained South Asian Muslim context. The One-Stop Crisis Centre model, providing medical, legal, police, and social services in a single location, addresses exactly the institutional fragmentation problem documented in the Maldivian system – though Bangladesh's scale and geographic context differs substantially from the Maldives' archipelago challenge.[^313]

### 5.26.4 Pacific SIDS: The Small-Island Governance Lesson

**Fiji:** Fiji's experience with gender equality policy in a multi-ethnic Pacific SIDS context offers lessons on managing gender reform in post-coup political environments. Fiji's family law reform and DV legislative development proceeded despite political instability, through a combination of civil society resilience and development partner sustained engagement. The Family Law Act 2003 (Fiji) created a dedicated Family Division of the Magistrates Court with specialised capacity.[^314]

**Samoa and the Pacific quota model:** Several Pacific SIDS – Samoa, Timor-Leste – have implemented gender quotas for parliamentary representation that produced rapid improvements in women's political participation, similar to the Maldives' local council quota success. The Pacific experience suggests that quota design matters: reserved seat models (where seats are specifically set aside for women) have produced more durable outcomes than candidate list quotas in small SIDS contexts.[^315]

**The SIDS common challenge:** Across Pacific and Indian Ocean SIDS, a common pattern emerges: small island geography creates severe outer island service access challenges that disproportionately affect women; small community dynamics simultaneously protect and constrain women; urbanisation concentrates gender-differentiated opportunity and risk in capital cities; and development partner programming provides essential resources but must be calibrated to local institutional capacity. The Maldives' challenges are shared, and SIDS regional policy learning through AOSIS and Pacific-Indian Ocean development cooperation offers under-exploited opportunities.[^316]

### 5.26.5 What International Comparison Suggests for Maldivian Reform

The comparative analysis suggests several lessons for Maldivian gender policy reform:

1. **Islamic jurisprudential framing is necessary, not optional.** The Morocco and Indonesia examples demonstrate that family law reform in Muslim-majority states succeeds when it operates through rather than against Islamic legal discourse. The Maldives' reform trajectory needs deeper investment in Islamic jurisprudential engagement.

2. **Structural interventions (quotas) produce rapid results.** The local council quota outcome – 4% to 33% in one election – demonstrates the Maldivian evidence for this international pattern. Extending structural interventions to parliament and other governance bodies should be a priority.

3. **Integrated service models address institutional fragmentation.** Bangladesh's One-Stop Crisis Centre model, adapted to the Maldives' geographic reality (perhaps as mobile units or regional integrated services at atoll capitals), addresses the fragmentation problem more systematically than the current multi-agency model.

4. **Development partner engagement is a structural feature, not a temporary fix.** Across all comparable SIDS, sustained development partner financing and technical capacity is a structural component of gender equality programming. The Maldives should manage its development partner relationships strategically – ensuring that programmes build domestic institutional capacity rather than creating dependency on external technical assistance.

5. **Civil society resilience is the most durable asset.** In Morocco, Indonesia, Bangladesh, and Pacific SIDS, civil society organisations (women's organisations, legal aid organisations, research organisations) have been the most persistent drivers of reform across political administrations. The Maldivian civil society gender sector – Hope for Women, MDN, FLC – represents an asset that should be protected and resourced through mechanisms insulated from political fluctuation.

---


## 5.101 The Maldivian Experience in Global Gender Equality Context


### 5.84.1 The Global Gender Gap Index

The **World Economic Forum (WEF) Global Gender Gap Index** provides an annual standardised cross-country comparison of gender equality on four dimensions: economic participation and opportunity, educational attainment, health and survival, and political empowerment. The Maldives' position on this index – and its trajectory over time – provides useful context for how the country compares internationally:

The Maldives typically scores:
- **High on educational attainment:** The near-parity in primary and secondary enrolment, and the female majority in tertiary education, produces a high score on the education sub-index
- **Moderate on health and survival:** The life expectancy advantage for women and adequate maternal health coverage produce moderate scores, though the COVID MMR spike affected trajectory
- **Low on economic participation:** The 36-percentage-point LFPR gap, gender pay gap, and low women's share of senior positions produce low economic participation scores
- **Very low on political empowerment:** The 4.6% parliamentary representation and historically low representation in executive positions produce very low political empowerment scores

The composite result is a position in the global middle – better than regional averages on some dimensions, worse on others – that reflects the genuine paradox of high female education, low female economic and political participation.

**Trend:** Without a comprehensive longitudinal analysis, the trend can be characterised as: education dimension improving (female tertiary majority growing); political empowerment improving modestly (local council quota); economic participation stagnating (LFPR gap not narrowing significantly); health and survival recovering from COVID spike.[^516]

### 5.84.2 The Human Development Index and Gender Inequality Index

The **UNDP Human Development Index (HDI)** and associated **Gender Inequality Index (GII)** provide a complementary international comparison. The Gender Inequality Index measures:
- Maternal mortality ratio
- Adolescent birth rate
- Share of seats in parliament (female)
- Population with at least some secondary education (male vs. female)
- Labour force participation rate (male vs. female)

The Maldives' GII performance reflects:
- Moderate MMR (improving from historical highs; COVID spike)
- Adolescent birth rate: not separately published but relevant to ASRH programming
- Parliamentary seats: among the world's lowest (4.6%)
- Secondary education: near-parity or female advantage
- LFPR: significant gap

The GII provides a composite measure that the Maldives can use for inter-country comparison and for assessing the aggregate trajectory of gender inequality over time.[^517]

---


## 5.102 SAARC, AOSIS, and Regional Gender Cooperation


### 5.62.1 Maldives in SAARC Gender Policy

The Maldives is a member of the **South Asian Association for Regional Cooperation (SAARC)**, which includes a gender dimension through the SAARC Technical Committee on Women in Development and regional conventions on trafficking. SAARC provides a regional framework for gender policy learning and coordination, though its actual influence on domestic policy has been limited by SAARC's general consensus-dependent, non-binding character.

Relevant SAARC gender instruments:
- **SAARC Convention on Preventing and Combating Trafficking in Women and Children for Prostitution (2002):** Established a regional framework for anti-trafficking cooperation; the Maldives has ratified but the NRM implementation gap documented in §5.16 means engagement with the SAARC framework has been limited
- **SAARC Social Charter (2004):** Includes gender equality commitments across education, health, and social protection

The Maldives' geographic distance from the South Asian mainland and its primarily maritime orientation limit the practical intensity of SAARC engagement. The trafficking trafficking dimension – where cross-border movement of trafficked persons from South Asia into the Maldives is the primary regional gender policy concern – is the most operationally significant SAARC connection.[^468]

### 5.62.2 AOSIS and SIDS Gender Solidarity

The **Alliance of Small Island States (AOSIS)** is the more politically significant regional grouping for Maldivian foreign policy on climate and development. AOSIS's gender dimension has grown with the integration of gender into climate justice advocacy:

- AOSIS positions on loss and damage financing, climate adaptation, and SIDS-specific development finance all have gender dimensions that the Maldives' gender-responsive climate financing strategy positions it to advocate on
- SIDS solidarity on gender issues at the UNFCCC and UN General Assembly creates opportunities for amplifying Maldivian gender policy positions that would receive less attention from a single-country advocacy
- Pacific SIDS and Indian Ocean SIDS share governance challenges (geographic dispersion, small population, development partner dependency) that produce shared gender policy problems and potential learning partnerships

**The AOSIS women's network:** Some AOSIS member states have developed women's networks and cross-SIDS gender policy learning initiatives. The Maldives' participation in these networks has been limited but is a potential channel for accessing practical experience from comparable contexts – particularly on the quota model (from Pacific SIDS), on climate-gender integration (from Pacific SIDS with GCF experience), and on tourism gender inclusion (from Caribbean SIDS with more gender-inclusive tourism sectors).[^469]

### 5.62.3 CEDAW Committee Interaction with Regional Bodies

The CEDAW Committee's Concluding Observations for the Maldives engage specifically with SIDS-relevant issues – the geographic service access challenge, the fisheries and climate economy's gender dimensions – in a way that reflects the Committee's growing sophistication in applying CEDAW standards to small island contexts. This SIDS-specific engagement makes CEDAW a more useful accountability tool than it might be if the Committee applied only one-size-fits-all standards.

The HRCM's engagement with the UN Human Rights Council's Universal Periodic Review (UPR) process also provides a regional accountability dimension: reviewing states from South Asia and the Pacific have made gender equality recommendations to the Maldives in UPR cycles, creating a regional peer accountability dimension.

---


## 5.103 Evidence-Based Interventions: What the International Literature Suggests


### 5.93.1 What Works in DV Prevention and Response

The international evidence on effective GBV prevention and response interventions is extensive and, in some domains, well-established. The following summarises the highest-quality evidence most relevant to the Maldivian context:

**Structural interventions:**
- **Economic empowerment combined with gender norm change** (combined programmes like IMAGE in South Africa, WORTH in Nepal): Strong evidence of DV reduction when economic empowerment programming is integrated with social norm change curricula. Economic empowerment alone – without norm change – has weaker DV effects and sometimes increases DV as women's increased autonomy triggers controlling responses.
- **School-based healthy relationships programmes** (e.g., Safe Dates, Fourth R): Strong evidence of reduced adolescent dating violence when comprehensive healthy relationships curricula are implemented with fidelity. Most effective when teacher-trained and with parental engagement component.
- **Gender quotas in politics:** Strong evidence from multiple countries (Rwanda, India's panchayat quota) that reserved seat quotas rapidly increase women's representation and that women elected through quotas vote on women's policy issues at similar rates to women elected without quotas.[^526]

**Response system interventions:**
- **Evidence-based prosecution (EBP):** Strong evidence from Australia, UK, and US that DV prosecution that relies on all available evidence (not only survivor testimony) increases conviction rates and reduces the negative impact of survivor withdrawal. EBP requires police investigation quality that is not currently achieved in the Maldivian system.
- **Specialist domestic violence courts:** Evidence from UK, Australia, and Canada that specialist DV courts with dedicated judges, victim advocacy, and coordinated case management produce better outcomes (higher conviction, lower reoffending) than generalist courts.
- **One-Stop Crisis Centres (OSCCs):** Strong evidence from Bangladesh, South Africa, and Philippines that integrated service delivery (medical, legal, police, social work) at a single location reduces attrition and improves survivor outcomes compared with fragmented multi-agency systems. The Maldivian system's institutional fragmentation is directly addressed by the OSCC model.
- **Perpetrator intervention programmes (PIPs):** Moderate evidence (the evidence base is mixed) that certified PIPs reduce reoffending, particularly when combined with criminal justice monitoring and survivor safety planning.[^527]

### 5.93.2 Applying the Evidence to Maldives

The international evidence translates to the Maldivian context with important adaptations:

**OSCC adaptation for atoll geography:** A traditional fixed-location OSCC is impossible at national scale in the Maldives – the OSCC must be mobile, virtual, or atoll-capital based. A model in which atoll capital health facilities serve as hub-OSTCs – with collocated FPA caseworker, FCPW office, and FLC representative – for their atoll catchment area, supplemented by digital case management for smaller islands, is the geographically adapted version of the OSCC model.

**EBP for small police forces:** Evidence-based prosecution requires FCPW officers who systematically document all available evidence, including 999 call recordings, witness statements, medical reports, and digital evidence. Small outer island police forces lack the equipment and training for this documentation. The AIM Project's FCPW training component is moving in this direction; completion and sustained funding for the training and equipment is the enabling requirement.

**School-based programmes with Islamic framing:** The international evidence on school-based healthy relationships programmes demonstrates effectiveness; adaptation to the Maldivian context requires Islamic framing of consent and healthy relationships content – drawing on Islamic ethics of mutuality, the prophetic model of spousal treatment, and Islamic jurisprudence's explicit protections of women's rights within marriage. This framing is achievable but requires curriculum development work with Islamic scholars as partners, not only with health and education professionals.

---


# Part 19: Statistics, Census Data, and Cross-Chapter References


## 5.104 The 2022 Census: Gender-Disaggregated Findings


### 5.88.1 Census 2022 as a Gender Data Source

The **Population and Housing Census 2022** – conducted by the National Bureau of Statistics – is the most recent comprehensive data collection on all Maldivians. While the Census is a demographic enumeration rather than a specialised gender survey, its data provides important context for gender policy analysis.

**Population distribution:** The Census 2022 documents a total population of approximately 515,000 Maldivians (plus migrant workers). The male-to-female ratio nationally is approximately 50:50 at birth and in younger cohorts, with variation in working-age cohorts driven by labour migration patterns:
- **Malé and Hulhumale':** More balanced gender distribution, with slight female majority in the 20-40 age cohort reflecting female migration for education and employment
- **Resort islands:** Significantly male-majority given the male-dominated resort workforce
- **Outer atolls:** Gender distribution varies by atoll-specific migration patterns; atolls with high male out-migration (for resort employment) have female-majority populations of working-age adults

**Household composition:** The Census documents that female-headed households constitute approximately 42% of all households nationally – the figure aligned with the HIES 2016 finding that has been used throughout this chapter. This Census confirmation of the HIES figure provides additional data reliability.

**Marital status:** Census 2022 data on marital status disaggregated by gender documents the divorced women exceed divorced men by 42.86% – a cross-sectional snapshot reflecting the different remarriage rates and social consequences of divorce for men vs. women (men remarry faster; women remain in divorced status longer).[^519]

### 5.88.2 Education Data from Census 2022

The Census 2022 captures educational attainment by gender for the population aged 15 and above:
- **No education/incomplete primary:** Gender gap typically favouring women slightly in younger cohorts (reflecting historical male dropout patterns) while older cohorts show the opposite
- **Secondary completion:** Females outperform males at secondary completion in younger cohorts – consistent with the 88% male secondary dropout finding in the Education chapter
- **Tertiary education:** Female tertiary completion rates exceeding males in all cohorts below age 40 – consistent with the Ministry of Higher Education's 59-64% female student population figures

The Census educational attainment data provides a complete-population verification of the survey-based education findings – confirming that the female educational advantage in the Maldives is a genuine population-level phenomenon, not a survey artefact.

### 5.88.3 Economic Activity Data from Census 2022

Census 2022 economic activity data (employment status and sector) provides gender-disaggregated labour market information more current than HIES 2016:
- Gender-specific employment rates, if published at the required level of disaggregation, could update the LFPR figures
- Sectoral distribution by gender – confirming or updating the 43% government employment figure for women
- Employment in specific industries including tourism, fishing, and manufacturing by gender

The extent to which the Census 2022 microdata has been published in gender-disaggregated form is relevant: the Statistics Compendium (Part XVII) should be the reference for available Census data; where Census 2022 gender-disaggregated tables have been published by NBS, they provide the most current large-sample gender data available.[^520]
