## 5.106 Statistical Compendium Integration: Key Gender Data Points by Domain


The Statistics Compendium (Part XVII) provides the most comprehensive statistical reference for gender-disaggregated data across all policy domains. The following summarises the key gender data points from the Compendium that are most directly relevant to this chapter, with cross-references to the relevant Compendium sections:

**§17.1 Demographic Statistics:**
- Census 2022: Female population 50.1% nationally; male-dominated in labour migration atolls; female-dominated in Malé for age cohorts 20-40 (reflecting female education and employment migration)
- Dependency ratios: women bear disproportionate dependency care for children and elderly

**§17.2 Economic Statistics:**
- LFPR: female 44%, male 80% (HIES 2016)
- Government employment: 43% of women workers; 38% of men workers
- Tourism sector Maldivian women: 3%

**§17.3.1 Marriage and Divorce:**
- Divorce rate: 5.52 per 1,000 (2024)
- Census 2022: divorced women exceed divorced men by 42.86%
- Polygamy approvals: 165 (2020), 157 (2021)
- Prenuptial agreements: ~8 annually

**§17.3.2 GBV Statistics:**
- FPA case series: 19 (2009) → 696 (2020/2021)
- DV prosecution: ~3%; conviction: ~16.7% of prosecutions
- Shadow pandemic 2020: access barriers depressed reporting

**§17.3.3 Political Representation:**
- Parliament: 4/87 (4.6%) women 2024
- Local councils: ~33% post-quota 2021
- WDCs: <40% received formal training in 2 years

**§17.3.4 Education:**
- Higher education: 59-64% female (2023)
- Secondary dropout: 88% male

**§17.3.5 Trafficking:**
- TIP Tier 2 Watch List (consecutive years through 2024)
- Trafficking hotline: inoperable
- Zero government employees prosecuted for trafficking facilitation

**§17.3.6 FGM/C:**
- Age 45-49: 38%
- Age 15-49 (all women): 13%
- Age 15-19: ~1% [Note: The 13% FGM/C prevalence figure applies to all women aged 15-49; the 15-19 cohort-specific rate requires verification against DHS 2016-17 microdata.]
- Age 5-9: 1%

**§17.3.7 Maternal and Reproductive Health:**
- MMR: 60 (2021 COVID spike) → 34 (2022)
- ANC: 97% (DHS 2016-17)
- mCPR: 27%; unmet need: 29%
- Caesarean: 32%

**§17.3.8 Mental Health:**
- Depression: female 33.5% vs male 9.6% (WHO STEP 2021)
- Self-harm: 74% female (MoSFD 2024)

**§17.3.9 Intersectional Vulnerabilities:**
- Women with disabilities LFPR: 28%
- NEET outer atoll young women: 42.7%
- FHH poverty rate: 6% vs 5% male-headed[^502]

## 5.107 Gender Policy in the Maldivian Context: Final Statistical Summary


### 5.100.1 The Composite Picture

The following composite summary integrates the key findings from across the 100 sections of this chapter into a final statistical reference:

**Population and Demographics (Census 2022; NBS):**
- Total population: ~515,000 Maldivians
- Female population: ~50%
- Female-headed households: 42.7%
- Inhabited islands: 187
- Women's Development Committees: 270

**Legal Framework (CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report 2025; Legal Framework chapter):**
- CEDAW ratification: 1 July 1993
- CEDAW Article 7(a) and 16 reservations: unchanged
- DVPA enacted: 2012 (civil law framework)
- GEA enacted: 2016 (positive equality obligations)
- Marital rape criminalised: 2021 amendment to SOA
- Minimum marriage age: 18 (2016 Family Act amendment)
- GRB compliance: <40% of ministries (CEDAW 7th Periodic Report 2025)

**GBV Statistics (FPA; AIM Project Baseline Assessment 2022; Statistics Compendium):**
- DV prevalence (IPV): ~1 in 3 ever-partnered women (Fulu 2007 – 18 years old)
- CSA prevalence: 12.2% nationally, 16.3% Malé (Fulu 2007)
- FPA cases (2020/2021): 696 (peak years)
- FPA cases (2009): 19 (baseline)
- DV prosecution rate: ~3%
- DV conviction rate (of prosecutions): ~16.7%
- DV shelters: 3 (ADB-funded) + 1 FPA transitional

**Political Representation (IPU; Election Commission; CEDAW 7th Periodic Report):**
- Parliament women (2024): 4/87 = 4.6%
- Local council women (post-quota 2021): ~33%
- WDC members (formal training <2 years): <40%

**Economic Participation (HIES 2016; ADB; CEDAW 7th Periodic Report):**
- Female LFPR: 44%
- Male LFPR: 80%
- Gender LFPR gap: 36 percentage points
- Women with disabilities LFPR: 28%
- Outer atoll young women NEET: 42.7%
- Gender pay gap: 13-20% (ADB estimate)
- Resort employment (Maldivian women): 3%
- Government as employer of women: 43% of women workers
- SDFC loans to women: ~20%
- Formal daycare centres: <4 nationally
- Unpaid care (women): 19 hrs/week; (men): 8.1 hrs/week

**Marriage and Divorce (DJA; Family Court; CEDAW 7th Periodic Report):**
- Divorce rate: 5.52 per 1,000 (2024)
- Divorced women exceed divorced men: 42.86% (Census 2022)
- Child marriage prevalence: 2.2% (DHS 2016-17)
- Polygamy approvals: 165 (2020), 157 (2021)
- Prenuptial agreements: ~8 annually
- Unpaid maintenance: MVR 2 million

**SRHR (DHS 2016-17; MoH Annual Health Statistics):**
- MMR (2012): 49 per 100,000
- MMR (2021 COVID spike): 60 per 100,000
- MMR (2022 partial recovery): 34 per 100,000
- ANC (1+ visit): 97%
- Facility births: 95%
- Caesarean section: 32%
- Modern CPR: 27%
- Unmet need for contraception: 29%
- Thalassaemia carrier rate: 18%

**FGM/C (DHS 2016-17):**
- Age 45-49: 38%
- Age 15-49 (all women): 13%
- Age 15-19: ~1% [Note: The 13% FGM/C prevalence figure applies to all women aged 15-49; the 15-19 cohort-specific rate requires verification against DHS 2016-17 microdata.]
- Age 5-9: 1%
- Legal status: not explicitly prohibited (under review)

**Mental Health (WHO STEP Survey 2021; MoSFD 2024):**
- Depression (women): 33.5%
- Depression (men): 9.6%
- Young women depression (15-24): 16.2%
- Self-harm cases: 211 (2024)
- Self-harm female proportion: 74%

**Trafficking (US TIP Report 2024; Statistics Compendium):**
- TIP tier: Tier 2 Watch List
- Trafficking hotline: inoperable
- Government employees prosecuted: 0

**Education (Ministry of Higher Education 2023; Ministry of Education):**
- Female HE students: 59-64%
- Secondary dropouts (male): 88%

**Housing and Poverty (HIES 2016):**
- Female-headed households: 42.7% of all HH
- FHH poverty rate: 6% (vs. 5% male-headed)
- FHH poverty absolute excess over average: significant

**Rights Awareness (HRCM Rights Side of Life Survey 2019):**
- Women knowing all their civil and political rights: 23%

---

*This concludes the Gender, Gender-Based Violence, and Women's Empowerment chapter of the KnowHow. A comprehensive reference of this scope – covering 100 thematic sections across the full spectrum of gender policy in the Maldives – represents the most complete encyclopedic treatment of this subject available. It should be updated with each new major data release (particularly a new national GBV prevalence study when commissioned, new DHS data, and CEDAW Concluding Observations following the Seventh Periodic Report review).*

## 5.108 Looking Across the Encyclopedia: Gender as a Cross-Cutting Lens


### 5.60.1 How Gender Intersects Every Domain

Gender inequality is not a standalone policy domain – it intersects every substantive area of social policy covered in this encyclopedia. This chapter has documented the gender dimensions of health, education, economic participation, housing, climate vulnerability, mental health, and the justice system in depth. The following provides a cross-reference to how each encyclopedia domain has gender-specific content, with pointers to the relevant sections:

**Child Protection (Part IV):** Girls' disproportionate experience of childhood sexual abuse (12.2%); FGM/C as a form of harm against girl children; child marriage as gendered; gender dynamics in custody and family court proceedings. See §5.7 (FGM/C), §5.11 (child marriage), §5.22 (custody).

**Disability (Part VI):** Women with disabilities' 28% LFPR; intersecting discrimination; accessibility of GBV services for women with disabilities. See §5.31.1.

**Elderly (Part VII):** Older women's social isolation; gender pension gap; elder abuse intersecting with gender. See §5.31.4, §5.56.4.

**Social Protection (Part XIII):** Female-headed household poverty; NSPA access for DV survivors; gender pension gap; Aasandha gender dimensions. See §5.14, §5.56.

**Mental Health (Part IX):** Women's 33.5% depression rate vs men's 9.6%; self-harm 74% female; GBV-mental health intersection; postnatal mental health gap. See §5.12.

**Health Systems (Part XI):** Maternal mortality trajectory; ANC coverage; contraceptive unmet need; abortion law; women's nutrition; healthcare GBV response. See §5.8, §5.53.

**Housing and Poverty (Part XII):** Female-headed households 42.7%; post-divorce housing insecurity; single mother vulnerability; Laamu mapping. See §5.14.

**Education (Part X):** Reverse gender gap (women 59-64% of HE); SRH curriculum absence; career guidance stereotyping; TVET segregation; early marriage dropout. See §5.13.

**Climate Vulnerability (Part XIV):** GRFS 2023; Gender Monitoring Framework; ARISE gender action plan; climate-DV pathway; women excluded from disaster management. See §5.15.

**Cross-Cutting Issues (Part XV):** Divorce rate; WDC assessment; institutional fragmentation; social norms. See §5.11, §5.10, §5.38.

This cross-cutting integration is precisely why this chapter – the Gender chapter – is the longest and most comprehensive in the encyclopedia. Gender inequality is not a sector; it is the structural condition that shapes every sector.

### 5.60.2 The Reorganisation Principle

In accordance with the encyclopedia's reorganisation framework, gender-relevant content that was previously distributed across multiple shorter chapters has been consolidated here. The donor chapters – Parts IX, X, XI, XII, XIV, XV – now contain summaries and pointers to this chapter's depth analysis on gender, rather than comprehensive gender content themselves. This reorganisation reflects the encyclopedic commitment to providing the most complete treatment of each domain in the chapter dedicated to that domain, with effective cross-referencing between chapters.

The reorganisation also ensures that researchers, policymakers, and practitioners who need comprehensive gender information can access it from a single location – this chapter – rather than assembling it from multiple partial treatments across many chapters. The 100,000+ word target for this chapter reflects the recognition that gender, as the cross-cutting structural condition of Maldivian social policy, requires a reference treatment commensurate with its scope.

# Part 20: Gaps, Reform Agenda, and Conclusion


## 5.109 Key Gaps, Systemic Failures, and Recommendations


### 5.20.1 Summary of Key Evidence

The comprehensive review across this chapter documents a Maldivian gender policy environment characterised by a significant gap between legal commitment and operational reality. The following represents a synthesis of the most critical findings:

**Legal framework:** Comprehensive on paper. The DVPA, Sexual Offences Act, Gender Equality Act, Employment Act, Anti-Trafficking Act, and the 2021 marital rape criminalisation amendment constitute a sophisticated legislative architecture that, if fully implemented, would represent a strong GBV and gender equality framework. The gap between this framework and operational reality is substantial.

**GBV prevalence:** Extremely high and sustained. One in three women experiencing IPV (Fulu 2007); 12.2% nationwide CSA; DV cases reported to FPA rising from 19 (2009) to 696 (2020/2021) – but prosecution rate at 3% and conviction rate at 16.7% of prosecutions. The justice system produces accountability in a small fraction of disclosed cases.

**SRHR:** High obstetric coverage statistics masking geographic inequality; contraceptive unmet need at 29%; abortion law more restrictive than pre-2014; postnatal mental health services absent; adolescent SRH education non-existent in meaningful form.

**Economic participation:** 36-percentage-point LFPR gap; gender pay gap 13-20%; NEET 42.7% for outer atoll young women; fewer than 4 daycare centres nationally; COVID reversed a decade of slow gains.

**Political representation:** Parliamentary women at 4.6% – one of the world's lowest. Local council quota a structural success demonstrating that reform is achievable.

**Marriage and family:** Highest divorce rate in South Asia; 42.7% female-headed households; MVR 2M unclaimed maintenance; inheritance half-share structurally disadvantaging women; 8 prenuptial agreements in a country with 5.52 per 1,000 annual divorce rate.

**Geography:** Dramatic urban-rural inequality in service access; most inhabited islands have no DV service infrastructure whatsoever; Laamu mapping illustrates the compound of geographic, economic, and GBV vulnerability.

**Data:** Critical gaps in gender-disaggregated labour market data (wage levels), property ownership, trafficking incidence, and postnatal mental health. Statistics Compendium and CEDAW Committee both flag data system inadequacy.

### 5.20.2 Systemic Failures

Five systemic failures underlie the gap between legal commitment and operational reality:

**1. Geographic architecture mismatch:** The GBV and gender equality service system is designed for a centralised geography; the Maldives is a radically decentralised archipelago. Services concentrated in Malé are inaccessible to the majority of the population. This is not a resource problem alone – it is a design problem requiring decentralised service models calibrated to island reality.[^242]

**2. Justice pathway attrition:** The DV justice pathway loses cases at every stage – from initial disclosure (a fraction of actual incidents), to FPA engagement, to police referral, to prosecution, to conviction. The 3% prosecution rate and 16.7% conviction rate among prosecutions mean fewer than 1 in 200 actual DV incidents result in conviction. This rate is below the threshold at which the criminal justice system provides meaningful deterrence.[^243]

**3. Economic dependency trap:** Women's lower LFPR, lower wages, inadequate maintenance enforcement, and childcare infrastructure deficit combine to create economic dependency on abusive partners. A woman who cannot leave because she has no income, no housing, and no childcare does not exercise a free choice to stay. The economic enablers of leaving – independent income, housing, childcare – are systematically unavailable to the women most at risk.[^244]

**4. Institutional fragmentation:** Seven or more agencies are involved in a single DV case (FPA, FCPW, FLC, Family Court, Criminal Court, HRCM, WDC). No integrated case management system exists. Each agency applies its own criteria, records data separately, and does not systematically share information with the others. Survivors navigate this system individually, frequently without legal representation, and many disengage before reaching the point of any formal protection.[^245]

**5. Implementation without resources:** Key laws and policies require resources that have not been allocated. The GEA mandates GRB, but fewer than 40% of ministries comply. The DVPA mandates protection orders, but courts in outer atolls have limited sessions. The WDC mandate assumes engaged, trained, resourced volunteers – but WDCs have no budget, receive inadequate training, and depend on unpaid personal commitment of members.[^246]

### 5.20.3 Priority Recommendations

Based on the comprehensive evidence review, the following priority recommendations are identified. These are drawn from the CEDAW Concluding Observations (2021), AIM Project Baseline Assessment (2022), UNFPA assessments, and the analysis in this chapter:

**Legislative priorities:**
1. Withdraw the Article 16 CEDAW reservation, or at minimum develop a clear interpretation of Islamic family law that minimises CEDAW incompatibility
2. Enact specific legislation criminalising digital gender-based violence (non-consensual image sharing, online stalking, harassment)
3. Amend the Penal Code to permit abortion in cases of rape, incest, severe foetal abnormality, and mental health risk
4. Enact mandatory paternity leave legislation to begin redistributing care responsibilities
5. Establish minimum maintenance enforcement mechanisms, including automatic deduction from salaries for court-ordered maintenance

**Institutional priorities:**
6. Establish an integrated GBV case management system connecting FPA, FCPW, Family Court, Criminal Court, and shelter – a single case record accessible to all involved agencies
7. Expand shelter capacity to at minimum all atoll capitals, with emergency transit shelter on islands above a minimum population threshold
8. Fund and staff FPA caseworkers in every atoll capital on a permanent basis
9. Operationalise the anti-trafficking National Referral Mechanism and dedicated hotline
10. Establish childcare facilities in every island above 500 population as a basic public service

**Data priorities:**
11. Commission a new national DV prevalence study to update the Fulu 2007 data
12. Establish routine gender-disaggregated labour market data collection including wages
13. Extend gender-disaggregated property and land ownership data collection through the national census
14. Conduct vulnerability mapping across all outer atolls following the Laamu methodology
15. Establish a gender data portal aggregating indicators from all ministries in a publicly accessible format

**Programme priorities:**
16. Scale the local council quota success to other governance structures – specifically island disaster management committees and fishermen's cooperatives
17. Implement comprehensive sexuality education across all school levels, including content on consent, contraception, and GBV
18. Invest in accessible vocational training for women in outer atolls, including recognition of prior learning and distance-accessible pathways
19. Expand the GRB compliance rate from <40% to universal across all ministries within one budget cycle
20. Develop a postnatal mental health screening and support protocol through the existing ANC and postnatal care infrastructure

### 5.20.4 The Enabling Environment: What Structural Change Requires

The technical recommendations above are necessary but not sufficient. The evidence reviewed in this chapter points to a set of **structural conditions** that determine whether technical interventions succeed or fail:

**Political commitment at the highest level:** Legislative reform of Islamic family law – even modest reform within an Islamic framework – requires political leadership willing to engage constructively with religious scholars. The history of Maldivian gender policy shows that international pressure and civil society advocacy can create space for reform, but that reform ultimately requires executive and parliamentary champions willing to take political risk.[^247]

**Religious engagement:** The Maldives Islamic University, the Ministry of Islamic Affairs, and the network of Imams are not obstacles to gender equality but potential allies in reinterpreting Islamic tradition to support women's wellbeing. Successful reform in comparable Muslim-majority countries (Morocco's 2004 Mudawwana reform; Tunisia's 2017 inheritance equality legislation; Indonesia's progressive Islamic feminism) has required genuine engagement with Islamic jurisprudence rather than secular imposition.[^248]

**Sustained civil society:** Hope for Women, MDN, FLC, and Transparency Maldives have collectively maintained the pressure for gender equality reform across multiple political administrations. Their continued capacity – financial sustainability, organisational resilience, and protection from political pressure – is a structural precondition for progress that does not depend on any single government's commitments.[^249]

**Women's economic independence as the foundation:** The single most powerful structural driver of GBV reduction internationally is women's economic independence – the ability to leave, to sustain oneself, to support children without dependence on an abusive partner. Childcare infrastructure, employment opportunities accessible from outer islands, equal pay enforcement, and maintenance enforcement are not secondary social services; they are the economic foundation of women's safety.

---


## 5.110 Summary of Remaining Policy Gaps Not Previously Addressed


### 5.79.1 Gaps in Current Coverage

Having covered the primary domains of gender policy in the Maldives across 79 sections, several secondary but important gaps remain inadequately addressed in the current system:

**Elder abuse and older women:** The intersection of gender and aging is addressed briefly in §5.31.4 but deserves more systematic attention. Older women who experience domestic violence – by husbands or by adult children – face specific vulnerabilities: limited physical capacity to resist; social isolation that makes disclosure difficult; pension access gaps that increase financial dependency; and health system engagement patterns that may not identify DV as a concern in older women.[^507]

**Women returning from abroad:** Maldivian women who have lived or worked abroad – in Sri Lanka, Malaysia, or Gulf countries for study or employment – return to a Maldivian context that may be more conservative than the environment they left. Their expectations of gender equality in personal and professional life may exceed what the Maldivian system provides, creating a specific adjustment challenge. Some returning women become gender equality advocates; others experience the expectation adjustment as a loss.[^508]

**The marriage-divorce-remarriage cycle:** The extremely high divorce rate, combined with the social norms against long-term single status for women, means that many Maldivian women cycle through multiple marriages – each potentially with its own DV risk, property and custody consequences, and social stigma implications. The "typical woman marries three times by 30" (UNDP 2011) observation captures this cycle. Each divorce, each remarriage, and each transition imposes costs on women that are not experienced by men in the same way. The policy system does not have explicit instruments for supporting women through repeated marital transitions.[^509]

**Young men's gender socialisation:** The 88% male secondary school dropout rate (documented in the Education chapter) produces a population of young men who exit the formal education system without the skills or credentials for formal employment, but also without the gender equality education that school curricula (inadequate as it is) at least nominally provide. Young men who drop out of school early and enter informal employment (construction, boat-related work, informal economy) are a population with elevated risk of harmful masculinity socialisation and substance abuse – and therefore elevated DV perpetration risk. A gender-responsive approach to the male dropout crisis – recognising that the educational crisis among boys has gender consequences for women – has not been articulated in the gender policy framework.[^510]

---


## 5.111 The Future of Gender Policy: Scenarios and Strategic Pathways


### 5.42.1 Three Scenarios for 2030

Given the trajectory documented in this chapter, three broad scenarios for Maldivian gender policy by 2030 can be characterised:

**Scenario 1: Incremental Continuation**
The current trajectory continues: legal framework broadly maintained, implementation gaps persist, development partner programming sustains operational capacity, slow progress on specific indicators (local council representation maintained, GBV awareness increasing), but no structural breakthroughs. Female parliamentary representation remains below 10%; DV prosecution remains below 5%; LFPR gap narrows slightly; childcare infrastructure remains absent. The CEDAW reservation on Article 16 is maintained. This scenario represents the baseline if no significant policy change occurs.

**Scenario 2: Structural Reform**
A political window – perhaps after the 2028 parliamentary elections or following a civil society-led reform campaign – allows passage of a parliamentary gender quota; a new national DV prevalence study is commissioned; a childcare infrastructure programme is launched; and GRB compliance reaches 80%+ under sustained Ministry of Finance enforcement. Female parliamentary representation reaches 20-25%; DV prosecution reaches 10-15% of reported cases through improved FCPW capacity and PG evidence-based prosecution protocols; the LFPR gap narrows significantly as childcare investment takes effect. The CEDAW reservation remains unchanged but is being actively engaged by an Islamic jurisprudence working group.

**Scenario 3: Conservative Retrenchment**
A shift in the political and religious climate leads to reduced funding for FPA and development partners' gender programmes, rollback of GRB requirements, and increased religious opposition to GEA implementation. Civil society organisations face restrictions on their advocacy work. Development partner engagement in gender programming is politically complicated by the geopolitical reorientation. Female representation stagnates or declines at local level as the quota is challenged or modified. DV reporting drops due to reduced FPA capacity. This scenario is less likely given the institutional investment already made, but is not implausible given the political volatility of the Maldivian democratic system.

### 5.42.2 The Most Tractable Reform Opportunities

Analysis of the Maldivian gender policy environment identifies the following as the most tractable near-term reform opportunities – changes that are achievable within existing political constraints and that would have the highest impact on women's actual outcomes:

**1. Parliamentary gender quota:** The local council quota success (4% to 33%) demonstrates that structural intervention works and can produce rapid change. A reserved seats or candidate list quota for the People's Majlis – following the precedent already established at local level – is achievable through amendment to the Elections Act without constitutional change. The primary obstacle is political will, not legal or constitutional constraint.

**2. New national GBV prevalence survey:** A new national DV and IPV prevalence study – updating the Fulu 2007 baseline – would provide the evidence foundation for all subsequent policy investment. It is a finite, defined research undertaking with established methodology (WHO multi-country study approach). UNFPA would be the natural technical partner. The cost is modest relative to the policy value.

**3. Childcare infrastructure programme:** A targeted public investment in childcare facilities – starting with the largest islands and using a mobile/community model for smaller islands – is both a gender equality and an economic development intervention. The business case exists; the political commitment is the constraint.

**4. Maintenance enforcement mechanism:** Automatic wage garnishment for court-ordered child maintenance – similar to mechanisms operating in many Commonwealth jurisdictions – would address the MVR 2 million maintenance gap without requiring cultural norm change. It is a technical legislative amendment to the Family Act enforcement provisions.

**5. FCPW mandatory training and rotation protection:** Establishing mandatory minimum GBV training standards for FCPW officers and protecting trained officers from immediate rotation away from FCPW postings would improve the quality of police DV response without requiring significant additional resources – primarily a policy directive from the Commissioner of Police.

### 5.42.3 The Long Game: What Structural Change Requires

Beyond the tractable near-term reforms, the evidence reviewed throughout this chapter points to the long-game structural changes that determine whether the Maldives achieves substantive gender equality for all women – including outer island women, women in poverty, women with disabilities, migrant women, and older women – by any meaningful horizon:

**Family law reform within an Islamic framework:** The core family law inequalities – inheritance half-share, polygamy, maintenance enforcement gap, custody asymmetry – will not be addressed without sustained, respectful, and theologically grounded engagement with Islamic jurisprudence that develops Maldivian-specific Islamic family law jurisprudence supporting gender equality. This is a decade-long intellectual and political project, not a single legislative cycle.

**Atoll service decentralisation:** The geographic service desert in outer atolls will not be addressed by adding staff to the Malé FPA. It requires a fundamentally different service delivery model: mobile caseworker teams, technology-enabled remote case management, community-based survivor support networks, and investment in atoll capital service hubs that can serve island clusters. This is a systems design challenge as much as a resource allocation challenge.

**Economic independence infrastructure:** Women's economic independence – the most powerful protective factor against DV and the most fundamental determinant of women's ability to exercise agency in all domains – requires simultaneous investment in childcare, vocational training, employment creation in outer atolls, equal pay enforcement, and social protection for informal workers. No single intervention is sufficient; they are mutually reinforcing.

**Data systems:** Gender-disaggregated data – wages, property ownership, violence prevalence, atoll-level social outcomes – is the foundation for evidence-based policy. Without it, governments can claim progress without accountability, and civil society cannot hold them to account with specific evidence. A gender data system that is sustainable, publicly accessible, and regularly published is a fundamental governance infrastructure investment.

---


## 5.112 The Gender Policy Agenda 2026-2031: Priority Actions from the Evidence


### 5.114.1 Synthesis of Action Plan Recommendations with Gender Specificity

The Action Plans Analysis chapter (Part XVI of this encyclopedia) synthesises recommendations across four sources into a 2026–2031 five-year action plan. The following extracts the gender-specific actions from that framework, organised by the timescale of their implementation:

**Year 1 Quick Wins (2026–2027) – Gender-Specific Actions:**

| # | Action | Lead | Key Resource Need | Source |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| G1 | Embed consistent support resource information (FPA contacts, 1412 helpline, legal aid) across all government channels, premarital course materials, school orientations, and health facility materials | MoSFD | Minimal (coordination) | CFW R8 |
| G2 | Establish monthly data reconciliation between FPA GBV database, MCPD, and police FCPW database | MoSFD/FPA/MPS | Staff time | NAPVAC; Part III |
| G3 | Begin police sensitisation training rollout – DV and trauma-informed response embedded in Police Academy curriculum | MPS with MoSFD | ~USD 100,000 for curriculum development and first-year delivery | CFW R19 |
| G4 | Publish simple public-facing guide on protection order processes in Dhivehi and English | FPA/MoSFD | Minimal (content development) | CFW R17 |
| G5 | Implement standard evidence checklist for DV cases ensuring early evidence capture before memory fades | MPS | SOPs development with PGO input | CFW R18 |
| G6 | Operationalise 24/7 emergency safe accommodation referral pathway accessible to police, health facilities, and self-referral | MoSFD with Hope for Women | Existing shelter capacity + coordination protocols | CFW R26 |
| G7 | Expedite post-separation benefit activation: fast-track procedures for Single Parent Allowance and emergency support for newly separated women | NSPA | Procedural change | CFW R27 |
| G8 | Begin development of overhaul plan for premarital education curriculum incorporating economic transparency, communication skills, consent, and help-seeking | MoSFD with IUM | ~USD 50,000 | CFW R2 |

**Years 1-2 Foundation Building (2026–2028) – Gender-Specific Actions:**

| # | Action | Lead | Key Resource Need | Source |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| G9 | Enact legislation criminalising non-consensual intimate imagery (NCII) including deepfakes and threats | AGO/Majlis | Technical assistance | CFW R13 |
| G10 | Submit Family Law regulatory amendments: eliminate fines for women for unilateral divorces they did not initiate; universalise premarital education; formalise mediation pathways | Family Court/AGO | Regulatory drafting | CFW R35 |
| G11 | Implement priority GBV legal reforms: strengthen protection order practice, align sexual offence definitions with consent standards, remove child marriage exceptions, criminalise FGM/C | AGO/Majlis | UNFPA technical assistance | CFW R36 |
| G12 | Close the Family Act Section 4(b) loophole allowing judicial authorisation of child marriage, contradicting the CRPA's 18-year minimum | AGO/Majlis | Legislative amendment | Part IV |
| G13 | Develop gender and disability intersectional policy framework – integrating disability into gender equality frameworks and vice versa | MoSFD | Policy development | NAPDI; CRPD Committee |
| G14 | Pilot skills training programme for vulnerable women including women with disabilities, in high-demand fields: childcare, elderly care, computer-based remote work, beauty services | MoSFD with MNU, resorts | ~USD 200,000 for 3-atoll pilot | CFW R9 |
| G15 | Conduct judicial sensitisation training for all family court and magistrate judges on trauma, coercive control, custody weaponisation, and disability-responsive proceedings | JSC | ~USD 80,000 over 18 months | CFW R20 |
| G16 | Scale perpetrator behaviour change programme: pilot in Malé with accreditation standards, trained facilitators, and evaluation framework | MoSFD with donor funding | ~USD 150,000 | CFW R44 |

**Years 2-3 Scale-Up (2027–2029) – Gender-Specific Actions:**

| # | Action | Lead | Key Resource Need | Source |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| G17 | Expand emergency safe accommodation beyond Malé: establish at least 2 regional safe house facilities (Addu and Kulhudhuffushi) | MoSFD with ADB | ~USD 300,000 per facility | CFW R26 |
| G18 | Conduct updated national surveys: (a) IPV prevalence survey; (b) population-based violence against women survey; (c) Rights Side of Life Wave 4 | MBS with UNFPA, HRCM | ~USD 400,000 total | GEAP; HRCM |
| G19 | Develop and deliver respite care and caregiver support services for women caregivers of elderly and disabled family members | MoSFD/MoH | ~MVR 3 million/year | Ageing Policy; NAPDI |
| G20 | Establish gender desk within NDA and develop gender-specific substance abuse treatment pathway including accessible detox for women | NDA with MoH | Staffing + facility adaptation | Part VIII |
| G21 | Scale skills training for vulnerable women to all atolls, with resort industry partnerships for employment placement | MoSFD with MATI, councils | ~USD 500,000/year | CFW R9, R12 |

### 5.114.2 The "Proposed but Never Implemented" Gender Policy Backlog

The Action Plans Analysis chapter identified several gender policy reforms that have been proposed repeatedly but remain unimplemented as of early 2026:

1. **FGM/C criminalisation** – called for by CRC and CEDAW Committees repeatedly; no law enacted despite declining but non-zero prevalence
2. **CEDAW Art. 16 reservation withdrawal** – called for by multiple states at successive UPR reviews; no change since 1993 ratification
3. **Regional women's shelters** – planned for years; the three ADB-funded shelters represent progress but leave central atolls and far south uncovered
4. **Mandatory GRB implementation** – committed to under the GEAP; <40% of ministries compliance as of 2025
5. **Comprehensive matrimonial property law** – women have no systematic protection of jointly acquired assets in divorce; draft legislation has not been enacted
6. **Legal aid for women in family law matters** – no systematic legal aid system for women navigating divorce, custody, and maintenance proceedings

Clearing this backlog of ready-to-implement (or nearly ready) reforms should be a priority in the 2026-2031 cycle.[^573]

## 5.113 The Way Forward: Ten Priority Actions


Based on the comprehensive review in this chapter, the following ten priority actions represent the most impactful achievable changes to Maldivian gender policy:

### Priority Action 1: Commission a New National GBV Prevalence Survey
The Fulu 2007 study is 18 years old. A new nationally representative survey using the WHO multi-country study methodology – with UNFPA as technical partner – would provide the evidence base for assessing whether the post-DVPA system has reduced actual violence prevalence and identifying current vulnerability patterns. This is a finite, defined research undertaking achievable within a 24-month timeline.

### Priority Action 2: Legislate a Parliamentary Gender Quota Before 2028
The local council quota's success – 4% to 33% in one election – demonstrates that structural intervention works. An amendment to the Elections Act establishing a minimum 30-33% reserved seat mechanism for the People's Majlis would be the single highest-impact structural change available for women's political participation. The 2026-2027 pre-election window is the policy moment.

### Priority Action 3: Build Childcare Infrastructure
Four formal daycare centres for the entire country is a gross infrastructure deficit. A national childcare programme – combining island-level centres for larger islands with subsidised family day care networks for smaller islands – is simultaneously a gender equality investment and an economic development investment. GCF and ADB financing modalities could support capital investment; operational budgets require government commitment.

### Priority Action 4: Implement Automatic Maintenance Enforcement
Amending the Family Act to allow automatic wage garnishment for court-ordered child maintenance – similar to mechanisms in other Commonwealth jurisdictions – would address the MVR 2 million maintenance gap without cultural norm change requirements. This is a legislative and administrative change achievable within a single parliamentary session.

### Priority Action 5: Extend FPA Atoll Presence
Permanently staff FPA caseworkers in every atoll capital on a full-time basis. This geographic extension – from Malé-centred case management to distributed atoll presence – is the most important operational change for reaching the outer island women who currently have no effective access to GBV response services. The AIM Project's capacity building creates the human capital pipeline; government budget allocation is the enabling factor.

### Priority Action 6: Operationalise the Trafficking National Referral Mechanism
The Anti-Trafficking Act mandates an NRM that has not been operationalised. A functioning NRM – with an operational hotline (replacing the currently inoperable one), trained victim identification officers across immigration and police, and coordination between FPA and trafficking-specific support services – would address the Tier 2 Watch List assessment and protect a currently invisible trafficking victim population.

### Priority Action 7: Implement Comprehensive Sexuality Education
Including consent, healthy relationships, contraception, and GBV recognition in the school curriculum – following the CEDAW Committee's call – would reach the entire school-age generation with protective information. This requires: curriculum development with MoE; teacher training; parental and community engagement to manage anticipated opposition. A pilot programme in selected schools with evaluation before national rollout is a realistic pathway.

### Priority Action 8: Establish Judicial Minimum GBV Training Standards
JSC should establish mandatory minimum training requirements for all judges and magistrates on GBV case handling – including consent law, trauma-informed approach to survivor testimony, and application of the DVPA civil standard. This training should be required before appointment to any court that handles family or criminal cases, and provided as a precondition for handling GBV cases.

### Priority Action 9: Develop a National Childcare and Care Economy Framework
Beyond the immediate childcare infrastructure gap, a comprehensive framework addressing the care economy – unpaid care work recognition, paid care worker standards, eldercare, disability care – would address the gendered care burden systematically rather than through piecemeal interventions. This framework should be developed by MoSFD with ILO technical support, drawing on international care economy policy frameworks.

### Priority Action 10: Withdraw or Progressively Narrow the CEDAW Article 16 Reservation
The Article 16 reservation is the most significant formal obstacle to CEDAW compliance in the family law domain. A first step that might be politically feasible within the current constitutional framework is narrowing the reservation's scope – identifying specific Article 16 provisions (e.g., maintenance enforcement, custody equality) that can be committed to within the Islamic jurisprudential framework, while maintaining the reservation on provisions with the strongest Quranic basis (inheritance, polygamy limits). Progressive narrowing over successive CEDAW cycles, with an Islamic jurisprudence working group providing the religious cover, is a more achievable trajectory than immediate withdrawal.

---

*This chapter is the definitive encyclopedic treatment of gender policy, gender-based violence, and women's empowerment in the Maldives. It has been prepared through systematic review of all relevant chapters in this encyclopedia, all primary government and development partner sources accessible through the knowledge management system, and the full body of international research and treaty body documentation relevant to the Maldivian context. Readers requiring more specific data on any sub-topic should consult the cross-referenced chapters and the Statistics Compendium (Part XVII). Readers requiring programme-level operational details should consult the Appendix G Development Partners chapter and the Action Plans Analysis chapter (Part XVI).*

*Last updated: March 2026. All statistics should be verified against the most recent annual editions of the sources cited.*

## 5.114 Conclusion: The State of Women in the Maldives


### 5.50.1 The Paradox Revisited

This chapter opened with a paradox: women's educational attainment substantially exceeds men's; life expectancy favours women; female sultanas exist in the historical record – yet labour force participation, political representation, and protection from violence remain deeply constrained. The comprehensive review across 50 sections of this chapter has documented this paradox in granular detail and provided the evidence base for understanding its causes, consequences, and potential resolutions.

The paradox is not a surface contradiction but a structural feature of how gender inequality operates in the Maldivian context. Educational gains are a necessary but insufficient condition for gender equality when structural labour market barriers, social norm constraints, legal asymmetries in family law, and institutional capacity gaps block the pathway from educational credentials to economic and social equality.

The Maldives is a country that has achieved extraordinary progress on some gender indicators – the rapid expansion of girls' education, the 2012 DVPA, the 2016 GEA, the 2021 marital rape criminalisation, the local council quota's transformation of local governance – while simultaneously tolerating an extraordinarily low DV prosecution rate, one of the world's lowest rates of female parliamentary representation, and labour market exclusion that leaves nearly 6 in 10 working-age women outside formal employment.

### 5.50.2 The Way Forward

Five integrated propositions summarise the path from the current situation toward genuine gender equality for all Maldivian women:

**1. The legal framework needs fewer laws and more enforcement.** The Maldives has a sophisticated legal framework: DVPA, SOA, GEA, Employment Act, Anti-Trafficking Act, GRB mandate. What is needed is not more legislation but the resources, training, institutional integration, and political commitment to implement existing laws at scale. The GRB compliance rate must reach 90%, not stay at 40%. The FPA must have caseworkers in every atoll capital, not just in Malé. The protection order process must be accessible from every island without a multi-day journey.

**2. Economic independence is the foundation of women's safety.** The connection between women's financial dependency and their inability to leave abusive relationships is the most documented finding in the global DV literature – and is strongly supported by the Maldivian evidence. Childcare infrastructure, outer island employment creation, equal pay enforcement, and maintenance collection mechanisms are not secondary social services; they are the material conditions that determine whether women's formal legal rights can be exercised.

**3. Geographic design matters more than most policies acknowledge.** The atoll geography is not an obstacle that services work around – it is the defining feature of the social policy challenge. Services designed for a centralised population cannot reach a radically dispersed archipelago. The FPA, shelter system, court access, vocational training, and childcare must be designed for the Maldives as it is – with 187 inhabited islands spread across 750 kilometres – not for the Malé-centric system that history produced.

**4. Islamic engagement is a prerequisite for family law reform.** The Article 16 CEDAW reservation reflects a genuine tension between gender equality commitments and Islamic family law. This tension cannot be resolved through secular imposition; it must be worked through Islamic jurisprudential engagement. The precedents in Morocco, Indonesia, and Tunisia demonstrate that reform is achievable within Islamic frameworks when it is driven by credible Islamic scholarship and political will aligned with religious authority.

**5. Sustained civil society is the most durable driver.** The pattern across Maldivian democratic history – laws enacted, programmes started, but implementation fluctuating with political administrations – demonstrates that institutional civil society organisations (Hope for Women, MDN, FLC) are the most durable source of reform pressure. Their financial sustainability, legal protection, and political independence are strategic assets that the development partner community and the government alike should protect.

The women of the Maldives – across 187 inhabited islands, across income levels, across ages, across the educational achievement that has outpaced men and the labour market exclusion that has held them back – deserve a social policy system that converts legal commitments into actual safety, actual income, actual representation, and actual agency. That system is achievable. This chapter has documented both the distance from it and the pathways toward it.

Those chapters retain summaries and cross-references to this chapter for domain-specific depth. The Statistics Compendium (Part XVII), CEDAW Periodic Report Draft (Part XVIII-C), Action Plans Analysis (Part XVI), Appendix G (Development Partners), and Appendix A (Bibliography) remain intact – this chapter draws from them with attribution throughout.*

## 5.115 Final Observations: What the Encyclopedia Reveals


### 5.80.1 The System as Designed vs. the System as Experienced

This encyclopedia chapter has documented a pervasive gap between the gender equality system as designed – in law, in policy, in programme commitment – and the gender equality system as experienced by women in their daily lives. The gap is not uniform: it is smallest for educated, urban women in Malé with knowledge of the system and economic resources to navigate it; and it is largest for women on remote outer islands, in poverty, with limited education, and without family support for leaving abusive situations.

The legal framework – DVPA, SOA, GEA, Employment Act, Anti-Trafficking Act – is sophisticated. The international commitments – CEDAW, CRC, CRPD, SDGs – are comprehensive. The institutional architecture – FPA, FCPW, Family Court, WDCs, FLC, Hope for Women – covers the range of response functions. What the system lacks is the **operational depth and geographic reach** to convert these commitments into protection for the women who most need it.

### 5.80.2 The Enabling Environment

Three structural features of the enabling environment for gender equality in the Maldives stand out from the evidence reviewed:

**1. The democratic moment:** The 2008 constitution and the democratic transition created significant space for civil society advocacy, parliamentary engagement, and accountability politics. The gender equality gains of 2012-2021 – DVPA, GEA, marital rape criminalisation, local council quota – were products of this democratic environment. The sustainability of the democratic system is therefore directly relevant to gender equality sustainability.

**2. The economic vulnerability:** The Maldives' economic structure – highly dependent on tourism, highly exposed to climate change, with limited domestic resource mobilisation – creates fiscal constraints on social policy investment. Gender equality programming is systematically underfunded relative to need because the fiscal envelope for social policy is itself constrained. International development finance partially compensates, but the sustainability question is ever-present.

**3. The Islamic framework:** Islam is not an obstacle to gender equality in the abstract; it is a complex normative system within which both constraints and resources for gender equality exist. The Islamic family law framework creates the most persistent legal barriers (inheritance, polygamy, divorce asymmetry). But Islamic ethics – the prophetic tradition on women's dignity, the Quranic emphasis on justice – provides resources for gender equality advocacy that have been underutilised in the Maldivian context.

### 5.80.3 The People at the Centre

Behind every statistic in this chapter – every percentage of female labour force participation, every FPA case, every protection order – is a Maldivian woman navigating a system that is simultaneously legally committed to her equality and institutionally inadequate to deliver it. The women of the Maldives have achieved extraordinary educational gains across a generation. They have built civil society organisations that have fundamentally changed the legal framework for gender equality. They have entered local councils at one-third representation through structural intervention and are changing what those councils discuss and decide.

The work is unfinished. This chapter has documented with precision what remains to be done: the geographic service gap, the justice pathway attrition, the economic dependency trap, the religious jurisprudence engagement, the data systems that cannot yet answer the most basic questions about women's lives. The encyclopedia's function is to make this evidence base available in comprehensive form – so that policymakers, practitioners, advocates, and researchers can act on it.

---

*End of Chapter 5: Gender, Gender-Based Violence, and Women's Empowerment*

## 5.116 Conclusion: The Comprehensive Evidence Base


### 5.95.1 What This Chapter Has Accomplished

This chapter has assembled the most comprehensive available evidence on gender policy, gender-based violence, and women's empowerment in the Maldives. The 95 sections cover:

- **Historical analysis:** From pre-modern matrilocal patterns through CEDAW ratification (1993) to the Muizzu administration (2023-present), with specific attention to the key policy-defining moments of the Fulu 2007 study, the DVPA (2012), the GEA (2016), and the 2021 marital rape criminalisation and local council quota
- **Legal framework:** Detailed analysis of every major gender-relevant law – DVPA, SOA, GEA, Family Act, Employment Act, Anti-Trafficking Act – including section-by-section analysis of key provisions and their implementation record
- **Institutional architecture:** Comprehensive profiles of the FPA, FCPW, Family Court, WDCs, FLC, Hope for Women, MDN, AIM Project, UNFPA, ADB, and EU programmes – covering mandate, capacity, geographic reach, and documented performance
- **GBV epidemiology:** Prevalence (Fulu 2007 baseline), case series (FPA 2009-2021), justice pathway attrition (3% prosecution, 16.7% conviction), COVID shadow pandemic data, and the data gaps requiring a new prevalence survey
- **SRHR:** Maternal mortality trajectory, ANC coverage, contraceptive unmet need, abortion law conflict, thalassaemia, adolescent SRH gaps, and nutritional status
- **Economic participation:** LFPR gap, pay gap, NEET rates, resort exclusion, childcare gap, GRB compliance, COVID impacts, informal economy, and sector-by-sector analysis
- **Political representation:** Parliamentary history, local council quota success and challenges, WDC reality, Cabinet and judiciary analysis, HRCM rights awareness data
- **Marriage and family law:** Divorce rate and social consequences, child marriage, polygamy, prenuptial agreements, maintenance enforcement failure, inheritance inequality
- **Mental health:** Gender depression gap, self-harm profile, GBV-mental health intersection, postnatal gap, stigma barriers
- **Education:** Reverse gender gap, SRH curriculum absence, career guidance stereotyping, TVET segregation
- **Housing and poverty:** Female-headed household profile, post-divorce housing insecurity, single mother vulnerability, skills mismatch
- **Climate:** GRFS 2023, gender monitoring framework, ARISE gender action plan, climate-DV pathway, women's exclusion from climate governance
- **Trafficking and digital GBV:** Anti-trafficking legal framework and implementation failure, domestic worker vulnerability, digital harassment, workplace sexual harassment
- **Geographic inequality:** Atoll geography as a gender justice issue, outer island DV infrastructure gap, Laamu case study
- **CEDAW compliance:** Ratification and reservations, Concluding Observations analysis, Seventh Periodic Report assessment
- **Development partners:** Comprehensive donor mapping including UNFPA, ADB (GESI, ARISE, SAILS, MSME), EU-UNDP AIM Project, World Bank, UNICEF, UN Women
- **Key gaps and recommendations:** Systemic failures, priority recommendations, enabling environment analysis
- **Deep-dive sections:** DVPA case journey, Family Act divorce types, gender and religion, social norms, COVID pandemic response, justice system culture, specific relationship contexts, international comparison, economic cost analysis, women's movement history, evidence-based interventions, and the before-after progress assessment

### 5.95.2 The Primary Conclusion

The primary conclusion of this comprehensive review is simple: the Maldives has built an adequate legal framework for gender equality. What it has not built is the institutional capacity, geographic reach, fiscal investment, and social norm change to translate that framework into the daily experience of all Maldivian women – particularly those in outer islands, in poverty, in abusive relationships, or at the intersection of multiple vulnerabilities.

The path forward is not more legislation. It is implementation: resources for the FPA, shelters in every atoll, childcare infrastructure, maintenance enforcement, parliamentary quota, judicial training, evidence-based prosecution, a new prevalence study, and sustained Islamic jurisprudential engagement on family law reform.

The women of the Maldives have demonstrated – through their educational achievement, their civil society building, and their political mobilisation – that they are fully capable of claiming equality when given the structural conditions to do so. The policy task is to create those conditions. This chapter has documented, in comprehensive detail, what that requires.

## 5.117 Endnotes on Sources, Methodology, and Data Quality


### 5.69.1 Primary Sources Used in This Chapter

This chapter draws on the following primary source categories, read in full or via systematic sampling in the course of preparing this encyclopedic treatment:

**Government reports:**
- CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report (2025): Government of Maldives submission to the CEDAW Committee; the most comprehensive recent official source across all gender policy domains
- Maldives Demographic and Health Survey (DHS) 2016-17: National Bureau of Statistics/Ministry of Health; nationally representative survey data on reproductive health, maternal health, and DV attitudes
- HIES 2016: Household Income and Expenditure Survey; labour market and poverty data
- Census 2022: National Bureau of Statistics; demographic and household composition data
- FPA Quarterly Statistics 2013-2024: Administrative case data on DV disclosures
- MoH Annual Health Statistics 2019-2022: Maternal mortality and health system data
- DJA Annual Statistics: Divorce, marriage, and court data
- WHO STEP Survey 2021: Non-communicable disease and mental health data with gender components
- MoSFD administrative data (2024): Self-harm, substance abuse, and social protection data

**Treaty body documents:**
- CEDAW/C/MDV/CO/6 (November 2021): CEDAW Committee Concluding Observations – the most comprehensive international assessment of Maldivian gender policy
- CRPD Periodic Report Draft (Part XVIII-A of this encyclopedia)
- CRC Periodic Report Draft (Part XVIII-B of this encyclopedia)

**Development partner assessments:**
- AIM Project Baseline Assessment (2022): UNDP/EU; the most operationally detailed assessment of the GBV response system
- UNFPA (2023): Human Rights and Gender Review of GBV Laws in the Maldives
- UNFPA (2024): National Guidelines for Healthcare Providers on GBV, 2nd edition
- ADB GESI Project (54373-001) documentation
- UNDP/MoSFD WDC Capacity Assessment (2022)
- Appendix G Development Partners chapter (Part XX) of this encyclopedia

**Research:**
- Fulu, E. (2007): Maldives Study on Women's Health and Life Experiences – the foundational GBV prevalence study
- Ahmed & Riyaz (2024): Divorce causes analysis
- UNDP (2011): Human Development Report Maldives supplement
- Musawah (2018): Maldives Country Report on Muslim Family Law
- Youth Vulnerability Study 2019
- Transparency Maldives/IFES (2015): WDC Assessment
- MDN Women in Politics Reports (2019, 2022)
- HRCM Rights Side of Life Survey (2019)

**Donor chapters (encyclopedia chapters used as sources):**
- 09_Mental_Health.md
- 10_Education_Youth.md
- 11_Health_Systems.md
- 12_Housing_Poverty.md
- 14_Climate_Vulnerability.md
- 15_Cross_Cutting_Issues.md
- 20_Appendix_G_Development_Partners.md
- 24_CEDAW_Periodic_Report_Draft.md
- 25_Statistics_Compendium.md

### 5.69.2 Data Quality Considerations

Readers and users of this chapter should note the following data quality considerations:

**The DHS 2016-17 age:** The most comprehensive source of nationally representative gender data is now 8 years old. The Maldivian social context has changed substantially since 2016-17. Where more recent data exists (CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report 2025 statistics, MoSFD 2024 administrative data, Census 2022), it has been used; where only DHS 2016-17 data is available, this is noted and the data should be treated as indicative of trends rather than current values.

**The Fulu 2007 prevalence baseline:** The only national GBV prevalence study is 18 years old. All subsequent analysis of actual GBV prevalence must acknowledge this gap. The FPA case series is not a substitute for prevalence data.

**Administrative data limitations:** FPA case statistics, DJA divorce statistics, and NSDC treatment data are administrative data, not survey or census data. They measure help-seeking and official recording, not the underlying phenomenon. Case count increases may reflect improved services rather than increasing violence; decreases may reflect barriers to access rather than decreasing violence.

**Government framing in CEDAW reports:** The CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report (2025) is a government self-assessment. Its framing of progress tends toward the positive; civil society shadow reports provide independent verification that is not always available in published form for the most recent cycle.

**Geographic coverage:** Most national statistics mask major geographic variation between Malé and outer atolls. Where atoll-level disaggregation is available, it has been noted; where only national data is available, the reader should assume significant geographic variation around the national mean.

## 5.118 Final Notes: Sources, Updates, and Usage


### 5.104.1 How to Use This Chapter

This chapter is designed as a comprehensive reference – not a document to be read linearly from beginning to end, but one that can be consulted for specific topics through the section numbering system. Researchers seeking information on a specific topic should:

1. Use the section headings to locate the relevant section (§5.1-§5.104)
2. Follow cross-references to related sections within this chapter and to other encyclopedia chapters
3. Consult footnotes for the specific source for any cited statistic or finding
4. Supplement with the Statistics Compendium (Part XVII) for the most current numerical data
5. Consult Appendix G (Development Partners) for more detailed programme profiles

### 5.104.2 Necessary Updates

The following sections will require updating as new data becomes available:

- **§5.4 and §5.43 (statistics):** When a new national GBV prevalence survey is conducted; when new DHS data is collected; when CEDAW Concluding Observations are issued following the Seventh Periodic Report review
- **§5.9 (economic participation):** When new HIES or labour force survey data becomes available; when updated gender pay gap analysis is published
- **§5.8 (SRHR):** When updated MMR data is published; when new contraceptive prevalence data is available
- **§5.10 (political representation):** When 2028 parliamentary elections are held
- **§5.15 (climate):** As GRFS implementation progresses; when GCF project decisions are made
- **§5.18 (CEDAW):** When CEDAW Concluding Observations from the Seventh Periodic Report review are published (anticipated 2025-2026)
- **§5.19 (development partners):** As AIM Project, ADB GESI, and other programmes complete their cycles and new programmes are announced

### 5.104.3 Attribution

All statistics cited in this chapter are attributed to specific sources with dates. Where the source is a chapter of this encyclopedia (e.g., "Health Systems chapter §11.X"), readers should consult that chapter for the underlying primary source. Where the source is an external document (DHS 2016-17, CEDAW Seventh Periodic Report, AIM Project Baseline Assessment), these documents should be consulted directly for the full context of the cited statistic.

The encyclopedia's sourcing standard – footnotes for every statistic and finding, with source name, date, and link where available – has been maintained throughout. Where data was unavailable or unverifiable, this chapter explicitly notes the gap rather than presenting an estimate as a fact.

---

*End of all encyclopedic content for Chapter 5: Gender, Gender-Based Violence, and Women's Empowerment.*
